Iacono, John Hamm and David Widger\r\n\r\n*** START OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK THE AMERICAN ***\r\n\r\ncover \r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nThe American\r\n\r\nby Henry James\r\n\r\n1877\r\n\r\n\r\nContents\r\n\r\n\r\n CHAPTER I\r\n CHAPTER II\r\n CHAPTER III\r\n CHAPTER IV\r\n CHAPTER V\r\n CHAPTER VI\r\n CHAPTER VII\r\n CHAPTER VIII\r\n CHAPTER IX\r\n CHAPTER X\r\n CHAPTER XI\r\n CHAPTER XII\r\n CHAPTER XIII\r\n CHAPTER XIV\r\n CHAPTER XV\r\n CHAPTER XVI\r\n CHAPTER XVII\r\n CHAPTER XVIII\r\n CHAPTER XIX\r\n CHAPTER XX\r\n CHAPTER XXI\r\n CHAPTER XXII\r\n CHAPTER XXIII\r\n CHAPTER XXIV\r\n CHAPTER XXV\r\n CHAPTER XXVI\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER I\r\n\r\n\r\nOn a brilliant day in May, in the year 1868, a gentleman was reclining\r\nat his ease on the great circular divan which at that period occupied\r\nthe centre of the Salon Carr\xc3\xa9, in the Museum of the Louvre. This\r\ncommodious ottoman has since been removed, to the extreme regret of all\r\nweak-kneed lovers of the fine arts, but the gentleman in question had\r\ntaken serene possession of its softest spot, and, with his head thrown\r\nback and his legs outstretched, was staring at Murillo\xe2\x80\x99s beautiful\r\nmoon-borne Madonna in profound enjoyment of his posture. He had removed\r\nhis hat, and flung down beside him a little red guide-book and an\r\nopera-glass. The day was warm; he was heated with walking, and he\r\nrepeatedly passed his handkerchief over his forehead, with a somewhat\r\nwearied gesture. And yet he was evidently not a man to whom fatigue was\r\nfamiliar; long, lean, and muscular, he suggested the sort of vigor that\r\nis commonly known as \xe2\x80\x9ctoughness.\xe2\x80\x9d But his exertions on this particular\r\nday had been of an unwonted sort, and he had performed great physical\r\nfeats which left him less jaded than his tranquil stroll through the\r\nLouvre. He had looked out all the pictures to which an asterisk was\r\naffixed in those formidable pages of fine print in his B\xc3\xa4deker; his\r\nattention had been strained and his eyes dazzled, and he had sat down\r\nwith an \xc3\xa6sthetic headache. He had looked, moreover, not only at all the\r\npictures, but at all the copies that were going forward around them, in\r\nthe hands of those innumerable young women in irreproachable toilets\r\nwho devote themselves, in France, to the propagation of masterpieces,\r\nand if the truth must be told, he had often admired the copy much more\r\nthan the original. His physiognomy would have sufficiently indicated\r\nthat he was a shrewd and capable fellow, and in truth he had often sat\r\nup all night over a bristling bundle of accounts, and heard the cock\r\ncrow without a yawn. But Raphael and Titian and Rubens were a new kind\r\nof arithmetic, and they inspired our friend, for the first time in his\r\nlife, with a vague self-mistrust.\r\n\r\nAn observer with anything of an eye for national types would have had\r\nno difficulty in determining the local origin of this undeveloped\r\nconnoisseur, and indeed such an observer might have felt a certain\r\nhumorous relish of the almost ideal completeness with which he filled\r\nout the national mould. The gentleman on the divan was a powerful\r\nspecimen of an American. But he was not only a fine American; he was in\r\nthe first place, physically, a fine man. He appeared to possess that\r\nkind of health and strength which, when found in perfection, are the\r\nmost impressive\xe2\x80\x94the physical capital which the owner does nothing to\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ckeep up.\xe2\x80\x9d If he was a muscular Christian, it was quite without knowing\r\nit. If it was necessary to walk to a remote spot, he walked, but he had\r\nnever known himself to \xe2\x80\x9cexercise.\xe2\x80\x9d He had no theory with regard to cold\r\nbathing or the use of Indian clubs; he was neither an oarsman, a\r\nrifleman, nor a fencer\xe2\x80\x94he had never had time for these amusements\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nhe was quite unaware that the saddle is recommended for certain forms\r\nof indigestion. He was by inclination a temperate man; but he had\r\nsupped the night before his visit to the Louvre at the Caf\xc3\xa9\r\nAnglais\xe2\x80\x94someone had told him it was an experience not to be omitted\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nhe had slept none the less the sleep of the just. His usual attitude\r\nand carriage were of a rather relaxed and lounging kind, but when under\r\na special inspiration, he straightened himself, he looked like a\r\ngrenadier on parade. He never smoked. He had been assured\xe2\x80\x94such things\r\nare said\xe2\x80\x94that cigars were excellent for the health, and he was quite\r\ncapable of believing it; but he knew as little about tobacco as about\r\nhom\xc5\x93opathy. He had a very well-formed head, with a shapely, symmetrical\r\nbalance of the frontal and the occipital development, and a good deal\r\nof straight, rather dry brown hair. His complexion was brown, and his\r\nnose had a bold well-marked arch. His eye was of a clear, cold gray,\r\nand save for a rather abundant moustache he was clean-shaved. He had\r\nthe flat jaw and sinewy neck which are frequent in the American type;\r\nbut the traces of national origin are a matter of expression even more\r\nthan of feature, and it was in this respect that our friend\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncountenance was supremely eloquent. The discriminating observer we have\r\nbeen supposing might, however, perfectly have measured its\r\nexpressiveness, and yet have been at a loss to describe it. It had that\r\ntypical vagueness which is not vacuity, that blankness which is not\r\nsimplicity, that look of being committed to nothing in particular, of\r\nstanding in an attitude of general hospitality to the chances of life,\r\nof being very much at one\xe2\x80\x99s own disposal so characteristic of many\r\nAmerican faces. It was our friend\xe2\x80\x99s eye that chiefly told his story; an\r\neye in which innocence and experience were singularly blended. It was\r\nfull of contradictory suggestions, and though it was by no means the\r\nglowing orb of a hero of romance, you could find in it almost anything\r\nyou looked for. Frigid and yet friendly, frank yet cautious, shrewd yet\r\ncredulous, positive yet sceptical, confident yet shy, extremely\r\nintelligent and extremely good-humored, there was something vaguely\r\ndefiant in its concessions, and something profoundly reassuring in its\r\nreserve. The cut of this gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s moustache, with the two premature\r\nwrinkles in the cheek above it, and the fashion of his garments, in\r\nwhich an exposed shirt-front and a cerulean cravat played perhaps an\r\nobtrusive part, completed the conditions of his identity. We have\r\napproached him, perhaps, at a not especially favorable moment; he is by\r\nno means sitting for his portrait. But listless as he lounges there,\r\nrather baffled on the \xc3\xa6sthetic question, and guilty of the damning\r\nfault (as we have lately discovered it to be) of confounding the merit\r\nof the artist with that of his work (for he admires the squinting\r\nMadonna of the young lady with the boyish coiffure, because he thinks\r\nthe young lady herself uncommonly taking), he is a sufficiently\r\npromising acquaintance. Decision, salubrity, jocosity, prosperity, seem\r\nto hover within his call; he is evidently a practical man, but the idea\r\nin his case, has undefined and mysterious boundaries, which invite the\r\nimagination to bestir itself on his behalf.\r\n\r\nAs the little copyist proceeded with her work, she sent every now and\r\nthen a responsive glance toward her admirer. The cultivation of the\r\nfine arts appeared to necessitate, to her mind, a great deal of\r\nby-play, a great standing off with folded arms and head drooping from\r\nside to side, stroking of a dimpled chin with a dimpled hand, sighing\r\nand frowning and patting of the foot, fumbling in disordered tresses\r\nfor wandering hair-pins. These performances were accompanied by a\r\nrestless glance, which lingered longer than elsewhere upon the\r\ngentleman we have described. At last he rose abruptly, put on his hat,\r\nand approached the young lady. He placed himself before her picture and\r\nlooked at it for some moments, during which she pretended to be quite\r\nunconscious of his inspection. Then, addressing her with the single\r\nword which constituted the strength of his French vocabulary, and\r\nholding up one finger in a manner which appeared to him to illuminate\r\nhis meaning, \xe2\x80\x9c_Combien?_\xe2\x80\x9d he abruptly demanded.\r\n\r\nThe artist stared a moment, gave a little pout, shrugged her shoulders,\r\nput down her palette and brushes, and stood rubbing her hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much?\xe2\x80\x9d said our friend, in English. \xe2\x80\x9c_Combien?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur wishes to buy it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the young lady in French.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery pretty, _splendide. Combien?_\xe2\x80\x9d repeated the American.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt pleases monsieur, my little picture? It\xe2\x80\x99s a very beautiful\r\nsubject,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young lady.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Madonna, yes; I am not a Catholic, but I want to buy it.\r\n_Combien?_ Write it here.\xe2\x80\x9d And he took a pencil from his pocket and\r\nshowed her the fly-leaf of his guide-book. She stood looking at him and\r\nscratching her chin with the pencil. \xe2\x80\x9cIs it not for sale?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\nAnd as she still stood reflecting, and looking at him with an eye\r\nwhich, in spite of her desire to treat this avidity of patronage as a\r\nvery old story, betrayed an almost touching incredulity, he was afraid\r\nhe had offended her. She was simply trying to look indifferent, and\r\nwondering how far she might go. \xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t made a mistake\xe2\x80\x94_pas\r\ninsult\xc3\xa9_, no?\xe2\x80\x9d her interlocutor continued. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you understand a\r\nlittle English?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young lady\xe2\x80\x99s aptitude for playing a part at short notice was\r\nremarkable. She fixed him with her conscious, perceptive eye and asked\r\nhim if he spoke no French. Then, \xe2\x80\x9c_Donnez!_\xe2\x80\x9d she said briefly, and took\r\nthe open guide-book. In the upper corner of the fly-leaf she traced a\r\nnumber, in a minute and extremely neat hand. Then she handed back the\r\nbook and took up her palette again.\r\n\r\nOur friend read the number: \xe2\x80\x9c2,000 francs.\xe2\x80\x9d He said nothing for a time,\r\nbut stood looking at the picture, while the copyist began actively to\r\ndabble with her paint. \xe2\x80\x9cFor a copy, isn\xe2\x80\x99t that a good deal?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked\r\nat last. \xe2\x80\x9c_Pas beaucoup?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young lady raised her eyes from her palette, scanned him from head\r\nto foot, and alighted with admirable sagacity upon exactly the right\r\nanswer. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, it\xe2\x80\x99s a good deal. But my copy has remarkable qualities,\r\nit is worth nothing less.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe gentleman in whom we are interested understood no French, but I\r\nhave said he was intelligent, and here is a good chance to prove it. He\r\napprehended, by a natural instinct, the meaning of the young woman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nphrase, and it gratified him to think that she was so honest. Beauty,\r\ntalent, virtue; she combined everything! \xe2\x80\x9cBut you must finish it,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9c_finish_, you know;\xe2\x80\x9d and he pointed to the unpainted hand of the\r\nfigure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it shall be finished in perfection; in the perfection of\r\nperfections!\xe2\x80\x9d cried mademoiselle; and to confirm her promise, she\r\ndeposited a rosy blotch in the middle of the Madonna\xe2\x80\x99s cheek.\r\n\r\nBut the American frowned. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, too red, too red!\xe2\x80\x9d he rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cHer\r\ncomplexion,\xe2\x80\x9d pointing to the Murillo, \xe2\x80\x9cis\xe2\x80\x94more delicate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDelicate? Oh, it shall be delicate, monsieur; delicate as S\xc3\xa8vres\r\n_biscuit_. I am going to tone that down; I know all the secrets of my\r\nart. And where will you allow us to send it to you? Your address?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy address? Oh yes!\xe2\x80\x9d And the gentleman drew a card from his\r\npocket-book and wrote something upon it. Then hesitating a moment he\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cIf I don\xe2\x80\x99t like it when it it\xe2\x80\x99s finished, you know, I shall not\r\nbe obliged to take it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young lady seemed as good a guesser as himself. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I am very sure\r\nthat monsieur is not capricious,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with a roguish smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCapricious?\xe2\x80\x9d And at this monsieur began to laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cOh no, I\xe2\x80\x99m not\r\ncapricious. I am very faithful. I am very constant. _Comprenez?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur is constant; I understand perfectly. It\xe2\x80\x99s a rare virtue. To\r\nrecompense you, you shall have your picture on the first possible day;\r\nnext week\xe2\x80\x94as soon as it is dry. I will take the card of monsieur.\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\nshe took it and read his name: \xe2\x80\x9cChristopher Newman.\xe2\x80\x9d Then she tried to\r\nrepeat it aloud, and laughed at her bad accent. \xe2\x80\x9cYour English names are\r\nso droll!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDroll?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Newman, laughing too. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you ever hear of\r\nChristopher Columbus?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Bien s\xc3\xbbr!_ He invented America; a very great man. And is he your\r\npatron?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy patron?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour patron-saint, in the calendar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, exactly; my parents named me for him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur is American?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you see it?\xe2\x80\x9d monsieur inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you mean to carry my little picture away over there?\xe2\x80\x9d and she\r\nexplained her phrase with a gesture.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I mean to buy a great many pictures\xe2\x80\x94_beaucoup, beaucoup_,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nChristopher Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe honor is not less for me,\xe2\x80\x9d the young lady answered, \xe2\x80\x9cfor I am sure\r\nmonsieur has a great deal of taste.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you must give me your card,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman said; \xe2\x80\x9cyour card, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young lady looked severe for an instant, and then said, \xe2\x80\x9cMy father\r\nwill wait upon you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut this time Mr. Newman\xe2\x80\x99s powers of divination were at fault. \xe2\x80\x9cYour\r\ncard, your address,\xe2\x80\x9d he simply repeated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy address?\xe2\x80\x9d said mademoiselle. Then with a little shrug, \xe2\x80\x9cHappily for\r\nyou, you are an American! It is the first time I ever gave my card to a\r\ngentleman.\xe2\x80\x9d And, taking from her pocket a rather greasy portemonnaie,\r\nshe extracted from it a small glazed visiting card, and presented the\r\nlatter to her patron. It was neatly inscribed in pencil, with a great\r\nmany flourishes, \xe2\x80\x9cMlle. No\xc3\xa9mie Nioche.\xe2\x80\x9d But Mr. Newman, unlike his\r\ncompanion, read the name with perfect gravity; all French names to him\r\nwere equally droll.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd precisely, here is my father, who has come to escort me home,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie. \xe2\x80\x9cHe speaks English. He will arrange with\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d And she turned to welcome a little old gentleman who came\r\nshuffling up, peering over his spectacles at Newman.\r\n\r\nM. Nioche wore a glossy wig, of an unnatural color which overhung his\r\nlittle meek, white, vacant face, and left it hardly more expressive\r\nthan the unfeatured block upon which these articles are displayed in\r\nthe barber\xe2\x80\x99s window. He was an exquisite image of shabby gentility. His\r\nscant ill-made coat, desperately brushed, his darned gloves, his highly\r\npolished boots, his rusty, shapely hat, told the story of a person who\r\nhad \xe2\x80\x9chad losses\xe2\x80\x9d and who clung to the spirit of nice habits even though\r\nthe letter had been hopelessly effaced. Among other things M. Nioche\r\nhad lost courage. Adversity had not only ruined him, it had frightened\r\nhim, and he was evidently going through his remnant of life on tiptoe,\r\nfor fear of waking up the hostile fates. If this strange gentleman was\r\nsaying anything improper to his daughter, M. Nioche would entreat him\r\nhuskily, as a particular favor, to forbear; but he would admit at the\r\nsame time that he was very presumptuous to ask for particular favors.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur has bought my picture,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfinished you\xe2\x80\x99ll carry it to him in a cab.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn a cab!\xe2\x80\x9d cried M. Nioche; and he stared, in a bewildered way, as if\r\nhe had seen the sun rising at midnight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you the young lady\xe2\x80\x99s father?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI think she said you\r\nspeak English.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeak English\xe2\x80\x94yes,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man slowly rubbing his hands. \xe2\x80\x9cI will\r\nbring it in a cab.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay something, then,\xe2\x80\x9d cried his daughter. \xe2\x80\x9cThank him a little\xe2\x80\x94not too\r\nmuch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA little, my daughter, a little?\xe2\x80\x9d said M. Nioche perplexed. \xe2\x80\x9cHow\r\nmuch?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo thousand!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t make a fuss or he\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ntake back his word.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo thousand!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the old man, and he began to fumble for his\r\nsnuff-box. He looked at Newman from head to foot; he looked at his\r\ndaughter and then at the picture. \xe2\x80\x9cTake care you don\xe2\x80\x99t spoil it!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ncried almost sublimely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must go home,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is a good day\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwork. Take care how you carry it!\xe2\x80\x9d And she began to put up her\r\nutensils.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can I thank you?\xe2\x80\x9d said M. Nioche. \xe2\x80\x9cMy English does not suffice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish I spoke French as well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, good-naturedly. \xe2\x80\x9cYour\r\ndaughter is very clever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d and M. Nioche looked over his spectacles with tearful eyes\r\nand nodded several times with a world of sadness. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has had an\r\neducation\xe2\x80\x94_tr\xc3\xa8s-sup\xc3\xa9rieure!_ Nothing was spared. Lessons in pastel at\r\nten francs the lesson, lessons in oil at twelve francs. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t look\r\nat the francs then. She\xe2\x80\x99s an _artiste_, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo I understand you to say that you have had reverses?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReverses? Oh, sir, misfortunes\xe2\x80\x94terrible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnsuccessful in business, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery unsuccessful, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, never fear, you\xe2\x80\x99ll get on your legs again,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman cheerily.\r\n\r\nThe old man drooped his head on one side and looked at him with an\r\nexpression of pain, as if this were an unfeeling jest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does he say?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie.\r\n\r\nM. Nioche took a pinch of snuff. \xe2\x80\x9cHe says I will make my fortune\r\nagain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps he will help you. And what else?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe says thou art very clever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very possible. You believe it yourself, my father?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBelieve it, my daughter? With this evidence!\xe2\x80\x9d And the old man turned\r\nafresh, with a staring, wondering homage, to the audacious daub on the\r\neasel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsk him, then, if he would not like to learn French.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo learn French?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo take lessons.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo take lessons, my daughter? From thee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom me, my child? How should I give lessons?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Pas de raisons!_ Ask him immediately!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie, with\r\nsoft brevity.\r\n\r\nM. Nioche stood aghast, but under his daughter\xe2\x80\x99s eye he collected his\r\nwits, and, doing his best to assume an agreeable smile, he executed her\r\ncommands. \xe2\x80\x9cWould it please you to receive instruction in our beautiful\r\nlanguage?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired, with an appealing quaver.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo study French?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman, staring.\r\n\r\nM. Nioche pressed his finger-tips together and slowly raised his\r\nshoulders. \xe2\x80\x9cA little conversation!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConversation\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s it!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie, who had caught\r\nthe word. \xe2\x80\x9cThe conversation of the best society.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur French conversation is famous, you know,\xe2\x80\x9d M. Nioche ventured to\r\ncontinue. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a great talent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut isn\xe2\x80\x99t it awfully difficult?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman, very simply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot to a man of _esprit_, like monsieur, an admirer of beauty in every\r\nform!\xe2\x80\x9d and M. Nioche cast a significant glance at his daughter\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nMadonna.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t fancy myself chattering French!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman with a laugh.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet, I suppose that the more a man knows the better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur expresses that very happily. _H\xc3\xa9las, oui!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose it would help me a great deal, knocking about Paris, to know\r\nthe language.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, there are so many things monsieur must want to say: difficult\r\nthings!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything I want to say is difficult. But you give lessons?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoor M. Nioche was embarrassed; he smiled more appealingly. \xe2\x80\x9cI am not a\r\nregular professor,\xe2\x80\x9d he admitted. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t nevertheless tell him that\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m a professor,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to his daughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell him it\xe2\x80\x99s a very exceptional chance,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mademoiselle\r\nNo\xc3\xa9mie; \xe2\x80\x9can _homme du monde_\xe2\x80\x94one gentleman conversing with another!\r\nRemember what you are\xe2\x80\x94what you have been!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA teacher of languages in neither case! Much more formerly and much\r\nless to-day! And if he asks the price of the lessons?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe won\xe2\x80\x99t ask it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat he pleases, I may say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever! That\xe2\x80\x99s bad style.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf he asks, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie had put on her bonnet and was tying the ribbons.\r\nShe smoothed them out, with her soft little chin thrust forward. \xe2\x80\x9cTen\r\nfrancs,\xe2\x80\x9d she said quickly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my daughter! I shall never dare.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t dare, then! He won\xe2\x80\x99t ask till the end of the lessons, and then I\r\nwill make out the bill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nM. Nioche turned to the confiding foreigner again, and stood rubbing\r\nhis hands, with an air of seeming to plead guilty which was not\r\nintenser only because it was habitually so striking. It never occurred\r\nto Newman to ask him for a guarantee of his skill in imparting\r\ninstruction; he supposed of course M. Nioche knew his own language, and\r\nhis appealing forlornness was quite the perfection of what the\r\nAmerican, for vague reasons, had always associated with all elderly\r\nforeigners of the lesson-giving class. Newman had never reflected upon\r\nphilological processes. His chief impression with regard to\r\nascertaining those mysterious correlatives of his familiar English\r\nvocables which were current in this extraordinary city of Paris was,\r\nthat it was simply a matter of a good deal of unwonted and rather\r\nridiculous muscular effort on his own part. \xe2\x80\x9cHow did you learn\r\nEnglish?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked of the old man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I was young, before my miseries. Oh, I was wide awake, then. My\r\nfather was a great _commer\xc3\xa7ant_; he placed me for a year in a\r\ncounting-house in England. Some of it stuck to me; but much I have\r\nforgotten!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much French can I learn in a month?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does he say?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie.\r\n\r\nM. Nioche explained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will speak like an angel!\xe2\x80\x9d said his daughter.\r\n\r\nBut the native integrity which had been vainly exerted to secure M.\r\nNioche\xe2\x80\x99s commercial prosperity flickered up again. \xe2\x80\x9c_Dame_, monsieur!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe answered. \xe2\x80\x9cAll I can teach you!\xe2\x80\x9d And then, recovering himself at a\r\nsign from his daughter, \xe2\x80\x9cI will wait upon you at your hotel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, I should like to learn French,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman went on, with\r\ndemocratic confidingness. \xe2\x80\x9cHang me if I should ever have thought of it!\r\nI took for granted it was impossible. But if you learned my language,\r\nwhy shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t I learn yours?\xe2\x80\x9d and his frank, friendly laugh drew the\r\nsting from the jest. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly, if we are going to converse, you know, you\r\nmust think of something cheerful to converse about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very good, sir; I am overcome!\xe2\x80\x9d said M. Nioche, throwing out\r\nhis hands. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you have cheerfulness and happiness for two!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman more seriously. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must be bright and lively;\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s part of the bargain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nM. Nioche bowed, with his hand on his heart. \xe2\x80\x9cVery well, sir; you have\r\nalready made me lively.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome and bring me my picture then; I will pay you for it, and we will\r\ntalk about that. That will be a cheerful subject!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie had collected her accessories, and she gave the\r\nprecious Madonna in charge to her father, who retreated backwards out\r\nof sight, holding it at arm\xe2\x80\x99s-length and reiterating his obeisance. The\r\nyoung lady gathered her shawl about her like a perfect Parisienne, and\r\nit was with the smile of a Parisienne that she took leave of her\r\npatron.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II\r\n\r\n\r\nHe wandered back to the divan and seated himself on the other side, in\r\nview of the great canvas on which Paul Veronese had depicted the\r\nmarriage-feast of Cana. Wearied as he was he found the picture\r\nentertaining; it had an illusion for him; it satisfied his conception,\r\nwhich was ambitious, of what a splendid banquet should be. In the\r\nleft-hand corner of the picture is a young woman with yellow tresses\r\nconfined in a golden head-dress; she is bending forward and listening,\r\nwith the smile of a charming woman at a dinner-party, to her neighbor.\r\nNewman detected her in the crowd, admired her, and perceived that she\r\ntoo had her votive copyist\xe2\x80\x94a young man with his hair standing on end.\r\nSuddenly he became conscious of the germ of the mania of the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ccollector;\xe2\x80\x9d he had taken the first step; why should he not go on? It\r\nwas only twenty minutes before that he had bought the first picture of\r\nhis life, and now he was already thinking of art-patronage as a\r\nfascinating pursuit. His reflections quickened his good-humor, and he\r\nwas on the point of approaching the young man with another \xe2\x80\x9c_Combien?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nTwo or three facts in this relation are noticeable, although the\r\nlogical chain which connects them may seem imperfect. He knew\r\nMademoiselle Nioche had asked too much; he bore her no grudge for doing\r\nso, and he was determined to pay the young man exactly the proper sum.\r\nAt this moment, however, his attention was attracted by a gentleman who\r\nhad come from another part of the room and whose manner was that of a\r\nstranger to the gallery, although he was equipped with neither\r\nguide-book nor opera-glass. He carried a white sun-umbrella, lined with\r\nblue silk, and he strolled in front of the Paul Veronese, vaguely\r\nlooking at it, but much too near to see anything but the grain of the\r\ncanvas. Opposite to Christopher Newman he paused and turned, and then\r\nour friend, who had been observing him, had a chance to verify a\r\nsuspicion aroused by an imperfect view of his face. The result of this\r\nlarger scrutiny was that he presently sprang to his feet, strode across\r\nthe room, and, with an outstretched hand, arrested the gentleman with\r\nthe blue-lined umbrella. The latter stared, but put out his hand at a\r\nventure. He was corpulent and rosy, and though his countenance, which\r\nwas ornamented with a beautiful flaxen beard, carefully divided in the\r\nmiddle and brushed outward at the sides, was not remarkable for\r\nintensity of expression, he looked like a person who would willingly\r\nshake hands with anyone. I know not what Newman thought of his face,\r\nbut he found a want of response in his grasp.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, come, come,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, laughing; \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t say, now, you don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nme\xe2\x80\x94if I have _not_ got a white parasol!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe sound of his voice quickened the other\xe2\x80\x99s memory, his face expanded\r\nto its fullest capacity, and he also broke into a laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy,\r\nNewman\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll be blowed! Where in the world\xe2\x80\x94I declare\xe2\x80\x94who would have\r\nthought? You know you have changed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou haven\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot for the better, no doubt. When did you get here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree days ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t you let me know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had no idea _you_ were here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been here these six years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must be eight or nine since we met.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething of that sort. We were very young.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was in St. Louis, during the war. You were in the army.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, not I! But you were.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe I was.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou came out all right?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI came out with my legs and arms\xe2\x80\x94and with satisfaction. All that seems\r\nvery far away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how long have you been in Europe?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeventeen days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFirst time?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, very much so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMade your everlasting fortune?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChristopher Newman was silent a moment, and then with a tranquil smile\r\nhe answered, \xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd come to Paris to spend it, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we shall see. So they carry those parasols here\xe2\x80\x94the men-folk?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course they do. They\xe2\x80\x99re great things. They understand comfort out\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere do you buy them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnywhere, everywhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Tristram, I\xe2\x80\x99m glad to get hold of you. You can show me the\r\nropes. I suppose you know Paris inside out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Tristram gave a mellow smile of self-gratulation. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I guess\r\nthere are not many men that can show me much. I\xe2\x80\x99ll take care of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a pity you were not here a few minutes ago. I have just bought a\r\npicture. You might have put the thing through for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBought a picture?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Tristram, looking vaguely round at the\r\nwalls. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, do they sell them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean a copy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I see. These,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Tristram, nodding at the Titians and\r\nVandykes, \xe2\x80\x9cthese, I suppose, are originals.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope so,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want a copy of a copy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Tristram, mysteriously, \xe2\x80\x9cyou can never tell. They\r\nimitate, you know, so deucedly well. It\xe2\x80\x99s like the jewellers, with\r\ntheir false stones. Go into the Palais Royal, there; you see\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Imitation\xe2\x80\x99 on half the windows. The law obliges them to stick it on,\r\nyou know; but you can\xe2\x80\x99t tell the things apart. To tell the truth,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr.\r\nTristram continued, with a wry face, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t do much in pictures. I\r\nleave that to my wife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you have got a wife?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t I mention it? She\xe2\x80\x99s a very nice woman; you must know her. She\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nup there in the Avenue d\xe2\x80\x99I\xc3\xa9na.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you are regularly fixed\xe2\x80\x94house and children and all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, a tip-top house and a couple of youngsters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Christopher Newman, stretching his arms a little, with a\r\nsigh, \xe2\x80\x9cI envy you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no! you don\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mr. Tristram, giving him a little poke\r\nwith his parasol.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon; I do!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you won\xe2\x80\x99t, then, when\xe2\x80\x94when\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t certainly mean when I have seen your establishment?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen you have seen Paris, my boy. You want to be your own master\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I have been my own master all my life, and I\xe2\x80\x99m tired of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, try Paris. How old are you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThirty-six.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_C\xe2\x80\x99est le bel \xc3\xa2ge_, as they say here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does that mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt means that a man shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t send away his plate till he has eaten\r\nhis fill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll that? I have just made arrangements to take French lessons.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you don\xe2\x80\x99t want any lessons. You\xe2\x80\x99ll pick it up. I never took any.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you speak French as well as English?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetter!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Tristram, roundly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a splendid language. You\r\ncan say all sorts of bright things in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d said Christopher Newman, with an earnest desire for\r\ninformation, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you must be bright to begin with.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a bit; that\xe2\x80\x99s just the beauty of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two friends, as they exchanged these remarks, had remained standing\r\nwhere they met, and leaning against the rail which protected the\r\npictures. Mr. Tristram at last declared that he was overcome with\r\nfatigue and should be happy to sit down. Newman recommended in the\r\nhighest terms the great divan on which he had been lounging, and they\r\nprepared to seat themselves. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is a great place; isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nNewman, with ardor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGreat place, great place. Finest thing in the world.\xe2\x80\x9d And then,\r\nsuddenly, Mr. Tristram hesitated and looked about him. \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose they\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t let you smoke here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman stared. \xe2\x80\x9cSmoke? I\xe2\x80\x99m sure I don\xe2\x80\x99t know. You know the regulations\r\nbetter than I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI? I never was here before!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever! in six years?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe my wife dragged me here once when we first came to Paris,\r\nbut I never found my way back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you say you know Paris so well!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t call this Paris!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mr. Tristram, with assurance. \xe2\x80\x9cCome;\r\nlet\xe2\x80\x99s go over to the Palais Royal and have a smoke.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t smoke,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA drink, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Mr. Tristram led his companion away. They passed through the\r\nglorious halls of the Louvre, down the staircases, along the cool, dim\r\ngalleries of sculpture, and out into the enormous court. Newman looked\r\nabout him as he went, but he made no comments, and it was only when\r\nthey at last emerged into the open air that he said to his friend, \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nseems to me that in your place I should have come here once a week.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Tristram. \xe2\x80\x9cYou think so, but you\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t. You wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have had time. You would always mean to go, but\r\nyou never would go. There\xe2\x80\x99s better fun than that, here in Paris.\r\nItaly\xe2\x80\x99s the place to see pictures; wait till you get there. There you\r\nhave to go; you can\xe2\x80\x99t do anything else. It\xe2\x80\x99s an awful country; you\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t get a decent cigar. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know why I went in there, to-day; I\r\nwas strolling along, rather hard up for amusement. I sort of noticed\r\nthe Louvre as I passed, and I thought I would go in and see what was\r\ngoing on. But if I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t found you there I should have felt rather\r\nsold. Hang it, I don\xe2\x80\x99t care for pictures; I prefer the reality!\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\nMr. Tristram tossed off this happy formula with an assurance which the\r\nnumerous class of persons suffering from an overdose of \xe2\x80\x9cculture\xe2\x80\x9d might\r\nhave envied him.\r\n\r\nThe two gentlemen proceeded along the Rue de Rivoli and into the Palais\r\nRoyal, where they seated themselves at one of the little tables\r\nstationed at the door of the caf\xc3\xa9 which projects into the great open\r\nquadrangle. The place was filled with people, the fountains were\r\nspouting, a band was playing, clusters of chairs were gathered beneath\r\nall the lime-trees, and buxom, white-capped nurses, seated along the\r\nbenches, were offering to their infant charges the amplest facilities\r\nfor nutrition. There was an easy, homely gaiety in the whole scene, and\r\nChristopher Newman felt that it was most characteristically Parisian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now,\xe2\x80\x9d began Mr. Tristram, when they had tested the decoction which\r\nhe had caused to be served to them, \xe2\x80\x9cnow just give an account of\r\nyourself. What are your ideas, what are your plans, where have you come\r\nfrom and where are you going? In the first place, where are you\r\nstaying?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt the Grand Hotel,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\nMr. Tristram puckered his plump visage. \xe2\x80\x9cThat won\xe2\x80\x99t do! You must\r\nchange.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChange?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s the finest hotel I ever was in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t want a \xe2\x80\x98fine\xe2\x80\x99 hotel; you want something small and quiet and\r\nelegant, where your bell is answered and you\xe2\x80\x94your person is\r\nrecognized.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey keep running to see if I have rung before I have touched the\r\nbell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman \xe2\x80\x9cand as for my person they are always bowing and\r\nscraping to it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you are always tipping them. That\xe2\x80\x99s very bad style.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlways? By no means. A man brought me something yesterday, and then\r\nstood loafing in a beggarly manner. I offered him a chair and asked him\r\nif he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t sit down. Was that bad style?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut he bolted, instantly. At any rate, the place amuses me. Hang your\r\nelegance, if it bores me. I sat in the court of the Grand Hotel last\r\nnight until two o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the morning, watching the coming and going,\r\nand the people knocking about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re easily pleased. But you can do as you choose\xe2\x80\x94a man in your\r\nshoes. You have made a pile of money, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have made enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHappy the man who can say that? Enough for what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEnough to rest awhile, to forget the confounded thing, to look about\r\nme, to see the world, to have a good time, to improve my mind, and, if\r\nthe fancy takes me, to marry a wife.\xe2\x80\x9d Newman spoke slowly, with a\r\ncertain dryness of accent and with frequent pauses. This was his\r\nhabitual mode of utterance, but it was especially marked in the words I\r\nhave just quoted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJupiter! There\xe2\x80\x99s a programme!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mr. Tristram. \xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, all\r\nthat takes money, especially the wife; unless indeed she gives it, as\r\nmine did. And what\xe2\x80\x99s the story? How have you done it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman had pushed his hat back from his forehead, folded his arms, and\r\nstretched his legs. He listened to the music, he looked about him at\r\nthe bustling crowd, at the plashing fountains, at the nurses and the\r\nbabies. \xe2\x80\x9cI have worked!\xe2\x80\x9d he answered at last.\r\n\r\nTristram looked at him for some moments, and allowed his placid eyes to\r\nmeasure his friend\xe2\x80\x99s generous longitude and rest upon his comfortably\r\ncontemplative face. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat have you worked at?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, at several things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you\xe2\x80\x99re a smart fellow, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman continued to look at the nurses and babies; they imparted to the\r\nscene a kind of primordial, pastoral simplicity. \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at\r\nlast, \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose I am.\xe2\x80\x9d And then, in answer to his companion\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ninquiries, he related briefly his history since their last meeting. It\r\nwas an intensely Western story, and it dealt with enterprises which it\r\nwill be needless to introduce to the reader in detail. Newman had come\r\nout of the war with a brevet of brigadier-general, an honor which in\r\nthis case\xe2\x80\x94without invidious comparisons\xe2\x80\x94had lighted upon shoulders\r\namply competent to bear it. But though he could manage a fight, when\r\nneed was, Newman heartily disliked the business; his four years in the\r\narmy had left him with an angry, bitter sense of the waste of precious\r\nthings\xe2\x80\x94life and time and money and \xe2\x80\x9csmartness\xe2\x80\x9d and the early freshness\r\nof purpose; and he had addressed himself to the pursuits of peace with\r\npassionate zest and energy. He was of course as penniless when he\r\nplucked off his shoulder-straps as when he put them on, and the only\r\ncapital at his disposal was his dogged resolution and his lively\r\nperception of ends and means. Exertion and action were as natural to\r\nhim as respiration; a more completely healthy mortal had never trod the\r\nelastic soil of the West. His experience, moreover, was as wide as his\r\ncapacity; when he was fourteen years old, necessity had taken him by\r\nhis slim young shoulders and pushed him into the street, to earn that\r\nnight\xe2\x80\x99s supper. He had not earned it but he had earned the next\r\nnight\xe2\x80\x99s, and afterwards, whenever he had had none, it was because he\r\nhad gone without it to use the money for something else, a keener\r\npleasure or a finer profit. He had turned his hand, with his brain in\r\nit, to many things; he had been enterprising, in an eminent sense of\r\nthe term; he had been adventurous and even reckless, and he had known\r\nbitter failure as well as brilliant success; but he was a born\r\nexperimentalist, and he had always found something to enjoy in the\r\npressure of necessity, even when it was as irritating as the haircloth\r\nshirt of the medi\xc3\xa6val monk. At one time failure seemed inexorably his\r\nportion; ill-luck became his bed-fellow, and whatever he touched he\r\nturned, not to gold, but to ashes. His most vivid conception of a\r\nsupernatural element in the world\xe2\x80\x99s affairs had come to him once when\r\nthis pertinacity of misfortune was at its climax; there seemed to him\r\nsomething stronger in life than his own will. But the mysterious\r\nsomething could only be the devil, and he was accordingly seized with\r\nan intense personal enmity to this impertinent force. He had known what\r\nit was to have utterly exhausted his credit, to be unable to raise a\r\ndollar, and to find himself at nightfall in a strange city, without a\r\npenny to mitigate its strangeness. It was under these circumstances\r\nthat he made his entrance into San Francisco, the scene, subsequently,\r\nof his happiest strokes of fortune. If he did not, like Dr. Franklin in\r\nPhiladelphia, march along the street munching a penny-loaf, it was only\r\nbecause he had not the penny-loaf necessary to the performance. In his\r\ndarkest days he had had but one simple, practical impulse\xe2\x80\x94the desire,\r\nas he would have phrased it, to see the thing through. He did so at\r\nlast, buffeted his way into smooth waters, and made money largely. It\r\nmust be admitted, rather nakedly, that Christopher Newman\xe2\x80\x99s sole aim in\r\nlife had been to make money; what he had been placed in the world for\r\nwas, to his own perception, simply to wrest a fortune, the bigger the\r\nbetter, from defiant opportunity. This idea completely filled his\r\nhorizon and satisfied his imagination. Upon the uses of money, upon\r\nwhat one might do with a life into which one had succeeded in injecting\r\nthe golden stream, he had up to his thirty-fifth year very scantily\r\nreflected. Life had been for him an open game, and he had played for\r\nhigh stakes. He had won at last and carried off his winnings; and now\r\nwhat was he to do with them? He was a man to whom, sooner or later, the\r\nquestion was sure to present itself, and the answer to it belongs to\r\nour story. A vague sense that more answers were possible than his\r\nphilosophy had hitherto dreamt of had already taken possession of him,\r\nand it seemed softly and agreeably to deepen as he lounged in this\r\nbrilliant corner of Paris with his friend.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must confess,\xe2\x80\x9d he presently went on, \xe2\x80\x9cthat here I don\xe2\x80\x99t feel at all\r\nsmart. My remarkable talents seem of no use. I feel as simple as a\r\nlittle child, and a little child might take me by the hand and lead me\r\nabout.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99ll be your little child,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tristram, jovially; \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll take\r\nyou by the hand. Trust yourself to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am a good worker,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman continued, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I rather think I am a poor\r\nloafer. I have come abroad to amuse myself, but I doubt whether I know\r\nhow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that\xe2\x80\x99s easily learned.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I may perhaps learn it, but I am afraid I shall never do it by\r\nrote. I have the best will in the world about it, but my genius doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlie in that direction. As a loafer I shall never be original, as I take\r\nit that you are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tristram, \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose I am original; like all those immoral\r\npictures in the Louvre.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBesides,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman continued, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to work at pleasure, any\r\nmore than I played at work. I want to take it easily. I feel\r\ndeliciously lazy, and I should like to spend six months as I am now,\r\nsitting under a tree and listening to a band. There\xe2\x80\x99s only one thing; I\r\nwant to hear some good music.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMusic and pictures! Lord, what refined tastes! You are what my wife\r\ncalls intellectual. I ain\xe2\x80\x99t, a bit. But we can find something better\r\nfor you to do than to sit under a tree. To begin with, you must come to\r\nthe club.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat club?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Occidental. You will see all the Americans there; all the best of\r\nthem, at least. Of course you play poker?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I say,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman, with energy, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are not going to lock me\r\nup in a club and stick me down at a card-table! I haven\xe2\x80\x99t come all this\r\nway for that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat the deuce _have_ you come for! You were glad enough to play poker\r\nin St. Louis, I recollect, when you cleaned me out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have come to see Europe, to get the best out of it I can. I want to\r\nsee all the great things, and do what the clever people do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe clever people? Much obliged. You set me down as a blockhead,\r\nthen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman was sitting sidewise in his chair, with his elbow on the back\r\nand his head leaning on his hand. Without moving he looked a while at\r\nhis companion with his dry, guarded, half-inscrutable, and yet\r\naltogether good-natured smile. \xe2\x80\x9cIntroduce me to your wife!\xe2\x80\x9d he said at\r\nlast.\r\n\r\nTristram bounced about in his chair. \xe2\x80\x9cUpon my word, I won\xe2\x80\x99t. She\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t want any help to turn up her nose at me, nor do you, either!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t turn up my nose at you, my dear fellow; nor at anyone, or\r\nanything. I\xe2\x80\x99m not proud, I assure you I\xe2\x80\x99m not proud. That\xe2\x80\x99s why I am\r\nwilling to take example by the clever people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if I\xe2\x80\x99m not the rose, as they say here, I have lived near it. I\r\ncan show you some clever people, too. Do you know General Packard? Do\r\nyou know C. P. Hatch? Do you know Miss Kitty Upjohn?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be happy to make their acquaintance; I want to cultivate\r\nsociety.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTristram seemed restless and suspicious; he eyed his friend askance,\r\nand then, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you up to, anyway?\xe2\x80\x9d he demanded. \xe2\x80\x9cAre you going to\r\nwrite a book?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChristopher Newman twisted one end of his moustache a while, in\r\nsilence, and at last he made answer. \xe2\x80\x9cOne day, a couple of months ago,\r\nsomething very curious happened to me. I had come on to New York on\r\nsome important business; it was rather a long story\xe2\x80\x94a question of\r\ngetting ahead of another party, in a certain particular way, in the\r\nstock-market. This other party had once played me a very mean trick. I\r\nowed him a grudge, I felt awfully savage at the time, and I vowed that,\r\nwhen I got a chance, I would, figuratively speaking, put his nose out\r\nof joint. There was a matter of some sixty thousand dollars at stake.\r\nIf I put it out of his way, it was a blow the fellow would feel, and he\r\nreally deserved no quarter. I jumped into a hack and went about my\r\nbusiness, and it was in this hack\xe2\x80\x94this immortal, historical hack\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nthe curious thing I speak of occurred. It was a hack like any other,\r\nonly a trifle dirtier, with a greasy line along the top of the drab\r\ncushions, as if it had been used for a great many Irish funerals. It is\r\npossible I took a nap; I had been traveling all night, and though I was\r\nexcited with my errand, I felt the want of sleep. At all events I woke\r\nup suddenly, from a sleep or from a kind of a reverie, with the most\r\nextraordinary feeling in the world\xe2\x80\x94a mortal disgust for the thing I was\r\ngoing to do. It came upon me like _that!_\xe2\x80\x9d and he snapped his\r\nfingers\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cas abruptly as an old wound that begins to ache. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ntell the meaning of it; I only felt that I loathed the whole business\r\nand wanted to wash my hands of it. The idea of losing that sixty\r\nthousand dollars, of letting it utterly slide and scuttle and never\r\nhearing of it again, seemed the sweetest thing in the world. And all\r\nthis took place quite independently of my will, and I sat watching it\r\nas if it were a play at the theatre. I could feel it going on inside of\r\nme. You may depend upon it that there are things going on inside of us\r\nthat we understand mighty little about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJupiter! you make my flesh creep!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Tristram. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd while you sat\r\nin your hack, watching the play, as you call it, the other man marched\r\nin and bagged your sixty thousand dollars?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not the least idea. I hope so, poor devil! but I never found\r\nout. We pulled up in front of the place I was going to in Wall Street,\r\nbut I sat still in the carriage, and at last the driver scrambled down\r\noff his seat to see whether his carriage had not turned into a hearse.\r\nI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have got out, any more than if I had been a corpse. What was\r\nthe matter with me? Momentary idiocy, you\xe2\x80\x99ll say. What I wanted to get\r\nout of was Wall Street. I told the man to drive down to the Brooklyn\r\nferry and to cross over. When we were over, I told him to drive me out\r\ninto the country. As I had told him originally to drive for dear life\r\ndown town, I suppose he thought me insane. Perhaps I was, but in that\r\ncase I am insane still. I spent the morning looking at the first green\r\nleaves on Long Island. I was sick of business; I wanted to throw it all\r\nup and break off short; I had money enough, or if I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t I ought to\r\nhave. I seemed to feel a new man inside my old skin, and I longed for a\r\nnew world. When you want a thing so very badly you had better treat\r\nyourself to it. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t understand the matter, not in the least; but I\r\ngave the old horse the bridle and let him find his way. As soon as I\r\ncould get out of the game I sailed for Europe. That is how I come to be\r\nsitting here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ought to have bought up that hack,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tristram; \xe2\x80\x9cit isn\xe2\x80\x99t a\r\nsafe vehicle to have about. And you have really sold out, then; you\r\nhave retired from business?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have made over my hand to a friend; when I feel disposed, I can take\r\nup the cards again. I dare say that a twelvemonth hence the operation\r\nwill be reversed. The pendulum will swing back again. I shall be\r\nsitting in a gondola or on a dromedary, and all of a sudden I shall\r\nwant to clear out. But for the present I am perfectly free. I have even\r\nbargained that I am to receive no business letters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s a real _caprice de prince_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tristram. \xe2\x80\x9cI back out; a\r\npoor devil like me can\xe2\x80\x99t help you to spend such very magnificent\r\nleisure as that. You should get introduced to the crowned heads.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman looked at him a moment, and then, with his easy smile, \xe2\x80\x9cHow does\r\none do it?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, I like that!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Tristram. \xe2\x80\x9cIt shows you are in earnest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I am in earnest. Didn\xe2\x80\x99t I say I wanted the best? I know the\r\nbest can\xe2\x80\x99t be had for mere money, but I rather think money will do a\r\ngood deal. In addition, I am willing to take a good deal of trouble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not bashful, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t the least idea. I want the biggest kind of entertainment a\r\nman can get. People, places, art, nature, everything! I want to see the\r\ntallest mountains, and the bluest lakes, and the finest pictures and\r\nthe handsomest churches, and the most celebrated men, and the most\r\nbeautiful women.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSettle down in Paris, then. There are no mountains that I know of, and\r\nthe only lake is in the Bois du Boulogne, and not particularly blue.\r\nBut there is everything else: plenty of pictures and churches, no end\r\nof celebrated men, and several beautiful women.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I can\xe2\x80\x99t settle down in Paris at this season, just as summer is\r\ncoming on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, for the summer go up to Trouville.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is Trouville?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe French Newport. Half the Americans go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it anywhere near the Alps?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout as near as Newport is to the Rocky Mountains.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I want to see Mont Blanc,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cand Amsterdam, and the\r\nRhine, and a lot of places. Venice in particular. I have great ideas\r\nabout Venice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Tristram, rising, \xe2\x80\x9cI see I shall have to introduce you\r\nto my wife!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III\r\n\r\n\r\nHe performed this ceremony on the following day, when, by appointment,\r\nChristopher Newman went to dine with him. Mr. and Mrs. Tristram lived\r\nbehind one of those chalk-colored fa\xc3\xa7ades which decorate with their\r\npompous sameness the broad avenues manufactured by Baron Haussmann in\r\nthe neighborhood of the Arc de Triomphe. Their apartment was rich in\r\nthe modern conveniences, and Tristram lost no time in calling his\r\nvisitor\xe2\x80\x99s attention to their principal household treasures, the\r\ngas-lamps and the furnace-holes. \xe2\x80\x9cWhenever you feel homesick,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cyou must come up here. We\xe2\x80\x99ll stick you down before a register, under a\r\ngood big burner, and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you will soon get over your homesickness,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Tristram.\r\n\r\nHer husband stared; his wife often had a tone which he found\r\ninscrutable he could not tell for his life whether she was in jest or\r\nin earnest. The truth is that circumstances had done much to cultivate\r\nin Mrs. Tristram a marked tendency to irony. Her taste on many points\r\ndiffered from that of her husband, and though she made frequent\r\nconcessions it must be confessed that her concessions were not always\r\ngraceful. They were founded upon a vague project she had of some day\r\ndoing something very positive, something a trifle passionate. What she\r\nmeant to do she could by no means have told you; but meanwhile,\r\nnevertheless, she was buying a good conscience, by instalments.\r\n\r\nIt should be added, without delay, to anticipate misconception, that\r\nher little scheme of independence did not definitely involve the\r\nassistance of another person, of the opposite sex; she was not saving\r\nup virtue to cover the expenses of a flirtation. For this there were\r\nvarious reasons. To begin with, she had a very plain face and she was\r\nentirely without illusions as to her appearance. She had taken its\r\nmeasure to a hair\xe2\x80\x99s breadth, she knew the worst and the best, she had\r\naccepted herself. It had not been, indeed, without a struggle. As a\r\nyoung girl she had spent hours with her back to her mirror, crying her\r\neyes out; and later she had from desperation and bravado adopted the\r\nhabit of proclaiming herself the most ill-favored of women, in order\r\nthat she might\xe2\x80\x94as in common politeness was inevitable\xe2\x80\x94be contradicted\r\nand reassured. It was since she had come to live in Europe that she had\r\nbegun to take the matter philosophically. Her observation, acutely\r\nexercised here, had suggested to her that a woman\xe2\x80\x99s first duty is not\r\nto be beautiful, but to be pleasing, and she encountered so many women\r\nwho pleased without beauty that she began to feel that she had\r\ndiscovered her mission. She had once heard an enthusiastic musician,\r\nout of patience with a gifted bungler, declare that a fine voice is\r\nreally an obstacle to singing properly; and it occurred to her that it\r\nmight perhaps be equally true that a beautiful face is an obstacle to\r\nthe acquisition of charming manners. Mrs. Tristram, then, undertook to\r\nbe exquisitely agreeable, and she brought to the task a really touching\r\ndevotion. How well she would have succeeded I am unable to say;\r\nunfortunately she broke off in the middle. Her own excuse was the want\r\nof encouragement in her immediate circle. But I am inclined to think\r\nthat she had not a real genius for the matter, or she would have\r\npursued the charming art for itself. The poor lady was very incomplete.\r\nShe fell back upon the harmonies of the toilet, which she thoroughly\r\nunderstood, and contented herself with dressing in perfection. She\r\nlived in Paris, which she pretended to detest, because it was only in\r\nParis that one could find things to exactly suit one\xe2\x80\x99s complexion.\r\nBesides out of Paris it was always more or less of a trouble to get\r\nten-button gloves. When she railed at this serviceable city and you\r\nasked her where she would prefer to reside, she returned some very\r\nunexpected answer. She would say in Copenhagen, or in Barcelona;\r\nhaving, while making the tour of Europe, spent a couple of days at each\r\nof these places. On the whole, with her poetic furbelows and her\r\nmisshapen, intelligent little face, she was, when you knew her, a\r\ndecidedly interesting woman. She was naturally shy, and if she had been\r\nborn a beauty, she would (having no vanity) probably have remained shy.\r\nNow, she was both diffident and importunate; extremely reserved\r\nsometimes with her friends, and strangely expansive with strangers. She\r\ndespised her husband; despised him too much, for she had been perfectly\r\nat liberty not to marry him. She had been in love with a clever man who\r\nhad slighted her, and she had married a fool in the hope that this\r\nthankless wit, reflecting on it, would conclude that she had no\r\nappreciation of merit, and that he had flattered himself in supposing\r\nthat she cared for his own. Restless, discontented, visionary, without\r\npersonal ambitions, but with a certain avidity of imagination, she was,\r\nas I have said before, eminently incomplete. She was full\xe2\x80\x94both for good\r\nand for ill\xe2\x80\x94of beginnings that came to nothing; but she had\r\nnevertheless, morally, a spark of the sacred fire.\r\n\r\nNewman was fond, under all circumstances, of the society of women, and\r\nnow that he was out of his native element and deprived of his habitual\r\ninterests, he turned to it for compensation. He took a great fancy to\r\nMrs. Tristram; she frankly repaid it, and after their first meeting he\r\npassed a great many hours in her drawing-room. After two or three talks\r\nthey were fast friends. Newman\xe2\x80\x99s manner with women was peculiar, and it\r\nrequired some ingenuity on a lady\xe2\x80\x99s part to discover that he admired\r\nher. He had no gallantry, in the usual sense of the term; no\r\ncompliments, no graces, no speeches. Very fond of what is called\r\nchaffing, in his dealings with men, he never found himself on a sofa\r\nbeside a member of the softer sex without feeling extremely serious. He\r\nwas not shy, and so far as awkwardness proceeds from a struggle with\r\nshyness, he was not awkward; grave, attentive, submissive, often\r\nsilent, he was simply swimming in a sort of rapture of respect. This\r\nemotion was not at all theoretic, it was not even in a high degree\r\nsentimental; he had thought very little about the \xe2\x80\x9cposition\xe2\x80\x9d of women,\r\nand he was not familiar either sympathetically or otherwise, with the\r\nimage of a President in petticoats. His attitude was simply the flower\r\nof his general good-nature, and a part of his instinctive and genuinely\r\ndemocratic assumption of everyone\xe2\x80\x99s right to lead an easy life. If a\r\nshaggy pauper had a right to bed and board and wages and a vote, women,\r\nof course, who were weaker than paupers, and whose physical tissue was\r\nin itself an appeal, should be maintained, sentimentally, at the public\r\nexpense. Newman was willing to be taxed for this purpose, largely, in\r\nproportion to his means. Moreover, many of the common traditions with\r\nregard to women were with him fresh personal impressions; he had never\r\nread a novel! He had been struck with their acuteness, their subtlety,\r\ntheir tact, their felicity of judgment. They seemed to him exquisitely\r\norganized. If it is true that one must always have in one\xe2\x80\x99s work here\r\nbelow a religion, or at least an ideal, of some sort, Newman found his\r\nmetaphysical inspiration in a vague acceptance of final responsibility\r\nto some illumined feminine brow.\r\n\r\nHe spent a great deal of time in listening to advice from Mrs.\r\nTristram; advice, it must be added, for which he had never asked. He\r\nwould have been incapable of asking for it, for he had no perception of\r\ndifficulties, and consequently no curiosity about remedies. The complex\r\nParisian world about him seemed a very simple affair; it was an\r\nimmense, amazing spectacle, but it neither inflamed his imagination nor\r\nirritated his curiosity. He kept his hands in his pockets, looked on\r\ngood-humoredly, desired to miss nothing important, observed a great\r\nmany things narrowly, and never reverted to himself. Mrs. Tristram\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cadvice\xe2\x80\x9d was a part of the show, and a more entertaining element, in\r\nher abundant gossip, than the others. He enjoyed her talking about\r\nhimself; it seemed a part of her beautiful ingenuity; but he never made\r\nan application of anything she said, or remembered it when he was away\r\nfrom her. For herself, she appropriated him; he was the most\r\ninteresting thing she had had to think about in many a month. She\r\nwished to do something with him\xe2\x80\x94she hardly knew what. There was so much\r\nof him; he was so rich and robust, so easy, friendly, well-disposed,\r\nthat he kept her fancy constantly on the alert. For the present, the\r\nonly thing she could do was to like him. She told him that he was\r\n\xe2\x80\x9chorribly Western,\xe2\x80\x9d but in this compliment the adverb was tinged with\r\ninsincerity. She led him about with her, introduced him to fifty\r\npeople, and took extreme satisfaction in her conquest. Newman accepted\r\nevery proposal, shook hands universally and promiscuously, and seemed\r\nequally unfamiliar with trepidation or with elation. Tom Tristram\r\ncomplained of his wife\xe2\x80\x99s avidity, and declared that he could never have\r\na clear five minutes with his friend. If he had known how things were\r\ngoing to turn out, he never would have brought him to the Avenue\r\nd\xe2\x80\x99I\xc3\xa9na. The two men, formerly, had not been intimate, but Newman\r\nremembered his earlier impression of his host, and did Mrs. Tristram,\r\nwho had by no means taken him into her confidence, but whose secret he\r\npresently discovered, the justice to admit that her husband was a\r\nrather degenerate mortal. At twenty-five he had been a good fellow, and\r\nin this respect he was unchanged; but of a man of his age one expected\r\nsomething more. People said he was sociable, but this was as much a\r\nmatter of course as for a dipped sponge to expand; and it was not a\r\nhigh order of sociability. He was a great gossip and tattler, and to\r\nproduce a laugh would hardly have spared the reputation of his aged\r\nmother. Newman had a kindness for old memories, but he found it\r\nimpossible not to perceive that Tristram was nowadays a very light\r\nweight. His only aspirations were to hold out at poker, at his club, to\r\nknow the names of all the _cocottes_, to shake hands all round, to ply\r\nhis rosy gullet with truffles and champagne, and to create\r\nuncomfortable eddies and obstructions among the constituent atoms of\r\nthe American colony. He was shamefully idle, spiritless, sensual,\r\nsnobbish. He irritated our friend by the tone of his allusions to their\r\nnative country, and Newman was at a loss to understand why the United\r\nStates were not good enough for Mr. Tristram. He had never been a very\r\nconscious patriot, but it vexed him to see them treated as little\r\nbetter than a vulgar smell in his friend\xe2\x80\x99s nostrils, and he finally\r\nbroke out and swore that they were the greatest country in the world,\r\nthat they could put all Europe into their breeches\xe2\x80\x99 pockets, and that\r\nan American who spoke ill of them ought to be carried home in irons and\r\ncompelled to live in Boston. (This, for Newman was putting it very\r\nvindictively.) Tristram was a comfortable man to snub, he bore no\r\nmalice, and he continued to insist on Newman\xe2\x80\x99s finishing his evening at\r\nthe Occidental Club.\r\n\r\nChristopher Newman dined several times in the Avenue d\xe2\x80\x99I\xc3\xa9na, and his\r\nhost always proposed an early adjournment to this institution. Mrs.\r\nTristram protested, and declared that her husband exhausted his\r\ningenuity in trying to displease her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, I never try, my love,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI know you loathe me quite\r\nenough when I take my chance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman hated to see a husband and wife on these terms, and he was sure\r\none or other of them must be very unhappy. He knew it was not Tristram.\r\nMrs. Tristram had a balcony before her windows, upon which, during the\r\nJune evenings, she was fond of sitting, and Newman used frankly to say\r\nthat he preferred the balcony to the club. It had a fringe of perfumed\r\nplants in tubs, and enabled you to look up the broad street and see the\r\nArch of Triumph vaguely massing its heroic sculptures in the summer\r\nstarlight. Sometimes Newman kept his promise of following Mr. Tristram,\r\nin half an hour, to the Occidental, and sometimes he forgot it. His\r\nhostess asked him a great many questions about himself, but on this\r\nsubject he was an indifferent talker. He was not what is called\r\nsubjective, though when he felt that her interest was sincere, he made\r\nan almost heroic attempt to be. He told her a great many things he had\r\ndone, and regaled her with anecdotes of Western life; she was from\r\nPhiladelphia, and with her eight years in Paris, talked of herself as a\r\nlanguid Oriental. But some other person was always the hero of the\r\ntale, by no means always to his advantage; and Newman\xe2\x80\x99s own emotions\r\nwere but scantily chronicled. She had an especial wish to know whether\r\nhe had ever been in love\xe2\x80\x94seriously, passionately\xe2\x80\x94and, failing to gather\r\nany satisfaction from his allusions, she at last directly inquired. He\r\nhesitated a while, and at last he said, \xe2\x80\x9cNo!\xe2\x80\x9d She declared that she was\r\ndelighted to hear it, as it confirmed her private conviction that he\r\nwas a man of no feeling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, very gravely. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you think so? How do you\r\nrecognize a man of feeling?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t make out,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Tristram, \xe2\x80\x9cwhether you are very simple or\r\nvery deep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m very deep. That\xe2\x80\x99s a fact.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe that if I were to tell you with a certain air that you have\r\nno feeling, you would implicitly believe me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA certain air?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cTry it and see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou would believe me, but you would not care,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Tristram.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have got it all wrong. I should care immensely, but I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbelieve you. The fact is I have never had time to feel things. I have\r\nhad to _do_ them, to make myself felt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can imagine that you may have done that tremendously, sometimes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, there\xe2\x80\x99s no mistake about that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen you are in a fury it can\xe2\x80\x99t be pleasant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am never in a fury.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAngry, then, or displeased.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am never angry, and it is so long since I have been displeased that\r\nI have quite forgotten it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Tristram, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you are never angry. A man\r\nought to be angry sometimes, and you are neither good enough nor bad\r\nenough always to keep your temper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI lose it perhaps once in five years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe time is coming round, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said his hostess. \xe2\x80\x9cBefore I have\r\nknown you six months I shall see you in a fine fury.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean to put me into one?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should not be sorry. You take things too coolly. It exasperates me.\r\nAnd then you are too happy. You have what must be the most agreeable\r\nthing in the world, the consciousness of having bought your pleasure\r\nbeforehand and paid for it. You have not a day of reckoning staring you\r\nin the face. Your reckonings are over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I suppose I am happy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, meditatively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have been odiously successful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuccessful in copper,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9conly so-so in railroads, and a\r\nhopeless fizzle in oil.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very disagreeable to know how Americans have made their money.\r\nNow you have the world before you. You have only to enjoy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I suppose I am very well off,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly I am tired of\r\nhaving it thrown up at me. Besides, there are several drawbacks. I am\r\nnot intellectual.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne doesn\xe2\x80\x99t expect it of you,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Tristram answered. Then in a\r\nmoment, \xe2\x80\x9cBesides, you are!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I mean to have a good time, whether or no,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI am\r\nnot cultivated, I am not even educated; I know nothing about history,\r\nor art, or foreign tongues, or any other learned matters. But I am not\r\na fool, either, and I shall undertake to know something about Europe by\r\nthe time I have done with it. I feel something under my ribs here,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nadded in a moment, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I can\xe2\x80\x99t explain\xe2\x80\x94a sort of a mighty hankering,\r\na desire to stretch out and haul in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBravo!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Tristram, \xe2\x80\x9cthat is very fine. You are the great\r\nWestern Barbarian, stepping forth in his innocence and might, gazing a\r\nwhile at this poor effete Old World and then swooping down on it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, come,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI am not a barbarian, by a good deal. I am\r\nvery much the reverse. I have seen barbarians; I know what they are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mean that you are a Comanche chief, or that you wear a blanket\r\nand feathers. There are different shades.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am a highly civilized man,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI stick to that. If you\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t believe it, I should like to prove it to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Tristram was silent a while. \xe2\x80\x9cI should like to make you prove it,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe said, at last. \xe2\x80\x9cI should like to put you in a difficult place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat has a little conceited sound!\xe2\x80\x9d his companion rejoined.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cI have a very good opinion of myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish I could put it to the test. Give me time and I will.\xe2\x80\x9d And Mrs.\r\nTristram remained silent for some time afterwards, as if she was trying\r\nto keep her pledge. It did not appear that evening that she succeeded;\r\nbut as he was rising to take his leave she passed suddenly, as she was\r\nvery apt to do, from the tone of unsparing persiflage to that of almost\r\ntremulous sympathy. \xe2\x80\x9cSpeaking seriously,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI believe in you,\r\nMr. Newman. You flatter my patriotism.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour patriotism?\xe2\x80\x9d Christopher demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven so. It would take too long to explain, and you probably would not\r\nunderstand. Besides, you might take it\xe2\x80\x94really, you might take it for a\r\ndeclaration. But it has nothing to do with you personally; it\xe2\x80\x99s what\r\nyou represent. Fortunately you don\xe2\x80\x99t know all that, or your conceit\r\nwould increase insufferably.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman stood staring and wondering what under the sun he \xe2\x80\x9crepresented.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cForgive all my meddlesome chatter and forget my advice. It is very\r\nsilly in me to undertake to tell you what to do. When you are\r\nembarrassed, do as you think best, and you will do very well. When you\r\nare in a difficulty, judge for yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall remember everything you have told me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are\r\nso many forms and ceremonies over here\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cForms and ceremonies are what I mean, of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but I want to observe them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t I as good a\r\nright as another? They don\xe2\x80\x99t scare me, and you needn\xe2\x80\x99t give me leave to\r\nviolate them. I won\xe2\x80\x99t take it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is not what I mean. I mean, observe them in your own way. Settle\r\nnice questions for yourself.
