Cut the knot or untie it, as you choose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I am sure I shall never fumble over it!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\nThe next time that he dined in the Avenue d\xe2\x80\x99I\xc3\xa9na was a Sunday, a day on\r\nwhich Mr. Tristram left the cards unshuffled, so that there was a trio\r\nin the evening on the balcony. The talk was of many things, and at last\r\nMrs. Tristram suddenly observed to Christopher Newman that it was high\r\ntime he should take a wife.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen to her; she has the audacity!\xe2\x80\x9d said Tristram, who on Sunday\r\nevenings was always rather acrimonious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose you have made up your mind not to marry?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs.\r\nTristram continued.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeaven forbid!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI am sternly resolved on it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very easy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tristram; \xe2\x80\x9cfatally easy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, I suppose you do not mean to wait till you are fifty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the contrary, I am in a great hurry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne would never suppose it. Do you expect a lady to come and propose\r\nto you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; I am willing to propose. I think a great deal about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me some of your thoughts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, slowly, \xe2\x80\x9cI want to marry very well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarry a woman of sixty, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tristram.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Well\xe2\x80\x99 in what sense?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn every sense. I shall be hard to please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must remember that, as the French proverb says, the most beautiful\r\ngirl in the world can give but what she has.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince you ask me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cI will say frankly that I want\r\nextremely to marry. It is time, to begin with: before I know it I shall\r\nbe forty. And then I\xe2\x80\x99m lonely and helpless and dull. But if I marry\r\nnow, so long as I didn\xe2\x80\x99t do it in hot haste when I was twenty, I must\r\ndo it with my eyes open. I want to do the thing in handsome style. I do\r\nnot only want to make no mistakes, but I want to make a great hit. I\r\nwant to take my pick. My wife must be a magnificent woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Voil\xc3\xa0 ce qui s\xe2\x80\x99appelle parler!_\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mrs. Tristram.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I have thought an immense deal about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps you think too much. The best thing is simply to fall in love.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I find the woman who pleases me, I shall love her enough. My wife\r\nshall be very comfortable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are superb! There\xe2\x80\x99s a chance for the magnificent women.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not fair.\xe2\x80\x9d Newman rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cYou draw a fellow out and put him\r\noff guard, and then you laugh at him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI assure you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Tristram, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I am very serious. To prove\r\nit, I will make you a proposal. Should you like me, as they say here,\r\nto marry you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo hunt up a wife for me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is already found. I will bring you together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, come,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tristram, \xe2\x80\x9cwe don\xe2\x80\x99t keep a matrimonial bureau. He will\r\nthink you want your commission.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPresent me to a woman who comes up to my notions,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cand I\r\nwill marry her tomorrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have a strange tone about it, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t quite understand you. I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t suppose you would be so coldblooded and calculating.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman was silent a while. \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, at last, \xe2\x80\x9cI want a great\r\nwoman. I stick to that. That\xe2\x80\x99s one thing I _can_ treat myself to, and\r\nif it is to be had I mean to have it. What else have I toiled and\r\nstruggled for, all these years? I have succeeded, and now what am I to\r\ndo with my success? To make it perfect, as I see it, there must be a\r\nbeautiful woman perched on the pile, like a statue on a monument. She\r\nmust be as good as she is beautiful, and as clever as she is good. I\r\ncan give my wife a good deal, so I am not afraid to ask a good deal\r\nmyself. She shall have everything a woman can desire; I shall not even\r\nobject to her being too good for me; she may be cleverer and wiser than\r\nI can understand, and I shall only be the better pleased. I want to\r\npossess, in a word, the best article in the market.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t you tell a fellow all this at the outset?\xe2\x80\x9d Tristram\r\ndemanded. \xe2\x80\x9cI have been trying so to make you fond of _me!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is very interesting,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Tristram. \xe2\x80\x9cI like to see a man\r\nknow his own mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have known mine for a long time,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman went on. \xe2\x80\x9cI made up my mind\r\ntolerably early in life that a beautiful wife was the thing best worth\r\nhaving, here below. It is the greatest victory over circumstances. When\r\nI say beautiful, I mean beautiful in mind and in manners, as well as in\r\nperson. It is a thing every man has an equal right to; he may get it if\r\nhe can. He doesn\xe2\x80\x99t have to be born with certain faculties, on purpose;\r\nhe needs only to be a man. Then he needs only to use his will, and such\r\nwits as he has, and to try.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt strikes me that your marriage is to be rather a matter of vanity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it is certain,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cthat if people notice my wife and\r\nadmire her, I shall be mightily tickled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter this,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mrs. Tristram, \xe2\x80\x9ccall any man modest!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut none of them will admire her so much as I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see you have a taste for splendor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman hesitated a little; and then, \xe2\x80\x9cI honestly believe I have!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I suppose you have already looked about you a good deal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA good deal, according to opportunity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you have seen nothing that satisfied you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, half reluctantly, \xe2\x80\x9cI am bound to say in honesty that\r\nI have seen nothing that really satisfied me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou remind me of the heroes of the French romantic poets, Rolla and\r\nFortunio and all those other insatiable gentlemen for whom nothing in\r\nthis world was handsome enough. But I see you are in earnest, and I\r\nshould like to help you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho the deuce is it, darling, that you are going to put upon him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nTristram cried. \xe2\x80\x9cWe know a good many pretty girls, thank Heaven, but\r\nmagnificent women are not so common.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you any objections to a foreigner?\xe2\x80\x9d his wife continued,\r\naddressing Newman, who had tilted back his chair and, with his feet on\r\na bar of the balcony railing and his hands in his pockets, was looking\r\nat the stars.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo Irish need apply,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tristram.\r\n\r\nNewman meditated a while. \xe2\x80\x9cAs a foreigner, no,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last; \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nhave no prejudices.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear fellow, you have no suspicions!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Tristram. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow what terrible customers these foreign women are; especially the\r\n\xe2\x80\x98magnificent\xe2\x80\x99 ones. How should you like a fair Circassian, with a\r\ndagger in her belt?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman administered a vigorous slap to his knee. \xe2\x80\x9cI would marry a\r\nJapanese, if she pleased me,\xe2\x80\x9d he affirmed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe had better confine ourselves to Europe,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Tristram. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nonly thing is, then, that the person be in herself to your taste?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is going to offer you an unappreciated governess!\xe2\x80\x9d Tristram\r\ngroaned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAssuredly. I won\xe2\x80\x99t deny that, other things being equal, I should\r\nprefer one of my own countrywomen. We should speak the same language,\r\nand that would be a comfort. But I am not afraid of a foreigner.\r\nBesides, I rather like the idea of taking in Europe, too. It enlarges\r\nthe field of selection. When you choose from a greater number, you can\r\nbring your choice to a finer point!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou talk like Sardanapalus!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Tristram.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou say all this to the right person,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman\xe2\x80\x99s hostess. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nhappen to number among my friends the loveliest woman in the world.\r\nNeither more nor less. I don\xe2\x80\x99t say a very charming person or a very\r\nestimable woman or a very great beauty; I say simply the loveliest\r\nwoman in the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe deuce!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Tristram, \xe2\x80\x9cyou have kept very quiet about her. Were\r\nyou afraid of me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have seen her,\xe2\x80\x9d said his wife, \xe2\x80\x9cbut you have no perception of such\r\nmerit as Claire\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, her name is Claire? I give it up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes your friend wish to marry?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot in the least. It is for you to make her change her mind. It will\r\nnot be easy; she has had one husband, and he gave her a low opinion of\r\nthe species.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, she is a widow, then?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you already afraid? She was married at eighteen, by her parents,\r\nin the French fashion, to a disagreeable old man. But he had the good\r\ntaste to die a couple of years afterward, and she is now twenty-five.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo she is French?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrench by her father, English by her mother. She is really more\r\nEnglish than French, and she speaks English as well as you or I\xe2\x80\x94or\r\nrather much better. She belongs to the very top of the basket, as they\r\nsay here. Her family, on each side, is of fabulous antiquity; her\r\nmother is the daughter of an English Catholic earl. Her father is dead,\r\nand since her widowhood she has lived with her mother and a married\r\nbrother. There is another brother, younger, who I believe is wild. They\r\nhave an old hotel in the Rue de l\xe2\x80\x99Universit\xc3\xa9, but their fortune is\r\nsmall, and they make a common household, for economy\xe2\x80\x99s sake. When I was\r\na girl I was put into a convent here for my education, while my father\r\nmade the tour of Europe. It was a silly thing to do with me, but it had\r\nthe advantage that it made me acquainted with Claire de Bellegarde. She\r\nwas younger than I but we became fast friends. I took a tremendous\r\nfancy to her, and she returned my passion as far as she could. They\r\nkept such a tight rein on her that she could do very little, and when I\r\nleft the convent she had to give me up. I was not of her _monde_; I am\r\nnot now, either, but we sometimes meet. They are terrible people\xe2\x80\x94her\r\n_monde_; all mounted upon stilts a mile high, and with pedigrees long\r\nin proportion. It is the skim of the milk of the old _noblesse_. Do you\r\nknow what a Legitimist is, or an Ultramontane? Go into Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s drawing-room some afternoon, at five o\xe2\x80\x99clock, and you will see\r\nthe best preserved specimens. I say go, but no one is admitted who\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t show his fifty quarterings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd this is the lady you propose to me to marry?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cA\r\nlady I can\xe2\x80\x99t even approach?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you said just now that you recognized no obstacles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman looked at Mrs. Tristram a while, stroking his moustache. \xe2\x80\x9cIs she\r\na beauty?\xe2\x80\x9d he demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, then it\xe2\x80\x99s no use\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is not a beauty, but she is beautiful, two very different things.\r\nA beauty has no faults in her face, the face of a beautiful woman may\r\nhave faults that only deepen its charm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI remember Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tristram. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is as plain as\r\na pike-staff. A man wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t look at her twice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn saying that _he_ would not look at her twice, my husband\r\nsufficiently describes her,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Tristram rejoined.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs she good; is she clever?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is perfect! I won\xe2\x80\x99t say more than that. When you are praising a\r\nperson to another who is to know her, it is bad policy to go into\r\ndetails. I won\xe2\x80\x99t exaggerate. I simply recommend her. Among all women I\r\nhave known she stands alone; she is of a different clay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to see her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, simply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will try to manage it. The only way will be to invite her to dinner.\r\nI have never invited her before, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that she will come.\r\nHer old feudal countess of a mother rules the family with an iron hand,\r\nand allows her to have no friends but of her own choosing, and to visit\r\nonly in a certain sacred circle. But I can at least ask her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this moment Mrs. Tristram was interrupted; a servant stepped out\r\nupon the balcony and announced that there were visitors in the\r\ndrawing-room. When Newman\xe2\x80\x99s hostess had gone in to receive her friends,\r\nTom Tristram approached his guest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t put your foot into _this_, my boy,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, puffing the last\r\nwhiffs of his cigar. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s nothing in it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman looked askance at him, inquisitive. \xe2\x80\x9cYou tell another story,\r\neh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say simply that Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 is a great white doll of a woman,\r\nwho cultivates quiet haughtiness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, she\xe2\x80\x99s haughty, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe looks at you as if you were so much thin air, and cares for you\r\nabout as much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is very proud, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cProud? As proud as I\xe2\x80\x99m humble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd not good-looking?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTristram shrugged his shoulders: \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a kind of beauty you must be\r\n_intellectual_ to understand. But I must go in and amuse the company.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSome time elapsed before Newman followed his friends into the\r\ndrawing-room. When he at last made his appearance there he remained but\r\na short time, and during this period sat perfectly silent, listening to\r\na lady to whom Mrs. Tristram had straightway introduced him and who\r\nchattered, without a pause, with the full force of an extraordinarily\r\nhigh-pitched voice. Newman gazed and attended. Presently he came to bid\r\ngood-night to Mrs. Tristram.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is that lady?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Dora Finch. How do you like her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s too noisy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is thought so bright! Certainly, you are fastidious,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\nTristram.\r\n\r\nNewman stood a moment, hesitating. Then at last, \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t forget about\r\nyour friend,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cMadame What\xe2\x80\x99s-her-name? the proud beauty. Ask\r\nher to dinner, and give me a good notice.\xe2\x80\x9d And with this he departed.\r\n\r\nSome days later he came back; it was in the afternoon. He found Mrs.\r\nTristram in her drawing-room; with her was a visitor, a woman young and\r\npretty, dressed in white. The two ladies had risen and the visitor was\r\napparently taking her leave. As Newman approached, he received from\r\nMrs. Tristram a glance of the most vivid significance, which he was not\r\nimmediately able to interpret.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is a good friend of ours,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, turning to her companion,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Christopher Newman. I have spoken of you to him and he has an\r\nextreme desire to make your acquaintance. If you had consented to come\r\nand dine, I should have offered him an opportunity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe stranger turned her face toward Newman, with a smile. He was not\r\nembarrassed, for his unconscious _sang-froid_ was boundless; but as he\r\nbecame aware that this was the proud and beautiful Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9,\r\nthe loveliest woman in the world, the promised perfection, the proposed\r\nideal, he made an instinctive movement to gather his wits together.\r\nThrough the slight preoccupation that it produced he had a sense of a\r\nlong, fair face, and of two eyes that were both brilliant and mild.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should have been most happy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. \xe2\x80\x9cUnfortunately,\r\nas I have been telling Mrs. Tristram, I go on Monday to the country.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman had made a solemn bow. \xe2\x80\x9cI am very sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cParis is getting too warm,\xe2\x80\x9d Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 added, taking her\r\nfriend\xe2\x80\x99s hand again in farewell.\r\n\r\nMrs. Tristram seemed to have formed a sudden and somewhat venturesome\r\nresolution, and she smiled more intensely, as women do when they take\r\nsuch resolution. \xe2\x80\x9cI want Mr. Newman to know you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, dropping\r\nher head on one side and looking at Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s bonnet ribbons.\r\n\r\nChristopher Newman stood gravely silent, while his native penetration\r\nadmonished him. Mrs. Tristram was determined to force her friend to\r\naddress him a word of encouragement which should be more than one of\r\nthe common formulas of politeness; and if she was prompted by charity,\r\nit was by the charity that begins at home. Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 was her\r\ndearest Claire, and her especial admiration but Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 had\r\nfound it impossible to dine with her and Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 should for\r\nonce be forced gently to render tribute to Mrs. Tristram.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would give me great pleasure,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, looking at Mrs. Tristram.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a great deal,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the latter, \xe2\x80\x9cfor Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 to say!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very much obliged to you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Tristram can speak\r\nbetter for me than I can speak for myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 looked at him again, with the same soft brightness.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you to be long in Paris?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall keep him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Tristram.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you are keeping _me!_\xe2\x80\x9d and Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 shook her friend\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA moment longer,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Tristram.\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 looked at Newman again; this time without her smile.\r\nHer eyes lingered a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cWill you come and see me?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\nMrs. Tristram kissed her. Newman expressed his thanks, and she took her\r\nleave. Her hostess went with her to the door, and left Newman alone a\r\nmoment. Presently she returned, rubbing her hands. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was a fortunate\r\nchance,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cShe had come to decline my invitation. You\r\ntriumphed on the spot, making her ask you, at the end of three minutes,\r\nto her house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was you who triumphed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must not be too hard upon\r\nher.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Tristram stared. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe did not strike me as so proud. I should say she was shy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very discriminating. And what do you think of her face?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s handsome!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think it was! Of course you will go and see her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo-morrow!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, not to-morrow; the next day. That will be Sunday; she leaves Paris\r\non Monday. If you don\xe2\x80\x99t see her; it will at least be a beginning.\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\nshe gave him Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s address.\r\n\r\nHe walked across the Seine, late in the summer afternoon, and made his\r\nway through those gray and silent streets of the Faubourg St. Germain\r\nwhose houses present to the outer world a face as impassive and as\r\nsuggestive of the concentration of privacy within as the blank walls of\r\nEastern seraglios. Newman thought it a queer way for rich people to\r\nlive; his ideal of grandeur was a splendid fa\xc3\xa7ade diffusing its\r\nbrilliancy outward too, irradiating hospitality. The house to which he\r\nhad been directed had a dark, dusty, painted portal, which swung open\r\nin answer to his ring. It admitted him into a wide, gravelled court,\r\nsurrounded on three sides with closed windows, and with a doorway\r\nfacing the street, approached by three steps and surmounted by a tin\r\ncanopy. The place was all in the shade; it answered to Newman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nconception of a convent. The portress could not tell him whether Madame\r\nde Cintr\xc3\xa9 was visible; he would please to apply at the farther door. He\r\ncrossed the court; a gentleman was sitting, bareheaded, on the steps of\r\nthe portico, playing with a beautiful pointer. He rose as Newman\r\napproached, and, as he laid his hand upon the bell, said with a smile,\r\nin English, that he was afraid Newman would be kept waiting; the\r\nservants were scattered, he himself had been ringing, he didn\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nwhat the deuce was in them. He was a young man, his English was\r\nexcellent, and his smile very frank. Newman pronounced the name of\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man, \xe2\x80\x9cthat my sister is visible. Come in, and\r\nif you will give me your card I will carry it to her myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman had been accompanied on his present errand by a slight\r\nsentiment, I will not say of defiance\xe2\x80\x94a readiness for aggression or\r\ndefence, as they might prove needful\xe2\x80\x94but of reflection, good-humored\r\nsuspicion. He took from his pocket, while he stood on the portico, a\r\ncard upon which, under his name, he had written the words \xe2\x80\x9cSan\r\nFrancisco,\xe2\x80\x9d and while he presented it he looked warily at his\r\ninterlocutor. His glance was singularly reassuring; he liked the young\r\nman\xe2\x80\x99s face; it strongly resembled that of Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. He was\r\nevidently her brother. The young man, on his side, had made a rapid\r\ninspection of Newman\xe2\x80\x99s person. He had taken the card and was about to\r\nenter the house with it when another figure appeared on the\r\nthreshold\xe2\x80\x94an older man, of a fine presence, wearing evening dress. He\r\nlooked hard at Newman, and Newman looked at him. \xe2\x80\x9cMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nthe younger man repeated, as an introduction of the visitor. The other\r\ntook the card from his hand, read it in a rapid glance, looked again at\r\nNewman from head to foot, hesitated a moment, and then said, gravely\r\nbut urbanely, \xe2\x80\x9cMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 is not at home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe younger man made a gesture, and then, turning to Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cI am very\r\nsorry, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nNewman gave him a friendly nod, to show that he bore him no malice, and\r\nretraced his steps. At the porter\xe2\x80\x99s lodge he stopped; the two men were\r\nstill standing on the portico.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is the gentleman with the dog?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked of the old woman who\r\nreappeared. He had begun to learn French.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is Monsieur le Comte.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the other?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is Monsieur le Marquis.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA marquis?\xe2\x80\x9d said Christopher in English, which the old woman\r\nfortunately did not understand. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, then he\xe2\x80\x99s not the butler!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV\r\n\r\n\r\nEarly one morning, before Christopher Newman was dressed, a little old\r\nman was ushered into his apartment, followed by a youth in a blouse,\r\nbearing a picture in a brilliant frame. Newman, among the distractions\r\nof Paris, had forgotten M. Nioche and his accomplished daughter; but\r\nthis was an effective reminder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid you had given me up, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man, after many\r\napologies and salutations. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have made you wait so many days. You\r\naccused us, perhaps, of inconstancy, of bad faith. But behold me at\r\nlast! And behold also the pretty Madonna. Place it on a chair, my\r\nfriend, in a good light, so that monsieur may admire it.\xe2\x80\x9d And M.\r\nNioche, addressing his companion, helped him to dispose the work of\r\nart.\r\n\r\nIt had been endued with a layer of varnish an inch thick and its frame,\r\nof an elaborate pattern, was at least a foot wide. It glittered and\r\ntwinkled in the morning light, and looked, to Newman\xe2\x80\x99s eyes,\r\nwonderfully splendid and precious. It seemed to him a very happy\r\npurchase, and he felt rich in the possession of it. He stood looking at\r\nit complacently, while he proceeded with his toilet, and M. Nioche, who\r\nhad dismissed his own attendant, hovered near, smiling and rubbing his\r\nhands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has wonderful _finesse_,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured, caressingly. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd here and\r\nthere are marvelous touches, you probably perceive them, sir. It\r\nattracted great attention on the Boulevard, as we came along. And then\r\na gradation of tones! That\xe2\x80\x99s what it is to know how to paint. I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsay it because I am her father, sir; but as one man of taste addressing\r\nanother I cannot help observing that you have there an exquisite work.\r\nIt is hard to produce such things and to have to part with them. If our\r\nmeans only allowed us the luxury of keeping it! I really may say, sir\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nand M. Nioche gave a little feebly insinuating laugh\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI really may say\r\nthat I envy you! You see,\xe2\x80\x9d he added in a moment, \xe2\x80\x9cwe have taken the\r\nliberty of offering you a frame. It increases by a trifle the value of\r\nthe work, and it will save you the annoyance\xe2\x80\x94so great for a person of\r\nyour delicacy\xe2\x80\x94of going about to bargain at the shops.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe language spoken by M. Nioche was a singular compound, which I\r\nshrink from the attempt to reproduce in its integrity. He had\r\napparently once possessed a certain knowledge of English, and his\r\naccent was oddly tinged with the cockneyism of the British metropolis.\r\nBut his learning had grown rusty with disuse, and his vocabulary was\r\ndefective and capricious. He had repaired it with large patches of\r\nFrench, with words anglicized by a process of his own, and with native\r\nidioms literally translated. The result, in the form in which he in all\r\nhumility presented it, would be scarcely comprehensible to the reader,\r\nso that I have ventured to trim and sift it. Newman only half\r\nunderstood it, but it amused him, and the old man\xe2\x80\x99s decent forlornness\r\nappealed to his democratic instincts. The assumption of a fatality in\r\nmisery always irritated his strong good nature\xe2\x80\x94it was almost the only\r\nthing that did so; and he felt the impulse to wipe it out, as it were,\r\nwith the sponge of his own prosperity. The papa of Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie,\r\nhowever, had apparently on this occasion been vigorously indoctrinated,\r\nand he showed a certain tremulous eagerness to cultivate unexpected\r\nopportunities.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much do I owe you, then, with the frame?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will make in all three thousand francs,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man, smiling\r\nagreeably, but folding his hands in instinctive suppliance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you give me a receipt?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have brought one,\xe2\x80\x9d said M. Nioche. \xe2\x80\x9cI took the liberty of drawing it\r\nup, in case monsieur should happen to desire to discharge his debt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAnd he drew a paper from his pocket-book and presented it to his\r\npatron. The document was written in a minute, fantastic hand, and\r\ncouched in the choicest language.\r\n\r\nNewman laid down the money, and M. Nioche dropped the napoleons one by\r\none, solemnly and lovingly, into an old leathern purse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how is your young lady?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cShe made a great\r\nimpression on me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn impression? Monsieur is very good. Monsieur admires her\r\nappearance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is very pretty, certainly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlas, yes, she is very pretty!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what is the harm in her being pretty?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nM. Nioche fixed his eyes upon a spot on the carpet and shook his head.\r\nThen looking up at Newman with a gaze that seemed to brighten and\r\nexpand, \xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur knows what Paris is. She is dangerous to beauty, when\r\nbeauty hasn\xe2\x80\x99t the sou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but that is not the case with your daughter. She is rich, now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery true; we are rich for six months. But if my daughter were a plain\r\ngirl I should sleep better all the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are afraid of the young men?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe young and the old!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe ought to get a husband.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, monsieur, one doesn\xe2\x80\x99t get a husband for nothing. Her husband must\r\ntake her as she is; I can\xe2\x80\x99t give her a sou. But the young men don\xe2\x80\x99t see\r\nwith that eye.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cher talent is in itself a dowry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, sir, it needs first to be converted into specie!\xe2\x80\x9d and M. Nioche\r\nslapped his purse tenderly before he stowed it away. \xe2\x80\x9cThe operation\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t take place every day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, your young men are very shabby,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman; \xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s all I can\r\nsay. They ought to pay for your daughter, and not ask money\r\nthemselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose are very noble ideas, monsieur; but what will you have? They are\r\nnot the ideas of this country. We want to know what we are about when\r\nwe marry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow big a portion does your daughter want?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nM. Nioche stared, as if he wondered what was coming next; but he\r\npromptly recovered himself, at a venture, and replied that he knew a\r\nvery nice young man, employed by an insurance company, who would\r\ncontent himself with fifteen thousand francs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet your daughter paint half a dozen pictures for me, and she shall\r\nhave her dowry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHalf a dozen pictures\xe2\x80\x94her dowry! Monsieur is not speaking\r\ninconsiderately?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf she will make me six or eight copies in the Louvre as pretty as\r\nthat Madonna, I will pay her the same price,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\nPoor M. Nioche was speechless a moment, with amazement and gratitude,\r\nand then he seized Newman\xe2\x80\x99s hand, pressed it between his own ten\r\nfingers, and gazed at him with watery eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cAs pretty as that? They\r\nshall be a thousand times prettier\xe2\x80\x94they shall be magnificent, sublime.\r\nAh, if I only knew how to paint, myself, sir, so that I might lend a\r\nhand! What can I do to thank you? _Voyons!_\xe2\x80\x9d And he pressed his\r\nforehead while he tried to think of something.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you have thanked me enough,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, here it is, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d cried M. Nioche. \xe2\x80\x9cTo express my gratitude, I\r\nwill charge you nothing for the lessons in French conversation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe lessons? I had quite forgotten them. Listening to your English,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nadded Newman, laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cis almost a lesson in French.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t profess to teach English, certainly,\xe2\x80\x9d said M. Nioche. \xe2\x80\x9cBut\r\nfor my own admirable tongue I am still at your service.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince you are here, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cwe will begin. This is a very\r\ngood hour. I am going to have my coffee; come every morning at\r\nhalf-past nine and have yours with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur offers me my coffee, also?\xe2\x80\x9d cried M. Nioche. \xe2\x80\x9cTruly, my\r\n_beaux jours_ are coming back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9clet us begin. The coffee is almighty hot. How do\r\nyou say that in French?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEvery day, then, for the following three weeks, the minutely\r\nrespectable figure of M. Nioche made its appearance, with a series of\r\nlittle inquiring and apologetic obeisances, among the aromatic fumes of\r\nNewman\xe2\x80\x99s morning beverage. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how much French our friend\r\nlearned, but, as he himself said, if the attempt did him no good, it\r\ncould at any rate do him no harm. And it amused him; it gratified that\r\nirregularly sociable side of his nature which had always expressed\r\nitself in a relish for ungrammatical conversation, and which often,\r\neven in his busy and preoccupied days, had made him sit on rail fences\r\nin young Western towns, in the twilight, in gossip hardly less than\r\nfraternal with humorous loafers and obscure fortune-seekers. He had\r\nnotions, wherever he went, about talking with the natives; he had been\r\nassured, and his judgment approved the advice, that in traveling abroad\r\nit was an excellent thing to look into the life of the country. M.\r\nNioche was very much of a native and, though his life might not be\r\nparticularly worth looking into, he was a palpable and smoothly-rounded\r\nunit in that picturesque Parisian civilization which offered our hero\r\nso much easy entertainment and propounded so many curious problems to\r\nhis inquiring and practical mind. Newman was fond of statistics; he\r\nliked to know how things were done; it gratified him to learn what\r\ntaxes were paid, what profits were gathered, what commercial habits\r\nprevailed, how the battle of life was fought. M. Nioche, as a reduced\r\ncapitalist, was familiar with these considerations, and he formulated\r\nhis information, which he was proud to be able to impart, in the\r\nneatest possible terms and with a pinch of snuff between finger and\r\nthumb. As a Frenchman\xe2\x80\x94quite apart from Newman\xe2\x80\x99s napoleons\xe2\x80\x94M. Nioche\r\nloved conversation, and even in his decay his urbanity had not grown\r\nrusty. As a Frenchman, too, he could give a clear account of things,\r\nand\xe2\x80\x94still as a Frenchman\xe2\x80\x94when his knowledge was at fault he could\r\nsupply its lapses with the most convenient and ingenious hypotheses.\r\nThe little shrunken financier was intensely delighted to have questions\r\nasked him, and he scraped together information, by frugal processes,\r\nand took notes, in his little greasy pocket-book, of incidents which\r\nmight interest his munificent friend. He read old almanacs at the\r\nbook-stalls on the quays, and he began to frequent another _caf\xc3\xa9_,\r\nwhere more newspapers were taken and his postprandial _demitasse_ cost\r\nhim a penny extra, and where he used to con the tattered sheets for\r\ncurious anecdotes, freaks of nature, and strange coincidences. He would\r\nrelate with solemnity the next morning that a child of five years of\r\nage had lately died at Bordeaux, whose brain had been found to weigh\r\nsixty ounces\xe2\x80\x94the brain of a Napoleon or a Washington! or that Madame\r\nP\xe2\x80\x94, _charcuti\xc3\xa8re_ in the Rue de Clichy, had found in the wadding of an\r\nold petticoat the sum of three hundred and sixty francs, which she had\r\nlost five years before. He pronounced his words with great distinctness\r\nand sonority, and Newman assured him that his way of dealing with the\r\nFrench tongue was very superior to the bewildering chatter that he\r\nheard in other mouths. Upon this M. Nioche\xe2\x80\x99s accent became more finely\r\ntrenchant than ever, he offered to read extracts from Lamartine, and he\r\nprotested that, although he did endeavor according to his feeble lights\r\nto cultivate refinement of diction, monsieur, if he wanted the real\r\nthing, should go to the Th\xc3\xa9\xc3\xa2tre Fran\xc3\xa7ais.\r\n\r\nNewman took an interest in French thriftiness and conceived a lively\r\nadmiration for Parisian economies. His own economic genius was so\r\nentirely for operations on a larger scale, and, to move at his ease, he\r\nneeded so imperatively the sense of great risks and great prizes, that\r\nhe found an ungrudging entertainment in the spectacle of fortunes made\r\nby the aggregation of copper coins, and in the minute subdivision of\r\nlabor and profit. He questioned M. Nioche about his own manner of life,\r\nand felt a friendly mixture of compassion and respect over the recital\r\nof his delicate frugalities. The worthy man told him how, at one\r\nperiod, he and his daughter had supported existence comfortably upon\r\nthe sum of fifteen sous _per diem_; recently, having succeeded in\r\nhauling ashore the last floating fragments of the wreck of his fortune,\r\nhis budget had been a trifle more ample. But they still had to count\r\ntheir sous very narrowly, and M. Nioche intimated with a sigh that\r\nMademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie did not bring to this task that zealous cooperation\r\nwhich might have been desired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what will you have?\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x99 he asked, philosophically. \xe2\x80\x9cOne is young,\r\none is pretty, one needs new dresses and fresh gloves; one can\xe2\x80\x99t wear\r\nshabby gowns among the splendors of the Louvre.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut your daughter earns enough to pay for her own clothes,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nNewman.\r\n\r\nM. Nioche looked at him with weak, uncertain eyes. He would have liked\r\nto be able to say that his daughter\xe2\x80\x99s talents were appreciated, and\r\nthat her crooked little daubs commanded a market; but it seemed a\r\nscandal to abuse the credulity of this free-handed stranger, who,\r\nwithout a suspicion or a question, had admitted him to equal social\r\nrights. He compromised, and declared that while it was obvious that\r\nMademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie\xe2\x80\x99s reproductions of the old masters had only to be\r\nseen to be coveted, the prices which, in consideration of their\r\naltogether peculiar degree of finish, she felt obliged to ask for them\r\nhad kept purchasers at a respectful distance. \xe2\x80\x9cPoor little one!\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nM. Nioche, with a sigh; \xe2\x80\x9cit is almost a pity that her work is so\r\nperfect! It would be in her interest to paint less well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut if Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie has this devotion to her art,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman once\r\nobserved, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy should you have those fears for her that you spoke of\r\nthe other day?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nM. Nioche meditated: there was an inconsistency in his position; it\r\nmade him chronically uncomfortable. Though he had no desire to destroy\r\nthe goose with the golden eggs\xe2\x80\x94Newman\xe2\x80\x99s benevolent confidence\xe2\x80\x94he felt a\r\ntremulous impulse to speak out all his trouble. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, she is an artist,\r\nmy dear sir, most assuredly,\xe2\x80\x9d he declared. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, to tell you the truth,\r\nshe is also a _franche coquette_. I am sorry to say,\xe2\x80\x9d he added in a\r\nmoment, shaking his head with a world of harmless bitterness, \xe2\x80\x9cthat she\r\ncomes honestly by it. Her mother was one before her!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were not happy with your wife?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman asked.\r\n\r\nM. Nioche gave half a dozen little backward jerks of his head. \xe2\x80\x9cShe was\r\nmy purgatory, monsieur!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe deceived you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnder my nose, year after year. I was too stupid, and the temptation\r\nwas too great. But I found her out at last. I have only been once in my\r\nlife a man to be afraid of; I know it very well; it was in that hour!\r\nNevertheless I don\xe2\x80\x99t like to think of it. I loved her\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t tell you\r\nhow much. She was a bad woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is not living?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has gone to her account.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHer influence on your daughter, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman encouragingly, \xe2\x80\x9cis\r\nnot to be feared.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe cared no more for her daughter than for the sole of her shoe! But\r\nNo\xc3\xa9mie has no need of influence. She is sufficient to herself. She is\r\nstronger than I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe doesn\xe2\x80\x99t obey you, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe can\xe2\x80\x99t obey, monsieur, since I don\xe2\x80\x99t command. What would be the\r\nuse? It would only irritate her and drive her to some _coup de t\xc3\xaate_.\r\nShe is very clever, like her mother; she would waste no time about it.\r\nAs a child\xe2\x80\x94when I was happy, or supposed I was\xe2\x80\x94she studied drawing and\r\npainting with first-class professors, and they assured me she had a\r\ntalent. I was delighted to believe it, and when I went into society I\r\nused to carry her pictures with me in a portfolio and hand them round\r\nto the company. I remember, once, a lady thought I was offering them\r\nfor sale, and I took it very ill. We don\xe2\x80\x99t know what we may come to!\r\nThen came my dark days, and my explosion with Madame Nioche. No\xc3\xa9mie had\r\nno more twenty-franc lessons; but in the course of time, when she grew\r\nolder, and it became highly expedient that she should do something that\r\nwould help to keep us alive, she bethought herself of her palette and\r\nbrushes. Some of our friends in the _quartier_ pronounced the idea\r\nfantastic: they recommended her to try bonnet making, to get a\r\nsituation in a shop, or\xe2\x80\x94if she was more ambitious\xe2\x80\x94to advertise for a\r\nplace of _dame de compagnie_. She did advertise, and an old lady wrote\r\nher a letter and bade her come and see her. The old lady liked her, and\r\noffered her her living and six hundred francs a year; but No\xc3\xa9mie\r\ndiscovered that she passed her life in her armchair and had only two\r\nvisitors, her confessor and her nephew: the confessor very strict, and\r\nthe nephew a man of fifty, with a broken nose and a government\r\nclerkship of two thousand francs. She threw her old lady over, bought a\r\npaint-box, a canvas, and a new dress, and went and set up her easel in\r\nthe Louvre. There in one place and another, she has passed the last two\r\nyears; I can\xe2\x80\x99t say it has made us millionaires. But No\xc3\xa9mie tells me\r\nthat Rome was not built in a day, that she is making great progress,\r\nthat I must leave her to her own devices. The fact is, without\r\nprejudice to her genius, that she has no idea of burying herself alive.\r\nShe likes to see the world, and to be seen. She says, herself, that she\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t work in the dark. With her appearance it is very natural. Only, I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t help worrying and trembling and wondering what may happen to her\r\nthere all alone, day after day, amid all that coming and going of\r\nstrangers. I can\xe2\x80\x99t be always at her side. I go with her in the morning,\r\nand I come to fetch her away, but she won\xe2\x80\x99t have me near her in the\r\ninterval; she says I make her nervous. As if it didn\xe2\x80\x99t make me nervous\r\nto wander about all day without her! Ah, if anything were to happen to\r\nher!\xe2\x80\x9d cried M. Nioche, clenching his two fists and jerking back his\r\nhead again, portentously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I guess nothing will happen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe I should shoot her!\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man, solemnly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, we\xe2\x80\x99ll marry her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9csince that\xe2\x80\x99s how you manage it;\r\nand I will go and see her tomorrow at the Louvre and pick out the\r\npictures she is to copy for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nM. Nioche had brought Newman a message from his daughter, in acceptance\r\nof his magnificent commission, the young lady declaring herself his\r\nmost devoted servant, promising her most zealous endeavor, and\r\nregretting that the proprieties forbade her coming to thank him in\r\nperson. The morning after the conversation just narrated, Newman\r\nreverted to his intention of meeting Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie at the Louvre.\r\nM. Nioche appeared preoccupied, and left his budget of anecdotes\r\nunopened; he took a great deal of snuff, and sent certain oblique,\r\nappealing glances toward his stalwart pupil. At last, when he was\r\ntaking his leave, he stood a moment, after he had polished his hat with\r\nhis calico pocket-handkerchief, with his small, pale eyes fixed\r\nstrangely upon Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d our hero demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse the solicitude of a father\xe2\x80\x99s heart!\xe2\x80\x9d said M. Nioche. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\ninspire me with boundless confidence, but I can\xe2\x80\x99t help giving you a\r\nwarning. After all, you are a man, you are young and at liberty. Let me\r\nbeseech you, then, to respect the innocence of Mademoiselle Nioche!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman had wondered what was coming, and at this he broke into a laugh.\r\nHe was on the point of declaring that his own innocence struck him as\r\nthe more exposed, but he contented himself with promising to treat the\r\nyoung girl with nothing less than veneration. He found her waiting for\r\nhim, seated upon the great divan in the Salon Carr\xc3\xa9. She was not in her\r\nworking-day costume, but wore her bonnet and gloves and carried her\r\nparasol, in honor of the occasion. These articles had been selected\r\nwith unerring taste, and a fresher, prettier image of youthful\r\nalertness and blooming discretion was not to be conceived. She made\r\nNewman a most respectful curtsey and expressed her gratitude for his\r\nliberality in a wonderfully graceful little speech. It annoyed him to\r\nhave a charming young girl stand there thanking him, and it made him\r\nfeel uncomfortable to think that this perfect young lady, with her\r\nexcellent manners and her finished intonation, was literally in his\r\npay. He assured her, in such French as he could muster, that the thing\r\nwas not worth mentioning, and that he considered her services a great\r\nfavor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhenever you please, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie, \xe2\x80\x9cwe will pass\r\nthe review.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey walked slowly round the room, then passed into the others and\r\nstrolled about for half an hour. Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie evidently relished\r\nher situation, and had no desire to bring her public interview with her\r\nstriking-looking patron to a close. Newman perceived that prosperity\r\nagreed with her. The little thin-lipped, peremptory air with which she\r\nhad addressed her father on the occasion of their former meeting had\r\ngiven place to the most lingering and caressing tones.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat sort of pictures do you desire?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked. \xe2\x80\x9cSacred, or profane?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, a few of each,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I want something bright and\r\ngay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething gay? There is nothing very gay in this solemn old Louvre.\r\nBut we will see what we can find. You speak French to-day like a charm.\r\nMy father has done wonders.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I am a bad subject,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI am too old to learn a\r\nlanguage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo old? _Quelle folie!_\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie, with a clear,\r\nshrill laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are a very young man. And how do you like my\r\nfather?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is a very nice old gentleman. He never laughs at my blunders.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is very _comme il faut_, my papa,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie, \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nas honest as the day. Oh, an exceptional probity! You could trust him\r\nwith millions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you always obey him?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cObey him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you do what he bids you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young girl stopped and looked at him; she had a spot of color in\r\neither cheek, and in her expressive French eye, which projected too\r\nmuch for perfect beauty, there was a slight gleam of audacity. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy do\r\nyou ask me that?\xe2\x80\x9d she demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I want to know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou think me a bad girl?\xe2\x80\x9d And she gave a strange smile.\r\n\r\nNewman looked at her a moment; he saw that she was pretty, but he was\r\nnot in the least dazzled. He remembered poor M. Nioche\xe2\x80\x99s solicitude for\r\nher \xe2\x80\x9cinnocence,\xe2\x80\x9d and he laughed as his eyes met hers. Her face was the\r\noddest mixture of youth and maturity, and beneath her candid brow her\r\nsearching little smile seemed to contain a world of ambiguous\r\nintentions. She was pretty enough, certainly to make her father\r\nnervous; but, as regards her innocence, Newman felt ready on the spot\r\nto affirm that she had never parted with it. She had simply never had\r\nany; she had been looking at the world since she was ten years old, and\r\nhe would have been a wise man who could tell her any secrets. In her\r\nlong mornings at the Louvre she had not only studied Madonnas and St.\r\nJohns; she had kept an eye upon all the variously embodied human nature\r\naround her, and she had formed her conclusions. In a certain sense, it\r\nseemed to Newman, M. Nioche might be at rest; his daughter might do\r\nsomething very audacious, but she would never do anything foolish.\r\nNewman, with his long-drawn, leisurely smile, and his even, unhurried\r\nutterance, was always, mentally, taking his time; and he asked himself,\r\nnow, what she was looking at him in that way for. He had an idea that\r\nshe would like him to confess that he did think her a bad girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last; \xe2\x80\x9cit would be very bad manners in me to judge\r\nyou that way. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut my father has complained to you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe says you are a coquette.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t go about saying such things to gentlemen! But you don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbelieve it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman gravely, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked at him again, gave a shrug and a smile, and then pointed to\r\na small Italian picture, a Marriage of St. Catherine. \xe2\x80\x9cHow should you\r\nlike that?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t please me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cThe young lady in the yellow\r\ndress is not pretty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you are a great connoisseur,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn pictures? Oh, no; I know very little about them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn pretty women, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn that I am hardly better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you say to that, then?\xe2\x80\x9d the young girl asked, indicating a\r\nsuperb Italian portrait of a lady. \xe2\x80\x9cI will do it for you on a smaller\r\nscale.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn a smaller scale? Why not as large as the original?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie glanced at the glowing splendor of the Venetian\r\nmasterpiece and gave a little toss of her head. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like that\r\nwoman. She looks stupid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do like her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cDecidedly, I must have her, as large as\r\nlife. And just as stupid as she is there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young girl fixed her eyes on him again, and with her mocking smile,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt certainly ought to be easy for me to make her look stupid!\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman, puzzled.\r\n\r\nShe gave another little shrug. \xe2\x80\x9cSeriously, then, you want that\r\nportrait\xe2\x80\x94the golden hair, the purple satin, the pearl necklace, the two\r\nmagnificent arms?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything\xe2\x80\x94just as it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould nothing else do, instead?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I want some other things, but I want that too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie turned away a moment, walked to the other side of\r\nthe hall, and stood there, looking vaguely about her. At last she came\r\nback. \xe2\x80\x9cIt must be charming to be able to order pictures at such a rate.\r\nVenetian portraits, as large as life! You go at it _en prince_. And you\r\nare going to travel about Europe that way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I intend to travel,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOrdering, buying, spending money?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I shall spend some money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very happy to have it. And you are perfectly free?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you mean, free?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have nothing to bother you\xe2\x80\x94no family, no wife, no _fianc\xc3\xa9e?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I am tolerably free.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very happy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie, gravely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Je le veux bien!_\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, proving that he had learned more\r\nFrench than he admitted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how long shall you stay in Paris?\xe2\x80\x9d the young girl went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly a few days more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you go away?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is getting hot, and I must go to Switzerland.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo Switzerland? That\xe2\x80\x99s a fine country. I would give my new parasol to\r\nsee it! Lakes and mountains, romantic valleys and icy peaks! Oh, I\r\ncongratulate you. Meanwhile, I shall sit here through all the hot\r\nsummer, daubing at your pictures.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, take your time about it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cDo them at your\r\nconvenience.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey walked farther and looked at a dozen other things. Newman pointed\r\nout what pleased him, and Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie generally criticised it,\r\nand proposed something else. Then suddenly she diverged and began to\r\ntalk about some personal matter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat made you speak to me the other day in the Salon Carr\xc3\xa9?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nabruptly asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI admired your picture.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you hesitated a long time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I do nothing rashly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I saw you watching me. But I never supposed you were going to\r\nspeak to me. I never dreamed I should be walking about here with you\r\nto-day. It\xe2\x80\x99s very curious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very natural,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I beg your pardon; not to me. Coquette as you think me, I have\r\nnever walked about in public with a gentleman before. What was my\r\nfather thinking of, when he consented to our interview?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was repenting of his unjust accusations,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Newman.\r\n\r\nMademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie remained silent; at last she dropped into a seat.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then, for those five it is fixed,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cFive copies as\r\nbrilliant and beautiful as I can make them. We have one more to choose.\r\nShouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you like one of those great Rubenses\xe2\x80\x94the marriage of Marie de\r\nM\xc3\xa9dicis? Just look at it and see how handsome it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes; I should like that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cFinish off with that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFinish off with that\xe2\x80\x94good!\xe2\x80\x9d And she laughed. She sat a moment, looking\r\nat him, and then she suddenly rose and stood before him, with her hands\r\nhanging and clasped in front of her. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said\r\nwith a smile. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand how a man can be so ignorant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I am ignorant, certainly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, putting his hands into his\r\npockets.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s ridiculous! I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how to paint.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t know how?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI paint like a cat; I can\xe2\x80\x99t draw a straight line. I never sold a\r\npicture until you bought that thing the other day.\xe2\x80\x9d And as she offered\r\nthis surprising information she continued to smile.\r\n\r\nNewman burst into a laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you tell me this?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause it irritates me to see a clever man blunder so. My pictures\r\nare grotesque.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the one I possess\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat one is rather worse than usual.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cI like it all the same!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked at him askance. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is a very pretty thing to say,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nanswered; \xe2\x80\x9cbut it is my duty to warn you before you go farther. This\r\norder of yours is impossible, you know. What do you take me for? It is\r\nwork for ten men. You pick out the six most difficult pictures in the\r\nLouvre, and you expect me to go to work as if I were sitting down to\r\nhem a dozen pocket handkerchiefs. I wanted to see how far you would\r\ngo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman looked at the young girl in some perplexity. In spite of the\r\nridiculous blunder of which he stood convicted, he was very far from\r\nbeing a simpleton, and he had a lively suspicion that Mademoiselle\r\nNo\xc3\xa9mie\xe2\x80\x99s sudden frankness was not essentially more honest than her\r\nleaving him in error would have been. She was playing a game; she was\r\nnot simply taking pity on his \xc3\xa6sthetic verdancy. What was it she\r\nexpected to win? The stakes were high and the risk was great; the prize\r\ntherefore must have been commensurate. But even granting that the prize\r\nmight be great, Newman could not resist a movement of admiration for\r\nhis companion\xe2\x80\x99s intrepidity. She was throwing away with one hand,\r\nwhatever she might intend to do with the other, a very handsome sum of\r\nmoney.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you joking,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cor are you serious?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, serious!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie, but with her extraordinary\r\nsmile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know very little about pictures or how they are painted. If you\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t do all that, of course you can\xe2\x80\x99t. Do what you can, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will be very bad,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cif you are determined it shall be bad, of\r\ncourse it will. But why do you go on painting badly?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can do nothing else; I have no real talent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are deceiving your father, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young girl hesitated a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cHe knows very well!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman declared; \xe2\x80\x9cI am sure he believes in you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is afraid of me. I go on painting badly, as you say, because I want\r\nto learn. I like it, at any rate. And I like being here; it is a place\r\nto come to, every day; it is better than sitting in a little dark, damp\r\nroom, on a court, or selling buttons and whalebones over a counter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course it is much more amusing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cBut for a poor girl\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t it rather an expensive amusement?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I am very wrong, there is no doubt about that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mademoiselle\r\nNo\xc3\xa9mie. \xe2\x80\x9cBut rather than earn my living as some girls do\xe2\x80\x94toiling with a\r\nneedle, in little black holes, out of the world\xe2\x80\x94I would throw myself\r\ninto the Seine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no need of that,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman answered; \xe2\x80\x9cyour father told you my\r\noffer?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour offer?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe wants you to marry, and I told him I would give you a chance to\r\nearn your _dot_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe told me all about it, and you see the account I make of it! Why\r\nshould you take such an interest in my marriage?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy interest was in your father. I hold to my offer; do what you can,\r\nand I will buy what you paint.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe stood for some time, meditating, with her eyes on the ground. At\r\nlast, looking up, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat sort of a husband can you get for twelve\r\nthousand francs?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour father tells me he knows some very good young men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGrocers and butchers and little _ma\xc3\xaetres de caf\xc3\xa9s!_ I will not marry\r\nat all if I can\xe2\x80\x99t marry well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would advise you not to be too fastidious,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all\r\nthe advice I can give you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very much vexed at what I have said!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the young girl. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nhas done me no good. But I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat good did you expect it to do you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it, simply.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman looked at her a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, your pictures may be bad,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cbut you are too clever for me, nevertheless. I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand\r\nyou. Good-bye!\xe2\x80\x9d And he put out his hand.\r\n\r\nShe made no response, and offered him no farewell. She turned away and\r\nseated herself sidewise on a bench, leaning her head on the back of her\r\nhand, which clasped the rail in front of the pictures. Newman stood a\r\nmoment and then turned on his heel and retreated. He had understood her\r\nbetter than he confessed; this singular scene was a practical\r\ncommentary upon her father\xe2\x80\x99s statement that she was a frank coquette.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen Newman related to Mrs. Tristram his fruitless visit to Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9, she urged him not to be discouraged, but to carry out his plan\r\nof \xe2\x80\x9cseeing Europe\xe2\x80\x9d during the summer, and return to Paris in the autumn\r\nand settle down comfortably for the winter. \xe2\x80\x9cMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 will\r\nkeep,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cshe is not a woman who will marry from one day to\r\nanother.\xe2\x80\x9d Newman made no distinct affirmation that he would come back\r\nto Paris; he even talked about Rome and the Nile, and abstained from\r\nprofessing any especial interest in Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s continued\r\nwidowhood. This circumstance was at variance with his habitual\r\nfrankness, and may perhaps be regarded as characteristic of the\r\nincipient stage of that passion which is more particularly known as the\r\nmysterious one. The truth is that the expression of a pair of eyes that\r\nwere at once brilliant and mild had become very familiar to his memory,\r\nand he would not easily have resigned himself to the prospect of never\r\nlooking into them again. He communicated to Mrs. Tristram a number of\r\nother facts, of greater or less importance, as you choose; but on this\r\nparticular point he kept his own counsel. He took a kindly leave of M.\r\nNioche, having assured him that, so far as he was concerned, the\r\nblue-cloaked Madonna herself might have been present at his interview\r\nwith Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie; and left the old man nursing his\r\nbreast-pocket, in an ecstasy which the acutest misfortune might have\r\nbeen defied to dissipate. Newman then started on his travels, with all\r\nhis usual appearance of slow-strolling leisure, and all his essential\r\ndirectness and intensity of aim. No man seemed less in a hurry, and yet\r\nno man achieved more in brief periods. He had certain practical\r\ninstincts which served him excellently in his trade of tourist. He\r\nfound his way in foreign cities by divination, his memory was excellent\r\nwhen once his attention had been at all cordially given, and he emerged\r\nfrom dialogues in foreign tongues, of which he had, formally, not\r\nunderstood a word, in full possession of the particular fact he had\r\ndesired to ascertain. His appetite for facts was capacious, and\r\nalthough many of those which he noted would have seemed woefully dry\r\nand colorless to the ordinary sentimental traveler, a careful\r\ninspection of the list would have shown that he had a soft spot in his\r\nimagination. In the charming city of Brussels\xe2\x80\x94his first stopping-place\r\nafter leaving Paris\xe2\x80\x94he asked a great many questions about the\r\nstreet-cars, and took extreme satisfaction in the reappearance of this\r\nfamiliar symbol of American civilization; but he was also greatly\r\nstruck with the beautiful Gothic tower of the H\xc3\xb4tel de Ville, and\r\nwondered whether it would not be possible to \xe2\x80\x9cget up\xe2\x80\x9d something like it\r\nin San Francisco. He stood for half an hour in the crowded square\r\nbefore this edifice, in imminent danger from carriage-wheels, listening\r\nto a toothless old cicerone mumble in broken English the touching\r\nhistory of Counts Egmont and Horn; and he wrote the names of these\r\ngentlemen\xe2\x80\x94for reasons best known to himself\xe2\x80\x94on the back of an old\r\nletter.\r\n\r\nAt the outset, on his leaving Paris, his curiosity had not been\r\nintense; passive entertainment, in the Champs \xc3\x89lys\xc3\xa9es and at the\r\ntheatres, seemed about as much as he need expect of himself, and\r\nalthough, as he had said to Tristram, he wanted to see the mysterious,\r\nsatisfying _best_, he had not the Grand Tour in the least on his\r\nconscience, and was not given to cross-questioning the amusement of the\r\nhour. He believed that Europe was made for him, and not he for Europe.\r\nHe had said that he wanted to improve his mind, but he would have felt\r\na certain embarrassment, a certain shame, even\xe2\x80\x94a false shame,\r\npossibly\xe2\x80\x94if he had caught himself looking intellectually into the\r\nmirror. Neither in this nor in any other respect had Newman a high\r\nsense of responsibility; it was his prime conviction that a man\xe2\x80\x99s life\r\nshould be easy, and that he should be able to resolve privilege into a\r\nmatter of course. The world, to his sense, was a great bazaar, where\r\none might stroll about and purchase handsome things; but he was no more\r\nconscious, individually, of social pressure than he admitted the\r\nexistence of such a thing as an obligatory purchase. He had not only a\r\ndislike, but a sort of moral mistrust, of uncomfortable thoughts, and\r\nit was both uncomfortable and slightly contemptible to feel obliged to\r\nsquare one\xe2\x80\x99s self with a standard. One\xe2\x80\x99s standard was the ideal of\r\none\xe2\x80\x99s own good-humored prosperity, the prosperity which enabled one to\r\ngive as well as take. To expand, without bothering about it\xe2\x80\x94without\r\nshiftless timidity on one side, or loquacious eagerness on the other\xe2\x80\x94to\r\nthe full compass of what he would have called a \xe2\x80\x9cpleasant\xe2\x80\x9d experience,\r\nwas Newman\xe2\x80\x99s most definite programme of life. He had always hated to\r\nhurry to catch railroad trains, and yet he had always caught them; and\r\njust so an undue solicitude for \xe2\x80\x9cculture\xe2\x80\x9d seemed a sort of silly\r\ndawdling at the station, a proceeding properly confined to women,\r\nforeigners, and other unpractical persons. All this admitted, Newman\r\nenjoyed his journey, when once he had fairly entered the current, as\r\nprofoundly as the most zealous _dilettante_. One\xe2\x80\x99s theories, after all,\r\nmatter little; it is one\xe2\x80\x99s humor that is the great thing. Our friend\r\nwas intelligent, and he could not help that. He lounged through Belgium\r\nand Holland and the Rhineland, through Switzerland and Northern Italy,\r\nplanning about nothing, but seeing everything. The guides and _valets\r\nde place_ found him an excellent subject. He was always approachable,\r\nfor he was much addicted to standing about in the vestibules and\r\nporticos of inns, and he availed himself little of the opportunities\r\nfor impressive seclusion which are so liberally offered in Europe to\r\ngentlemen who travel with long purses. When an excursion, a church, a\r\ngallery, a ruin, was proposed to him, the first thing Newman usually\r\ndid, after surveying his postulant in silence, from head to foot, was\r\nto sit down at a little table and order something to drink. The\r\ncicerone, during this process, usually retreated to a respectful\r\ndistance; otherwise I am not sure that Newman would not have bidden him\r\nsit down and have a glass also, and tell him as an honest fellow\r\nwhether his church or his gallery was really worth a man\xe2\x80\x99s trouble. At\r\nlast he rose and stretched his long legs, beckoned to the man of\r\nmonuments, looked at his watch, and fixed his eye on his adversary.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked. \xe2\x80\x9cHow far?\xe2\x80\x9d And whatever the answer was,\r\nalthough he sometimes seemed to hesitate, he never declined. He stepped\r\ninto an open cab, made his conductor sit beside him to answer\r\nquestions, bade the driver go fast (he had a particular aversion to\r\nslow driving) and rolled, in all probability through a dusty suburb, to\r\nthe goal of his pilgrimage. If the goal was a disappointment, if the\r\nchurch was meagre, or the ruin a heap of rubbish, Newman never\r\nprotested or berated his cicerone; he looked with an impartial eye upon\r\ngreat monuments and small, made the guide recite his lesson, listened\r\nto it religiously, asked if there was nothing else to be seen in the\r\nneighborhood, and drove back again at a rattling pace. It is to be\r\nfeared that his perception of the difference between good architecture\r\nand bad was not acute, and that he might sometimes have been seen\r\ngazing with culpable serenity at inferior productions. Ugly churches\r\nwere a part of his pastime in Europe, as well as beautiful ones, and\r\nhis tour was altogether a pastime. But there is sometimes nothing like\r\nthe imagination of these people who have none, and Newman, now and\r\nthen, in an unguided stroll in a foreign city, before some lonely,\r\nsad-towered church, or some angular image of one who had rendered civic\r\nservice in an unknown past, had felt a singular inward tremor. It was\r\nnot an excitement or a perplexity; it was a placid, fathomless sense of\r\ndiversion.\r\n\r\nHe encountered by chance in Holland a young American, with whom, for a\r\ntime, he formed a sort of traveler\xe2\x80\x99s partnership. They were men of a\r\nvery different cast, but each, in his way, was so good a fellow that,\r\nfor a few weeks at least, it seemed something of a pleasure to share\r\nthe chances of the road. Newman\xe2\x80\x99s comrade, whose name was Babcock, was\r\na young Unitarian minister, a small, spare, neatly-attired man, with a\r\nstrikingly candid physiognomy. He was a native of Dorchester,\r\nMassachusetts, and had spiritual charge of a small congregation in\r\nanother suburb of the New England metropolis. His digestion was weak\r\nand he lived chiefly on Graham bread and hominy\xe2\x80\x94a regimen to which he\r\nwas so much attached that his tour seemed to him destined to be\r\nblighted when, on landing on the Continent, he found that these\r\ndelicacies did not flourish under the _table d\xe2\x80\x99h\xc3\xb4te_ system. In Paris\r\nhe had purchased a bag of hominy at an establishment which called\r\nitself an American Agency, and at which the New York illustrated papers\r\nwere also to be procured, and he had carried it about with him, and\r\nshown extreme serenity and fortitude in the somewhat delicate position\r\nof having his hominy prepared for him and served at anomalous hours, at\r\nthe hotels he successively visited. Newman had once spent a morning, in\r\nthe course of business, at Mr. Babcock\xe2\x80\x99s birthplace, and, for reasons\r\ntoo recondite to unfold, his visit there always assumed in his mind a\r\njocular cast. To carry out his joke, which certainly seems poor so long\r\nas it is not explained, he used often to address his companion as\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDorchester.\xe2\x80\x9d Fellow-travelers very soon grow intimate but it is highly\r\nimprobable that at home these extremely dissimilar characters would\r\nhave found any very convenient points of contact. They were, indeed, as\r\ndifferent as possible. Newman, who never reflected on such matters,\r\naccepted the situation with great equanimity, but Babcock used to\r\nmeditate over it privately; used often, indeed, to retire to his room\r\nearly in the evening for the express purpose of considering it\r\nconscientiously and impartially. He was not sure that it was a good\r\nthing for him to associate with our hero, whose way of taking life was\r\nso little his own. Newman was an excellent, generous fellow; Mr.\r\nBabcock sometimes said to himself that he was a _noble_ fellow, and,\r\ncertainly, it was impossible not to like him. But would it not be\r\ndesirable to try to exert an influence upon him, to try to quicken his\r\nmoral life and sharpen his sense of duty? He liked everything, he\r\naccepted everything, he found amusement in everything; he was not\r\ndiscriminating, he had not a high tone. The young man from Dorchester\r\naccused Newman of a fault which he considered very grave, and which he\r\ndid his best to avoid: what he would have called a want of \xe2\x80\x9cmoral\r\nreaction.\xe2\x80\x9d Poor Mr. Babcock was extremely fond of pictures and\r\nchurches, and carried Mrs. Jameson\xe2\x80\x99s works about in his trunk; he\r\ndelighted in \xc3\xa6sthetic analysis, and received peculiar impressions from\r\neverything he saw. But nevertheless in his secret soul he detested\r\nEurope, and he felt an irritating need to protest against Newman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngross intellectual hospitality. Mr. Babcock\xe2\x80\x99s moral _malaise_, I am\r\nafraid, lay deeper than where any definition of mine can reach it. He\r\nmistrusted the European temperament, he suffered from the European\r\nclimate, he hated the European dinner-hour; European life seemed to him\r\nunscrupulous and impure. And yet he had an exquisite sense of beauty;\r\nand as beauty was often inextricably associated with the above\r\ndispleasing conditions, as he wished, above all, to be just and\r\ndispassionate, and as he was, furthermore, extremely devoted to\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cculture,\xe2\x80\x9d he could not bring himself to decide that Europe was utterly\r\nbad. But he thought it was very bad indeed, and his quarrel with Newman\r\nwas that this unregulated epicure had a sadly insufficient perception\r\nof the bad. Babcock himself really knew as little about the bad, in any\r\nquarter of the world, as a nursing infant, his most vivid realization\r\nof evil had been the discovery that one of his college classmates, who\r\nwas studying architecture in Paris had a love affair with a young woman\r\nwho did not expect him to marry her. Babcock had related this incident\r\nto Newman, and our hero had applied an epithet of an unflattering sort\r\nto the young girl. The next day his companion asked him whether he was\r\nvery sure he had used exactly the right word to characterize the young\r\narchitect\xe2\x80\x99s mistress. Newman stared and laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are a great\r\nmany words to express that idea,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cyou can take your choice!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I mean,\xe2\x80\x9d said Babcock, \xe2\x80\x9cwas she possibly not to be considered in a\r\ndifferent light? Don\xe2\x80\x99t you think she _really_ expected him to marry\r\nher?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure I don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cVery likely she did; I have no\r\ndoubt she is a grand woman.\xe2\x80\x9d And he began to laugh again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean that either,\xe2\x80\x9d said Babcock, \xe2\x80\x9cI was only afraid that I\r\nmight have seemed yesterday not to remember\xe2\x80\x94not to consider; well, I\r\nthink I will write to Percival about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd he had written to Percival (who answered him in a really impudent\r\nfashion), and he had reflected that it was somehow, raw and reckless in\r\nNewman to assume in that off-hand manner that the young woman in Paris\r\nmight be \xe2\x80\x9cgrand.\xe2\x80\x9d The brevity of Newman\xe2\x80\x99s judgments very often shocked\r\nand discomposed him. He had a way of damning people without farther\r\nappeal, or of pronouncing them capital company in the face of\r\nuncomfortable symptoms, which seemed unworthy of a man whose conscience\r\nhad been properly cultivated.
