Newman thought it a\r\ndamp, gloomy place to live in, and was puzzled by the obstructive and\r\nfragmentary character of the furniture.\r\n\r\nBellegarde, according to the custom of his country talked very\r\ngenerously about himself, and unveiled the mysteries of his private\r\nhistory with an unsparing hand. Inevitably, he had a vast deal to say\r\nabout women, and he used frequently to indulge in sentimental and\r\nironical apostrophes to these authors of his joys and woes. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, the\r\nwomen, the women, and the things they have made me do!\xe2\x80\x9d he would\r\nexclaim with a lustrous eye. \xe2\x80\x9c_C\xe2\x80\x99est \xc3\xa9gal_, of all the follies and\r\nstupidities I have committed for them I would not have missed one!\xe2\x80\x9d On\r\nthis subject Newman maintained an habitual reserve; to expatiate\r\nlargely upon it had always seemed to him a proceeding vaguely analogous\r\nto the cooing of pigeons and the chattering of monkeys, and even\r\ninconsistent with a fully developed human character. But Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nconfidences greatly amused him, and rarely displeased him, for the\r\ngenerous young Frenchman was not a cynic. \xe2\x80\x9cI really think,\xe2\x80\x9d he had once\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I am not more depraved than most of my contemporaries. They\r\nare tolerably depraved, my contemporaries!\xe2\x80\x9d He said wonderfully pretty\r\nthings about his female friends, and, numerous and various as they had\r\nbeen, declared that on the whole there was more good in them than harm.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you are not to take that as advice,\xe2\x80\x9d he added. \xe2\x80\x9cAs an authority I\r\nam very untrustworthy. I\xe2\x80\x99m prejudiced in their favor; I\xe2\x80\x99m an\r\n_idealist!_\xe2\x80\x9d Newman listened to him with his impartial smile, and was\r\nglad, for his own sake, that he had fine feelings; but he mentally\r\nrepudiated the idea of a Frenchman having discovered any merit in the\r\namiable sex which he himself did not suspect. M. de Bellegarde,\r\nhowever, did not confine his conversation to the autobiographical\r\nchannel; he questioned our hero largely as to the events of his own\r\nlife, and Newman told him some better stories than any that Bellegarde\r\ncarried in his budget. He narrated his career, in fact, from the\r\nbeginning, through all its variations, and whenever his companion\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncredulity, or his habits of gentility, appeared to protest, it amused\r\nhim to heighten the color of the episode. Newman had sat with Western\r\nhumorists in knots, round cast-iron stoves, and seen \xe2\x80\x9ctall\xe2\x80\x9d stories\r\ngrow taller without toppling over, and his own imagination had learned\r\nthe trick of piling up consistent wonders. Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s regular\r\nattitude at last became that of laughing self-defense; to maintain his\r\nreputation as an all-knowing Frenchman, he doubted of everything,\r\nwholesale. The result of this was that Newman found it impossible to\r\nconvince him of certain time-honored verities.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the details don\xe2\x80\x99t matter,\xe2\x80\x9d said M. de Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have\r\nevidently had some surprising adventures; you have seen some strange\r\nsides of life, you have revolved to and fro over a whole continent as I\r\nwalked up and down the Boulevard. You are a man of the world with a\r\nvengeance! You have spent some deadly dull hours, and you have done\r\nsome extremely disagreeable things: you have shoveled sand, as a boy,\r\nfor supper, and you have eaten roast dog in a gold-diggers\xe2\x80\x99 camp. You\r\nhave stood casting up figures for ten hours at a time, and you have sat\r\nthrough Methodist sermons for the sake of looking at a pretty girl in\r\nanother pew. All that is rather stiff, as we say. But at any rate you\r\nhave done something and you are something; you have used your will and\r\nyou have made your fortune. You have not stupified yourself with\r\ndebauchery and you have not mortgaged your fortune to social\r\nconveniences. You take things easily, and you have fewer prejudices\r\neven than I, who pretend to have none, but who in reality have three or\r\nfour. Happy man, you are strong and you are free. But what the deuce,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ndemanded the young man in conclusion, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you propose to do with such\r\nadvantages? Really to use them you need a better world than this. There\r\nis nothing worth your while here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I think there is something,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cI will tell you some other time!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn this way our hero delayed from day to day broaching a subject which\r\nhe had very much at heart. Meanwhile, however, he was growing\r\npractically familiar with it; in other words, he had called again,\r\nthree times, on Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. On only two of these occasions had he\r\nfound her at home, and on each of them she had other visitors. Her\r\nvisitors were numerous and extremely loquacious, and they exacted much\r\nof their hostess\xe2\x80\x99s attention. She found time, however, to bestow a\r\nlittle of it on Newman, in an occasional vague smile, the very\r\nvagueness of which pleased him, allowing him as it did to fill it out\r\nmentally, both at the time and afterwards, with such meanings as most\r\npleased him. He sat by without speaking, looking at the entrances and\r\nexits, the greetings and chatterings, of Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s visitors.\r\nHe felt as if he were at the play, and as if his own speaking would be\r\nan interruption; sometimes he wished he had a book, to follow the\r\ndialogue; he half expected to see a woman in a white cap and pink\r\nribbons come and offer him one for two francs. Some of the ladies\r\nlooked at him very hard\xe2\x80\x94or very soft, as you please; others seemed\r\nprofoundly unconscious of his presence. The men looked only at Madame\r\nde Cintr\xc3\xa9. This was inevitable; for whether one called her beautiful or\r\nnot, she entirely occupied and filled one\xe2\x80\x99s vision, just as an\r\nagreeable sound fills one\xe2\x80\x99s ear. Newman had but twenty distinct words\r\nwith her, but he carried away an impression to which solemn promises\r\ncould not have given a higher value. She was part of the play that he\r\nwas seeing acted, quite as much as her companions; but how she filled\r\nthe stage and how much better she did it! Whether she rose or seated\r\nherself; whether she went with her departing friends to the door and\r\nlifted up the heavy curtain as they passed out, and stood an instant\r\nlooking after them and giving them the last nod; or whether she leaned\r\nback in her chair with her arms crossed and her eyes resting, listening\r\nand smiling; she gave Newman the feeling that he should like to have\r\nher always before him, moving slowly to and fro along the whole scale\r\nof expressive hospitality. If it might be _to_ him, it would be well;\r\nif it might be _for_ him, it would be still better! She was so tall and\r\nyet so light, so active and yet so still, so elegant and yet so simple,\r\nso frank and yet so mysterious! It was the mystery\xe2\x80\x94it was what she was\r\noff the stage, as it were\xe2\x80\x94that interested Newman most of all. He could\r\nnot have told you what warrant he had for talking about mysteries; if\r\nit had been his habit to express himself in poetic figures he might\r\nhave said that in observing Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 he seemed to see the vague\r\ncircle which sometimes accompanies the partly-filled disk of the moon.\r\nIt was not that she was reserved; on the contrary, she was as frank as\r\nflowing water. But he was sure she had qualities which she herself did\r\nnot suspect.\r\n\r\nHe had abstained for several reasons from saying some of these things\r\nto Bellegarde. One reason was that before proceeding to any act he was\r\nalways circumspect, conjectural, contemplative; he had little\r\neagerness, as became a man who felt that whenever he really began to\r\nmove he walked with long steps. And then, it simply pleased him not to\r\nspeak\xe2\x80\x94it occupied him, it excited him. But one day Bellegarde had been\r\ndining with him, at a restaurant, and they had sat long over their\r\ndinner. On rising from it, Bellegarde proposed that, to help them\r\nthrough the rest of the evening, they should go and see Madame\r\nDandelard. Madame Dandelard was a little Italian lady who had married a\r\nFrenchman who proved to be a rake and a brute and the torment of her\r\nlife. Her husband had spent all her money, and then, lacking the means\r\nof obtaining more expensive pleasures, had taken, in his duller hours,\r\nto beating her. She had a blue spot somewhere, which she showed to\r\nseveral persons, including Bellegarde. She had obtained a separation\r\nfrom her husband, collected the scraps of her fortune (they were very\r\nmeagre) and come to live in Paris, where she was staying at a _h\xc3\xb4tel\r\ngarni_. She was always looking for an apartment, and visiting,\r\ninquiringly, those of other people. She was very pretty, very\r\nchildlike, and she made very extraordinary remarks. Bellegarde had made\r\nher acquaintance, and the source of his interest in her was, according\r\nto his own declaration, a curiosity as to what would become of her.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is poor, she is pretty, and she is silly,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cit seems to\r\nme she can go only one way. It\xe2\x80\x99s a pity, but it can\xe2\x80\x99t be helped. I will\r\ngive her six months. She has nothing to fear from me, but I am watching\r\nthe process. I am curious to see just how things will go. Yes, I know\r\nwhat you are going to say: this horrible Paris hardens one\xe2\x80\x99s heart. But\r\nit quickens one\xe2\x80\x99s wits, and it ends by teaching one a refinement of\r\nobservation! To see this little woman\xe2\x80\x99s little drama play itself out,\r\nnow, is, for me, an intellectual pleasure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf she is going to throw herself away,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman had said, \xe2\x80\x9cyou ought to\r\nstop her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop her? How stop her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTalk to her; give her some good advice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBellegarde laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cHeaven deliver us both! Imagine the situation! Go\r\nand advise her yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was after this that Newman had gone with Bellegarde to see Madame\r\nDandelard. When they came away, Bellegarde reproached his companion.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere was your famous advice?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t hear a word of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I give it up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, simply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you are as bad as I!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bellegarde.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, because I don\xe2\x80\x99t take an \xe2\x80\x98intellectual pleasure\xe2\x80\x99 in her prospective\r\nadventures. I don\xe2\x80\x99t in the least want to see her going down hill. I had\r\nrather look the other way. But why,\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, in a moment, \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nget your sister to go and see her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBellegarde stared. \xe2\x80\x9cGo and see Madame Dandelard\xe2\x80\x94my sister?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe might talk to her to very good purpose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBellegarde shook his head with sudden gravity. \xe2\x80\x9cMy sister can\xe2\x80\x99t see\r\nthat sort of person. Madame Dandelard is nothing at all; they would\r\nnever meet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cthat your sister might see whom she\r\npleased.\xe2\x80\x9d And he privately resolved that after he knew her a little\r\nbetter he would ask Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 to go and talk to the foolish\r\nlittle Italian lady.\r\n\r\nAfter his dinner with Bellegarde, on the occasion I have mentioned, he\r\ndemurred to his companion\xe2\x80\x99s proposal that they should go again and\r\nlisten to Madame Dandelard describe her sorrows and her bruises.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have something better in mind,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9ccome home with me and\r\nfinish the evening before my fire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBellegarde always welcomed the prospect of a long stretch of\r\nconversation, and before long the two men sat watching the great blaze\r\nwhich scattered its scintillations over the high adornments of Newman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nball-room.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me something about your sister,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman began abruptly.\r\n\r\nBellegarde turned and gave him a quick look. \xe2\x80\x9cNow that I think of it,\r\nyou have never yet asked me a question about her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know that very well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it is because you don\xe2\x80\x99t trust me, you are very right,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nBellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t talk of her rationally. I admire her too much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTalk of her as you can,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cLet yourself go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we are very good friends; we are such a brother and sister as\r\nhave not been seen since Orestes and Electra. You have seen her; you\r\nknow what she is: tall, thin, light, imposing, and gentle, half a\r\n_grande dame_ and half an angel; a mixture of pride and humility, of\r\nthe eagle and the dove. She looks like a statue which had failed as\r\nstone, resigned itself to its grave defects, and come to life as flesh\r\nand blood, to wear white capes and long trains. All I can say is that\r\nshe really possesses every merit that her face, her glance, her smile,\r\nthe tone of her voice, lead you to expect; it is saying a great deal.\r\nAs a general thing, when a woman seems very charming, I should say\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Beware!\xe2\x80\x99 But in proportion as Claire seems charming you may fold your\r\narms and let yourself float with the current; you are safe. She is so\r\ngood! I have never seen a woman half so perfect or so complete. She has\r\neverything; that is all I can say about her. There!\xe2\x80\x9d Bellegarde\r\nconcluded; \xe2\x80\x9cI told you I should rhapsodize.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman was silent a while, as if he were turning over his companion\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwords. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is very good, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDivinely good!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKind, charitable, gentle, generous?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGenerosity itself; kindness double-distilled!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs she clever?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is the most intelligent woman I know. Try her, some day, with\r\nsomething difficult, and you will see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs she fond of admiration?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Parbleu!_\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bellegarde; \xe2\x80\x9cwhat woman is not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, when they are too fond of admiration they commit all kinds of\r\nfollies to get it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not say she was too fond!\xe2\x80\x9d Bellegarde exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cHeaven forbid\r\nI should say anything so idiotic. She is not _too_ anything! If I were\r\nto say she was ugly, I should not mean she was too ugly. She is fond of\r\npleasing, and if you are pleased she is grateful. If you are not\r\npleased, she lets it pass and thinks the worst neither of you nor of\r\nherself. I imagine, though, she hopes the saints in heaven are, for I\r\nam sure she is incapable of trying to please by any means of which they\r\nwould disapprove.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs she grave or gay?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is both; not alternately, for she is always the same. There is\r\ngravity in her gaiety, and gaiety in her gravity. But there is no\r\nreason why she should be particularly gay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs she unhappy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t say that, for unhappiness is according as one takes things,\r\nand Claire takes them according to some receipt communicated to her by\r\nthe Blessed Virgin in a vision. To be unhappy is to be disagreeable,\r\nwhich, for her, is out of the question. So she has arranged her\r\ncircumstances so as to be happy in them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is a philosopher,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, she is simply a very nice woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHer circumstances, at any rate, have been disagreeable?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBellegarde hesitated a moment\xe2\x80\x94a thing he very rarely did. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, my dear\r\nfellow, if I go into the history of my family I shall give you more\r\nthan you bargain for.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, on the contrary, I bargain for that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall have to appoint a special s\xc3\xa9ance, then, beginning early.\r\nSuffice it for the present that Claire has not slept on roses. She made\r\nat eighteen a marriage that was expected to be brilliant, but that\r\nturned out like a lamp that goes out; all smoke and bad smell. M. de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9 was sixty years old, and an odious old gentleman. He lived,\r\nhowever, but a short time, and after his death his family pounced upon\r\nhis money, brought a lawsuit against his widow, and pushed things very\r\nhard. Their case was a good one, for M. de Cintr\xc3\xa9, who had been trustee\r\nfor some of his relatives, appeared to have been guilty of some very\r\nirregular practices. In the course of the suit some revelations were\r\nmade as to his private history which my sister found so displeasing\r\nthat she ceased to defend herself and washed her hands of the property.\r\nThis required some pluck, for she was between two fires, her husband\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfamily opposing her and her own family forcing her. My mother and my\r\nbrother wished her to cleave to what they regarded as her rights. But\r\nshe resisted firmly, and at last bought her freedom\xe2\x80\x94obtained my\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x99s assent to dropping the suit at the price of a promise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was the promise?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo do anything else, for the next ten years, that was asked of\r\nher\xe2\x80\x94anything, that is, but marry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe had disliked her husband very much?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one knows how much!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe marriage had been made in your horrible French way,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman\r\ncontinued, \xe2\x80\x9cmade by the two families, without her having any voice?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was a chapter for a novel. She saw M. de Cintr\xc3\xa9 for the first time\r\na month before the wedding, after everything, to the minutest detail,\r\nhad been arranged. She turned white when she looked at him, and white\r\nshe remained till her wedding-day. The evening before the ceremony she\r\nswooned away, and she spent the whole night in sobs. My mother sat\r\nholding her two hands, and my brother walked up and down the room. I\r\ndeclared it was revolting and told my sister publicly that if she would\r\nrefuse, downright, I would stand by her. I was told to go about my\r\nbusiness, and she became Comtesse de Cintr\xc3\xa9.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour brother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, reflectively, \xe2\x80\x9cmust be a very nice young\r\nman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is very nice, though he is not young. He is upward of fifty,\r\nfifteen years my senior. He has been a father to my sister and me. He\r\nis a very remarkable man; he has the best manners in France. He is\r\nextremely clever; indeed he is very learned. He is writing a history of\r\nThe Princesses of France Who Never Married.\xe2\x80\x9d This was said by\r\nBellegarde with extreme gravity, looking straight at Newman, and with\r\nan eye that betokened no mental reservation; or that, at least, almost\r\nbetokened none.\r\n\r\nNewman perhaps discovered there what little there was, for he presently\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t love your brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bellegarde, ceremoniously; \xe2\x80\x9cwell-bred people\r\nalways love their brothers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t love him, then!\xe2\x80\x9d Newman answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait till you know him!\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Bellegarde, and this time he smiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs your mother also very remarkable?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman asked, after a pause.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor my mother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bellegarde, now with intense gravity, \xe2\x80\x9cI have the\r\nhighest admiration. She is a very extraordinary woman. You cannot\r\napproach her without perceiving it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is the daughter, I believe, of an English nobleman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf the Earl of St. Dunstan\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs the Earl of St. Dunstan\xe2\x80\x99s a very old family?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo-so; the sixteenth century. It is on my father\xe2\x80\x99s side that we go\r\nback\xe2\x80\x94back, back, back. The family antiquaries themselves lose breath.\r\nAt last they stop, panting and fanning themselves, somewhere in the\r\nninth century, under Charlemagne. That is where we begin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no mistake about it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure I hope not. We have been mistaken at least for several\r\ncenturies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you have always married into old families?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs a rule; though in so long a stretch of time there have been some\r\nexceptions. Three or four Bellegardes, in the seventeenth and\r\neighteenth centuries, took wives out of the _bourgeoisie_\xe2\x80\x94married\r\nlawyers\xe2\x80\x99 daughters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA lawyer\xe2\x80\x99s daughter; that\xe2\x80\x99s very bad, is it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHorrible! one of us, in the Middle Ages, did better: he married a\r\nbeggar-maid, like King Cophetua. That was really better; it was like\r\nmarrying a bird or a monkey; one didn\xe2\x80\x99t have to think about her family\r\nat all. Our women have always done well; they have never even gone into\r\nthe _petite noblesse_. There is, I believe, not a case on record of a\r\nmisalliance among the women.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman turned this over for a while, and, then at last he said, \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\noffered, the first time you came to see me to render me any service you\r\ncould. I told you that some time I would mention something you might\r\ndo. Do you remember?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRemember? I have been counting the hours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well; here\xe2\x80\x99s your chance. Do what you can to make your sister\r\nthink well of me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBellegarde stared, with a smile. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure she thinks as well of\r\nyou as possible, already.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn opinion founded on seeing me three or four times? That is putting\r\nme off with very little. I want something more. I have been thinking of\r\nit a good deal, and at last I have decided to tell you. I should like\r\nvery much to marry Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBellegarde had been looking at him with quickened expectancy, and with\r\nthe smile with which he had greeted Newman\xe2\x80\x99s allusion to his promised\r\nrequest. At this last announcement he continued to gaze; but his smile\r\nwent through two or three curious phases. It felt, apparently, a\r\nmomentary impulse to broaden; but this it immediately checked. Then it\r\nremained for some instants taking counsel with itself, at the end of\r\nwhich it decreed a retreat. It slowly effaced itself and left a look of\r\nseriousness modified by the desire not to be rude. Extreme surprise had\r\ncome into the Count Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s face; but he had reflected that it would\r\nbe uncivil to leave it there. And yet, what the deuce was he to do with\r\nit? He got up, in his agitation, and stood before the chimney-piece,\r\nstill looking at Newman. He was a longer time thinking what to say than\r\none would have expected.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you can\xe2\x80\x99t render me the service I ask,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9csay it out!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me hear it again, distinctly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very\r\nimportant, you know. I shall plead your cause with my sister, because\r\nyou want\xe2\x80\x94you want to marry her? That\xe2\x80\x99s it, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t say plead my cause, exactly; I shall try and do that\r\nmyself. But say a good word for me, now and then\xe2\x80\x94let her know that you\r\nthink well of me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this, Bellegarde gave a little light laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat I want chiefly, after all,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman went on, \xe2\x80\x9cis just to let you\r\nknow what I have in mind. I suppose that is what you expect, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\r\nI want to do what is customary over here. If there is anything\r\nparticular to be done, let me know and I will do it. I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t for the\r\nworld approach Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 without all the proper forms. If I\r\nought to go and tell your mother, why I will go and tell her. I will go\r\nand tell your brother, even. I will go and tell anyone you please. As I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know anyone else, I begin by telling you. But that, if it is a\r\nsocial obligation, is a pleasure as well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I see\xe2\x80\x94I see,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bellegarde, lightly stroking his chin. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nhave a very right feeling about it, but I\xe2\x80\x99m glad you have begun with\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d He paused, hesitated, and then turned away and walked slowly the\r\nlength of the room. Newman got up and stood leaning against the\r\nmantel-shelf, with his hands in his pockets, watching Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npromenade. The young Frenchman came back and stopped in front of him.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI give it up,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cI will not pretend I am not surprised. I\r\nam\xe2\x80\x94hugely! _Ouf!_ It\xe2\x80\x99s a relief.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat sort of news is always a surprise,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cNo matter what\r\nyou have done, people are never prepared. But if you are so surprised,\r\nI hope at least you are pleased.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cI am going to be tremendously frank. I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow whether I am pleased or horrified.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you are pleased, I shall be glad,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cand I shall\r\nbe\xe2\x80\x94encouraged. If you are horrified, I shall be sorry, but I shall not\r\nbe discouraged. You must make the best of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is quite right\xe2\x80\x94that is your only possible attitude. You are\r\nperfectly serious?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAm I a Frenchman, that I should not be?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cBut why is it,\r\nby the bye, that you should be horrified?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBellegarde raised his hand to the back of his head and rubbed his hair\r\nquickly up and down, thrusting out the tip of his tongue as he did so.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you are not noble, for instance,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe devil I am not!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bellegarde a little more seriously, \xe2\x80\x9cI did not know you had\r\na title.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA title? What do you mean by a title?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cA count, a duke,\r\na marquis? I don\xe2\x80\x99t know anything about that, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know who is and\r\nwho is not. But I say I am noble. I don\xe2\x80\x99t exactly know what you mean by\r\nit, but it\xe2\x80\x99s a fine word and a fine idea; I put in a claim to it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what have you to show, my dear fellow, what proofs?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnything you please! But you don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose I am going to undertake to\r\nprove that I am noble. It is for you to prove the contrary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s easily done. You have manufactured wash-tubs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman stared a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cTherefore I am not noble? I don\xe2\x80\x99t see it. Tell\r\nme something I have _not_ done\xe2\x80\x94something I cannot do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou cannot marry a woman like Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 for the asking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe you mean,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman slowly, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I am not good enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBrutally speaking\xe2\x80\x94yes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBellegarde had hesitated a moment, and while he hesitated Newman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nattentive glance had grown somewhat eager. In answer to these last\r\nwords he for a moment said nothing. He simply blushed a little. Then he\r\nraised his eyes to the ceiling and stood looking at one of the rosy\r\ncherubs that was painted upon it. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course I don\xe2\x80\x99t expect to marry\r\nany woman for the asking,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last; \xe2\x80\x9cI expect first to make\r\nmyself acceptable to her. She must like me, to begin with. But that I\r\nam not good enough to make a trial is rather a surprise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBellegarde wore a look of mingled perplexity, sympathy, and amusement.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should not hesitate, then, to go up to-morrow and ask a duchess to\r\nmarry you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot if I thought she would suit me. But I am very fastidious; she\r\nmight not at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s amusement began to prevail. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you should be surprised\r\nif she refused you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman hesitated a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cIt sounds conceited to say yes, but\r\nnevertheless I think I should. For I should make a very handsome\r\noffer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat would it be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything she wishes. If I get hold of a woman that comes up to my\r\nstandard, I shall think nothing too good for her. I have been a long\r\ntime looking, and I find such women are rare. To combine the qualities\r\nI require seems to be difficult, but when the difficulty is vanquished\r\nit deserves a reward. My wife shall have a good position, and I\xe2\x80\x99m not\r\nafraid to say that I shall be a good husband.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd these qualities that you require\xe2\x80\x94what are they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoodness, beauty, intelligence, a fine education, personal\r\nelegance\xe2\x80\x94everything, in a word, that makes a splendid woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd noble birth, evidently,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bellegarde.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, throw that in, by all means, if it\xe2\x80\x99s there. The more the better!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd my sister seems to you to have all these things?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is exactly what I have been looking for. She is my dream\r\nrealized.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you would make her a very good husband?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is what I wanted you to tell her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBellegarde laid his hand on his companion\xe2\x80\x99s arm a moment, looked at him\r\nwith his head on one side, from head to foot, and then, with a loud\r\nlaugh, and shaking the other hand in the air, turned away. He walked\r\nagain the length of the room, and again he came back and stationed\r\nhimself in front of Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cAll this is very interesting\xe2\x80\x94it is very\r\ncurious. In what I said just now I was speaking, not for myself, but\r\nfor my tradition, my superstitions. For myself, really, your proposal\r\ntickles me. It startled me at first, but the more I think of it the\r\nmore I see in it. It\xe2\x80\x99s no use attempting to explain anything; you won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nunderstand me. After all, I don\xe2\x80\x99t see why you need; it\xe2\x80\x99s no great\r\nloss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, if there is anything more to explain, try it! I want to proceed\r\nwith my eyes open. I will do my best to understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bellegarde, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s disagreeable to me; I give it up. I liked\r\nyou the first time I saw you, and I will abide by that. It would be\r\nquite odious for me to come talking to you as if I could patronize you.\r\nI have told you before that I envy you; _vous m\xe2\x80\x99imposez_, as we say. I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t know you much until within five minutes. So we will let things\r\ngo, and I will say nothing to you that, if our positions were reversed,\r\nyou would not say to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI do not know whether in renouncing the mysterious opportunity to which\r\nhe alluded, Bellegarde felt that he was doing something very generous.\r\nIf so, he was not rewarded; his generosity was not appreciated. Newman\r\nquite failed to recognize the young Frenchman\xe2\x80\x99s power to wound his\r\nfeelings, and he had now no sense of escaping or coming off easily. He\r\ndid not thank his companion even with a glance. \xe2\x80\x9cMy eyes are open,\r\nthough,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cso far as that you have practically told me that\r\nyour family and your friends will turn up their noses at me. I have\r\nnever thought much about the reasons that make it proper for people to\r\nturn up their noses, and so I can only decide the question off-hand.\r\nLooking at it in that way I can\xe2\x80\x99t see anything in it. I simply think,\r\nif you want to know, that I\xe2\x80\x99m as good as the best. Who the best are, I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t pretend to say. I have never thought much about that either. To\r\ntell the truth, I have always had rather a good opinion of myself; a\r\nman who is successful can\xe2\x80\x99t help it. But I will admit that I was\r\nconceited. What I don\xe2\x80\x99t say yes to is that I don\xe2\x80\x99t stand high\xe2\x80\x94as high\r\nas anyone else. This is a line of speculation I should not have chosen,\r\nbut you must remember you began it yourself. I should never have\r\ndreamed that I was on the defensive, or that I had to justify myself;\r\nbut if your people will have it so, I will do my best.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you offered, a while ago, to make your court as we say, to my\r\nmother and my brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDamn it!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cI want to be polite.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood!\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Bellegarde; \xe2\x80\x9cthis will go far, it will be very\r\nentertaining. Excuse my speaking of it in that cold-blooded fashion,\r\nbut the matter must, of necessity, be for me something of a spectacle.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s positively exciting. But apart from that I sympathize with you,\r\nand I shall be actor, so far as I can, as well as spectator. You are a\r\ncapital fellow; I believe in you and I back you. The simple fact that\r\nyou appreciate my sister will serve as the proof I was asking for. All\r\nmen are equal\xe2\x80\x94especially men of taste!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think,\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman presently, \xe2\x80\x9cthat Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 is\r\ndetermined not to marry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is my impression. But that is not against you; it\xe2\x80\x99s for you to\r\nmake her change her mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid it will be hard,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, gravely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think it will be easy. In a general way I don\xe2\x80\x99t see why a\r\nwidow should ever marry again. She has gained the benefits of\r\nmatrimony\xe2\x80\x94freedom and consideration\xe2\x80\x94and she has got rid of the\r\ndrawbacks. Why should she put her head into the noose again? Her usual\r\nmotive is ambition: if a man can offer her a great position, make her a\r\nprincess or an ambassadress she may think the compensation sufficient.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd\xe2\x80\x94in that way\xe2\x80\x94is Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 ambitious?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho knows?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bellegarde, with a profound shrug. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t pretend\r\nto say all that she is or all that she is not. I think she might be\r\ntouched by the prospect of becoming the wife of a great man. But in a\r\ncertain way, I believe, whatever she does will be the _improbable_.\r\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t be too confident, but don\xe2\x80\x99t absolutely doubt. Your best chance\r\nfor success will be precisely in being, to her mind, unusual,\r\nunexpected, original. Don\xe2\x80\x99t try to be anyone else; be simply yourself,\r\nout and out. Something or other can\xe2\x80\x99t fail to come of it; I am very\r\ncurious to see what.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am much obliged to you for your advice,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nadded with a smile, \xe2\x80\x9cI am glad, for your sake, I am going to be so\r\namusing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will be more than amusing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bellegarde; \xe2\x80\x9cit will be inspiring.\r\nI look at it from my point of view, and you from yours. After all,\r\nanything for a change! And only yesterday I was yawning so as to\r\ndislocate my jaw, and declaring that there was nothing new under the\r\nsun! If it isn\xe2\x80\x99t new to see you come into the family as a suitor, I am\r\nvery much mistaken. Let me say that, my dear fellow; I won\xe2\x80\x99t call it\r\nanything else, bad or good; I will simply call it _new_.\xe2\x80\x9d And overcome\r\nwith a sense of the novelty thus foreshadowed, Valentin de Bellegarde\r\nthrew himself into a deep armchair before the fire, and, with a fixed,\r\nintense smile, seemed to read a vision of it in the flame of the logs.\r\nAfter a while he looked up. \xe2\x80\x9cGo ahead, my boy; you have my good\r\nwishes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it is really a pity you don\xe2\x80\x99t understand me,\r\nthat you don\xe2\x80\x99t know just what I am doing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t do anything wrong. Leave me to\r\nmyself, rather, or defy me, out and out. I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t lay any load on\r\nyour conscience.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBellegarde sprang up again; he was evidently excited; there was a\r\nwarmer spark even than usual in his eye. \xe2\x80\x9cYou never will understand\xe2\x80\x94you\r\nnever will know,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cand if you succeed, and I turn out to have\r\nhelped you, you will never be grateful, not as I shall deserve you\r\nshould be. You will be an excellent fellow always, but you will not be\r\ngrateful. But it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter, for I shall get my own fun out of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAnd he broke into an extravagant laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cYou look puzzled,\xe2\x80\x9d he added;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cyou look almost frightened.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt _is_ a pity,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand you. I shall\r\nlose some very good jokes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told you, you remember, that we were very strange people,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nBellegarde went on. \xe2\x80\x9cI give you warning again. We are! My mother is\r\nstrange, my brother is strange, and I verily believe that I am stranger\r\nthan either. You will even find my sister a little strange. Old trees\r\nhave crooked branches, old houses have queer cracks, old races have odd\r\nsecrets. Remember that we are eight hundred years old!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman; \xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s the sort of thing I came to Europe\r\nfor. You come into my programme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Touchez-l\xc3\xa0_, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bellegarde, putting out his hand. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nbargain: I accept you; I espouse your cause. It\xe2\x80\x99s because I like you,\r\nin a great measure; but that is not the only reason!\xe2\x80\x9d And he stood\r\nholding Newman\xe2\x80\x99s hand and looking at him askance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the other one?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am in the Opposition. I dislike someone else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour brother?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman, in his unmodulated voice.\r\n\r\nBellegarde laid his fingers upon his lips with a whispered _hush!_ \xe2\x80\x9cOld\r\nraces have strange secrets!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cPut yourself into motion, come\r\nand see my sister, and be assured of my sympathy!\xe2\x80\x9d And on this he took\r\nhis leave.\r\n\r\nNewman dropped into a chair before his fire, and sat a long time\r\nstaring into the blaze.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX\r\n\r\n\r\nHe went to see Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 the next day, and was informed by the\r\nservant that she was at home. He passed as usual up the large, cold\r\nstaircase and through a spacious vestibule above, where the walls\r\nseemed all composed of small door panels, touched with long-faded\r\ngilding; whence he was ushered into the sitting-room in which he had\r\nalready been received. It was empty, and the servant told him that\r\nMadame la Comtesse would presently appear. He had time, while he\r\nwaited, to wonder whether Bellegarde had seen his sister since the\r\nevening before, and whether in this case he had spoken to her of their\r\ntalk. In this case Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s receiving him was an\r\nencouragement. He felt a certain trepidation as he reflected that she\r\nmight come in with the knowledge of his supreme admiration and of the\r\nproject he had built upon it in her eyes; but the feeling was not\r\ndisagreeable. Her face could wear no look that would make it less\r\nbeautiful, and he was sure beforehand that however she might take the\r\nproposal he had in reserve, she would not take it in scorn or in irony.\r\nHe had a feeling that if she could only read the bottom of his heart\r\nand measure the extent of his good will toward her, she would be\r\nentirely kind.\r\n\r\nShe came in at last, after so long an interval that he wondered whether\r\nshe had been hesitating. She smiled with her usual frankness, and held\r\nout her hand; she looked at him straight with her soft and luminous\r\neyes, and said, without a tremor in her voice, that she was glad to see\r\nhim and that she hoped he was well. He found in her what he had found\r\nbefore\xe2\x80\x94that faint perfume of a personal shyness worn away by contact\r\nwith the world, but the more perceptible the more closely you\r\napproached her. This lingering diffidence seemed to give a peculiar\r\nvalue to what was definite and assured in her manner; it made it seem\r\nlike an accomplishment, a beautiful talent, something that one might\r\ncompare to an exquisite touch in a pianist. It was, in fact, Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cauthority,\xe2\x80\x9d as they say of artists, that especially impressed\r\nand fascinated Newman; he always came back to the feeling that when he\r\nshould complete himself by taking a wife, that was the way he should\r\nlike his wife to interpret him to the world. The only trouble, indeed,\r\nwas that when the instrument was so perfect it seemed to interpose too\r\nmuch between you and the genius that used it. Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 gave\r\nNewman the sense of an elaborate education, of her having passed\r\nthrough mysterious ceremonies and processes of culture in her youth, of\r\nher having been fashioned and made flexible to certain exalted social\r\nneeds. All this, as I have affirmed, made her seem rare and precious\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nvery expensive article, as he would have said, and one which a man with\r\nan ambition to have everything about him of the best would find it\r\nhighly agreeable to possess. But looking at the matter with an eye to\r\nprivate felicity, Newman wondered where, in so exquisite a compound,\r\nnature and art showed their dividing line. Where did the special\r\nintention separate from the habit of good manners? Where did urbanity\r\nend and sincerity begin? Newman asked himself these questions even\r\nwhile he stood ready to accept the admired object in all its\r\ncomplexity; he felt that he could do so in profound security, and\r\nexamine its mechanism afterwards, at leisure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very glad to find you alone,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know I have never had\r\nsuch good luck before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you have seemed before very well contented with your luck,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have sat and watched my visitors with an air of\r\nquiet amusement. What have you thought of them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I have thought the ladies were very elegant and very graceful, and\r\nwonderfully quick at repartee. But what I have chiefly thought has been\r\nthat they only helped me to admire you.\xe2\x80\x9d This was not gallantry on\r\nNewman\xe2\x80\x99s part\xe2\x80\x94an art in which he was quite unversed. It was simply the\r\ninstinct of the practical man, who had made up his mind what he wanted,\r\nand was now beginning to take active steps to obtain it.\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 started slightly, and raised her eyebrows; she had\r\nevidently not expected so fervid a compliment. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, in that case,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid with a laugh, \xe2\x80\x9cyour finding me alone is not good luck for me. I\r\nhope someone will come in quickly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope not,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI have something particular to say to you.\r\nHave you seen your brother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I saw him an hour ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid he tell you that he had seen me last night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe said so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd did he tell you what we had talked about?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 hesitated a moment. As Newman asked these questions\r\nshe had grown a little pale, as if she regarded what was coming as\r\nnecessary, but not as agreeable. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you give him a message to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was not exactly a message\xe2\x80\x94I asked him to render me a service.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe service was to sing your praises, was it not?\xe2\x80\x9d And she accompanied\r\nthis question with a little smile, as if to make it easier to herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that is what it really amounts to,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cDid he sing my\r\npraises?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe spoke very well of you. But when I know that it was by your special\r\nrequest, of course I must take his eulogy with a grain of salt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that makes no difference,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cYour brother would not\r\nhave spoken well of me unless he believed what he was saying. He is too\r\nhonest for that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you very deep?\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. \xe2\x80\x9cAre you trying to please\r\nme by praising my brother? I confess it is a good way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor me, any way that succeeds will be good. I will praise your brother\r\nall day, if that will help me. He is a noble little fellow. He has made\r\nme feel, in promising to do what he can to help me, that I can depend\r\nupon him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t make too much of that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. \xe2\x80\x9cHe can help you\r\nvery little.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I must work my way myself. I know that very well; I only\r\nwant a chance to. In consenting to see me, after what he told you, you\r\nalmost seem to be giving me a chance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am seeing you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, slowly and gravely, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause\r\nI promised my brother I would.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlessings on your brother\xe2\x80\x99s head!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat I told him last\r\nevening was this: that I admired you more than any woman I had ever\r\nseen, and that I should like immensely to make you my wife.\xe2\x80\x9d He uttered\r\nthese words with great directness and firmness, and without any sense\r\nof confusion. He was full of his idea, he had completely mastered it,\r\nand he seemed to look down on Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, with all her gathered\r\nelegance, from the height of his bracing good conscience. It is\r\nprobable that this particular tone and manner were the very best he\r\ncould have hit upon. Yet the light, just visibly forced smile with\r\nwhich his companion had listened to him died away, and she sat looking\r\nat him with her lips parted and her face as solemn as a tragic mask.\r\nThere was evidently something very painful to her in the scene to which\r\nhe was subjecting her, and yet her impatience of it found no angry\r\nvoice. Newman wondered whether he was hurting her; he could not imagine\r\nwhy the liberal devotion he meant to express should be disagreeable. He\r\ngot up and stood before her, leaning one hand on the chimney-piece. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nknow I have seen you very little to say this,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cso little that\r\nit may make what I say seem disrespectful. That is my misfortune! I\r\ncould have said it the first time I saw you. Really, I had seen you\r\nbefore; I had seen you in imagination; you seemed almost an old friend.\r\nSo what I say is not mere gallantry and compliments and nonsense\xe2\x80\x94I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t talk that way, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how, and I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t, to you, if I\r\ncould. It\xe2\x80\x99s as serious as such words can be. I feel as if I knew you\r\nand knew what a beautiful, admirable woman you are. I shall know\r\nbetter, perhaps, some day, but I have a general notion now. You are\r\njust the woman I have been looking for, except that you are far more\r\nperfect. I won\xe2\x80\x99t make any protestations and vows, but you can trust me.\r\nIt is very soon, I know, to say all this; it is almost offensive. But\r\nwhy not gain time if one can? And if you want time to reflect\xe2\x80\x94of course\r\nyou do\xe2\x80\x94the sooner you begin, the better for me. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you\r\nthink of me; but there is no great mystery about me; you see what I am.\r\nYour brother told me that my antecedents and occupations were against\r\nme; that your family stands, somehow, on a higher level than I do. That\r\nis an idea which of course I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand and don\xe2\x80\x99t accept. But you\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t care anything about that. I can assure you that I am a very solid\r\nfellow, and that if I give my mind to it I can arrange things so that\r\nin a very few years I shall not need to waste time in explaining who I\r\nam and what I am. You will decide for yourself whether you like me or\r\nnot. What there is you see before you. I honestly believe I have no\r\nhidden vices or nasty tricks. I am kind, kind, kind! Everything that a\r\nman can give a woman I will give you. I have a large fortune, a very\r\nlarge fortune; some day, if you will allow me, I will go into details.\r\nIf you want brilliancy, everything in the way of brilliancy that money\r\ncan give you, you shall have. And as regards anything you may give up,\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t take for granted too much that its place cannot be filled. Leave\r\nthat to me; I\xe2\x80\x99ll take care of you; I shall know what you need. Energy\r\nand ingenuity can arrange everything. I\xe2\x80\x99m a strong man! There, I have\r\nsaid what I had on my heart! It was better to get it off. I am very\r\nsorry if it\xe2\x80\x99s disagreeable to you; but think how much better it is that\r\nthings should be clear. Don\xe2\x80\x99t answer me now, if you don\xe2\x80\x99t wish it.\r\nThink about it, think about it as slowly as you please. Of course I\r\nhaven\xe2\x80\x99t said, I can\xe2\x80\x99t say, half I mean, especially about my admiration\r\nfor you. But take a favorable view of me; it will only be just.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDuring this speech, the longest that Newman had ever made, Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9 kept her gaze fixed upon him, and it expanded at the last into a\r\nsort of fascinated stare. When he ceased speaking she lowered her eyes\r\nand sat for some moments looking down and straight before her. Then she\r\nslowly rose to her feet, and a pair of exceptionally keen eyes would\r\nhave perceived that she was trembling a little in the movement. She\r\nstill looked extremely serious. \xe2\x80\x9cI am very much obliged to you for your\r\noffer,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems very strange, but I am glad you spoke\r\nwithout waiting any longer. It is better the subject should be\r\ndismissed. I appreciate all you say; you do me great honor. But I have\r\ndecided not to marry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t say that!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman, in a tone absolutely _na\xc3\xaff_ from\r\nits pleading and caressing cadence. She had turned away, and it made\r\nher stop a moment with her back to him. \xe2\x80\x9cThink better of that. You are\r\ntoo young, too beautiful, too much made to be happy and to make others\r\nhappy. If you are afraid of losing your freedom, I can assure you that\r\nthis freedom here, this life you now lead, is a dreary bondage to what\r\nI will offer you. You shall do things that I don\xe2\x80\x99t think you have ever\r\nthought of. I will take you anywhere in the wide world that you\r\npropose. Are you unhappy? You give me a feeling that you _are_ unhappy.\r\nYou have no right to be, or to be made so. Let me come in and put an\r\nend to it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 stood there a moment longer, looking away from him. If\r\nshe was touched by the way he spoke, the thing was conceivable. His\r\nvoice, always very mild and interrogative, gradually became as soft and\r\nas tenderly argumentative as if he had been talking to a much-loved\r\nchild. He stood watching her, and she presently turned round again, but\r\nthis time she did not look at him, and she spoke in a quietness in\r\nwhich there was a visible trace of effort.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are a great many reasons why I should not marry,\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cmore than I can explain to you. As for my happiness, I am very happy.\r\nYour offer seems strange to me, for more reasons also than I can say.\r\nOf course you have a perfect right to make it. But I cannot accept\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94it is impossible. Please never speak of this matter again. If you\r\ncannot promise me this, I must ask you not to come back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy is it impossible?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman demanded. \xe2\x80\x9cYou may think it is, at\r\nfirst, without its really being so. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t expect you to be pleased\r\nat first, but I do believe that if you will think of it a good while,\r\nyou may be satisfied.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. \xe2\x80\x9cThink how little I know\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery little, of course, and therefore I don\xe2\x80\x99t ask for your ultimatum\r\non the spot. I only ask you not to say no, and to let me hope. I will\r\nwait as long as you desire. Meanwhile you can see more of me and know\r\nme better, look at me as a possible husband\xe2\x80\x94as a candidate\xe2\x80\x94and make up\r\nyour mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSomething was going on, rapidly, in Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s thoughts; she\r\nwas weighing a question there, beneath Newman\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, weighing it and\r\ndeciding it. \xe2\x80\x9cFrom the moment I don\xe2\x80\x99t very respectfully beg you to\r\nleave the house and never return,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI listen to you, I seem\r\nto give you hope. I _have_ listened to you\xe2\x80\x94against my judgment. It is\r\nbecause you are eloquent. If I had been told this morning that I should\r\nconsent to consider you as a possible husband, I should have thought my\r\ninformant a little crazy. I _am_ listening to you, you see!\xe2\x80\x9d And she\r\nthrew her hands out for a moment and let them drop with a gesture in\r\nwhich there was just the slightest expression of appealing weakness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, as far as saying goes, I have said everything,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nbelieve in you, without restriction, and I think all the good of you\r\nthat it is possible to think of a human creature. I firmly believe that\r\nin marrying me you will be _safe_. As I said just now,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on with\r\na smile, \xe2\x80\x9cI have no bad ways. I can _do_ so much for you. And if you\r\nare afraid that I am not what you have been accustomed to, not refined\r\nand delicate and punctilious, you may easily carry that too far. I _am_\r\ndelicate! You shall see!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 walked some distance away, and paused before a great\r\nplant, an azalea, which was flourishing in a porcelain tub before her\r\nwindow. She plucked off one of the flowers and, twisting it in her\r\nfingers, retraced her steps. Then she sat down in silence, and her\r\nattitude seemed to be a consent that Newman should say more.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should you say it is impossible you should marry?\xe2\x80\x9d he continued.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe only thing that could make it really impossible would be your\r\nbeing already married. Is it because you have been unhappy in marriage?\r\nThat is all the more reason! Is it because your family exert a pressure\r\nupon you, interfere with you, annoy you? That is still another reason;\r\nyou ought to be perfectly free, and marriage will make you so. I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsay anything against your family\xe2\x80\x94understand that!\xe2\x80\x9d added Newman, with\r\nan eagerness which might have made a perspicacious observer smile.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhatever way you feel toward them is the right way, and anything that\r\nyou should wish me to do to make myself agreeable to them I will do as\r\nwell as I know how. Depend upon that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 rose again and came toward the fireplace, near which\r\nNewman was standing. The expression of pain and embarrassment had\r\npassed out of her face, and it was illuminated with something which,\r\nthis time at least, Newman need not have been perplexed whether to\r\nattribute to habit or to intention, to art or to nature. She had the\r\nair of a woman who has stepped across the frontier of friendship and,\r\nlooking around her, finds the region vast. A certain checked and\r\ncontrolled exaltation seemed mingled with the usual level radiance of\r\nher glance. \xe2\x80\x9cI will not refuse to see you again,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause\r\nmuch of what you have said has given me pleasure. But I will see you\r\nonly on this condition: that you say nothing more in the same way for a\r\nlong time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor how long?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor six months. It must be a solemn promise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, I promise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye, then,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, and extended her hand.\r\n\r\nHe held it a moment, as if he were going to say something more. But he\r\nonly looked at her; then he took his departure.\r\n\r\nThat evening, on the Boulevard, he met Valentin de Bellegarde. After\r\nthey had exchanged greetings, Newman told him that he had seen Madame\r\nde Cintr\xc3\xa9 a few hours before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cI dined in the Rue de l\xe2\x80\x99Universit\xc3\xa9.\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\nthen, for some moments, both men were silent. Newman wished to ask\r\nBellegarde what visible impression his visit had made and the Count\r\nValentin had a question of his own. Bellegarde spoke first.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s none of my business, but what the deuce did you say to my\r\nsister?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am willing to tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I made her an offer of\r\nmarriage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlready!\xe2\x80\x9d And the young man gave a whistle. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Time is money!\xe2\x80\x99 Is that\r\nwhat you say in America? And Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9?\xe2\x80\x9d he added, with an\r\ninterrogative inflection.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe did not accept my offer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe couldn\xe2\x80\x99t, you know, in that way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I\xe2\x80\x99m to see her again,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, the strangeness of woman!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Bellegarde. Then he stopped,\r\nand held Newman off at arms\xe2\x80\x99-length. \xe2\x80\x9cI look at you with respect!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nexclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have achieved what we call a personal success!\r\nImmediately, now, I must present you to my brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhenever you please!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X\r\n\r\n\r\nNewman continued to see his friends the Tristrams with a good deal of\r\nfrequency, though if you had listened to Mrs. Tristram\xe2\x80\x99s account of the\r\nmatter you would have supposed that they had been cynically repudiated\r\nfor the sake of grander acquaintance. \xe2\x80\x9cWe were all very well so long as\r\nwe had no rivals\xe2\x80\x94we were better than nothing. But now that you have\r\nbecome the fashion, and have your pick every day of three invitations\r\nto dinner, we are tossed into the corner. I am sure it is very good of\r\nyou to come and see us once a month; I wonder you don\xe2\x80\x99t send us your\r\ncards in an envelope. When you do, pray have them with black edges; it\r\nwill be for the death of my last illusion.\xe2\x80\x9d It was in this incisive\r\nstrain that Mrs. Tristram moralized over Newman\xe2\x80\x99s so-called neglect,\r\nwhich was in reality a most exemplary constancy. Of course she was\r\njoking, but there was always something ironical in her jokes, as there\r\nwas always something jocular in her gravity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know no better proof that I have treated you very well,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman had\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cthan the fact that you make so free with my character.\r\nFamiliarity breeds contempt; I have made myself too cheap. If I had a\r\nlittle proper pride I would stay away a while, and when you asked me to\r\ndinner say I was going to the Princess Borealska\xe2\x80\x99s. But I have not any\r\npride where my pleasure is concerned, and to keep you in the humor to\r\nsee me\xe2\x80\x94if you must see me only to call me bad names\xe2\x80\x94I will agree to\r\nanything you choose; I will admit that I am the biggest snob in Paris.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nNewman, in fact, had declined an invitation personally given by the\r\nPrincess Borealska, an inquiring Polish lady to whom he had been\r\npresented, on the ground that on that particular day he always dined at\r\nMrs. Tristram\xe2\x80\x99s; and it was only a tenderly perverse theory of his\r\nhostess of the Avenue d\xe2\x80\x99I\xc3\xa9na that he was faithless to his early\r\nfriendships. She needed the theory to explain a certain moral\r\nirritation by which she was often visited; though, if this explanation\r\nwas unsound, a deeper analyst than I must give the right one. Having\r\nlaunched our hero upon the current which was bearing him so rapidly\r\nalong, she appeared but half-pleased at its swiftness. She had\r\nsucceeded too well; she had played her game too cleverly and she wished\r\nto mix up the cards. Newman had told her, in due season, that her\r\nfriend was \xe2\x80\x9csatisfactory.\xe2\x80\x9d The epithet was not romantic, but Mrs.\r\nTristram had no difficulty in perceiving that, in essentials, the\r\nfeeling which lay beneath it was. Indeed, the mild, expansive brevity\r\nwith which it was uttered, and a certain look, at once appealing and\r\ninscrutable, that issued from Newman\xe2\x80\x99s half-closed eyes as he leaned\r\nhis head against the back of his chair, seemed to her the most eloquent\r\nattestation of a mature sentiment that she had ever encountered. Newman\r\nwas, according to the French phrase, only abounding in her own sense,\r\nbut his temperate raptures exerted a singular effect upon the ardor\r\nwhich she herself had so freely manifested a few months before. She now\r\nseemed inclined to take a purely critical view of Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, and\r\nwished to have it understood that she did not in the least answer for\r\nher being a compendium of all the virtues. \xe2\x80\x9cNo woman was ever so good\r\nas that woman seems,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cRemember what Shakespeare calls\r\nDesdemona; \xe2\x80\x98a supersubtle Venetian.\xe2\x80\x99 Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 is a supersubtle\r\nParisian. She is a charming woman, and she has five hundred merits; but\r\nyou had better keep that in mind.\xe2\x80\x9d Was Mrs. Tristram simply finding out\r\nthat she was jealous of her dear friend on the other side of the Seine,\r\nand that in undertaking to provide Newman with an ideal wife she had\r\ncounted too much on her own disinterestedness? We may be permitted to\r\ndoubt it. The inconsistent little lady of the Avenue d\xe2\x80\x99I\xc3\xa9na had an\r\ninsuperable need of changing her place, intellectually. She had a\r\nlively imagination, and she was capable, at certain times, of imagining\r\nthe direct reverse of her most cherished beliefs, with a vividness more\r\nintense than that of conviction. She got tired of thinking aright; but\r\nthere was no serious harm in it, as she got equally tired of thinking\r\nwrong. In the midst of her mysterious perversities she had admirable\r\nflashes of justice. One of these occurred when Newman related to her\r\nthat he had made a formal proposal to Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. He repeated in\r\na few words what he had said, and in a great many what she had\r\nanswered. Mrs. Tristram listened with extreme interest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut after all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cthere is nothing to congratulate me\r\nupon. It is not a triumph.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Tristram; \xe2\x80\x9cit is a great triumph. It is\r\na great triumph that she did not silence you at the first word, and\r\nrequest you never to speak to her again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see that,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course you don\xe2\x80\x99t; Heaven forbid you should! When I told you to go\r\non your own way and do what came into your head, I had no idea you\r\nwould go over the ground so fast. I never dreamed you would offer\r\nyourself after five or six morning-calls. As yet, what had you done to\r\nmake her like you? You had simply sat\xe2\x80\x94not very straight\xe2\x80\x94and stared at\r\nher. But she does like you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat remains to be seen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, that is proved. What will come of it remains to be seen. That you\r\nshould propose to marry her, without more ado, could never have come\r\ninto her head. You can form very little idea of what passed through her\r\nmind as you spoke; if she ever really marries you, the affair will be\r\ncharacterized by the usual justice of all human beings towards women.\r\nYou will think you take generous views of her; but you will never begin\r\nto know through what a strange sea of feeling she passed before she\r\naccepted you. As she stood there in front of you the other day, she\r\nplunged into it. She said \xe2\x80\x98Why not?\xe2\x80\x99 to something which, a few hours\r\nearlier, had been inconceivable. She turned about on a thousand\r\ngathered prejudices and traditions as on a pivot, and looked where she\r\nhad never looked hitherto. When I think of it\xe2\x80\x94when I think of Claire de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9 and all that she represents, there seems to me something very\r\nfine in it. When I recommended you to try your fortune with her I of\r\ncourse thought well of you, and in spite of your sins I think so still.\r\nBut I confess I don\xe2\x80\x99t see quite what you are and what you have done, to\r\nmake such a woman do this sort of thing for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, there is something very fine in it!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman with a laugh,\r\nrepeating her words. He took an extreme satisfaction in hearing that\r\nthere was something fine in it. He had not the least doubt of it\r\nhimself, but he had already begun to value the world\xe2\x80\x99s admiration of\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, as adding to the prospective glory of possession.\r\n\r\nIt was immediately after this conversation that Valentin de Bellegarde\r\ncame to conduct his friend to the Rue de l\xe2\x80\x99Universit\xc3\xa9 to present him to\r\nthe other members of his family. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are already introduced,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand you have begun to be talked about. My sister has mentioned your\r\nsuccessive visits to my mother, and it was an accident that my mother\r\nwas present at none of them. I have spoken of you as an American of\r\nimmense wealth, and the best fellow in the world, who is looking for\r\nsomething very superior in the way of a wife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cthat Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 has related to\r\nyour mother the last conversation I had with her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very certain that she has not; she will keep her own counsel.\r\nMeanwhile you must make your way with the rest of the family. Thus much\r\nis known about you: you have made a great fortune in trade, you are a\r\nlittle eccentric, and you frankly admire our dear Claire. My\r\nsister-in-law, whom you remember seeing in Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsitting-room, took, it appears, a fancy to you; she has described you\r\nas having _beaucoup de cachet_. My mother, therefore, is curious to see\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe expects to laugh at me, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe never laughs. If she does not like you, don\xe2\x80\x99t hope to purchase\r\nfavor by being amusing. Take warning by me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis conversation took place in the evening, and half an hour later\r\nValentin ushered his companion into an apartment of the house of the\r\nRue de l\xe2\x80\x99Universit\xc3\xa9 into which he had not yet penetrated, the salon of\r\nthe dowager Marquise de Bellegarde. It was a vast, high room, with\r\nelaborate and ponderous mouldings, painted a whitish gray, along the\r\nupper portion of the walls and the ceiling; with a great deal of faded\r\nand carefully repaired tapestry in the doorways and chair-backs; a\r\nTurkey carpet in light colors, still soft and deep, in spite of great\r\nantiquity, on the floor, and portraits of each of Madame de\r\nBellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s children, at the age of ten, suspended against an old\r\nscreen of red silk. The room was illumined, exactly enough for\r\nconversation, by half a dozen candles, placed in odd corners, at a\r\ngreat distance apart. In a deep armchair, near the fire, sat an old\r\nlady in black; at the other end of the room another person was seated\r\nat the piano, playing a very expressive waltz. In this latter person\r\nNewman recognized the young Marquise de Bellegarde.\r\n\r\nValentin presented his friend, and Newman walked up to the old lady by\r\nthe fire and shook hands with her. He received a rapid impression of a\r\nwhite, delicate, aged face, with a high forehead, a small mouth, and a\r\npair of cold blue eyes which had kept much of the freshness of youth.\r\nMadame de Bellegarde looked hard at him, and returned his hand-shake\r\nwith a sort of British positiveness which reminded him that she was the\r\ndaughter of the Earl of St. Dunstan\xe2\x80\x99s. Her daughter-in-law stopped\r\nplaying and gave him an agreeable smile. Newman sat down and looked\r\nabout him, while Valentin went and kissed the hand of the young\r\nmarquise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ought to have seen you before,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have\r\npaid several visits to my daughter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, smiling; \xe2\x80\x9cMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 and I are old\r\nfriends by this time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have gone fast,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot so fast as I should like,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, bravely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you are very ambitious,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the old lady.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I confess I am,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, smiling.\r\n\r\nMadame de Bellegarde looked at him with her cold fine eyes, and he\r\nreturned her gaze, reflecting that she was a possible adversary and\r\ntrying to take her measure. Their eyes remained in contact for some\r\nmoments. Then Madame de Bellegarde looked away, and without smiling, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nam very ambitious, too,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nNewman felt that taking her measure was not easy; she was a formidable,\r\ninscrutable little woman. She resembled her daughter, and yet she was\r\nutterly unlike her. The coloring in Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 was the same, and\r\nthe high delicacy of her brow and nose was hereditary. But her face was\r\na larger and freer copy, and her mouth in especial a happy divergence\r\nfrom that conservative orifice, a little pair of lips at once plump and\r\npinched, that looked, when closed, as if they could not open wider than\r\nto swallow a gooseberry or to emit an \xe2\x80\x9cOh, dear, no!\xe2\x80\x9d which probably\r\nhad been thought to give the finishing touch to the aristocratic\r\nprettiness of the Lady Emmeline Atheling as represented, forty years\r\nbefore, in several Books of Beauty. Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s face had, to\r\nNewman\xe2\x80\x99s eye, a range of expression as delightfully vast as the\r\nwind-streaked, cloud-flecked distance on a Western prairie. But her\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x99s white, intense, respectable countenance, with its formal gaze,\r\nand its circumscribed smile, suggested a document signed and sealed; a\r\nthing of parchment, ink, and ruled lines. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is a woman of\r\nconventions and proprieties,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself as he looked at her;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cher world is the world of things immutably decreed. But how she is at\r\nhome in it, and what a paradise she finds it. She walks about in it as\r\nif it were a blooming park, a Garden of Eden; and when she sees \xe2\x80\x98This\r\nis genteel,\xe2\x80\x99 or \xe2\x80\x98This is improper,\xe2\x80\x99 written on a mile-stone she stops\r\necstatically, as if she were listening to a nightingale or smelling a\r\nrose.\xe2\x80\x9d Madame de Bellegarde wore a little black velvet hood tied under\r\nher chin, and she was wrapped in an old black cashmere shawl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are an American?\xe2\x80\x9d she said presently. \xe2\x80\x9cI have seen several\r\nAmericans.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are several in Paris,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman jocosely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, really?\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was in England I saw\r\nthese, or somewhere else; not in Paris. I think it must have been in\r\nthe Pyrenees, many years ago. I am told your ladies are very pretty.\r\nOne of these ladies was very pretty! such a wonderful complexion! She\r\npresented me a note of introduction from someone\xe2\x80\x94I forgot whom\xe2\x80\x94and she\r\nsent with it a note of her own. I kept her letter a long time\r\nafterwards, it was so strangely expressed. I used to know some of the\r\nphrases by heart. But I have forgotten them now, it is so many years\r\nago. Since then I have seen no more Americans. I think my\r\ndaughter-in-law has; she is a great gad-about, she sees everyone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this the younger lady came rustling forward, pinching in a very\r\nslender waist, and casting idly preoccupied glances over the front of\r\nher dress, which was apparently designed for a ball. She was, in a\r\nsingular way, at once ugly and pretty; she had protuberant eyes, and\r\nlips strangely red. She reminded Newman of his friend, Mademoiselle\r\nNioche; this was what that much-obstructed young lady would have liked\r\nto be. Valentin de Bellegarde walked behind her at a distance, hopping\r\nabout to keep off the far-spreading train of her dress.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ought to show more of your shoulders behind,\xe2\x80\x9d he said very\r\ngravely. \xe2\x80\x9cYou might as well wear a standing ruff as such a dress as\r\nthat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young woman turned her back to the mirror over the chimney-piece,\r\nand glanced behind her, to verify Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s assertion. The mirror\r\ndescended low, and yet it reflected nothing but a large unclad flesh\r\nsurface. The young marquise put her hands behind her and gave a\r\ndownward pull to the waist of her dress. \xe2\x80\x9cLike that, you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nasked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a little better,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bellegarde in the same tone, \xe2\x80\x9cbut it\r\nleaves a good deal to be desired.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I never go to extremes,\xe2\x80\x9d said his sister-in-law. And then, turning\r\nto Madame de Bellegarde, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat were you calling me just now, madame?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI called you a gad-about,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old lady. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I might call you\r\nsomething else, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA gad-about? What an ugly word! What does it mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA very beautiful person,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman ventured to say, seeing that it was\r\nin French.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a pretty compliment but a bad translation,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young\r\nmarquise. And then, looking at him a moment, \xe2\x80\x9cDo you dance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a step.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very wrong,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, simply. And with another look at her\r\nback in the mirror she turned away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you like Paris?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the old lady, who was apparently wondering\r\nwhat was the proper way to talk to an American.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, rather,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. And then he added with a friendly\r\nintonation, \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t say I know it. I know my house\xe2\x80\x94I know my friends\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nParis.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you lose a great deal,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, sympathetically.\r\n\r\nMadame de Bellegarde stared; it was presumably the first time she had\r\nbeen condoled with on her losses.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am content with what I have,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with dignity.\r\n\r\nNewman\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, at this moment, were wandering round the room, which\r\nstruck him as rather sad and shabby; passing from the high casements,\r\nwith their small, thickly-framed panes, to the sallow tints of two or\r\nthree portraits in pastel, of the last century, which hung between\r\nthem.
