He ought, obviously, to have answered that the contentment of his\r\nhostess was quite natural\xe2\x80\x94she had a great deal; but the idea did not\r\noccur to him during the pause of some moments which followed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my dear mother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin, coming and leaning against the\r\nchimney-piece, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat do you think of my dear friend Newman? Is he not\r\nthe excellent fellow I told you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy acquaintance with Mr. Newman has not gone very far,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de\r\nBellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cI can as yet only appreciate his great politeness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy mother is a great judge of these matters,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin to Newman.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you have satisfied her, it is a triumph.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope I shall satisfy you, some day,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, looking at the old\r\nlady. \xe2\x80\x9cI have done nothing yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must not listen to my son; he will bring you into trouble. He is a\r\nsad scatterbrain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I like him\xe2\x80\x94I like him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, genially.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe amuses you, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, perfectly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you hear that, Valentin?\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cYou amuse Mr.\r\nNewman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps we shall all come to that!\xe2\x80\x9d Valentin exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must see my other son,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is much\r\nbetter than this one. But he will not amuse you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Valentin, reflectively. \xe2\x80\x9cBut we\r\nshall very soon see. Here comes _Monsieur mon fr\xc3\xa8re_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe door had just opened to give ingress to a gentleman who stepped\r\nforward and whose face Newman remembered. He had been the author of our\r\nhero\xe2\x80\x99s discomfiture the first time he tried to present himself to\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. Valentin de Bellegarde went to meet his brother,\r\nlooked at him a moment, and then, taking him by the arm, led him up to\r\nNewman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is my excellent friend Mr. Newman,\xe2\x80\x9d he said very blandly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nmust know him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am delighted to know Mr. Newman,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquis with a low bow,\r\nbut without offering his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is the old woman at second-hand,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman said to himself, as he\r\nreturned M. de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s greeting. And this was the starting-point\r\nof a speculative theory, in his mind, that the late marquis had been a\r\nvery amiable foreigner, with an inclination to take life easily and a\r\nsense that it was difficult for the husband of the stilted little lady\r\nby the fire to do so. But if he had taken little comfort in his wife he\r\nhad taken much in his two younger children, who were after his own\r\nheart, while Madame de Bellegarde had paired with her eldest-born.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy brother has spoken to me of you,\xe2\x80\x9d said M. de Bellegarde; \xe2\x80\x9cand as\r\nyou are also acquainted with my sister, it was time we should meet.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nturned to his mother and gallantly bent over her hand, touching it with\r\nhis lips, and then he assumed an attitude before the chimney-piece.\r\nWith his long, lean face, his high-bridged nose and his small, opaque\r\neye he looked much like an Englishman. His whiskers were fair and\r\nglossy, and he had a large dimple, of unmistakably British origin, in\r\nthe middle of his handsome chin. He was \xe2\x80\x9cdistinguished\xe2\x80\x9d to the tips of\r\nhis polished nails, and there was not a movement of his fine,\r\nperpendicular person that was not noble and majestic. Newman had never\r\nyet been confronted with such an incarnation of the art of taking one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nself seriously; he felt a sort of impulse to step backward, as you do\r\nto get a view of a great fa\xc3\xa7ade.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUrbain,\xe2\x80\x9d said young Madame de Bellegarde, who had apparently been\r\nwaiting for her husband to take her to her ball, \xe2\x80\x9cI call your attention\r\nto the fact that I am dressed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a good idea,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Valentin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am at your orders, my dear friend,\xe2\x80\x9d said M. de Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly,\r\nyou must allow me first the pleasure of a little conversation with Mr.\r\nNewman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, if you are going to a party, don\xe2\x80\x99t let me keep you,\xe2\x80\x9d objected\r\nNewman. \xe2\x80\x9cI am very sure we shall meet again. Indeed, if you would like\r\nto converse with me I will gladly name an hour.\xe2\x80\x9d He was eager to make\r\nit known that he would readily answer all questions and satisfy all\r\nexactions.\r\n\r\nM. de Bellegarde stood in a well-balanced position before the fire,\r\ncaressing one of his fair whiskers with one of his white hands, and\r\nlooking at Newman, half askance, with eyes from which a particular ray\r\nof observation made its way through a general meaningless smile. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is\r\nvery kind of you to make such an offer,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I am not\r\nmistaken, your occupations are such as to make your time precious. You\r\nare in\xe2\x80\x94a\xe2\x80\x94as we say, _dans les affaires_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn business, you mean? Oh no, I have thrown business overboard for the\r\npresent. I am \xe2\x80\x98loafing,\xe2\x80\x99 as _we_ say. My time is quite my own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you are taking a holiday,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined M. de Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Loafing.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nYes, I have heard that expression.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Newman is American,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy brother is a great ethnologist,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn ethnologist?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, you collect negroes\xe2\x80\x99 skulls, and\r\nthat sort of thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe marquis looked hard at his brother, and began to caress his other\r\nwhisker. Then, turning to Newman, with sustained urbanity, \xe2\x80\x9cYou are\r\ntraveling for your pleasure?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I am knocking about to pick up one thing and another. Of course I\r\nget a good deal of pleasure out of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat especially interests you?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired the marquis.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, everything interests me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI am not particular.\r\nManufactures are what I care most about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat has been your specialty?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t say I have any specialty. My specialty has been to make the\r\nlargest possible fortune in the shortest possible time.\xe2\x80\x9d Newman made\r\nthis last remark very deliberately; he wished to open the way, if it\r\nwere necessary, to an authoritative statement of his means.\r\n\r\nM. de Bellegarde laughed agreeably. \xe2\x80\x9cI hope you have succeeded,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I have made a fortune in a reasonable time. I am not so old, you\r\nsee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cParis is a very good place to spend a fortune. I wish you great\r\nenjoyment of yours.\xe2\x80\x9d And M. de Bellegarde drew forth his gloves and\r\nbegan to put them on.\r\n\r\nNewman for a few moments watched him sliding his white hands into the\r\nwhite kid, and as he did so his feelings took a singular turn. M. de\r\nBellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s good wishes seemed to descend out of the white expanse of\r\nhis sublime serenity with the soft, scattered movement of a shower of\r\nsnow-flakes. Yet Newman was not irritated; he did not feel that he was\r\nbeing patronized; he was conscious of no especial impulse to introduce\r\na discord into so noble a harmony. Only he felt himself suddenly in\r\npersonal contact with the forces with which his friend Valentin had\r\ntold him that he would have to contend, and he became sensible of their\r\nintensity. He wished to make some answering manifestation, to stretch\r\nhimself out at his own length, to sound a note at the uttermost end of\r\n_his_ scale. It must be added that if this impulse was not vicious or\r\nmalicious, it was by no means void of humorous expectancy. Newman was\r\nquite as ready to give play to that loosely-adjusted smile of his, if\r\nhis hosts should happen to be shocked, as he was far from deliberately\r\nplanning to shock them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cParis is a very good place for idle people,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cor it is a very\r\ngood place if your family has been settled here for a long time, and\r\nyou have made acquaintances and got your relations round you; or if you\r\nhave got a good big house like this, and a wife and children and mother\r\nand sister, and everything comfortable. I don\xe2\x80\x99t like that way of living\r\nall in rooms next door to each other. But I am not an idler. I try to\r\nbe, but I can\xe2\x80\x99t manage it; it goes against the grain. My business\r\nhabits are too deep-seated. Then, I haven\xe2\x80\x99t any house to call my own,\r\nor anything in the way of a family. My sisters are five thousand miles\r\naway, my mother died when I was a youngster, and I haven\xe2\x80\x99t any wife; I\r\nwish I had! So, you see, I don\xe2\x80\x99t exactly know what to do with myself. I\r\nam not fond of books, as you are, sir, and I get tired of dining out\r\nand going to the opera. I miss my business activity. You see, I began\r\nto earn my living when I was almost a baby, and until a few months ago\r\nI have never had my hand off the plow. Elegant leisure comes hard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis speech was followed by a profound silence of some moments, on the\r\npart of Newman\xe2\x80\x99s entertainers. Valentin stood looking at him fixedly,\r\nwith his hands in his pockets, and then he slowly, with a half-sidling\r\nmotion, went out of the door. The marquis continued to draw on his\r\ngloves and to smile benignantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou began to earn your living when you were a mere baby?\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nmarquise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHardly more\xe2\x80\x94a small boy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou say you are not fond of books,\xe2\x80\x9d said M. de Bellegarde; \xe2\x80\x9cbut you\r\nmust do yourself the justice to remember that your studies were\r\ninterrupted early.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is very true; on my tenth birthday I stopped going to school. I\r\nthought it was a grand way to keep it. But I picked up some information\r\nafterwards,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, reassuringly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have some sisters?\xe2\x80\x9d asked old Madame de Bellegarde.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, two sisters. Splendid women!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope that for them the hardships of life commenced less early.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey married very early, if you call that a hardship, as girls do in\r\nour Western country. One of them is married to the owner of the largest\r\nindia-rubber house in the West.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you make houses also of india-rubber?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired the marquise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can stretch them as your family increases,\xe2\x80\x9d said young Madame de\r\nBellegarde, who was muffling herself in a long white shawl.\r\n\r\nNewman indulged in a burst of hilarity, and explained that the house in\r\nwhich his brother-in-law lived was a large wooden structure, but that\r\nhe manufactured and sold india-rubber on a colossal scale.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy children have some little india-rubber shoes which they put on when\r\nthey go to play in the Tuileries in damp weather,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young\r\nmarquise. \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder whether your brother-in-law made them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery likely,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman; \xe2\x80\x9cif he did, you may be very sure they are\r\nwell made.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you must not be discouraged,\xe2\x80\x9d said M. de Bellegarde, with vague\r\nurbanity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean to be. I have a project which gives me plenty to\r\nthink about, and that is an occupation.\xe2\x80\x9d And then Newman was silent a\r\nmoment, hesitating, yet thinking rapidly; he wished to make his point,\r\nand yet to do so forced him to speak out in a way that was disagreeable\r\nto him. Nevertheless he continued, addressing himself to old Madame de\r\nBellegarde, \xe2\x80\x9cI will tell you my project; perhaps you can help me. I\r\nwant to take a wife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a very good project, but I am no matchmaker,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old lady.\r\n\r\nNewman looked at her an instant, and then, with perfect sincerity, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nshould have thought you were,\xe2\x80\x9d he declared.\r\n\r\nMadame de Bellegarde appeared to think him too sincere. She murmured\r\nsomething sharply in French, and fixed her eyes on her son. At this\r\nmoment the door of the room was thrown open, and with a rapid step\r\nValentin reappeared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have a message for you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to his sister-in-law. \xe2\x80\x9cClaire bids\r\nme to request you not to start for your ball. She will go with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClaire will go with us!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the young marquise. \xe2\x80\x9c_En voil\xc3\xa0, du\r\nnouveau!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has changed her mind; she decided half an hour ago, and she is\r\nsticking the last diamond into her hair,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat has taken possession of my daughter?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Madame de\r\nBellegarde, sternly. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has not been into the world these three\r\nyears. She was dressed in\r\nwhite; but a long blue cloak, which hung almost to her feet, was\r\nfastened across her shoulders by a silver clasp. She had tossed it\r\nback, however, and her long white arms were uncovered. In her dense,\r\nfair hair there glittered a dozen diamonds. She looked serious and,\r\nNewman thought, rather pale; but she glanced round her, and, when she\r\nsaw him, smiled and put out her hand. He thought her tremendously\r\nhandsome. He had a chance to look at her full in the face, for she\r\nstood a moment in the centre of the room, hesitating, apparently, what\r\nshe should do, without meeting his eyes. Then she went up to her\r\nmother, who sat in her deep chair by the fire, looking at Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9 almost fiercely. With her back turned to the others, Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9 held her cloak apart to show her dress.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think of me?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you are audacious,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquise. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was but three days\r\nago, when I asked you, as a particular favor to myself, to go to the\r\nDuchess de Lusignan\xe2\x80\x99s, that you told me you were going nowhere and that\r\none must be consistent. Is this your consistency? Why should you\r\ndistinguish Madame Robineau? Who is it you wish to please to-night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish to please myself, dear mother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. And she\r\nbent over and kissed the old lady.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like surprises, my sister,\xe2\x80\x9d said Urbain de Bellegarde;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cespecially when one is on the point of entering a drawing-room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman at this juncture felt inspired to speak. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, if you are going\r\ninto a room with Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, you needn\xe2\x80\x99t be afraid of being\r\nnoticed yourself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nM. de Bellegarde turned to his sister with a smile too intense to be\r\neasy. \xe2\x80\x9cI hope you appreciate a compliment that is paid you at your\r\nbrother\xe2\x80\x99s expense,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cCome, come, madame.\xe2\x80\x9d And offering Madame\r\nde Cintr\xc3\xa9 his arm he led her rapidly out of the room. Valentin rendered\r\nthe same service to young Madame de Bellegarde, who had apparently been\r\nreflecting on the fact that the ball-dress of her sister-in-law was\r\nmuch less brilliant than her own, and yet had failed to derive absolute\r\ncomfort from the reflection. With a farewell smile she sought the\r\ncomplement of her consolation in the eyes of the American visitor, and\r\nperceiving in them a certain mysterious brilliancy, it is not\r\nimprobable that she may have flattered herself she had found it.\r\n\r\nNewman, left alone with old Madame de Bellegarde, stood before her a\r\nfew moments in silence. \xe2\x80\x9cYour daughter is very beautiful,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at\r\nlast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is very strange,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad to hear it,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman rejoined, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cIt makes me hope.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHope what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat she will consent, some day, to marry me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old lady slowly rose to her feet. \xe2\x80\x9cThat really is your project,\r\nthen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; will you favor it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFavor it?\xe2\x80\x9d Madame de Bellegarde looked at him a moment and then shook\r\nher head. \xe2\x80\x9cNo!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, softly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you suffer it, then? Will you let it pass?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you ask. I am a very proud and meddlesome old\r\nwoman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I am very rich,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\nMadame de Bellegarde fixed her eyes on the floor, and Newman thought it\r\nprobable she was weighing the reasons in favor of resenting the\r\nbrutality of this remark. But at last, looking up, she said simply,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow rich?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman expressed his income in a round number which had the magnificent\r\nsound that large aggregations of dollars put on when they are\r\ntranslated into francs. He added a few remarks of a financial\r\ncharacter, which completed a sufficiently striking presentment of his\r\nresources.\r\n\r\nMadame de Bellegarde listened in silence. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are very frank,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid finally. \xe2\x80\x9cI will be the same. I would rather favor you, on the\r\nwhole, than suffer you. It will be easier.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am thankful for any terms,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, for the present, you\r\nhave suffered me long enough. Good night!\xe2\x80\x9d And he took his leave.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI\r\n\r\n\r\nNewman, on his return to Paris, had not resumed the study of French\r\nconversation with M. Nioche; he found that he had too many other uses\r\nfor his time. M. Nioche, however, came to see him very promptly, having\r\nlearned his whereabouts by a mysterious process to which his patron\r\nnever obtained the key. The shrunken little capitalist repeated his\r\nvisit more than once. He seemed oppressed by a humiliating sense of\r\nhaving been overpaid, and wished apparently to redeem his debt by the\r\noffer of grammatical and statistical information in small installments.\r\nHe wore the same decently melancholy aspect as a few months before; a\r\nfew months more or less of brushing could make little difference in the\r\nantique lustre of his coat and hat. But the poor old man\xe2\x80\x99s spirit was a\r\ntrifle more threadbare; it seemed to have received some hard rubs\r\nduring the summer. Newman inquired with interest about Mademoiselle\r\nNo\xc3\xa9mie; and M. Nioche, at first, for answer, simply looked at him in\r\nlachrymose silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t ask me, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last. \xe2\x80\x9cI sit and watch her, but I can\r\ndo nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean that she misconducts herself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know, I am sure. I can\xe2\x80\x99t follow her. I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand her.\r\nShe has something in her head; I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what she is trying to do.\r\nShe is too deep for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes she continue to go to the Louvre? Has she made any of those\r\ncopies for me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe goes to the Louvre, but I see nothing of the copies. She has\r\nsomething on her easel; I suppose it is one of the pictures you\r\nordered. Such a magnificent order ought to give her fairy-fingers. But\r\nshe is not in earnest. I can\xe2\x80\x99t say anything to her; I am afraid of her.\r\nOne evening, last summer, when I took her to walk in the Champs\r\n\xc3\x89lys\xc3\xa9es, she said some things to me that frightened me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat were they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse an unhappy father from telling you,\xe2\x80\x9d said M. Nioche, unfolding\r\nhis calico pocket-handkerchief.\r\n\r\nNewman promised himself to pay Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie another visit at the\r\nLouvre. He was curious about the progress of his copies, but it must be\r\nadded that he was still more curious about the progress of the young\r\nlady herself. He went one afternoon to the great museum, and wandered\r\nthrough several of the rooms in fruitless quest of her. He was bending\r\nhis steps to the long hall of the Italian masters, when suddenly he\r\nfound himself face to face with Valentin de Bellegarde. The young\r\nFrenchman greeted him with ardor, and assured him that he was a\r\ngodsend. He himself was in the worst of humors and he wanted someone to\r\ncontradict.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn a bad humor among all these beautiful things?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nthought you were so fond of pictures, especially the old black ones.\r\nThere are two or three here that ought to keep you in spirits.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Valentin, \xe2\x80\x9cI am not in a mood for pictures, and\r\nthe more beautiful they are the less I like them. Their great staring\r\neyes and fixed positions irritate me. I feel as if I were at some big,\r\ndull party, in a room full of people I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t wish to speak to. What\r\nshould I care for their beauty? It\xe2\x80\x99s a bore, and, worse still, it\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nreproach. I have a great many _ennuis_; I feel vicious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf the Louvre has so little comfort for you, why in the world did you\r\ncome here?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is one of my _ennuis_. I came to meet my cousin\xe2\x80\x94a dreadful\r\nEnglish cousin, a member of my mother\xe2\x80\x99s family\xe2\x80\x94who is in Paris for a\r\nweek for her husband, and who wishes me to point out the \xe2\x80\x98principal\r\nbeauties.\xe2\x80\x99 Imagine a woman who wears a green crape bonnet in December\r\nand has straps sticking out of the ankles of her interminable boots! My\r\nmother begged I would do something to oblige them. I have undertaken to\r\nplay _valet de place_ this afternoon. They were to have met me here at\r\ntwo o\xe2\x80\x99clock, and I have been waiting for them twenty minutes. Why\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t she arrive? She has at least a pair of feet to carry her. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know whether to be furious at their playing me false, or\r\ndelighted to have escaped them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think in your place I would be furious,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause they\r\nmay arrive yet, and then your fury will still be of use to you. Whereas\r\nif you were delighted and they were afterwards to turn up, you might\r\nnot know what to do with your delight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou give me excellent advice, and I already feel better. I will be\r\nfurious; I will let them go to the deuce and I myself will go with\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94unless by chance you too have a rendezvous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not exactly a rendezvous,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I have in fact come\r\nto see a person, not a picture.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA woman, presumably?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA young lady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin, \xe2\x80\x9cI hope for you with all my heart that she is\r\nnot clothed in green tulle and that her feet are not too much out of\r\nfocus.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know much about her feet, but she has very pretty hands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nValentin gave a sigh. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd on that assurance I must part with you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not certain of finding my young lady,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cand I am not\r\nquite prepared to lose your company on the chance. It does not strike\r\nme as particularly desirable to introduce you to her, and yet I should\r\nrather like to have your opinion of her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs she pretty?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI guess you will think so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBellegarde passed his arm into that of his companion. \xe2\x80\x9cConduct me to\r\nher on the instant! I should be ashamed to make a pretty woman wait for\r\nmy verdict.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman suffered himself to be gently propelled in the direction in\r\nwhich he had been walking, but his step was not rapid. He was turning\r\nsomething over in his mind. The two men passed into the long gallery of\r\nthe Italian masters, and Newman, after having scanned for a moment its\r\nbrilliant vista, turned aside into the smaller apartment devoted to the\r\nsame school, on the left. It contained very few persons, but at the\r\nfarther end of it sat Mademoiselle Nioche, before her easel. She was\r\nnot at work; her palette and brushes had been laid down beside her, her\r\nhands were folded in her lap, and she was leaning back in her chair and\r\nlooking intently at two ladies on the other side of the hall, who, with\r\ntheir backs turned to her, had stopped before one of the pictures.\r\nThese ladies were apparently persons of high fashion; they were dressed\r\nwith great splendor, and their long silken trains and furbelows were\r\nspread over the polished floor. It was at their dresses Mademoiselle\r\nNo\xc3\xa9mie was looking, though what she was thinking of I am unable to say.\r\nI hazard the supposition that she was saying to herself that to be able\r\nto drag such a train over a polished floor was a felicity worth any\r\nprice. Her reflections, at any rate, were disturbed by the advent of\r\nNewman and his companion. She glanced at them quickly, and then,\r\ncoloring a little, rose and stood before her easel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI came here on purpose to see you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman in his bad French,\r\noffering to shake hands. And then, like a good American, he introduced\r\nValentin formally: \xe2\x80\x9cAllow me to make you acquainted with the Comte\r\nValentin de Bellegarde.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nValentin made a bow which must have seemed to Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie quite\r\nin harmony with the impressiveness of his title, but the graceful\r\nbrevity of her own response made no concession to underbred surprise.\r\nShe turned to Newman, putting up her hands to her hair and smoothing\r\nits delicately-felt roughness. Then, rapidly, she turned the canvas\r\nthat was on her easel over upon its face. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have not forgotten me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall never forget you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cYou may be sure of that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young girl, \xe2\x80\x9cthere are a great many different ways of\r\nremembering a person.\xe2\x80\x9d And she looked straight at Valentin de\r\nBellegarde, who was looking at her as a gentleman may when a \xe2\x80\x9cverdict\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nis expected of him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you painted anything for me?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cHave you been\r\nindustrious?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I have done nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d And taking up her palette, she began to mix\r\nher colors at hazard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut your father tells me you have come here constantly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have nowhere else to go! Here, all summer, it was cool, at least.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeing here, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cyou might have tried something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told you before,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered, softly, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how to\r\npaint.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you have something charming on your easel, now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cif you would only let me see it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe spread out her two hands, with the fingers expanded, over the back\r\nof the canvas\xe2\x80\x94those hands which Newman had called pretty, and which, in\r\nspite of several paint-stains, Valentin could now admire. \xe2\x80\x9cMy painting\r\nis not charming,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is the only thing about you that is not, then, mademoiselle,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth\r\nValentin, gallantly.\r\n\r\nShe took up her little canvas and silently passed it to him. He looked\r\nat it, and in a moment she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI am sure you are a judge.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, \xe2\x80\x9cI am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know, then, that that is very bad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Mon Dieu_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin, shrugging his shoulders \xe2\x80\x9clet us\r\ndistinguish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know that I ought not to attempt to paint,\xe2\x80\x9d the young girl\r\ncontinued.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrankly, then, mademoiselle, I think you ought not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe began to look at the dresses of the two splendid ladies again\xe2\x80\x94a\r\npoint on which, having risked one conjecture, I think I may risk\r\nanother. While she was looking at the ladies she was seeing Valentin de\r\nBellegarde. He, at all events, was seeing her. He put down the\r\nroughly-besmeared canvas and addressed a little click with his tongue,\r\naccompanied by an elevation of the eyebrows, to Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere have you been all these months?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie of\r\nour hero. \xe2\x80\x9cYou took those great journeys, you amused yourself well?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI amused myself well enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very glad,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie with extreme gentleness, and\r\nshe began to dabble in her colors again. She was singularly pretty,\r\nwith the look of serious sympathy that she threw into her face.\r\n\r\nValentin took advantage of her downcast eyes to telegraph again to his\r\ncompanion. He renewed his mysterious physiognomical play, making at the\r\nsame time a rapid tremulous movement in the air with his fingers. He\r\nwas evidently finding Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie extremely interesting; the\r\nblue devils had departed, leaving the field clear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me something about your travels,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the young girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I went to Switzerland,\xe2\x80\x94to Geneva and Zermatt and Z\xc3\xbcrich and all\r\nthose places you know; and down to Venice, and all through Germany, and\r\ndown the Rhine, and into Holland and Belgium\xe2\x80\x94the regular round. How do\r\nyou say that, in French\xe2\x80\x94the regular round?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman asked of Valentin.\r\n\r\nMademoiselle Nioche fixed her eyes an instant on Bellegarde, and then\r\nwith a little smile, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen he\r\nsays so much at once. Would you be so good as to translate?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would rather talk to you out of my own head,\xe2\x80\x9d Valentin declared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, gravely, still in his bad French, \xe2\x80\x9cyou must not talk\r\nto Mademoiselle Nioche, because you say discouraging things. You ought\r\nto tell her to work, to persevere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd we French, mademoiselle,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin, \xe2\x80\x9care accused of being\r\nfalse flatterers!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want any flattery, I want only the truth. But I know the\r\ntruth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll I say is that I suspect there are some things that you can do\r\nbetter than paint,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know the truth\xe2\x80\x94I know the truth,\xe2\x80\x9d Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie repeated. And,\r\ndipping a brush into a clot of red paint, she drew a great horizontal\r\ndaub across her unfinished picture.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is that?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman.\r\n\r\nWithout answering, she drew another long crimson daub, in a vertical\r\ndirection, down the middle of her canvas, and so, in a moment,\r\ncompleted the rough indication of a cross. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is the sign of the\r\ntruth,\xe2\x80\x9d she said at last.\r\n\r\nThe two men looked at each other, and Valentin indulged in another\r\nflash of physiognomical eloquence. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have spoiled your picture,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know that very well. It was the only thing to do with it. I had sat\r\nlooking at it all day without touching it. I had begun to hate it. It\r\nseemed to me something was going to happen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like it better that way than as it was before,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin. \xe2\x80\x9cNow\r\nit is more interesting. It tells a story. Is it for sale?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything I have is for sale,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much is this thing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTen thousand francs,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young girl, without a smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything that Mademoiselle Nioche may do at present is mine in\r\nadvance,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cIt makes part of an order I gave her some\r\nmonths ago. So you can\xe2\x80\x99t have this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur will lose nothing by it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young girl, looking at\r\nValentin. And she began to put up her utensils.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall have gained a charming memory,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are going\r\naway? your day is over?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy father is coming to fetch me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie.\r\n\r\nShe had hardly spoken when, through the door behind her, which opens on\r\none of the great white stone staircases of the Louvre, M. Nioche made\r\nhis appearance. He came in with his usual even, patient shuffle, and he\r\nmade a low salute to the two gentlemen who were standing before his\r\ndaughter\xe2\x80\x99s easel. Newman shook his hands with muscular friendliness,\r\nand Valentin returned his greeting with extreme deference. While the\r\nold man stood waiting for No\xc3\xa9mie to make a parcel of her implements, he\r\nlet his mild, oblique gaze hover toward Bellegarde, who was watching\r\nMademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie put on her bonnet and mantle. Valentin was at no\r\npains to disguise his scrutiny. He looked at a pretty girl as he would\r\nhave listened to a piece of music. Attention, in each case, was simple\r\ngood manners. M. Nioche at last took his daughter\xe2\x80\x99s paint-box in one\r\nhand and the bedaubed canvas, after giving it a solemn, puzzled stare,\r\nin the other, and led the way to the door. Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie made the\r\nyoung men the salute of a duchess, and followed her father.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat do you think of her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is very remarkable. _Diable, diable, diable!_\xe2\x80\x9d repeated M. de\r\nBellegarde, reflectively; \xe2\x80\x9cshe is very remarkable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid she is a sad little adventuress,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a little one\xe2\x80\x94a great one. She has the material.\xe2\x80\x9d And Valentin\r\nbegan to walk away slowly, looking vaguely at the pictures on the\r\nwalls, with a thoughtful illumination in his eye. Nothing could have\r\nappealed to his imagination more than the possible adventures of a\r\nyoung lady endowed with the \xe2\x80\x9cmaterial\xe2\x80\x9d of Mademoiselle Nioche. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is\r\nvery interesting,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is a beautiful type.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA beautiful type? What the deuce do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean from the artistic point of view. She is an artist,\xe2\x80\x94outside of\r\nher painting, which obviously is execrable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut she is not beautiful. I don\xe2\x80\x99t even think her very pretty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is quite pretty enough for her purposes, and it is a face and\r\nfigure on which everything tells. If she were prettier she would be\r\nless intelligent, and her intelligence is half of her charm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn what way,\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman, who was much amused at his companion\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nimmediate philosophisation of Mademoiselle Nioche, \xe2\x80\x9cdoes her\r\nintelligence strike you as so remarkable?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has taken the measure of life, and she has determined to _be_\r\nsomething\xe2\x80\x94to succeed at any cost. Her painting, of course, is a mere\r\ntrick to gain time. She is waiting for her chance; she wishes to launch\r\nherself, and to do it well. She knows her Paris. She is one of fifty\r\nthousand, so far as the mere ambition goes; but I am very sure that in\r\nthe way of resolution and capacity she is a rarity. And in one\r\ngift\xe2\x80\x94perfect heartlessness\xe2\x80\x94I will warrant she is unsurpassed. She has\r\nnot as much heart as will go on the point of a needle. That is an\r\nimmense virtue. Yes, she is one of the celebrities of the future.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeaven help us!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9chow far the artistic point of view may\r\ntake a man! But in this case I must request that you don\xe2\x80\x99t let it take\r\nyou too far. You have learned a wonderful deal about Mademoiselle\r\nNo\xc3\xa9mie in a quarter of an hour. Let that suffice; don\xe2\x80\x99t follow up your\r\nresearches.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear fellow,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bellegarde with warmth, \xe2\x80\x9cI hope I have too good\r\nmanners to intrude.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not intruding. The girl is nothing to me. In fact, I rather\r\ndislike her. But I like her poor old father, and for his sake I beg you\r\nto abstain from any attempt to verify your theories.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the sake of that seedy old gentleman who came to fetch her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ndemanded Valentin, stopping short. And on Newman\xe2\x80\x99s assenting, \xe2\x80\x9cAh no,\r\nah no,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on with a smile. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are quite wrong, my dear fellow;\r\nyou needn\xe2\x80\x99t mind him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI verily believe that you are accusing the poor gentleman of being\r\ncapable of rejoicing in his daughter\xe2\x80\x99s dishonor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Voyons!_\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin; \xe2\x80\x9cwho is he? what is he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is what he looks like: as poor as a rat, but very high-toned.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly. I noticed him perfectly; be sure I do him justice. He has had\r\nlosses, _des malheurs_, as we say. He is very low-spirited, and his\r\ndaughter is too much for him. He is the pink of respectability, and he\r\nhas sixty years of honesty on his back. All this I perfectly\r\nappreciate. But I know my fellow-men and my fellow-Parisians, and I\r\nwill make a bargain with you.\xe2\x80\x9d Newman gave ear to his bargain and he\r\nwent on. \xe2\x80\x9cHe would rather his daughter were a good girl than a bad one,\r\nbut if the worst comes to the worst, the old man will not do what\r\nVirginius did. Success justifies everything. If Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie\r\nmakes a figure, her papa will feel\xe2\x80\x94well, we will call it relieved. And\r\nshe will make a figure. The old gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s future is assured.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what Virginius did, but M. Nioche will shoot Miss\r\nNo\xc3\xa9mie,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cAfter that, I suppose his future will be assured\r\nin some snug prison.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not a cynic; I am simply an observer,\xe2\x80\x9d Valentin rejoined.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie interests me; she is extremely remarkable. If\r\nthere is a good reason, in honor or decency, for dismissing her from my\r\nthoughts forever, I am perfectly willing to do it. Your estimate of the\r\npapa\xe2\x80\x99s sensibilities is a good reason until it is invalidated. I\r\npromise you not to look at the young girl again until you tell me that\r\nyou have changed your mind about the papa. When he has given distinct\r\nproof of being a philosopher, you will raise your interdict. Do you\r\nagree to that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean to bribe him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you admit, then, that he is bribable? No, he would ask too much,\r\nand it would not be exactly fair. I mean simply to wait. You will\r\ncontinue, I suppose, to see this interesting couple, and you will give\r\nme the news yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cif the old man turns out a humbug, you may do\r\nwhat you please. I wash my hands of the matter. For the girl herself,\r\nyou may be at rest. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what harm she may do to me, but I\r\ncertainly can\xe2\x80\x99t hurt her. It seems to me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you are\r\nvery well matched. You are both hard cases, and M. Nioche and I, I\r\nbelieve, are the only virtuous men to be found in Paris.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSoon after this M. de Bellegarde, in punishment for his levity,\r\nreceived a stern poke in the back from a pointed instrument. Turning\r\nquickly round he found the weapon to be a parasol wielded by a lady in\r\ngreen gauze bonnet. Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s English cousins had been drifting about\r\nunpiloted, and evidently deemed that they had a grievance. Newman left\r\nhim to their mercies, but with a boundless faith in his power to plead\r\nhis cause.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XII\r\n\r\n\r\nThree days after his introduction to the family of Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9,\r\nNewman, coming in toward evening, found upon his table the card of the\r\nMarquis de Bellegarde. On the following day he received a note\r\ninforming him that the Marquise de Bellegarde would be grateful for the\r\nhonor of his company at dinner.\r\n\r\nHe went, of course, though he had to break another engagement to do it.\r\nHe was ushered into the room in which Madame de Bellegarde had received\r\nhim before, and here he found his venerable hostess, surrounded by her\r\nentire family. The room was lighted only by the crackling fire, which\r\nilluminated the very small pink slippers of a lady who, seated in a low\r\nchair, was stretching out her toes before it. This lady was the younger\r\nMadame de Bellegarde. Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 was seated at the other end of\r\nthe room, holding a little girl against her knee, the child of her\r\nbrother Urbain, to whom she was apparently relating a wonderful story.\r\nValentin was sitting on a puff, close to his sister-in-law, into whose\r\near he was certainly distilling the finest nonsense. The marquis was\r\nstationed before the fire, with his head erect and his hands behind\r\nhim, in an attitude of formal expectancy.\r\n\r\nOld Madame de Bellegarde stood up to give Newman her greeting, and\r\nthere was that in the way she did so which seemed to measure narrowly\r\nthe extent of her condescension. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are all alone, you see, we have\r\nasked no one else,\xe2\x80\x9d she said austerely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very glad you didn\xe2\x80\x99t; this is much more sociable,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood evening, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d and he offered his hand to the marquis.\r\n\r\nM. de Bellegarde was affable, but in spite of his dignity he was\r\nrestless. He began to pace up and down the room, he looked out of the\r\nlong windows, he took up books and laid them down again. Young Madame\r\nde Bellegarde gave Newman her hand without moving and without looking\r\nat him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may think that is coldness,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Valentin; \xe2\x80\x9cbut it is not,\r\nit is warmth. It shows she is treating you as an intimate. Now she\r\ndetests me, and yet she is always looking at me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo wonder I detest you if I am always looking at you!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the lady.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf Mr. Newman does not like my way of shaking hands, I will do it\r\nagain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut this charming privilege was lost upon our hero, who was already\r\nmaking his way across the room to Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. She looked at him\r\nas she shook hands, but she went on with the story she was telling her\r\nlittle niece. She had only two or three phrases to add, but they were\r\napparently of great moment. She deepened her voice, smiling as she did\r\nso, and the little girl gazed at her with round eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut in the end the young prince married the beautiful Florabella,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, \xe2\x80\x9cand carried her off to live with him in the\r\nLand of the Pink Sky. There she was so happy that she forgot all her\r\ntroubles, and went out to drive every day of her life in an ivory coach\r\ndrawn by five hundred white mice. Poor Florabella,\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed to\r\nNewman, \xe2\x80\x9chad suffered terribly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe had had nothing to eat for six months,\xe2\x80\x9d said little Blanche.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but when the six months were over, she had a plum-cake as big as\r\nthat ottoman,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. \xe2\x80\x9cThat quite set her up again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a checkered career!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cAre you very fond of\r\nchildren?\xe2\x80\x9d He was certain that she was, but he wished to make her say\r\nit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like to talk with them,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered; \xe2\x80\x9cwe can talk with them so\r\nmuch more seriously than with grown persons. That is great nonsense\r\nthat I have been telling Blanche, but it is a great deal more serious\r\nthan most of what we say in society.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish you would talk to me, then, as if I were Blanche\xe2\x80\x99s age,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nNewman, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cWere you happy at your ball the other night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEcstatically!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow you are talking the nonsense that we talk in society,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nNewman. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was my own fault if I was not happy. The ball was very pretty, and\r\neveryone very amiable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was on your conscience,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you had annoyed your\r\nmother and your brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 looked at him a moment without answering. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is\r\ntrue,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied at last. \xe2\x80\x9cI had undertaken more than I could carry\r\nout. I have very little courage; I am not a heroine.\xe2\x80\x9d She said this\r\nwith a certain soft emphasis; but then, changing her tone, \xe2\x80\x9cI could\r\nnever have gone through the sufferings of the beautiful Florabella,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe added, not even for her prospective rewards.\r\n\r\nDinner was announced, and Newman betook himself to the side of the old\r\nMadame de Bellegarde. The dining-room, at the end of a cold corridor,\r\nwas vast and sombre; the dinner was simple and delicately excellent.\r\nNewman wondered whether Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 had had something to do with\r\nordering the repast and greatly hoped she had. Once seated at table,\r\nwith the various members of the ancient house of Bellegarde around him,\r\nhe asked himself the meaning of his position. Was the old lady\r\nresponding to his advances? Did the fact that he was a solitary guest\r\naugment his credit or diminish it? Were they ashamed to show him to\r\nother people, or did they wish to give him a sign of sudden adoption\r\ninto their last reserve of favor? Newman was on his guard; he was\r\nwatchful and conjectural; and yet at the same time he was vaguely\r\nindifferent. Whether they gave him a long rope or a short one he was\r\nthere now, and Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 was opposite to him. She had a tall\r\ncandlestick on each side of her; she would sit there for the next hour,\r\nand that was enough. The dinner was extremely solemn and measured; he\r\nwondered whether this was always the state of things in \xe2\x80\x9cold families.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nMadame de Bellegarde held her head very high, and fixed her eyes, which\r\nlooked peculiarly sharp in her little, finely-wrinkled white face, very\r\nintently upon the table-service. The marquis appeared to have decided\r\nthat the fine arts offered a safe subject of conversation, as not\r\nleading to startling personal revelations. Every now and then, having\r\nlearned from Newman that he had been through the museums of Europe, he\r\nuttered some polished aphorism upon the flesh-tints of Rubens and the\r\ngood taste of Sansovino. His manners seemed to indicate a fine, nervous\r\ndread that something disagreeable might happen if the atmosphere were\r\nnot purified by allusions of a thoroughly superior cast. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat under\r\nthe sun is the man afraid of?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman asked himself. \xe2\x80\x9cDoes he think I\r\nam going to offer to swap jack-knives with him?\xe2\x80\x9d It was useless to shut\r\nhis eyes to the fact that the marquis was profoundly disagreeable to\r\nhim. He had never been a man of strong personal aversions; his nerves\r\nhad not been at the mercy of the mystical qualities of his neighbors.\r\nBut here was a man towards whom he was irresistibly in opposition; a\r\nman of forms and phrases and postures; a man full of possible\r\nimpertinences and treacheries. M. de Bellegarde made him feel as if he\r\nwere standing bare-footed on a marble floor; and yet, to gain his\r\ndesire, Newman felt perfectly able to stand. He wondered what Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9 thought of his being accepted, if accepted it was. There was no\r\njudging from her face, which expressed simply the desire to be gracious\r\nin a manner which should require as little explicit recognition as\r\npossible. Young Madame de Bellegarde had always the same manners; she\r\nwas always preoccupied, distracted, listening to everything and hearing\r\nnothing, looking at her dress, her rings, her finger-nails, seeming\r\nrather bored, and yet puzzling you to decide what was her ideal of\r\nsocial diversion. Newman was enlightened on this point later. Even\r\nValentin did not quite seem master of his wits; his vivacity was fitful\r\nand forced, yet Newman observed that in the lapses of his talk he\r\nappeared excited. His eyes had an intenser spark than usual. The effect\r\nof all this was that Newman, for the first time in his life, was not\r\nhimself; that he measured his movements, and counted his words, and\r\nresolved that if the occasion demanded that he should appear to have\r\nswallowed a ramrod, he would meet the emergency.\r\n\r\nAfter dinner M. de Bellegarde proposed to his guest that they should go\r\ninto the smoking-room, and he led the way toward a small, somewhat\r\nmusty apartment, the walls of which were ornamented with old hangings\r\nof stamped leather and trophies of rusty arms. Newman refused a cigar,\r\nbut he established himself upon one of the divans, while the marquis\r\npuffed his own weed before the fire-place, and Valentin sat looking\r\nthrough the light fumes of a cigarette from one to the other.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t keep quiet any longer,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin, at last. \xe2\x80\x9cI must tell\r\nyou the news and congratulate you. My brother seems unable to come to\r\nthe point; he revolves around his announcement like the priest around\r\nthe altar. You are accepted as a candidate for the hand of our sister.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cValentin, be a little proper!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the marquis, with a look of\r\nthe most delicate irritation contracting the bridge of his high nose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere has been a family council,\xe2\x80\x9d the young man continued; \xe2\x80\x9cmy mother\r\nand Urbain have put their heads together, and even my testimony has not\r\nbeen altogether excluded. My mother and the marquis sat at a table\r\ncovered with green cloth; my sister-in-law and I were on a bench\r\nagainst the wall. It was like a committee at the Corps L\xc3\xa9gislatif. We\r\nwere called up, one after the other, to testify. We spoke of you very\r\nhandsomely. Madame de Bellegarde said that if she had not been told who\r\nyou were, she would have taken you for a duke\xe2\x80\x94an American duke, the\r\nDuke of California. I said that I could warrant you grateful for the\r\nsmallest favors\xe2\x80\x94modest, humble, unassuming. I was sure that you would\r\nknow your own place, always, and never give us occasion to remind you\r\nof certain differences. After all, you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it if you were not\r\na duke. There were none in your country; but if there had been, it was\r\ncertain that, smart and active as you are, you would have got the pick\r\nof the titles. At this point I was ordered to sit down, but I think I\r\nmade an impression in your favor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nM. de Bellegarde looked at his brother with dangerous coldness, and\r\ngave a smile as thin as the edge of a knife. Then he removed a spark of\r\ncigar-ash from the sleeve of his coat; he fixed his eyes for a while on\r\nthe cornice of the room, and at last he inserted one of his white hands\r\ninto the breast of his waistcoat. \xe2\x80\x9cI must apologize to you for the\r\ndeplorable levity of my brother,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cand I must notify you that\r\nthis is probably not the last time that his want of tact will cause you\r\nserious embarrassment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I confess I have no tact,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin. \xe2\x80\x9cIs your embarrassment\r\nreally painful, Newman? The marquis will put you right again; his own\r\ntouch is deliciously delicate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cValentin, I am sorry to say,\xe2\x80\x9d the marquis continued, \xe2\x80\x9chas never\r\npossessed the tone, the manner, that belongs to a young man in his\r\nposition. It has been a great affliction to his mother, who is very\r\nfond of the old traditions. But you must remember that he speaks for no\r\none but himself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind him, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, good-humoredly. \xe2\x80\x9cI know what\r\nhe amounts to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the good old times,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin, \xe2\x80\x9cmarquises and counts used to\r\nhave their appointed fools and jesters, to crack jokes for them.\r\nNowadays we see a great strapping democrat keeping a count about him to\r\nplay the fool. It\xe2\x80\x99s a good situation, but I certainly am very\r\ndegenerate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nM. de Bellegarde fixed his eyes for some time on the floor. \xe2\x80\x9cMy mother\r\ninformed me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said presently, \xe2\x80\x9cof the announcement that you made to\r\nher the other evening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat I desired to marry your sister?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat you wished to arrange a marriage,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquis, slowly,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwith my sister, the Comtesse de Cintr\xc3\xa9. The proposal was serious, and\r\nrequired, on my mother\xe2\x80\x99s part, a great deal of reflection. She\r\nnaturally took me into her counsels, and I gave my most zealous\r\nattention to the subject. There was a great deal to be considered; more\r\nthan you appear to imagine. We have viewed the question on all its\r\nfaces, we have weighed one thing against another. Our conclusion has\r\nbeen that we favor your suit. My mother has desired me to inform you of\r\nour decision. She will have the honor of saying a few words to you on\r\nthe subject herself. Meanwhile, by us, the heads of the family, you are\r\naccepted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman got up and came nearer to the marquis. \xe2\x80\x9cYou will do nothing to\r\nhinder me, and all you can to help me, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will recommend my sister to accept you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman passed his hand over his face, and pressed it for a moment upon\r\nhis eyes. This promise had a great sound, and yet the pleasure he took\r\nin it was embittered by his having to stand there so and receive his\r\npassport from M. de Bellegarde. The idea of having this gentleman mixed\r\nup with his wooing and wedding was more and more disagreeable to him.\r\nBut Newman had resolved to go through the mill, as he imagined it, and\r\nhe would not cry out at the first turn of the wheel. He was silent a\r\nwhile, and then he said, with a certain dryness which Valentin told him\r\nafterwards had a very grand air, \xe2\x80\x9cI am much obliged to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI take note of the promise,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin, \xe2\x80\x9cI register the vow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nM. de Bellegarde began to gaze at the cornice again; he apparently had\r\nsomething more to say. \xe2\x80\x9cI must do my mother the justice,\xe2\x80\x9d he resumed,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must do myself the justice, to say that our decision was not easy.\r\nSuch an arrangement was not what we had expected. The idea that my\r\nsister should marry a gentleman\xe2\x80\x94ah\xe2\x80\x94in business was something of a\r\nnovelty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo I told you, you know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin raising his finger at Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe novelty has not quite worn away, I confess,\xe2\x80\x9d the marquis went on;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cperhaps it never will, entirely. But possibly that is not altogether\r\nto be regretted,\xe2\x80\x9d and he gave his thin smile again. \xe2\x80\x9cIt may be that the\r\ntime has come when we should make some concession to novelty. There had\r\nbeen no novelties in our house for a great many years. I made the\r\nobservation to my mother, and she did me the honor to admit that it was\r\nworthy of attention.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear brother,\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted Valentin, \xe2\x80\x9cis not your memory just here\r\nleading you the least bit astray? Our mother is, I may say,\r\ndistinguished for her small respect of abstract reasoning. Are you very\r\nsure that she replied to your striking proposition in the gracious\r\nmanner you describe? You know how terribly incisive she is sometimes.\r\nDidn\xe2\x80\x99t she, rather, do you the honor to say, \xe2\x80\x98A fiddlestick for your\r\nphrases! There are better reasons than that?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOther reasons were discussed,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquis, without looking at\r\nValentin, but with an audible tremor in his voice; \xe2\x80\x9csome of them\r\npossibly were better. We are conservative, Mr. Newman, but we are not\r\nalso bigots. We judged the matter liberally. We have no doubt that\r\neverything will be comfortable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman had stood listening to these remarks with his arms folded and\r\nhis eyes fastened upon M. de Bellegarde, \xe2\x80\x9cComfortable?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with a\r\nsort of grim flatness of intonation. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t we be comfortable?\r\nIf you are not, it will be your own fault; I have everything to make\r\n_me_ so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy brother means that with the lapse of time you may get used to the\r\nchange\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and Valentin paused, to light another cigarette.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat change?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman in the same tone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUrbain,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin, very gravely, \xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid that Mr. Newman\r\ndoes not quite realize the change. We ought to insist upon that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy brother goes too far,\xe2\x80\x9d said M. de Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is his fatal want\r\nof tact again. It is my mother\xe2\x80\x99s wish, and mine, that no such allusions\r\nshould be made. Pray never make them yourself. We prefer to assume that\r\nthe person accepted as the possible husband of my sister is one of\r\nourselves, and that he should have no explanations to make. With a\r\nlittle discretion on both sides, everything, I think, will be easy.\r\nThat is exactly what I wished to say\xe2\x80\x94that we quite understand what we\r\nhave undertaken, and that you may depend upon our adhering to our\r\nresolution.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nValentin shook his hands in the air and then buried his face in them.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have less tact than I might have, no doubt; but oh, my brother, if\r\nyou knew what you yourself were saying!\xe2\x80\x9d And he went off into a long\r\nlaugh.\r\n\r\nM. de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s face flushed a little, but he held his head higher,\r\nas if to repudiate this concession to vulgar perturbability. \xe2\x80\x9cI am sure\r\nyou understand me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand you at all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you needn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmind that. I don\xe2\x80\x99t care. In fact, I think I had better not understand\r\nyou. I might not like it. That wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t suit me at all, you know. I\r\nwant to marry your sister, that\xe2\x80\x99s all; to do it as quickly as possible,\r\nand to find fault with nothing. I don\xe2\x80\x99t care how I do it. I am not\r\nmarrying you, you know, sir. I have got my leave, and that is all I\r\nwant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had better receive the last word from my mother,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nmarquis.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good; I will go and get it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman; and he prepared to\r\nreturn to the drawing-room.\r\n\r\nM. de Bellegarde made a motion for him to pass first, and when Newman\r\nhad gone out he shut himself into the room with Valentin. Newman had\r\nbeen a trifle bewildered by the audacious irony of the younger brother,\r\nand he had not needed its aid to point the moral of M. de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntranscendent patronage. He had wit enough to appreciate the force of\r\nthat civility which consists in calling your attention to the\r\nimpertinences it spares you. But he had felt warmly the delicate\r\nsympathy with himself that underlay Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s fraternal irreverence,\r\nand he was most unwilling that his friend should pay a tax upon it. He\r\npaused a moment in the corridor, after he had gone a few steps,\r\nexpecting to hear the resonance of M. de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s displeasure; but\r\nhe detected only a perfect stillness. The stillness itself seemed a\r\ntrifle portentous; he reflected however that he had no right to stand\r\nlistening, and he made his way back to the salon. In his absence\r\nseveral persons had come in. They were scattered about the room in\r\ngroups, two or three of them having passed into a small boudoir, next\r\nto the drawing-room, which had now been lighted and opened. Old Madame\r\nde Bellegarde was in her place by the fire, talking to a very old\r\ngentleman in a wig and a profuse white neck cloth of the fashion of\r\n1820. Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 was bending a listening head to the historic\r\nconfidences of an old lady who was presumably the wife of the old\r\ngentleman in the neckcloth, an old lady in a red satin dress and an\r\nermine cape, who wore across her forehead a band with a topaz set in\r\nit. Young Madame de Bellegarde, when Newman came in, left some people\r\namong whom she was sitting, and took the place that she had occupied\r\nbefore dinner. Then she gave a little push to the puff that stood near\r\nher, and by a glance at Newman seemed to indicate that she had placed\r\nit in position for him. He went and took possession of it; the\r\nmarquis\xe2\x80\x99s wife amused and puzzled him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know your secret,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, in her bad but charming English; \xe2\x80\x9cyou\r\nneed make no mystery of it. You wish to marry my sister-in-law. _C\xe2\x80\x99est\r\nun beau choix_. A man like you ought to marry a tall, thin woman. You\r\nmust know that I have spoken in your favor; you owe me a famous taper!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have spoken to Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, not that. You may think it strange, but my sister-in-law and I\r\nare not so intimate as that. No; I spoke to my husband and my\r\nmother-in-law; I said I was sure we could do what we chose with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am much obliged to you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, laughing; \xe2\x80\x9cbut you can\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know that very well; I didn\xe2\x80\x99t believe a word of it. But I wanted you\r\nto come into the house; I thought we should be friends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very sure of it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be too sure. If you like Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 so much, perhaps you\r\nwill not like me. We are as different as blue and pink. But you and I\r\nhave something in common. I have come into this family by marriage; you\r\nwant to come into it in the same way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, I don\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI only want to take Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9 out of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, to cast your nets you have to go into the water. Our positions\r\nare alike; we shall be able to compare notes. What do you think of my\r\nhusband? It\xe2\x80\x99s a strange question, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it? But I shall ask you some\r\nstranger ones yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps a stranger one will be easier to answer,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nmight try me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you get off very well; the old Comte de la Rochefid\xc3\xa8le, yonder,\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do it better. I told them that if we only gave you a chance\r\nyou would be a perfect _talon rouge_. I know something about men.\r\nBesides, you and I belong to the same camp. I am a ferocious democrat.\r\nBy birth I am _vieille roche_; a good little bit of the history of\r\nFrance is the history of my family. Oh, you never heard of us, of\r\ncourse! _Ce que c\xe2\x80\x99est que la gloire!_ We are much better than the\r\nBellegardes, at any rate. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t care a pin for my pedigree; I\r\nwant to belong to my time. I\xe2\x80\x99m a revolutionist, a radical, a child of\r\nthe age! I am sure I go beyond you. I like clever people, wherever they\r\ncome from, and I take my amusement wherever I find it. I don\xe2\x80\x99t pout at\r\nthe Empire; here all the world pouts at the Empire. Of course I have to\r\nmind what I say; but I expect to take my revenge with you.\xe2\x80\x9d Madame de\r\nBellegarde discoursed for some time longer in this sympathetic strain,\r\nwith an eager abundance which seemed to indicate that her opportunities\r\nfor revealing her esoteric philosophy were indeed rare. She hoped that\r\nNewman would never be afraid of her, however he might be with the\r\nothers, for, really, she went very far indeed. \xe2\x80\x9cStrong people\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94_le gens\r\nforts_\xe2\x80\x94were in her opinion equal, all the world over. Newman listened\r\nto her with an attention at once beguiled and irritated. He wondered\r\nwhat the deuce she, too, was driving at, with her hope that he would\r\nnot be afraid of her and her protestations of equality. In so far as he\r\ncould understand her, she was wrong; a silly, rattling woman was\r\ncertainly not the equal of a sensible man, preoccupied with an\r\nambitious passion. Madame de Bellegarde stopped suddenly, and looked at\r\nhim sharply, shaking her fan. \xe2\x80\x9cI see you don\xe2\x80\x99t believe me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cyou are too much on your guard. You will not form an alliance,\r\noffensive or defensive? You are very wrong; I could help you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman answered that he was very grateful and that he would certainly\r\nask for help; she should see. \xe2\x80\x9cBut first of all,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI must help\r\nmyself.\xe2\x80\x9d And he went to join Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been telling Madame de la Rochefid\xc3\xa8le that you are an\r\nAmerican,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, as he came up. \xe2\x80\x9cIt interests her greatly. Her\r\nfather went over with the French troops to help you in your battles in\r\nthe last century, and she has always, in consequence, wanted greatly to\r\nsee an American. But she has never succeeded till to-night. You are the\r\nfirst\xe2\x80\x94to her knowledge\xe2\x80\x94that she has ever looked at.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de la Rochefid\xc3\xa8le had an aged, cadaverous face, with a falling\r\nof the lower jaw which prevented her from bringing her lips together,\r\nand reduced her conversations to a series of impressive but\r\ninarticulate gutturals. She raised an antique eyeglass, elaborately\r\nmounted in chased silver, and looked at Newman from head to foot. Then\r\nshe said something to which he listened deferentially, but which he\r\ncompletely failed to understand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadame de la Rochefid\xc3\xa8le says that she is convinced that she must have\r\nseen Americans without knowing it,\xe2\x80\x9d Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 explained. Newman\r\nthought it probable she had seen a great many things without knowing\r\nit; and the old lady, again addressing herself to utterance,\r\ndeclared\xe2\x80\x94as interpreted by Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x94that she wished she had\r\nknown it.\r\n\r\nAt this moment the old gentleman who had been talking to the elder\r\nMadame de Bellegarde drew near, leading the marquise on his arm. His\r\nwife pointed out Newman to him, apparently explaining his remarkable\r\norigin. M. de la Rochefid\xc3\xa8le, whose old age was rosy and rotund, spoke\r\nvery neatly and clearly, almost as prettily, Newman thought, as M.\r\nNioche. When he had been enlightened, he turned to Newman with an\r\ninimitable elderly grace.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur is by no means the first American that I have seen,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlmost the first person I ever saw\xe2\x80\x94to notice him\xe2\x80\x94was an American.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, sympathetically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe great Dr. Franklin,\xe2\x80\x9d said M. de la Rochefid\xc3\xa8le. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course I was\r\nvery young. He was received very well in our _monde._\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot better than Mr. Newman,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cI beg he will\r\noffer his arm into the other room. I could have offered no higher\r\nprivilege to Dr. Franklin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman, complying with Madame de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s request, perceived that\r\nher two sons had returned to the drawing-room. He scanned their faces\r\nan instant for traces of the scene that had followed his separation\r\nfrom them, but the marquis seemed neither more nor less frigidly grand\r\nthan usual, and Valentin was kissing ladies\xe2\x80\x99 hands with at least his\r\nhabitual air of self-abandonment to the act. Madame de Bellegarde gave\r\na glance at her eldest son, and by the time she had crossed the\r\nthreshold of her boudoir he was at her side. The room was now empty and\r\noffered a sufficient degree of privacy. The old lady disengaged herself\r\nfrom Newman\xe2\x80\x99s arm and rested her hand on the arm of the marquis; and in\r\nthis position she stood a moment, holding her head high and biting her\r\nsmall under-lip. I am afraid the picture was lost upon Newman, but\r\nMadame de Bellegarde was, in fact, at this moment a striking image of\r\nthe dignity which\xe2\x80\x94even in the case of a little time-shrunken old\r\nlady\xe2\x80\x94may reside in the habit of unquestioned authority and the\r\nabsoluteness of a social theory favorable to yourself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy son has spoken to you as I desired,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cand you understand\r\nthat we shall not interfere. The rest will lie with yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cM. de Bellegarde told me several things I didn\xe2\x80\x99t understand,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nNewman, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I made out that. You will leave me open field. I am much\r\nobliged.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish to add a word that my son probably did not feel at liberty to\r\nsay,\xe2\x80\x9d the marquise rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cI must say it for my own peace of mind.\r\nWe are stretching a point; we are doing you a great favor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, your son said it very well; didn\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot so well as my mother,\xe2\x80\x9d declared the marquis.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can only repeat\xe2\x80\x94I am much obliged.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is proper I should tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d Madame de Bellegarde went on, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I\r\nam very proud, and that I hold my head very high. I may be wrong, but I\r\nam too old to change. At least I know it, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t pretend to\r\nanything else. Don\xe2\x80\x99t flatter yourself that my daughter is not proud.\r\nShe is proud in her own way\xe2\x80\x94a somewhat different way from mine. You\r\nwill have to make your terms with that. Even Valentin is proud, if you\r\ntouch the right spot\xe2\x80\x94or the wrong one. Urbain is proud; that you see\r\nfor yourself. Sometimes I think he is a little too proud; but I\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t change him. He is the best of my children; he cleaves to his\r\nold mother. But I have said enough to show you that we are all proud\r\ntogether. It is well that you should know the sort of people you have\r\ncome among.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cI can only say, in return, that I am _not_ proud;\r\nI shan\xe2\x80\x99t mind you! But you speak as if you intended to be very\r\ndisagreeable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall not enjoy having my daughter marry you, and I shall not\r\npretend to enjoy it. If you don\xe2\x80\x99t mind that, so much the better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you stick to your own side of the contract we shall not quarrel;\r\nthat is all I ask of you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cKeep your hands off, and give\r\nme an open field. I am very much in earnest, and there is not the\r\nslightest danger of my getting discouraged or backing out. You will\r\nhave me constantly before your eyes; if you don\xe2\x80\x99t like it, I am sorry\r\nfor you. I will do for your daughter, if she will accept me, everything\r\nthat a man can do for a woman. I am happy to tell you that, as a\r\npromise\xe2\x80\x94a pledge. I consider that on your side you make me an equal\r\npledge. You will not back out, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you mean by \xe2\x80\x98backing out,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquise. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nsuggests a movement of which I think no Bellegarde has ever been\r\nguilty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur word is our word,\xe2\x80\x9d said Urbain. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have given it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cI am very glad you are so proud. It makes me\r\nbelieve that you will keep it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe marquise was silent a moment, and then, suddenly, \xe2\x80\x9cI shall always\r\nbe polite to you, Mr. Newman,\xe2\x80\x9d she declared, \xe2\x80\x9cbut, decidedly, I shall\r\nnever like you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be too sure,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am so sure that I will ask you to take me back to my armchair\r\nwithout the least fear of having my sentiments modified by the service\r\nyou render me.\xe2\x80\x9d And Madame de Bellegarde took his arm, and returned to\r\nthe salon and to her customary place.\r\n\r\nM. de la Rochefid\xc3\xa8le and his wife were preparing to take their leave,\r\nand Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s interview with the mumbling old lady was at an\r\nend. She stood looking about her, asking herself, apparently to whom\r\nshe should next speak, when Newman came up to her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour mother has given me leave\xe2\x80\x94very solemnly\xe2\x80\x94to come here often,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cI mean to come often.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be glad to see you,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered simply. And then, in a\r\nmoment: \xe2\x80\x9cYou probably think it very strange that there should be such a\r\nsolemnity\xe2\x80\x94as you say\xe2\x80\x94about your coming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, yes; I do, rather.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you remember what my brother Valentin said, the first time you came\r\nto see me\xe2\x80\x94that we were a strange, strange family?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was not the first time I came, but the second,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery true. Valentin annoyed me at the time, but now I know you better,\r\nI may tell you he was right. If you come often, you will see!\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 turned away.\r\n\r\nNewman watched her a while, talking with other people, and then he took\r\nhis leave. He shook hands last with Valentin de Bellegarde, who came\r\nout with him to the top of the staircase. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, you have got your\r\npermit,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin. \xe2\x80\x9cI hope you liked the process.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like your sister, more than ever. But don\xe2\x80\x99t worry your brother any\r\nmore for my sake,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman added. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mind him. I am afraid he came\r\ndown on you in the smoking-room, after I went out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen my brother comes down on me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin, \xe2\x80\x9che falls hard. I\r\nhave a peculiar way of receiving him. I must say,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\r\nthey came up to the mark much sooner than I expected. I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nunderstand it, they must have had to turn the screw pretty tight. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\na tribute to your millions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\xe2\x80\x99s the most precious one they have ever received,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nNewman.\r\n\r\nHe was turning away when Valentin stopped him, looking at him with a\r\nbrilliant, softly-cynical glance. \xe2\x80\x9cI should like to know whether,\r\nwithin a few days, you have seen your venerable friend M.
