Nioche.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was yesterday at my rooms,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did he tell you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing particular.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou didn\xe2\x80\x99t see the muzzle of a pistol sticking out of his pocket?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you driving at?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman demanded. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought he seemed rather\r\ncheerful for him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nValentin broke into a laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cI am delighted to hear it! I win my bet.\r\nMademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie has thrown her cap over the mill, as we say. She\r\nhas left the paternal domicile. She is launched! And M. Nioche is\r\nrather cheerful\xe2\x80\x94_for him!_ Don\xe2\x80\x99t brandish your tomahawk at that rate; I\r\nhave not seen her nor communicated with her since that day at the\r\nLouvre. Andromeda has found another Perseus than I. My information is\r\nexact; on such matters it always is. I suppose that now you will raise\r\nyour protest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy protest be hanged!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Newman, disgustedly.\r\n\r\nBut his tone found no echo in that in which Valentin, with his hand on\r\nthe door, to return to his mother\xe2\x80\x99s apartment, exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cBut I shall\r\nsee her now! She is very remarkable\xe2\x80\x94she is very remarkable!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIII\r\n\r\n\r\nNewman kept his promise, or his menace, of going often to the Rue de\r\nl\xe2\x80\x99Universit\xc3\xa9, and during the next six weeks he saw Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\r\nmore times than he could have numbered. He flattered himself that he\r\nwas not in love, but his biographer may be supposed to know better. He\r\nclaimed, at least, none of the exemptions and emoluments of the\r\nromantic passion. Love, he believed, made a fool of a man, and his\r\npresent emotion was not folly but wisdom; wisdom sound, serene,\r\nwell-directed. What he felt was an intense, all-consuming tenderness,\r\nwhich had for its object an extraordinarily graceful and delicate, and\r\nat the same time impressive, woman who lived in a large gray house on\r\nthe left bank of the Seine. This tenderness turned very often into a\r\npositive heartache; a sign in which, certainly, Newman ought to have\r\nread the appellation which science has conferred upon his sentiment.\r\nWhen the heart has a heavy weight upon it, it hardly matters whether\r\nthe weight be of gold or of lead; when, at any rate, happiness passes\r\ninto that place in which it becomes identical with pain, a man may\r\nadmit that the reign of wisdom is temporarily suspended. Newman wished\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 so well that nothing he could think of doing for her\r\nin the future rose to the high standard which his present mood had set\r\nitself. She seemed to him so felicitous a product of nature and\r\ncircumstance that his invention, musing on future combinations, was\r\nconstantly catching its breath with the fear of stumbling into some\r\nbrutal compression or mutilation of her beautiful personal harmony.\r\nThis is what I mean by Newman\xe2\x80\x99s tenderness: Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 pleased\r\nhim so, exactly as she was, that his desire to interpose between her\r\nand the troubles of life had the quality of a young mother\xe2\x80\x99s eagerness\r\nto protect the sleep of her first-born child. Newman was simply\r\ncharmed, and he handled his charm as if it were a music-box which would\r\nstop if one shook it. There can be no better proof of the hankering\r\nepicure that is hidden in every man\xe2\x80\x99s temperament, waiting for a signal\r\nfrom some divine confederate that he may safely peep out. Newman at\r\nlast was enjoying, purely, freely, deeply. Certain of Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s personal qualities\xe2\x80\x94the luminous sweetness of her eyes, the\r\ndelicate mobility of her face, the deep liquidity of her voice\xe2\x80\x94filled\r\nall his consciousness. A rose-crowned Greek of old, gazing at a marble\r\ngoddess with his whole bright intellect resting satisfied in the act,\r\ncould not have been a more complete embodiment of the wisdom that loses\r\nitself in the enjoyment of quiet harmonies.\r\n\r\nHe made no violent love to her\xe2\x80\x94no sentimental speeches. He never\r\ntrespassed on what she had made him understand was for the present\r\nforbidden ground. But he had, nevertheless, a comfortable sense that\r\nshe knew better from day to day how much he admired her. Though in\r\ngeneral he was no great talker, he talked much, and he succeeded\r\nperfectly in making her say many things. He was not afraid of boring\r\nher, either by his discourse or by his silence; and whether or no he\r\ndid occasionally bore her, it is probable that on the whole she liked\r\nhim only the better for his absence of embarrassed scruples. Her\r\nvisitors, coming in often while Newman sat there, found a tall, lean,\r\nsilent man in a half-lounging attitude, who laughed out sometimes when\r\nno one had meant to be droll, and remained grave in the presence of\r\ncalculated witticisms, for appreciation of which he had apparently not\r\nthe proper culture.\r\n\r\nIt must be confessed that the number of subjects upon which Newman had\r\nno ideas was extremely large, and it must be added that as regards\r\nthose subjects upon which he was without ideas he was also perfectly\r\nwithout words. He had little of the small change of conversation, and\r\nhis stock of ready-made formulas and phrases was the scantiest. On the\r\nother hand he had plenty of attention to bestow, and his estimate of\r\nthe importance of a topic did not depend upon the number of clever\r\nthings he could say about it. He himself was almost never bored, and\r\nthere was no man with whom it would have been a greater mistake to\r\nsuppose that silence meant displeasure. What it was that entertained\r\nhim during some of his speechless sessions I must, however, confess\r\nmyself unable to determine. We know in a general way that a great many\r\nthings which were old stories to a great many people had the charm of\r\nnovelty to him, but a complete list of his new impressions would\r\nprobably contain a number of surprises for us. He told Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\r\na hundred long stories; he explained to her, in talking of the United\r\nStates, the working of various local institutions and mercantile\r\ncustoms. Judging by the sequel she was interested, but one would not\r\nhave been sure of it beforehand. As regards her own talk, Newman was\r\nvery sure himself that she herself enjoyed it: this was as a sort of\r\namendment to the portrait that Mrs. Tristram had drawn of her. He\r\ndiscovered that she had naturally an abundance of gaiety. He had been\r\nright at first in saying she was shy; her shyness, in a woman whose\r\ncircumstances and tranquil beauty afforded every facility for\r\nwell-mannered hardihood, was only a charm the more. For Newman it had\r\nlasted some time, and even when it went it left something behind it\r\nwhich for a while performed the same office. Was this the tearful\r\nsecret of which Mrs. Tristram had had a glimpse, and of which, as of\r\nher friend\xe2\x80\x99s reserve, her high-breeding, and her profundity, she had\r\ngiven a sketch of which the outlines were, perhaps, rather too heavy?\r\nNewman supposed so, but he found himself wondering less every day what\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s secrets might be, and more convinced that secrets\r\nwere, in themselves, hateful things to her. She was a woman for the\r\nlight, not for the shade; and her natural line was not picturesque\r\nreserve and mysterious melancholy, but frank, joyous, brilliant action,\r\nwith just so much meditation as was necessary, and not a grain more. To\r\nthis, apparently, he had succeeded in bringing her back. He felt,\r\nhimself, that he was an antidote to oppressive secrets; what he offered\r\nher was, in fact, above all things a vast, sunny immunity from the need\r\nof having any.\r\n\r\nHe often passed his evenings, when Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 had so appointed\r\nit, at the chilly fireside of Madame de Bellegarde, contenting himself\r\nwith looking across the room, through narrowed eyelids, at his\r\nmistress, who always made a point, before her family, of talking to\r\nsomeone else. Madame de Bellegarde sat by the fire conversing neatly\r\nand coldly with whomsoever approached her, and glancing round the room\r\nwith her slowly-restless eye, the effect of which, when it lighted upon\r\nhim, was to Newman\xe2\x80\x99s sense identical with that of a sudden spurt of\r\ndamp air. When he shook hands with her he always asked her with a laugh\r\nwhether she could \xe2\x80\x9cstand him\xe2\x80\x9d another evening, and she replied, without\r\na laugh, that thank God she had always been able to do her duty.\r\nNewman, talking once of the marquise to Mrs. Tristram, said that after\r\nall it was very easy to get on with her; it always was easy to get on\r\nwith out-and-out rascals.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd is it by that elegant term,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Tristram, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you\r\ndesignate the Marquise de Bellegarde?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cshe is wicked, she is an old sinner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is her crime?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs. Tristram.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t wonder if she had murdered someone\xe2\x80\x94all from a sense of\r\nduty, of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can you be so dreadful?\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Mrs. Tristram.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not dreadful. I am speaking of her favorably.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray what will you say when you want to be severe?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall keep my severity for someone else\xe2\x80\x94for the marquis. There\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nman I can\xe2\x80\x99t swallow, mix the drink as I will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what has _he_ done?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t quite make out; it is something dreadfully bad, something mean\r\nand underhand, and not redeemed by audacity, as his mother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmisdemeanors may have been. If he has never committed murder, he has at\r\nleast turned his back and looked the other way while someone else was\r\ncommitting it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn spite of this invidious hypothesis, which must be taken for nothing\r\nmore than an example of the capricious play of \xe2\x80\x9cAmerican humor,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman\r\ndid his best to maintain an easy and friendly style of communication\r\nwith M. de Bellegarde. So long as he was in personal contact with\r\npeople he disliked extremely to have anything to forgive them, and he\r\nwas capable of a good deal of unsuspected imaginative effort (for the\r\nsake of his own personal comfort) to assume for the time that they were\r\ngood fellows. He did his best to treat the marquis as one; he believed\r\nhonestly, moreover, that he could not, in reason, be such a confounded\r\nfool as he seemed. Newman\xe2\x80\x99s familiarity was never importunate; his\r\nsense of human equality was not an aggressive taste or an \xc3\xa6sthetic\r\ntheory, but something as natural and organic as a physical appetite\r\nwhich had never been put on a scanty allowance and consequently was\r\ninnocent of ungraceful eagerness. His tranquil unsuspectingness of the\r\nrelativity of his own place in the social scale was probably irritating\r\nto M. de Bellegarde, who saw himself reflected in the mind of his\r\npotential brother-in-law in a crude and colorless form, unpleasantly\r\ndissimilar to the impressive image projected upon his own intellectual\r\nmirror. He never forgot himself for an instant, and replied to what he\r\nmust have considered Newman\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cadvances\xe2\x80\x9d with mechanical politeness.\r\nNewman, who was constantly forgetting himself, and indulging in an\r\nunlimited amount of irresponsible inquiry and conjecture, now and then\r\nfound himself confronted by the conscious, ironical smile of his host.\r\nWhat the deuce M. de Bellegarde was smiling at he was at a loss to\r\ndivine. M. de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s smile may be supposed to have been, for\r\nhimself, a compromise between a great many emotions. So long as he\r\nsmiled he was polite, and it was proper he should be polite. A smile,\r\nmoreover, committed him to nothing more than politeness, and left the\r\ndegree of politeness agreeably vague. A smile, too, was neither\r\ndissent\xe2\x80\x94which was too serious\xe2\x80\x94nor agreement, which might have brought\r\non terrible complications. And then a smile covered his own personal\r\ndignity, which in this critical situation he was resolved to keep\r\nimmaculate; it was quite enough that the glory of his house should pass\r\ninto eclipse. Between him and Newman, his whole manner seemed to\r\ndeclare there could be no interchange of opinion; he was holding his\r\nbreath so as not to inhale the odor of democracy. Newman was far from\r\nbeing versed in European politics, but he liked to have a general idea\r\nof what was going on about him, and he accordingly asked M. de\r\nBellegarde several times what he thought of public affairs. M. de\r\nBellegarde answered with suave concision that he thought as ill of them\r\nas possible, that they were going from bad to worse, and that the age\r\nwas rotten to its core. This gave Newman, for the moment, an almost\r\nkindly feeling for the marquis; he pitied a man for whom the world was\r\nso cheerless a place, and the next time he saw M. de Bellegarde he\r\nattempted to call his attention to some of the brilliant features of\r\nthe time. The marquis presently replied that he had but a single\r\npolitical conviction, which was enough for him: he believed in the\r\ndivine right of Henry of Bourbon, Fifth of his name, to the throne of\r\nFrance. Newman stared, and after this he ceased to talk politics with\r\nM. de Bellegarde. He was not horrified nor scandalized, he was not even\r\namused; he felt as he should have felt if he had discovered in M. de\r\nBellegarde a taste for certain oddities of diet; an appetite, for\r\ninstance, for fishbones or nutshells. Under these circumstances, of\r\ncourse, he would never have broached dietary questions with him.\r\n\r\nOne afternoon, on his calling on Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, Newman was requested\r\nby the servant to wait a few moments, as his hostess was not at\r\nliberty. He walked about the room a while, taking up her books,\r\nsmelling her flowers, and looking at her prints and photographs (which\r\nhe thought prodigiously pretty), and at last he heard the opening of a\r\ndoor to which his back was turned. On the threshold stood an old woman\r\nwhom he remembered to have met several times in entering and leaving\r\nthe house. She was tall and straight and dressed in black, and she wore\r\na cap which, if Newman had been initiated into such mysteries, would\r\nhave been a sufficient assurance that she was not a Frenchwoman; a cap\r\nof pure British composition. She had a pale, decent, depressed-looking\r\nface, and a clear, dull, English eye. She looked at Newman a moment,\r\nboth intently and timidly, and then she dropped a short, straight\r\nEnglish curtsey.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 begs you will kindly wait,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has just\r\ncome in; she will soon have finished dressing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I will wait as long as she wants,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cPray tell her not\r\nto hurry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said the woman, softly; and then, instead of retiring\r\nwith her message, she advanced into the room. She looked about her for\r\na moment, and presently went to a table and began to arrange certain\r\nbooks and knick-knacks. Newman was struck with the high respectability\r\nof her appearance; he was afraid to address her as a servant. She\r\nbusied herself for some moments with putting the table in order and\r\npulling the curtains straight, while Newman walked slowly to and fro.\r\nHe perceived at last from her reflection in the mirror, as he was\r\npassing that her hands were idle and that she was looking at him\r\nintently. She evidently wished to say something, and Newman, perceiving\r\nit, helped her to begin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are English?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, please,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered, quickly and softly; \xe2\x80\x9cI was born in\r\nWiltshire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what do you think of Paris?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think of Paris, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d she said in the same tone. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is so\r\nlong since I have been here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you have been here very long?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is more than forty years, sir. I came over with Lady Emmeline.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean with old Madame de Bellegarde?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir. I came with her when she was married. I was my lady\xe2\x80\x99s own\r\nwoman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you have been with her ever since?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been in the house ever since. My lady has taken a younger\r\nperson. You see I am very old. I do nothing regular now. But I keep\r\nabout.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou look very strong and well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, observing the erectness\r\nof her figure, and a certain venerable rosiness in her cheek.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank God I am not ill, sir; I hope I know my duty too well to go\r\npanting and coughing about the house. But I am an old woman, sir, and\r\nit is as an old woman that I venture to speak to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, speak out,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, curiously. \xe2\x80\x9cYou needn\xe2\x80\x99t be afraid of me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir. I think you are kind. I have seen you before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the stairs, you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir. When you have been coming to see the countess. I have taken\r\nthe liberty of noticing that you come often.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes; I come very often,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cYou need not have\r\nbeen wide-awake to notice that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have noticed it with pleasure, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said the ancient tirewoman,\r\ngravely. And she stood looking at Newman with a strange expression of\r\nface. The old instinct of deference and humility was there; the habit\r\nof decent self-effacement and knowledge of her \xe2\x80\x9cown place.\xe2\x80\x9d But there\r\nmingled with it a certain mild audacity, born of the occasion and of a\r\nsense, probably, of Newman\xe2\x80\x99s unprecedented approachableness, and,\r\nbeyond this, a vague indifference to the old proprieties; as if my\r\nlady\xe2\x80\x99s own woman had at last begun to reflect that, since my lady had\r\ntaken another person, she had a slight reversionary property in\r\nherself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou take a great interest in the family?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA deep interest, sir. Especially in the countess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad of that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. And in a moment he added, smiling,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo do I!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo I suppose, sir. We can\xe2\x80\x99t help noticing these things and having our\r\nideas; can we, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean as a servant?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, there it is, sir. I am afraid that when I let my thoughts meddle\r\nwith such matters I am no longer a servant. But I am so devoted to the\r\ncountess; if she were my own child I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t love her more. That is\r\nhow I come to be so bold, sir. They say you want to marry her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman eyed his interlocutress and satisfied himself that she was not a\r\ngossip, but a zealot; she looked anxious, appealing, discreet. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is\r\nquite true,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI want to marry Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd to take her away to America?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will take her wherever she wants to go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe farther away the better, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the old woman, with\r\nsudden intensity. But she checked herself, and, taking up a\r\npaper-weight in mosaic, began to polish it with her black apron. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t mean anything against the house or the family, sir. But I think a\r\ngreat change would do the poor countess good. It is very sad here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it\xe2\x80\x99s not very lively,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cBut Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 is gay\r\nherself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is everything that is good. You will not be vexed to hear that she\r\nhas been gayer for a couple of months past than she had been in many a\r\nday before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman was delighted to gather this testimony to the prosperity of his\r\nsuit, but he repressed all violent marks of elation. \xe2\x80\x9cHas Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9 been in bad spirits before this?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor lady, she had good reason. M. de Cintr\xc3\xa9 was no husband for a\r\nsweet young lady like that. And then, as I say, it has been a sad\r\nhouse. It is better, in my humble opinion, that she were out of it. So,\r\nif you will excuse me for saying so, I hope she will marry you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope she will!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you must not lose courage, sir, if she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t make up her mind at\r\nonce. That is what I wanted to beg of you, sir. Don\xe2\x80\x99t give it up, sir.\r\nYou will not take it ill if I say it\xe2\x80\x99s a great risk for any lady at any\r\ntime; all the more when she has got rid of one bad bargain. But if she\r\ncan marry a good, kind, respectable gentleman, I think she had better\r\nmake up her mind to it. They speak very well of you, sir, in the house,\r\nand, if you will allow me to say so, I like your face. You have a very\r\ndifferent appearance from the late count, he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t five feet high. And\r\nthey say your fortune is beyond everything. There\xe2\x80\x99s no harm in that. So\r\nI beseech you to be patient, sir, and bide your time. If I don\xe2\x80\x99t say\r\nthis to you, sir, perhaps no one will. Of course it is not for me to\r\nmake any promises. I can answer for nothing. But I think your chance is\r\nnot so bad, sir. I am nothing but a weary old woman in my quiet corner,\r\nbut one woman understands another, and I think I make out the countess.\r\nI received her in my arms when she came into the world and her first\r\nwedding day was the saddest of my life. She owes it to me to show me\r\nanother and a brighter one. If you will hold firm, sir\xe2\x80\x94and you look as\r\nif you would\xe2\x80\x94I think we may see it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am much obliged to you for your encouragement,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman,\r\nheartily. \xe2\x80\x9cOne can\xe2\x80\x99t have too much. I mean to hold firm. And if Madame\r\nde Cintr\xc3\xa9 marries me you must come and live with her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old woman looked at him strangely, with her soft, lifeless eyes.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt may seem a heartless thing to say, sir, when one has been forty\r\nyears in a house, but I may tell you that I should like to leave this\r\nplace.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s just the time to say it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, fervently. \xe2\x80\x9cAfter\r\nforty years one wants a change.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very kind, sir;\xe2\x80\x9d and this faithful servant dropped another\r\ncurtsey and seemed disposed to retire. But she lingered a moment and\r\ngave a timid, joyless smile. Newman was disappointed, and his fingers\r\nstole half shyly half irritably into his waistcoat-pocket. His\r\ninformant noticed the movement. \xe2\x80\x9cThank God I am not a Frenchwoman,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I were, I would tell you with a brazen simper, old as I am,\r\nthat if you please, monsieur, my information is worth something. Let me\r\ntell you so in my own decent English way. It _is_ worth something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much, please?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSimply this: a promise not to hint to the countess that I have said\r\nthese things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf that is all, you have it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is all, sir. Thank you, sir. Good day, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d And having once more\r\nslid down telescope-wise into her scanty petticoats, the old woman\r\ndeparted. At the same moment Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 came in by an opposite\r\ndoor. She noticed the movement of the other _porti\xc3\xa8re_ and asked Newman\r\nwho had been entertaining him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe British female!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cAn old lady in a black dress and a\r\ncap, who curtsies up and down, and expresses herself ever so well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn old lady who curtsies and expresses herself?... Ah, you mean poor\r\nMrs. Bread. I happen to know that you have made a conquest of her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Cake, she ought to be called,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is very sweet.\r\nShe is a delicious old woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 looked at him a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat can she have said to\r\nyou? She is an excellent creature, but we think her rather dismal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman answered presently, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I like her because she\r\nhas lived near you so long. Since your birth, she told me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, simply; \xe2\x80\x9cshe is very faithful; I can\r\ntrust her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman had never made any reflections to this lady upon her mother and\r\nher brother Urbain; had given no hint of the impression they made upon\r\nhim. But, as if she had guessed his thoughts, she seemed careful to\r\navoid all occasion for making him speak of them. She never alluded to\r\nher mother\xe2\x80\x99s domestic decrees; she never quoted the opinions of the\r\nmarquis. They had talked, however, of Valentin, and she had made no\r\nsecret of her extreme affection for her younger brother. Newman\r\nlistened sometimes with a certain harmless jealousy; he would have\r\nliked to divert some of her tender allusions to his own credit. Once\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 told him with a little air of triumph about something\r\nthat Valentin had done which she thought very much to his honor. It was\r\na service he had rendered to an old friend of the family; something\r\nmore \xe2\x80\x9cserious\xe2\x80\x9d than Valentin was usually supposed capable of being.\r\nNewman said he was glad to hear of it, and then began to talk about\r\nsomething which lay upon his own heart. Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 listened, but\r\nafter a while she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like the way you speak of my brother\r\nValentin.\xe2\x80\x9d Hereupon Newman, surprised, said that he had never spoken of\r\nhim but kindly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is too kindly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is a kindness that costs\r\nnothing; it is the kindness you show to a child. It is as if you didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nrespect him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRespect him? Why I think I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou think? If you are not sure, it is no respect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you respect him?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you do, I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf one loves a person, that is a question one is not bound to answer,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should not have asked it of me, then. I am very fond of your\r\nbrother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe amuses you. But you would not like to resemble him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like to resemble anyone. It is hard enough work resembling\r\none\xe2\x80\x99s self.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean,\xe2\x80\x9d asked Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, \xe2\x80\x9cby resembling one\xe2\x80\x99s self?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, doing what is expected of one. Doing one\xe2\x80\x99s duty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that is only when one is very good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, a great many people are good,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cValentin is quite\r\ngood enough for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 was silent for a short time. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is not good enough\r\nfor me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said at last. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish he would do something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat can he do?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing. Yet he is very clever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a proof of cleverness,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cto be happy without doing\r\nanything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think Valentin is happy, in reality. He is clever, generous,\r\nbrave; but what is there to show for it? To me there is something sad\r\nin his life, and sometimes I have a sort of foreboding about him. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know why, but I fancy he will have some great trouble\xe2\x80\x94perhaps an\r\nunhappy end.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, leave him to me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, jovially. \xe2\x80\x9cI will watch over him\r\nand keep harm away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOne evening, in Madame de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s salon, the conversation had\r\nflagged most sensibly. The marquis walked up and down in silence, like\r\na sentinel at the door of some smooth-fronted citadel of the\r\nproprieties; his mother sat staring at the fire; young Madame de\r\nBellegarde worked at an enormous band of tapestry. Usually there were\r\nthree or four visitors, but on this occasion a violent storm\r\nsufficiently accounted for the absence of even the most devoted\r\nhabitu\xc3\xa9s. In the long silences the howling of the wind and the beating\r\nof the rain were distinctly audible. Newman sat perfectly still,\r\nwatching the clock, determined to stay till the stroke of eleven, but\r\nnot a moment longer. Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 had turned her back to the\r\ncircle, and had been standing for some time within the uplifted curtain\r\nof a window, with her forehead against the pane, gazing out into the\r\ndeluged darkness. Suddenly she turned round toward her sister-in-law.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s sake,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with peculiar eagerness, \xe2\x80\x9cgo to the\r\npiano and play something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Bellegarde held up her tapestry and pointed to a little white\r\nflower. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t ask me to leave this. I am in the midst of a\r\nmasterpiece. My flower is going to smell very sweet; I am putting in\r\nthe smell with this gold-colored silk. I am holding my breath; I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nleave off. Play something yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is absurd for me to play when you are present,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9. But the next moment she went to the piano and began to strike\r\nthe keys with vehemence. She played for some time, rapidly and\r\nbrilliantly; when she stopped, Newman went to the piano and asked her\r\nto begin again. She shook her head, and, on his insisting, she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nhave not been playing for you; I have been playing for myself.\xe2\x80\x9d She\r\nwent back to the window again and looked out, and shortly afterwards\r\nleft the room. When Newman took leave, Urbain de Bellegarde accompanied\r\nhim, as he always did, just three steps down the staircase. At the\r\nbottom stood a servant with his overcoat. He had just put it on when he\r\nsaw Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 coming towards him across the vestibule.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall you be at home on Friday?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman asked.\r\n\r\nShe looked at him a moment before answering his question. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlike my mother and my brother,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nHe hesitated a moment, and then he said softly, \xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe laid her hand on the balustrade and prepared to ascend the stairs,\r\nfixing her eyes on the first step.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I shall be at home on Friday,\xe2\x80\x9d and she passed up the wide dusky\r\nstaircase.\r\n\r\nOn the Friday, as soon as he came in, she asked him to please to tell\r\nher why he disliked her family.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDislike your family?\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cThat has a horrid sound. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsay so, did I? I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean it, if I did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish you would tell me what you think of them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think of any of them but you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is because you dislike them. Speak the truth; you can\xe2\x80\x99t offend\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t exactly love your brother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI remember\r\nnow. But what is the use of my saying so? I had forgotten it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are too good-natured,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 gravely. Then, as if\r\nto avoid the appearance of inviting him to speak ill of the marquis,\r\nshe turned away, motioning him to sit down.\r\n\r\nBut he remained standing before her and said presently, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is of\r\nmuch more importance is that they don\xe2\x80\x99t like me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94they don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd don\xe2\x80\x99t you think they are wrong?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman asked. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe I\r\nam a man to dislike.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose that a man who may be liked may also be disliked. And my\r\nbrother\xe2\x80\x94my mother,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, \xe2\x80\x9chave not made you angry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sometimes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have never shown it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo much the better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, so much the better. They think they have treated you very well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no doubt they might have handled me much more roughly,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nNewman. \xe2\x80\x9cI am much obliged to them. Honestly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are generous,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a disagreeable\r\nposition.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor them, you mean. Not for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot when their sins are forgiven!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cThey don\xe2\x80\x99t think I am\r\nas good as they are. I do. But we shan\xe2\x80\x99t quarrel about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t even agree with you without saying something that has a\r\ndisagreeable sound. The presumption was against you. That you probably\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman sat down and looked at her for some time. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I\r\nreally understand it. But when you say it, I believe it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a poor reason,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it\xe2\x80\x99s a very good one. You have a high spirit, a high standard; but\r\nwith you it\xe2\x80\x99s all natural and unaffected; you don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to have stuck\r\nyour head into a vise, as if you were sitting for the photograph of\r\npropriety. You think of me as a fellow who has had no idea in life but\r\nto make money and drive sharp bargains. That\xe2\x80\x99s a fair description of\r\nme, but it is not the whole story. A man ought to care for something\r\nelse, though I don\xe2\x80\x99t know exactly what. I cared for money-making, but I\r\nnever cared particularly for the money. There was nothing else to do,\r\nand it was impossible to be idle. I have been very easy to others, and\r\nto myself. I have done most of the things that people asked me\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmean rascals. As regards your mother and your brother,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman added,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthere is only one point upon which I feel that I might quarrel with\r\nthem. I don\xe2\x80\x99t ask them to sing my praises to you, but I ask them to let\r\nyou alone. If I thought they talked ill of me to you, I should come\r\ndown upon them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have let me alone, as you say. They have not talked ill of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn that case,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cI declare they are only too good for\r\nthis world!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 appeared to find something startling in his\r\nexclamation. She would, perhaps, have replied, but at this moment the\r\ndoor was thrown open and Urbain de Bellegarde stepped across the\r\nthreshold. He appeared surprised at finding Newman, but his surprise\r\nwas but a momentary shadow across the surface of an unwonted joviality.\r\nNewman had never seen the marquis so exhilarated; his pale, unlighted\r\ncountenance had a sort of thin transfiguration. He held open the door\r\nfor someone else to enter, and presently appeared old Madame de\r\nBellegarde, leaning on the arm of a gentleman whom Newman had not seen\r\nbefore. He had already risen, and Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 rose, as she always\r\ndid before her mother. The marquis, who had greeted Newman almost\r\ngenially, stood apart, slowly rubbing his hands. His mother came\r\nforward with her companion. She gave a majestic little nod at Newman,\r\nand then she released the strange gentleman, that he might make his bow\r\nto her daughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy daughter,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI have brought you an unknown relative, Lord\r\nDeepmere. Lord Deepmere is our cousin, but he has done only to-day what\r\nhe ought to have done long ago\xe2\x80\x94come to make our acquaintance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 smiled, and offered Lord Deepmere her hand. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is\r\nvery extraordinary,\xe2\x80\x9d said this noble laggard, \xe2\x80\x9cbut this is the first\r\ntime that I have ever been in Paris for more than three or four weeks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how long have you been here now?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, for the last two months,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lord Deepmere.\r\n\r\nThese two remarks might have constituted an impertinence; but a glance\r\nat Lord Deepmere\xe2\x80\x99s face would have satisfied you, as it apparently\r\nsatisfied Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, that they constituted only a _na\xc3\xafvet\xc3\xa9_.\r\nWhen his companions were seated, Newman, who was out of the\r\nconversation, occupied himself with observing the newcomer.\r\nObservation, however, as regards Lord Deepmere\xe2\x80\x99s person; had no great\r\nrange. He was a small, meagre man, of some three and thirty years of\r\nage, with a bald head, a short nose and no front teeth in the upper\r\njaw; he had round, candid blue eyes, and several pimples on his chin.\r\nHe was evidently very shy, and he laughed a great deal, catching his\r\nbreath with an odd, startling sound, as the most convenient imitation\r\nof repose. His physiognomy denoted great simplicity, a certain amount\r\nof brutality, and probable failure in the past to profit by rare\r\neducational advantages. He remarked that Paris was awfully jolly, but\r\nthat for real, thorough-paced entertainment it was nothing to Dublin.\r\nHe even preferred Dublin to London. Had Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 ever been to\r\nDublin? They must all come over there some day, and he would show them\r\nsome Irish sport. He always went to Ireland for the fishing, and he\r\ncame to Paris for the new Offenbach things. They always brought them\r\nout in Dublin, but he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t wait. He had been nine times to hear La\r\nPomme de Paris. Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, leaning back, with her arms folded,\r\nlooked at Lord Deepmere with a more visibly puzzled face than she\r\nusually showed to society. Madame de Bellegarde, on the other hand,\r\nwore a fixed smile. The marquis said that among light operas his\r\nfavorite was the Gazza Ladra. The marquise then began a series of\r\ninquiries about the duke and the cardinal, the old countess and Lady\r\nBarbara, after listening to which, and to Lord Deepmere\xe2\x80\x99s somewhat\r\nirreverent responses, for a quarter of an hour, Newman rose to take his\r\nleave. The marquis went with him three steps into the hall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs he Irish?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman, nodding in the direction of the visitor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis mother was the daughter of Lord Finucane,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquis; \xe2\x80\x9che\r\nhas great Irish estates. Lady Bridget, in the complete absence of male\r\nheirs, either direct or collateral\xe2\x80\x94a most extraordinary\r\ncircumstance\xe2\x80\x94came in for everything. But Lord Deepmere\xe2\x80\x99s title is\r\nEnglish and his English property is immense. He is a charming young\r\nman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman answered nothing, but he detained the marquis as the latter was\r\nbeginning gracefully to recede. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is a good time for me to thank\r\nyou,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cfor sticking so punctiliously to our bargain, for doing\r\nso much to help me on with your sister.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe marquis stared. \xe2\x80\x9cReally, I have done nothing that I can boast of,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh don\xe2\x80\x99t be modest,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman answered, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t flatter\r\nmyself that I am doing so well simply by my own merit. And thank your\r\nmother for me, too!\xe2\x80\x9d And he turned away, leaving M. de Bellegarde\r\nlooking after him.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIV\r\n\r\n\r\nThe next time Newman came to the Rue de l\xe2\x80\x99Universit\xc3\xa9 he had the good\r\nfortune to find Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 alone. He had come with a definite\r\nintention, and he lost no time in executing it. She wore, moreover, a\r\nlook which he eagerly interpreted as expectancy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been coming to see you for six months, now,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cand I\r\nhave never spoken to you a second time of marriage. That was what you\r\nasked me; I obeyed. Could any man have done better?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have acted with great delicacy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99m going to change now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mean that I am\r\ngoing to be indelicate; but I\xe2\x80\x99m going to go back to where I began. I\r\n_am_ back there. I have been all round the circle. Or rather, I have\r\nnever been away from here. I have never ceased to want what I wanted\r\nthen. Only now I am more sure of it, if possible; I am more sure of\r\nmyself, and more sure of you. I know you better, though I don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nanything I didn\xe2\x80\x99t believe three months ago. You are everything\xe2\x80\x94you are\r\nbeyond everything\xe2\x80\x94I can imagine or desire. You know me now; you _must_\r\nknow me. I won\xe2\x80\x99t say that you have seen the best\xe2\x80\x94but you have seen the\r\nworst. I hope you have been thinking all this while. You must have seen\r\nthat I was only waiting; you can\xe2\x80\x99t suppose that I was changing. What\r\nwill you say to me, now? Say that everything is clear and reasonable,\r\nand that I have been very patient and considerate, and deserve my\r\nreward. And then give me your hand. Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 do that. Do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew you were only waiting,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cand I was very sure this day\r\nwould come. I have thought about it a great deal. At first I was half\r\nafraid of it. But I am not afraid of it now.\xe2\x80\x9d She paused a moment, and\r\nthen she added, \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a relief.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was sitting on a low chair, and Newman was on an ottoman, near her.\r\nHe leaned a little and took her hand, which for an instant she let him\r\nkeep. \xe2\x80\x9cThat means that I have not waited for nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. She\r\nlooked at him for a moment, and he saw her eyes fill with tears. \xe2\x80\x9cWith\r\nme,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cyou will be as safe\xe2\x80\x94as safe\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and even in his ardor he\r\nhesitated a moment for a comparison\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cas safe,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with a kind of\r\nsimple solemnity, \xe2\x80\x9cas in your father\xe2\x80\x99s arms.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStill she looked at him and her tears increased. Then, abruptly, she\r\nburied her face on the cushioned arm of the sofa beside her chair, and\r\nbroke into noiseless sobs. \xe2\x80\x9cI am weak\xe2\x80\x94I am weak,\xe2\x80\x9d he heard her say.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll the more reason why you should give yourself up to me,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nanswered. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy are you troubled? There is nothing but happiness. Is\r\nthat so hard to believe?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo you everything seems so simple,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, raising her head. \xe2\x80\x9cBut\r\nthings are not so. I like you extremely. I liked you six months ago,\r\nand now I am sure of it, as you say you are sure. But it is not easy,\r\nsimply for that, to decide to marry you. There are a great many things\r\nto think about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere ought to be only one thing to think about\xe2\x80\x94that we love each\r\nother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. And as she remained silent he quickly added, \xe2\x80\x9cVery\r\ngood, if you can\xe2\x80\x99t accept that, don\xe2\x80\x99t tell me so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should be very glad to think of nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d she said at last; \xe2\x80\x9cnot to\r\nthink at all; only to shut both my eyes and give myself up. But I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t. I\xe2\x80\x99m cold, I\xe2\x80\x99m old, I\xe2\x80\x99m a coward; I never supposed I should marry\r\nagain, and it seems to me very strange I should ever have listened to\r\nyou. When I used to think, as a girl, of what I should do if I were to\r\nmarry freely, by my own choice, I thought of a very different man from\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s nothing against me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman with an immense smile; \xe2\x80\x9cyour\r\ntaste was not formed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis smile made Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 smile. \xe2\x80\x9cHave you formed it?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\nAnd then she said, in a different tone, \xe2\x80\x9cWhere do you wish to live?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnywhere in the wide world you like. We can easily settle that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know why I ask you,\xe2\x80\x9d she presently continued. \xe2\x80\x9cI care very\r\nlittle. I think if I were to marry you I could live almost anywhere.\r\nYou have some false ideas about me; you think that I need a great many\r\nthings\xe2\x80\x94that I must have a brilliant, worldly life. I am sure you are\r\nprepared to take a great deal of trouble to give me such things. But\r\nthat is very arbitrary; I have done nothing to prove that.\xe2\x80\x9d She paused\r\nagain, looking at him, and her mingled sound and silence were so sweet\r\nto him that he had no wish to hurry her, any more than he would have\r\nhad a wish to hurry a golden sunrise. \xe2\x80\x9cYour being so different, which\r\nat first seemed a difficulty, a trouble, began one day to seem to me a\r\npleasure, a great pleasure. I was glad you were different. And yet if I\r\nhad said so, no one would have understood me; I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean simply to my\r\nfamily.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey would have said I was a queer monster, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey would have said I could never be happy with you\xe2\x80\x94you were too\r\ndifferent; and I would have said it was just _because_ you were so\r\ndifferent that I might be happy. But they would have given better\r\nreasons than I. My only reason\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and she paused again.\r\n\r\nBut this time, in the midst of his golden sunrise, Newman felt the\r\nimpulse to grasp at a rosy cloud. \xe2\x80\x9cYour only reason is that you love\r\nme!\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured with an eloquent gesture, and for want of a better\r\nreason Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 reconciled herself to this one.\r\n\r\nNewman came back the next day, and in the vestibule, as he entered the\r\nhouse, he encountered his friend Mrs. Bread. She was wandering about in\r\nhonorable idleness, and when his eyes fell upon her she delivered him\r\none of her curtsies. Then turning to the servant who had admitted him,\r\nshe said, with the combined majesty of her native superiority and of a\r\nrugged English accent, \xe2\x80\x9cYou may retire; I will have the honor of\r\nconducting monsieur.\xe2\x80\x9d In spite of this combination, however, it\r\nappeared to Newman that her voice had a slight quaver, as if the tone\r\nof command were not habitual to it. The man gave her an impertinent\r\nstare, but he walked slowly away, and she led Newman upstairs. At half\r\nits course the staircase gave a bend, forming a little platform. In the\r\nangle of the wall stood an indifferent statue of an eighteenth-century\r\nnymph, simpering, sallow, and cracked. Here Mrs. Bread stopped and\r\nlooked with shy kindness at her companion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know the good news, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have a good right to be first to know it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have\r\ntaken such a friendly interest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread turned away and began to blow the dust off the statue, as if\r\nthis might be mockery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you want to congratulate me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI am greatly\r\nobliged.\xe2\x80\x9d And then he added, \xe2\x80\x9cYou gave me much pleasure the other day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe turned around, apparently reassured. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are not to think that I\r\nhave been told anything,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cI have only guessed. But when I\r\nlooked at you, as you came in, I was sure I had guessed aright.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very sharp,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI am sure that in your quiet way\r\nyou see everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not a fool, sir, thank God. I have guessed something else\r\nbeside,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI needn\xe2\x80\x99t tell you that, sir; I don\xe2\x80\x99t think you would believe it. At\r\nany rate it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t please you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, tell me nothing but what will please me,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is\r\nthe way you began.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, sir, I suppose you won\xe2\x80\x99t be vexed to hear that the sooner\r\neverything is over the better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe sooner we are married, you mean? The better for me, certainly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe better for everyone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe better for you, perhaps. You know you are coming to live with us,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m extremely obliged to you, sir, but it is not of myself I was\r\nthinking. I only wanted, if I might take the liberty, to recommend you\r\nto lose no time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhom are you afraid of?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread looked up the staircase and then down and then she looked at\r\nthe undusted nymph, as if she possibly had sentient ears. \xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid\r\nof everyone,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat an uncomfortable state of mind!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cDoes \xe2\x80\x98everyone\xe2\x80\x99\r\nwish to prevent my marriage?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid of already having said too much,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Bread replied. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t take it back, but I won\xe2\x80\x99t say any more.\xe2\x80\x9d And she took her way up\r\nthe staircase again and led him into Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s salon.\r\n\r\nNewman indulged in a brief and silent imprecation when he found that\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 was not alone. With her sat her mother, and in the\r\nmiddle of the room stood young Madame de Bellegarde, in her bonnet and\r\nmantle. The old marquise, who was leaning back in her chair with a hand\r\nclasping the knob of each arm, looked at him fixedly without moving.\r\nShe seemed barely conscious of his greeting; she appeared to be musing\r\nintently. Newman said to himself that her daughter had been announcing\r\nher engagement and that the old lady found the morsel hard to swallow.\r\nBut Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, as she gave him her hand gave him also a look by\r\nwhich she appeared to mean that he should understand something. Was it\r\na warning or a request? Did she wish to enjoin speech or silence? He\r\nwas puzzled, and young Madame de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s pretty grin gave him no\r\ninformation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not told my mother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 abruptly, looking at\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTold me what?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded the marquise. \xe2\x80\x9cYou tell me too little; you\r\nshould tell me everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is what I do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame Urbain, with a little laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet _me_ tell your mother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\nThe old lady stared at him again, and then turned to her daughter. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nare going to marry him?\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, softly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Oui, ma m\xc3\xa8re_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour daughter has consented, to my great happiness,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd when was this arrangement made?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Madame de Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nseem to be picking up the news by chance!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy suspense came to an end yesterday,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how long was mine to have lasted?\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquise to her\r\ndaughter. She spoke without irritation; with a sort of cold, noble\r\ndispleasure.\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 stood silent, with her eyes on the ground. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is over\r\nnow,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is my son\xe2\x80\x94where is Urbain?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the marquise. \xe2\x80\x9cSend for your\r\nbrother and inform him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nYoung Madame de Bellegarde laid her hand on the bell-rope. \xe2\x80\x9cHe was to\r\nmake some visits with me, and I was to go and knock\xe2\x80\x94very softly, very\r\nsoftly\xe2\x80\x94at the door of his study. But he can come to me!\xe2\x80\x9d She pulled the\r\nbell, and in a few moments Mrs. Bread appeared, with a face of calm\r\ninquiry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSend for your brother,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old lady.\r\n\r\nBut Newman felt an irresistible impulse to speak, and to speak in a\r\ncertain way. \xe2\x80\x9cTell the marquis we want him,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to Mrs. Bread, who\r\nquietly retired.\r\n\r\nYoung Madame de Bellegarde went to her sister-in-law and embraced her.\r\nThen she turned to Newman, with an intense smile. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is charming. I\r\ncongratulate you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI congratulate you, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde, with extreme\r\nsolemnity. \xe2\x80\x9cMy daughter is an extraordinarily good woman. She may have\r\nfaults, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t know them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy mother does not often make jokes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9; \xe2\x80\x9cbut when\r\nshe does they are terrible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is ravishing,\xe2\x80\x9d the Marquise Urbain resumed, looking at her\r\nsister-in-law, with her head on one side. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, I congratulate you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 turned away, and, taking up a piece of tapestry, began\r\nto ply the needle. Some minutes of silence elapsed, which were\r\ninterrupted by the arrival of M. de Bellegarde. He came in with his hat\r\nin his hand, gloved, and was followed by his brother Valentin, who\r\nappeared to have just entered the house. M. de Bellegarde looked around\r\nthe circle and greeted Newman with his usual finely-measured courtesy.\r\nValentin saluted his mother and his sisters, and, as he shook hands\r\nwith Newman, gave him a glance of acute interrogation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Arrivez donc, messieurs!_\xe2\x80\x9d cried young Madame de Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have\r\ngreat news for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeak to your brother, my daughter,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old lady.\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 had been looking at her tapestry. She raised her eyes\r\nto her brother. \xe2\x80\x9cI have accepted Mr. Newman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour sister has consented,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cYou see after all, I knew\r\nwhat I was about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am charmed!\xe2\x80\x9d said M. de Bellegarde, with superior benignity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo am I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin to Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cThe marquis and I are charmed. I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t marry, myself, but I can understand it. I can\xe2\x80\x99t stand on my head,\r\nbut I can applaud a clever acrobat. My dear sister, I bless your\r\nunion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe marquis stood looking for a while into the crown of his hat. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\r\nhave been prepared,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last \xe2\x80\x9cbut it is inevitable that in face\r\nof the event one should experience a certain emotion.\xe2\x80\x9d And he gave a\r\nmost unhilarious smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI feel no emotion that I was not perfectly prepared for,\xe2\x80\x9d said his\r\nmother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t say that for myself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, smiling but differently\r\nfrom the marquis. \xe2\x80\x9cI am happier than I expected to be. I suppose it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthe sight of your happiness!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t exaggerate that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde, getting up and\r\nlaying her hand upon her daughter\xe2\x80\x99s arm. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t expect an honest\r\nold woman to thank you for taking away her beautiful, only daughter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou forgot me, dear madame,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young marquise demurely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, she is very beautiful,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd when is the wedding, pray?\xe2\x80\x9d asked young Madame de Bellegarde; \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nmust have a month to think over a dress.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat must be discussed,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, we will discuss it, and let you know!\xe2\x80\x9d Newman exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no doubt we shall agree,\xe2\x80\x9d said Urbain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you don\xe2\x80\x99t agree with Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, you will be very\r\nunreasonable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, come, Urbain,\xe2\x80\x9d said young Madame de Bellegarde, \xe2\x80\x9cI must go\r\nstraight to my tailor\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old lady had been standing with her hand on her daughter\xe2\x80\x99s arm,\r\nlooking at her fixedly. She gave a little sigh, and murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\r\ndid _not_ expect it! You are a fortunate man,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, turning to\r\nNewman, with an expressive nod.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I know that!\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI feel tremendously proud. I feel like\r\ncrying it on the housetops,\xe2\x80\x94like stopping people in the street to tell\r\nthem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Bellegarde narrowed her lips. \xe2\x80\x9cPray don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe more people that know it, the better,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman declared. \xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyet announced it here, but I telegraphed it this morning to America.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTelegraphed it to America?\xe2\x80\x9d the old lady murmured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo New York, to St. Louis, and to San Francisco; those are the\r\nprincipal cities, you know. To-morrow I shall tell my friends here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you many?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Madame de Bellegarde, in a tone of which I am\r\nafraid that Newman but partly measured the impertinence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEnough to bring me a great many hand-shakes and congratulations. To\r\nsay nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, in a moment, \xe2\x80\x9cof those I shall receive from\r\nyour friends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey will not use the telegraph,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquise, taking her\r\ndeparture.\r\n\r\nM. de Bellegarde, whose wife, her imagination having apparently taken\r\nflight to the tailor\xe2\x80\x99s, was fluttering her silken wings in emulation,\r\nshook hands with Newman, and said with a more persuasive accent than\r\nthe latter had ever heard him use, \xe2\x80\x9cYou may count upon me.\xe2\x80\x9d Then his\r\nwife led him away.\r\n\r\nValentin stood looking from his sister to our hero. \xe2\x80\x9cI hope you both\r\nreflected seriously,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 smiled. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have neither your powers of reflection nor\r\nyour depth of seriousness; but we have done our best.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I have a great regard for each of you,\xe2\x80\x9d Valentin continued. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nare charming young people. But I am not satisfied, on the whole, that\r\nyou belong to that small and superior class\xe2\x80\x94that exquisite group\r\ncomposed of persons who are worthy to remain unmarried. These are rare\r\nsouls; they are the salt of the earth. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean to be\r\ninvidious; the marrying people are often very nice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cValentin holds that women should marry, and that men should not,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know how he arranges it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI arrange it by adoring you, my sister,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin ardently.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAdore someone whom you can marry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI will arrange that\r\nfor you some day. I foresee that I am going to turn apostle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nValentin was on the threshold; he looked back a moment with a face that\r\nhad turned grave. \xe2\x80\x9cI adore someone I can\xe2\x80\x99t marry!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. And he\r\ndropped the _porti\xc3\xa8re_ and departed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey don\xe2\x80\x99t like it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, standing alone before Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, after a moment; \xe2\x80\x9cthey don\xe2\x80\x99t like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now, do you mind that?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, after another interval.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a mistake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t help it. I should prefer that my mother were pleased.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy the deuce,\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cis she not pleased? She gave you\r\nleave to marry me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery true; I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand it. And yet I do \xe2\x80\x98mind it,\xe2\x80\x99 as you say.\r\nYou will call it superstitious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat will depend upon how much you let it bother you. Then I shall\r\ncall it an awful bore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will keep it to myself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, \xe2\x80\x9cIt shall not bother\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d And then they talked of their marriage-day, and Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\r\nassented unreservedly to Newman\xe2\x80\x99s desire to have it fixed for an early\r\ndate.\r\n\r\nNewman\xe2\x80\x99s telegrams were answered with interest. Having dispatched but\r\nthree electric missives, he received no less than eight gratulatory\r\nbulletins in return. He put them into his pocket-book, and the next\r\ntime he encountered old Madame de Bellegarde drew them forth and\r\ndisplayed them to her. This, it must be confessed, was a slightly\r\nmalicious stroke; the reader must judge in what degree the offense was\r\nvenial. Newman knew that the marquise disliked his telegrams, though he\r\ncould see no sufficient reason for it. Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, on the other\r\nhand, liked them, and, most of them being of a humorous cast, laughed\r\nat them immoderately, and inquired into the character of their authors.\r\nNewman, now that his prize was gained, felt a peculiar desire that his\r\ntriumph should be manifest. He more than suspected that the Bellegardes\r\nwere keeping quiet about it, and allowing it, in their select circle,\r\nbut a limited resonance; and it pleased him to think that if he were to\r\ntake the trouble he might, as he phrased it, break all the windows. No\r\nman likes being repudiated, and yet Newman, if he was not flattered,\r\nwas not exactly offended. He had not this good excuse for his somewhat\r\naggressive impulse to promulgate his felicity; his sentiment was of\r\nanother quality. He wanted for once to make the heads of the house of\r\nBellegarde _feel_ him; he knew not when he should have another chance.\r\nHe had had for the past six months a sense of the old lady and her son\r\nlooking straight over his head, and he was now resolved that they\r\nshould toe a mark which he would give himself the satisfaction of\r\ndrawing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is like seeing a bottle emptied when the wine is poured too\r\nslowly,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to Mrs. Tristram. \xe2\x80\x9cThey make me want to joggle their\r\nelbows and force them to spill their wine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo this Mrs. Tristram answered that he had better leave them alone and\r\nlet them do things in their own way. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must make allowances for\r\nthem,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is natural enough that they should hang fire a\r\nlittle. They thought they accepted you when you made your application;\r\nbut they are not people of imagination, they could not project\r\nthemselves into the future, and now they will have to begin again. But\r\nthey _are_ people of honor, and they will do whatever is necessary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman spent a few moments in narrow-eyed meditation. \xe2\x80\x9cI am not hard on\r\nthem,\xe2\x80\x9d he presently said, \xe2\x80\x9cand to prove it I will invite them all to a\r\nfestival.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo a festival?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have been laughing at my great gilded rooms all winter; I will\r\nshow you that they are good for something. I will give a party. What is\r\nthe grandest thing one can do here? I will hire all the great singers\r\nfrom the opera, and all the first people from the Th\xc3\xa9\xc3\xa2tre Fran\xc3\xa7ais, and\r\nI will give an entertainment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd whom will you invite?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou, first of all. And then the old lady and her son. And then\r\neveryone among her friends whom I have met at her house or elsewhere,\r\neveryone who has shown me the minimum of politeness, every duke of them\r\nand his wife. And then all my friends, without exception: Miss Kitty\r\nUpjohn, Miss Dora Finch, General Packard, C. P Hatch, and all the rest.\r\nAnd everyone shall know what it is about, that is, to celebrate my\r\nengagement to the Countess de Cintr\xc3\xa9. What do you think of the idea?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think it is odious!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Tristram. And then in a moment: \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nthink it is delicious!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe very next evening Newman repaired to Madame de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s salon,\r\nwhere he found her surrounded by her children, and invited her to honor\r\nhis poor dwelling by her presence on a certain evening a fortnight\r\ndistant.\r\n\r\nThe marquise stared a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear sir,\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat do you\r\nwant to do to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo make you acquainted with a few people, and then to place you in a\r\nvery easy chair and ask you to listen to Madame Frezzolini\xe2\x80\x99s singing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean to give a concert?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething of that sort.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd to have a crowd of people?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll my friends, and I hope some of yours and your daughter\xe2\x80\x99s. I want\r\nto celebrate my engagement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt seemed to Newman that Madame de Bellegarde turned pale. She opened\r\nher fan, a fine old painted fan of the last century, and looked at the\r\npicture, which represented a _f\xc3\xaate champ\xc3\xaatre_\xe2\x80\x94a lady with a guitar,\r\nsinging, and a group of dancers round a garlanded Hermes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe go out so little,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the marquis, \xe2\x80\x9csince my poor father\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndeath.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut _my_ dear father is still alive, my friend,\xe2\x80\x9d said his wife. \xe2\x80\x9cI am\r\nonly waiting for my invitation to accept it,\xe2\x80\x9d and she glanced with\r\namiable confidence at Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cIt will be magnificent; I am very sure\r\nof that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI am sorry to say, to the discredit of Newman\xe2\x80\x99s gallantry, that this\r\nlady\xe2\x80\x99s invitation was not then and there bestowed; he was giving all\r\nhis attention to the old marquise. She looked up at last, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t think of letting you offer me a f\xc3\xaate,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cuntil I have\r\noffered you one. We want to present you to our friends; we will invite\r\nthem all. We have it very much at heart. We must do things in order.\r\nCome to me about the 25th; I will let you know the exact day\r\nimmediately. We shall not have anyone so fine as Madame Frezzolini, but\r\nwe shall have some very good people. After that you may talk of your\r\nown f\xc3\xaate.\xe2\x80\x9d The old lady spoke with a certain quick eagerness, smiling\r\nmore agreeably as she went on.\r\n\r\nIt seemed to Newman a handsome proposal, and such proposals always\r\ntouched the sources of his good-nature. He said to Madame de Bellegarde\r\nthat he should be glad to come on the 25th or any other day, and that\r\nit mattered very little whether he met his friends at her house or at\r\nhis own. I have said that Newman was observant, but it must be admitted\r\nthat on this occasion he failed to notice a certain delicate glance\r\nwhich passed between Madame de Bellegarde and the marquis, and which we\r\nmay presume to have been a commentary upon the innocence displayed in\r\nthat latter clause of his speech.\r\n\r\nValentin de Bellegarde walked away with Newman that evening, and when\r\nthey had left the Rue de l\xe2\x80\x99Universit\xc3\xa9 some distance behind them he said\r\nreflectively, \xe2\x80\x9cMy mother is very strong\xe2\x80\x94very strong.\xe2\x80\x9d Then in answer to\r\nan interrogative movement of Newman\xe2\x80\x99s he continued, \xe2\x80\x9cShe was driven to\r\nthe wall, but you would never have thought it. Her f\xc3\xaate of the 25th was\r\nan invention of the moment. She had no idea whatever of giving a f\xc3\xaate,\r\nbut finding it the only issue from your proposal, she looked straight\r\nat the dose\xe2\x80\x94excuse the expression\xe2\x80\x94and bolted it, as you saw, without\r\nwinking. She is very strong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear me!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, divided between relish and compassion. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncare a straw for her f\xc3\xaate, I am willing to take the will for the deed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin, with a little inconsequent touch of family\r\npride. \xe2\x80\x9cThe thing will be done now, and done handsomely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XV\r\n\r\n\r\nValentin de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s announcement of the secession of Mademoiselle\r\nNioche from her father\xe2\x80\x99s domicile and his irreverent reflections upon\r\nthe attitude of this anxious parent in so grave a catastrophe, received\r\na practical commentary in the fact that M. Nioche was slow to seek\r\nanother interview with his late pupil. It had cost Newman some disgust\r\nto be forced to assent to Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s somewhat cynical interpretation of\r\nthe old man\xe2\x80\x99s philosophy, and, though circumstances seemed to indicate\r\nthat he had not given himself up to a noble despair, Newman thought it\r\nvery possible he might be suffering more keenly than was apparent. M.\r\nNioche had been in the habit of paying him a respectful little visit\r\nevery two or three weeks and his absence might be a proof quite as much\r\nof extreme depression as of a desire to conceal the success with which\r\nhe had patched up his sorrow. Newman presently learned from Valentin\r\nseveral details touching this new phase of Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncareer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told you she was remarkable,\xe2\x80\x9d this unshrinking observer declared,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand the way she has managed this performance proves it. She has had\r\nother chances, but she was resolved to take none but the best. She did\r\nyou the honor to think for a while that you might be such a chance. You\r\nwere not; so she gathered up her patience and waited a while longer. At\r\nlast her occasion came along, and she made her move with her eyes wide\r\nopen. I am very sure she had no innocence to lose, but she had all her\r\nrespectability. Dubious little damsel as you thought her, she had kept\r\na firm hold of that; nothing could be proved against her, and she was\r\ndetermined not to let her reputation go till she had got her\r\nequivalent. About her equivalent she had high ideas. Apparently her\r\nideal has been satisfied. It is fifty years old, bald-headed, and deaf,\r\nbut it is very easy about money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd where in the world,\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cdid you pick up this valuable\r\ninformation?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn conversation. Remember my frivolous habits. In conversation with a\r\nyoung woman engaged in the humble trade of glove-cleaner, who keeps a\r\nsmall shop in the Rue St. Roch. M. Nioche lives in the same house, up\r\nsix pair of stairs, across the court, in and out of whose ill-swept\r\ndoorway Miss No\xc3\xa9mie has been flitting for the last five years. The\r\nlittle glove-cleaner was an old acquaintance; she used to be the friend\r\nof a friend of mine, who has married and dropped such friends. I often\r\nsaw her in his society. As soon as I espied her behind her clear little\r\nwindow-pane, I recollected her. I had on a spotlessly fresh pair of\r\ngloves, but I went in and held up my hands, and said to her, \xe2\x80\x98Dear\r\nmademoiselle, what will you ask me for cleaning these?\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Dear count,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nshe answered immediately, \xe2\x80\x98I will clean them for you for nothing.\xe2\x80\x99 She\r\nhad instantly recognized me, and I had to hear her history for the last\r\nsix years. But after that, I put her upon that of her neighbors. She\r\nknows and admires No\xc3\xa9mie, and she told me what I have just repeated.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA month elapsed without M. Nioche reappearing, and Newman, who every\r\nmorning read two or three suicides in the _Figaro_, began to suspect\r\nthat, mortification proving stubborn, he had sought a balm for his\r\nwounded pride in the waters of the Seine. He had a note of M. Nioche\xe2\x80\x99s\r\naddress in his pocket-book, and finding himself one day in the\r\n_quartier_, he determined, in so far as he might, to clear up his\r\ndoubts. He repaired to the house in the Rue St. Roch which bore the\r\nrecorded number, and observed in a neighboring basement, behind a\r\ndangling row of neatly inflated gloves, the attentive physiognomy of\r\nBellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s informant\xe2\x80\x94a sallow person in a dressing-gown\xe2\x80\x94peering into\r\nthe street as if she were expecting that amiable nobleman to pass\r\nagain. But it was not to her that Newman applied; he simply asked of\r\nthe portress if M. Nioche were at home. The portress replied, as the\r\nportress invariably replies, that her lodger had gone out barely three\r\nminutes before; but then, through the little square hole of her\r\nlodge-window taking the measure of Newman\xe2\x80\x99s fortunes, and seeing them,\r\nby an unspecified process, refresh the dry places of servitude to\r\noccupants of fifth floors on courts, she added that M. Nioche would\r\nhave had just time to reach the Caf\xc3\xa9 de la Patrie, round the second\r\ncorner to the left, at which establishment he regularly spent his\r\nafternoons. Newman thanked her for the information, took the second\r\nturning to the left, and arrived at the Caf\xc3\xa9 de la Patrie. He felt a\r\nmomentary hesitation to go in; was it not rather mean to \xe2\x80\x9cfollow up\xe2\x80\x9d\r\npoor old Nioche at that rate? But there passed across his vision an\r\nimage of a haggard little septuagenarian taking measured sips of a\r\nglass of sugar and water and finding them quite impotent to sweeten his\r\ndesolation. He opened the door and entered, perceiving nothing at first\r\nbut a dense cloud of tobacco smoke. Across this, however, in a corner,\r\nhe presently descried the figure of M. Nioche, stirring the contents of\r\na deep glass, with a lady seated in front of him. The lady\xe2\x80\x99s back was\r\nturned to Newman, but M. Nioche very soon perceived and recognized his\r\nvisitor. Newman had gone toward him, and the old man rose slowly,\r\ngazing at him with a more blighted expression even than usual.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you are drinking hot punch,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you are not\r\ndead. That\xe2\x80\x99s all right. Don\xe2\x80\x99t move.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nM. Nioche stood staring, with a fallen jaw, not daring to put out his\r\nhand. The lady, who sat facing him, turned round in her place and\r\nglanced upward with a spirited toss of her head, displaying the\r\nagreeable features of his daughter. She looked at Newman sharply, to\r\nsee how he was looking at her, then\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what she\r\ndiscovered\xe2\x80\x94she said graciously, \xe2\x80\x9cHow d\xe2\x80\x99 ye do, monsieur? won\xe2\x80\x99t you come\r\ninto our little corner?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you come\xe2\x80\x94did you come after _me?_\xe2\x80\x9d asked M. Nioche very softly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI went to your house to see what had become of you. I thought you\r\nmight be sick,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very good of you, as always,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I am not\r\nwell. Yes, I am _seek_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsk monsieur to sit down,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mademoiselle Nioche. \xe2\x80\x9cGar\xc3\xa7on, bring a\r\nchair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you do us the honor to _seat?_\xe2\x80\x9d said M.
