Nioche, timorously, and\r\nwith a double foreignness of accent.\r\n\r\nNewman said to himself that he had better see the thing out and he took\r\na chair at the end of the table, with Mademoiselle Nioche on his left\r\nand her father on the other side. \xe2\x80\x9cYou will take something, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Miss No\xc3\xa9mie, who was sipping a glass of madeira. Newman said that\r\nhe believed not, and then she turned to her papa with a smile. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat an\r\nhonor, eh? he has come only for us.\xe2\x80\x9d M. Nioche drained his pungent\r\nglass at a long draught, and looked out from eyes more lachrymose in\r\nconsequence. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you didn\xe2\x80\x99t come for me, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie went\r\non. \xe2\x80\x9cYou didn\xe2\x80\x99t expect to find me here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman observed the change in her appearance. She was very elegant and\r\nprettier than before; she looked a year or two older, and it was\r\nnoticeable that, to the eye, she had only gained in respectability. She\r\nlooked \xe2\x80\x9clady-like.\xe2\x80\x9d She was dressed in quiet colors, and wore her\r\nexpensively unobtrusive toilet with a grace that might have come from\r\nyears of practice. Her present self-possession and _aplomb_ struck\r\nNewman as really infernal, and he inclined to agree with Valentin de\r\nBellegarde that the young lady was very remarkable. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, to tell the\r\ntruth, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t come for you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cand I didn\xe2\x80\x99t expect to find\r\nyou. I was told,\xe2\x80\x9d he added in a moment \xe2\x80\x9cthat you had left your father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Quelle horreur!_\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mademoiselle Nioche with a smile. \xe2\x80\x9cDoes one\r\nleave one\xe2\x80\x99s father? You have the proof of the contrary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, convincing proof,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman glancing at M. Nioche. The old man\r\ncaught his glance obliquely, with his faded, deprecating eye, and then,\r\nlifting his empty glass, pretended to drink again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho told you that?\xe2\x80\x9d No\xc3\xa9mie demanded. \xe2\x80\x9cI know very well. You are not polite.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am embarrassed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI set you a better example. I know M. de Bellegarde told you. In the first place,\r\nI haven\xe2\x80\x99t left my father; I am much too fond of him. Isn\xe2\x80\x99t it so,\r\nlittle father? M. de Bellegarde is a charming young man; it is\r\nimpossible to be cleverer. First of all, he never offered to help me to a\r\n_dot_ and a husband.\xe2\x80\x9d And Mademoiselle Nioche paused, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsay that is in his favor, for I do you justice. I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid you know little about women.\r\nYou were not _galant_; you were not what you might have been.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman flushed a trifle fiercely. \xe2\x80\x9cCome!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed \xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s rather\r\nstrong. Then she stopped a minute,\r\nlooking at Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cTell M. de Bellegarde, when he wants news of me, to\r\ncome and get it from _me!_\xe2\x80\x9d And she turned and departed, the\r\nwhite-aproned waiter, with a bow, holding the door wide open for her.\r\n\r\nM. Nioche sat motionless, and Newman hardly knew what to say to him.\r\nThe old man looked dismally foolish. \xe2\x80\x9cSo you determined not to shoot\r\nher, after all,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman said presently.\r\n\r\nM. Nioche, without moving, raised his eyes and gave him a long,\r\npeculiar look. It seemed to confess everything, and yet not to ask for\r\npity, nor to pretend, on the other hand, to a rugged ability to do\r\nwithout it. It might have expressed the state of mind of an innocuous\r\ninsect, flat in shape and conscious of the impending pressure of a\r\nboot-sole, and reflecting that he was perhaps too flat to be crushed.\r\nM. Nioche\xe2\x80\x99s gaze was a profession of moral flatness. \xe2\x80\x9cYou despise me\r\nterribly,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, in the weakest possible voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cit is none of my business. It\xe2\x80\x99s a good plan to\r\ntake things easily.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI made you too many fine speeches,\xe2\x80\x9d M. That is why I came to look you\r\nup.\xe2\x80\x9d And he began to button his coat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeither,\xe2\x80\x9d said M. As he passed along the glazed side of the caf\xc3\xa9,\r\non reaching the street, he saw the old man motioning the waiter, with a\r\nmelancholy gesture, to replenish his glass.\r\n\r\nOne day, a week after his visit to the Caf\xc3\xa9 de la Patrie, he called\r\nupon Valentin de Bellegarde, and by good fortune found him at home.\r\nNewman spoke of his interview with M. Nioche and his daughter, and said\r\nhe was afraid Valentin had judged the old man correctly. He had found\r\nthe couple hobnobbing together in all amity; the old gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s rigor\r\nwas purely theoretic. Newman confessed that he was disappointed; he\r\nshould have expected to see M. Nioche take high ground.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHigh ground, my dear fellow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin, laughing; \xe2\x80\x9cthere is no\r\nhigh ground for him to take. The only perceptible eminence in M.\r\nNioche\xe2\x80\x99s horizon is Montmartre, which is not an edifying quarter. You\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t go mountaineering in a flat country.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe remarked, indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cthat he has not forgiven her. But\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99ll never find it out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must do him the justice to suppose he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t like the thing,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nValentin rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cMademoiselle Nioche is like the great artists whose\r\nbiographies we read, who at the beginning of their career have suffered\r\nopposition in the domestic circle. Their vocation has not been\r\nrecognized by their families, but the world has done it justice.\r\nMademoiselle Nioche has a vocation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, come,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, impatiently, \xe2\x80\x9cyou take the little baggage too\r\nseriously.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know I do; but when one has nothing to think about, one must think\r\nof little baggages. I suppose it is better to be serious about light\r\nthings than not to be serious at all. This little baggage entertains\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, she has discovered that. She is not so much tickled,\r\nby the way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had better go and tell her,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cShe gave me a\r\nmessage for you of some such drift.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBless your quiet imagination,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin, \xe2\x80\x9cI have been to see\r\nher\xe2\x80\x94three times in five days. She is a charming hostess; we talk of\r\nShakespeare and the musical glasses. She is extremely clever and a very\r\ncurious type; not at all coarse or wanting to be coarse; determined not\r\nto be. She means to take very good care of herself. She is extremely\r\nperfect; she is as hard and clear-cut as some little figure of a\r\nsea-nymph in an antique intaglio, and I will warrant that she has not a\r\ngrain more of sentiment or heart than if she was scooped out of a big\r\namethyst. Extremely\r\npretty,\xe2\x80\x94really, when you know her, she is wonderfully\r\npretty,\xe2\x80\x94intelligent, determined, ambitious, unscrupulous, capable of\r\nlooking at a man strangled without changing color, she is upon my\r\nhonor, extremely entertaining.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a fine list of attractions,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman; \xe2\x80\x9cthey would serve as a\r\npolice-detective\xe2\x80\x99s description of a favorite criminal. He stuck it into\r\nthe frame of his mirror and eyed it with some complacency; it seemed an\r\nagreeable emblem of triumph, documentary evidence that his prize was\r\ngained. I mean the long pedigrees and the high noses, and all that.\r\nSome of them are awful idiots; I advise you to take them up\r\ncautiously.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I guess I shall like them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI am prepared to like\r\nevery one and everything in these days; I am in high good-humor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nValentin looked at him a moment in silence and then dropped himself\r\ninto a chair with an unwonted air of weariness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHappy man!\xe2\x80\x9d he said with a sigh. \xe2\x80\x9cTake care you don\xe2\x80\x99t become\r\noffensive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf anyone chooses to take offense, he may. I can\xe2\x80\x99t die of illness, I am too\r\nridiculously tough; and the time for dying of old age won\xe2\x80\x99t come round\r\nyet a while. I can\xe2\x80\x99t lose my wife, I shall take too good care of her. I\r\nmay lose my money, or a large part of it; but that won\xe2\x80\x99t matter, for I\r\nshall make twice as much again. So what have I to be afraid of?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not afraid it may be rather a mistake for an American man of\r\nbusiness to marry a French countess?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the countess, possibly; but not for the man of business, if you\r\nmean me! But my countess shall not be disappointed; I answer for her\r\nhappiness!\xe2\x80\x9d And as if he felt the impulse to celebrate his happy\r\ncertitude by a bonfire, he got up to throw a couple of logs upon the\r\nalready blazing hearth. Valentin watched for a few moments the\r\nquickened flame, and then, with his head leaning on his hand, gave a\r\nmelancholy sigh. \xe2\x80\x9cGot a headache?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Je suis triste_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin, with Gallic simplicity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are sad, eh? It is about the lady you said the other night that\r\nyou adored and that you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t marry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid I really say that? It seemed to me afterwards that the words had\r\nescaped me. Before Claire it was bad taste. But I felt gloomy as I\r\nspoke, and I feel gloomy still. Why did you ever introduce me to that\r\ngirl?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s No\xc3\xa9mie, is it? Lord deliver us! You don\xe2\x80\x99t mean to say you are\r\nlovesick about her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLovesick, no; it\xe2\x80\x99s not a grand passion. But the cold-blooded little\r\ndemon sticks in my thoughts; she has bitten me with those even little\r\nteeth of hers; I feel as if I might turn rabid and do something crazy\r\nin consequence. It\xe2\x80\x99s very low, it\xe2\x80\x99s disgustingly low. She\xe2\x80\x99s the most\r\nmercenary little jade in Europe. Yet she really affects my peace of\r\nmind; she is always running in my head. It\xe2\x80\x99s a striking contrast to\r\nyour noble and virtuous attachment\xe2\x80\x94a vile contrast! It is rather\r\npitiful that it should be the best I am able to do for myself at my\r\npresent respectable age. I am a nice young man, eh, _en somme?_ You\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t warrant my future, as you do your own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDrop that girl, short,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman; \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t go near her again, and\r\nyour future will do. Come over to America and I will get you a place in\r\na bank.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is easy to say drop her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin, with a light laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t drop a pretty woman like that. One must be polite, even with\r\nNo\xc3\xa9mie. Besides, I\xe2\x80\x99ll not have her suppose I am afraid of her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo, between politeness and vanity, you will get deeper into the mud?\r\nKeep them both for something better. Remember, too, that I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want\r\nto introduce you to her; you insisted. I had a sort of uneasy feeling\r\nabout it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t reproach you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin. \xe2\x80\x9cHeaven forbid! I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nfor the world have missed knowing her. She is really extraordinary. The\r\nway she has already spread her wings is amazing. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know when a\r\nwoman has amused me more. But excuse me,\xe2\x80\x9d he added in an instant; \xe2\x80\x9cshe\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t amuse you, at second hand, and the subject is an impure one.\r\nLet us talk of something else.\xe2\x80\x9d Valentin introduced another topic, but\r\nwithin five minutes Newman observed that, by a bold transition, he had\r\nreverted to Mademoiselle Nioche, and was giving pictures of her manners\r\nand quoting specimens of her _mots_. These were very witty, and, for a\r\nyoung woman who six months before had been painting the most artless\r\nmadonnas, startlingly cynical. But at last, abruptly, he stopped,\r\nbecame thoughtful, and for some time afterwards said nothing. When he\r\nrose to go it was evident that his thoughts were still running upon\r\nMademoiselle Nioche. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, she\xe2\x80\x99s a frightful little monster!\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVI\r\n\r\n\r\nThe next ten days were the happiest that Newman had ever known. He saw\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 every day, and never saw either old Madame de\r\nBellegarde or the elder of his prospective brothers-in-law. Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9 at last seemed to think it becoming to apologize for their never\r\nbeing present. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are much taken up,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cwith doing the\r\nhonors of Paris to Lord Deepmere.\xe2\x80\x9d There was a smile in her gravity as\r\nshe made this declaration, and it deepened as she added, \xe2\x80\x9cHe is our\r\nseventh cousin, you know, and blood is thicker than water. And then, he\r\nis so interesting!\xe2\x80\x9d And with this she laughed.\r\n\r\nNewman met young Madame de Bellegarde two or three times, always\r\nroaming about with graceful vagueness, as if in search of an\r\nunattainable ideal of amusement. She always reminded him of a painted\r\nperfume-bottle with a crack in it; but he had grown to have a kindly\r\nfeeling for her, based on the fact of her owing conjugal allegiance to\r\nUrbain de Bellegarde. He pitied M. de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s wife, especially\r\nsince she was a silly, thirstily-smiling little brunette, with a\r\nsuggestion of an unregulated heart. The small marquise sometimes looked\r\nat him with an intensity too marked not to be innocent, for coquetry is\r\nmore finely shaded. She apparently wanted to ask him something or tell\r\nhim something; he wondered what it was. But he was shy of giving her an\r\nopportunity, because, if her communication bore upon the aridity of her\r\nmatrimonial lot, he was at a loss to see how he could help her. He had\r\na fancy, however, of her coming up to him some day and saying (after\r\nlooking around behind her) with a little passionate hiss, \xe2\x80\x9cI know you\r\ndetest my husband; let me have the pleasure of assuring you for once\r\nthat you are right. Pity a poor woman who is married to a clock-image\r\nin _papier-m\xc3\xa2ch\xc3\xa9!_\xe2\x80\x9d Possessing, however, in default of a competent\r\nknowledge of the principles of etiquette, a very downright sense of the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cmeanness\xe2\x80\x9d of certain actions, it seemed to him to belong to his\r\nposition to keep on his guard; he was not going to put it into the\r\npower of these people to say that in their house he had done anything\r\nunpleasant. As it was, Madame de Bellegarde used to give him news of\r\nthe dress she meant to wear at his wedding, and which had not yet, in\r\nher creative imagination, in spite of many interviews with the tailor,\r\nresolved itself into its composite totality. \xe2\x80\x9cI told you pale blue bows\r\non the sleeves, at the elbows,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut to-day I don\xe2\x80\x99t see my\r\nblue bows at all. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what has become of them. To-day I see\r\npink\xe2\x80\x94a tender pink. And then I pass through strange, dull phases in\r\nwhich neither blue nor pink says anything to me. And yet I must have\r\nthe bows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave them green or yellow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Malheureux!_\xe2\x80\x9d the little marquise would cry. \xe2\x80\x9cGreen bows would break\r\nyour marriage\xe2\x80\x94your children would be illegitimate!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 was calmly happy before the world, and Newman had the\r\nfelicity of fancying that before him, when the world was absent, she\r\nwas almost agitatedly happy. She said very tender things. \xe2\x80\x9cI take no\r\npleasure in you. You never give me a chance to scold you, to correct\r\nyou. I bargained for that, I expected to enjoy it. But you won\xe2\x80\x99t do\r\nanything dreadful; you are dismally inoffensive. It is very stupid;\r\nthere is no excitement for me; I might as well be marrying someone\r\nelse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid it\xe2\x80\x99s the worst I can do,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman would say in answer to\r\nthis. \xe2\x80\x9cKindly overlook the deficiency.\xe2\x80\x9d He assured her that he, at\r\nleast, would never scold her; she was perfectly satisfactory. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you\r\nonly knew,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9chow exactly you are what I coveted! And I am\r\nbeginning to understand why I coveted it; the having it makes all the\r\ndifference that I expected. Never was a man so pleased with his good\r\nfortune. You have been holding your head for a week past just as I\r\nwanted my wife to hold hers. You say just the things I want her to say.\r\nYou walk about the room just as I want her to walk. You have just the\r\ntaste in dress that I want her to have. In short, you come up to the\r\nmark, and, I can tell you, my mark was high.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThese observations seemed to make Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 rather grave. At\r\nlast she said, \xe2\x80\x9cDepend upon it, I don\xe2\x80\x99t come up to the mark; your mark\r\nis too high. I am not all that you suppose; I am a much smaller affair.\r\nShe is a magnificent woman, your ideal. Pray, how did she come to such\r\nperfection?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was never anything else,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI really believe,\xe2\x80\x9d Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 went on, \xe2\x80\x9cthat she is better than\r\nmy own ideal. Do you know that is a very handsome compliment? Well,\r\nsir, I will make her my own!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Tristram came to see her dear Claire after Newman had announced\r\nhis engagement, and she told our hero the next day that his good\r\nfortune was simply absurd. \xe2\x80\x9cFor the ridiculous part of it is,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you are evidently going to be as happy as if you were\r\nmarrying Miss Smith or Miss Thompson. I call it a brilliant match for\r\nyou, but you get brilliancy without paying any tax upon it. Those\r\nthings are usually a compromise, but here you have everything, and\r\nnothing crowds anything else out. You will be brilliantly happy as\r\nwell.\xe2\x80\x9d Newman thanked her for her pleasant, encouraging way of saying\r\nthings; no woman could encourage or discourage better. Tristram\xe2\x80\x99s way\r\nof saying things was different; he had been taken by his wife to call\r\nupon Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, and he gave an account of the expedition.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t catch me giving an opinion on your countess this time,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid; \xe2\x80\x9cI put my foot in it once. That\xe2\x80\x99s a d\xe2\x80\x94d underhand thing to do, by\r\nthe way\xe2\x80\x94coming round to sound a fellow upon the woman you are going to\r\nmarry. You deserve anything you get. Then of course you rush and tell\r\nher, and she takes care to make it pleasant for the poor spiteful\r\nwretch the first time he calls. I will do you the justice to say,\r\nhowever, that you don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to have told Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9; or if you\r\nhave, she\xe2\x80\x99s uncommonly magnanimous. She was very nice; she was\r\ntremendously polite. She and Lizzie sat on the sofa, pressing each\r\nother\xe2\x80\x99s hands and calling each other _ch\xc3\xa8re belle_, and Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9 sent me with every third word a magnificent smile, as if to give\r\nme to understand that I too was a handsome dear. She quite made up for\r\npast neglect, I assure you; she was very pleasant and sociable. Only in\r\nan evil hour it came into her head to say that she must present us to\r\nher mother\xe2\x80\x94her mother wished to know your friends. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to\r\nknow her mother, and I was on the point of telling Lizzie to go in\r\nalone and let me wait for her outside. But Lizzie, with her usual\r\ninfernal ingenuity, guessed my purpose and reduced me by a glance of\r\nher eye. So they marched off arm in arm, and I followed as I could. We\r\nfound the old lady in her armchair, twiddling her aristocratic thumbs.\r\nShe looked at Lizzie from head to foot; but at that game Lizzie, to do\r\nher justice, was a match for her. My wife told her we were great\r\nfriends of Mr. Newman. The marquise started a moment, and then said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh, Mr. Newman! My daughter has made up her mind to marry a Mr.\r\nNewman.\xe2\x80\x99 Then Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 began to fondle Lizzie again, and said\r\nit was this dear lady that had planned the match and brought them\r\ntogether. \xe2\x80\x98Oh, \xe2\x80\x98tis you I have to thank for my American son-in-law,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nthe old lady said to Mrs. Tristram. \xe2\x80\x98It was a very clever thought of\r\nyours. Be sure of my gratitude.\xe2\x80\x99 And then she began to look at me and\r\npresently said, \xe2\x80\x98Pray, are you engaged in some species of manufacture?\xe2\x80\x99\r\nI wanted to say that I manufactured broom-sticks for old witches to\r\nride on, but Lizzie got in ahead of me. \xe2\x80\x98My husband, Madame la\r\nMarquise,\xe2\x80\x99 she said, \xe2\x80\x98belongs to that unfortunate class of persons who\r\nhave no profession and no business, and do very little good in the\r\nworld.\xe2\x80\x99 To get her poke at the old woman she didn\xe2\x80\x99t care where she\r\nshoved me. \xe2\x80\x98Dear me,\xe2\x80\x99 said the marquise, \xe2\x80\x98we all have our duties.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98I\r\nam sorry mine compel me to take leave of you,\xe2\x80\x99 said Lizzie. And we\r\nbundled out again. But you have a mother-in-law, in all the force of\r\nthe term.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cmy mother-in-law desires nothing better than to let\r\nme alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBetimes, on the evening of the 27th, he went to Madame de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nball. The old house in the Rue de l\xe2\x80\x99Universit\xc3\xa9 looked strangely\r\nbrilliant. In the circle of light projected from the outer gate a\r\ndetachment of the populace stood watching the carriages roll in; the\r\ncourt was illumined with flaring torches and the portico carpeted with\r\ncrimson. When Newman arrived there were but a few people present. The\r\nmarquise and her two daughters were at the top of the staircase, where\r\nthe sallow old nymph in the angle peeped out from a bower of plants.\r\nMadame de Bellegarde, in purple and fine laces, looked like an old lady\r\npainted by Vandyke; Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 was dressed in white. The old lady\r\ngreeted Newman with majestic formality, and looking round her, called\r\nseveral of the persons who were standing near. They were elderly\r\ngentlemen, of what Valentin de Bellegarde had designated as the\r\nhigh-nosed category; two or three of them wore cordons and stars. They\r\napproached with measured alertness, and the marquise said that she\r\nwished to present them to Mr. Newman, who was going to marry her\r\ndaughter. Then she introduced successively three dukes, three counts,\r\nand a baron. These gentlemen bowed and smiled most agreeably, and\r\nNewman indulged in a series of impartial hand-shakes, accompanied by a\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHappy to make your acquaintance, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d He looked at Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9,\r\nbut she was not looking at him. If his personal self-consciousness had\r\nbeen of a nature to make him constantly refer to her, as the critic\r\nbefore whom, in company, he played his part, he might have found it a\r\nflattering proof of her confidence that he never caught her eyes\r\nresting upon him. It is a reflection Newman did not make, but we\r\nnevertheless risk it, that in spite of this circumstance she probably\r\nsaw every movement of his little finger. Young Madame de Bellegarde was\r\ndressed in an audacious toilet of crimson crape, bestrewn with huge\r\nsilver moons\xe2\x80\x94thin crescent and full disks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t say anything about my dress,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI feel,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, \xe2\x80\x9cas if I were looking at you through a\r\ntelescope. It is very strange.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it is strange it matches the occasion. But I am not a heavenly\r\nbody.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never saw the sky at midnight that particular shade of crimson,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is my originality; anyone could have chosen blue. My\r\nsister-in-law would have chosen a lovely shade of blue, with a dozen\r\nlittle delicate moons. But I think crimson is much more amusing. And I\r\ngive my idea, which is moonshine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMoonshine and bloodshed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA murder by moonlight,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Madame de Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a\r\ndelicious idea for a toilet! To make it complete, there is the silver\r\ndagger, you see, stuck into my hair. But here comes Lord Deepmere,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nadded in a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cI must find out what he thinks of it.\xe2\x80\x9d Lord\r\nDeepmere came up, looking very red in the face, and laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cLord\r\nDeepmere can\xe2\x80\x99t decide which he prefers, my sister-in-law or me,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMadame de Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cHe likes Claire because she is his cousin, and\r\nme because I am not. But he has no right to make love to Claire,\r\nwhereas I am perfectly _disponible_. It is very wrong to make love to a\r\nwoman who is engaged, but it is very wrong not to make love to a woman\r\nwho is married.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s very jolly making love to married women,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lord Deepmere,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbecause they can\xe2\x80\x99t ask you to marry them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that what the others do, the spinsters?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh dear, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lord Deepmere; \xe2\x80\x9cin England all the girls ask a\r\nfellow to marry them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd a fellow brutally refuses,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, really, you know, a fellow can\xe2\x80\x99t marry any girl that asks him,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid his lordship.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour cousin won\xe2\x80\x99t ask you. She is going to marry Mr. Newman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that\xe2\x80\x99s a very different thing!\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Lord Deepmere.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou would have accepted _her_, I suppose. That makes me hope that\r\nafter all you prefer me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, when things are nice I never prefer one to the other,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nyoung Englishman. \xe2\x80\x9cI take them all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, what a horror! I won\xe2\x80\x99t be taken in that way; I must be kept\r\napart,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Madame de Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Newman is much better; he\r\nknows how to choose. Oh, he chooses as if he were threading a needle.\r\nHe prefers Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 to any conceivable creature or thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you can\xe2\x80\x99t help my being her cousin,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lord Deepmere to\r\nNewman, with candid hilarity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, I can\xe2\x80\x99t help that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, laughing back; \xe2\x80\x9cneither can\r\nshe!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you can\xe2\x80\x99t help my dancing with her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lord Deepmere, with\r\nsturdy simplicity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could prevent that only by dancing with her myself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut unfortunately I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how to dance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you may dance without knowing how; may you not, milord?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMadame de Bellegarde. But to this Lord Deepmere replied that a fellow\r\nought to know how to dance if he didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to make an ass of himself;\r\nand at this moment Urbain de Bellegarde joined the group, slow-stepping\r\nand with his hands behind him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is a very splendid entertainment,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, cheerfully. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nold house looks very bright.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf _you_ are pleased, we are content,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquis, lifting his\r\nshoulders and bending them forward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I suspect everyone is pleased,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cHow can they help\r\nbeing pleased when the first thing they see as they come in is your\r\nsister, standing there as beautiful as an angel?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, she is very beautiful,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined the marquis, solemnly. \xe2\x80\x9cBut that\r\nis not so great a source of satisfaction to other people, naturally, as\r\nto you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I am satisfied, marquis, I am satisfied,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, with his\r\nprotracted enunciation. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd now tell me,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, looking round,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwho some of your friends are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nM. de Bellegarde looked about him in silence, with his head bent and\r\nhis hand raised to his lower lip, which he slowly rubbed. A stream of\r\npeople had been pouring into the salon in which Newman stood with his\r\nhost, the rooms were filling up and the spectacle had become brilliant.\r\nIt borrowed its splendor chiefly from the shining shoulders and profuse\r\njewels of the women, and from the voluminous elegance of their dresses.\r\nThere were no uniforms, as Madame de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s door was inexorably\r\nclosed against the myrmidons of the upstart power which then ruled the\r\nfortunes of France, and the great company of smiling and chattering\r\nfaces was not graced by any very frequent suggestions of harmonious\r\nbeauty. It is a pity, nevertheless, that Newman had not been a\r\nphysiognomist, for a great many of the faces were irregularly\r\nagreeable, expressive, and suggestive. If the occasion had been\r\ndifferent they would hardly have pleased him; he would have thought the\r\nwomen not pretty enough and the men too smirking; but he was now in a\r\nhumor to receive none but agreeable impressions, and he looked no more\r\nnarrowly than to perceive that everyone was brilliant, and to feel that\r\nthe sun of their brilliancy was a part of his credit. \xe2\x80\x9cI will present\r\nyou to some people,\xe2\x80\x9d said M. de Bellegarde after a while. \xe2\x80\x9cI will make\r\na point of it, in fact. You will allow me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I will shake hands with anyone you want,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cYour\r\nmother just introduced me to half a dozen old gentlemen. Take care you\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t pick up the same parties again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho are the gentlemen to whom my mother presented you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon my word, I forgot them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cThe people here\r\nlook very much alike.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suspect they have not forgotten you,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquis. And he began\r\nto walk through the rooms. Newman, to keep near him in the crowd, took\r\nhis arm; after which for some time, the marquis walked straight along,\r\nin silence. At last, reaching the farther end of the suite of\r\nreception-rooms, Newman found himself in the presence of a lady of\r\nmonstrous proportions, seated in a very capacious armchair, with\r\nseveral persons standing in a semicircle round her. This little group\r\nhad divided as the marquis came up, and M. de Bellegarde stepped\r\nforward and stood for an instant silent and obsequious, with his hat\r\nraised to his lips, as Newman had seen some gentlemen stand in churches\r\nas soon as they entered their pews. The lady, indeed, bore a very fair\r\nlikeness to a reverend effigy in some idolatrous shrine. She was\r\nmonumentally stout and imperturbably serene. Her aspect was to Newman\r\nalmost formidable; he had a troubled consciousness of a triple chin, a\r\nsmall piercing eye, a vast expanse of uncovered bosom, a nodding and\r\ntwinkling tiara of plumes and gems, and an immense circumference of\r\nsatin petticoat. With her little circle of beholders this remarkable\r\nwoman reminded him of the Fat Lady at a fair. She fixed her small,\r\nunwinking eyes at the new-comers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear duchess,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquis, \xe2\x80\x9clet me present you our good friend\r\nMr. Newman, of whom you have heard us speak. Wishing to make Mr. Newman\r\nknown to those who are dear to us, I could not possibly fail to begin\r\nwith you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCharmed, dear friend; charmed, monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d said the duchess in a voice\r\nwhich, though small and shrill, was not disagreeable, while Newman\r\nexecuted his obeisance. \xe2\x80\x9cI came on purpose to see monsieur. I hope he\r\nappreciates the compliment. You have only to look at me to do so, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe continued, sweeping her person with a much-encompassing glance.\r\nNewman hardly knew what to say, though it seemed that to a duchess who\r\njoked about her corpulence one might say almost anything. On hearing\r\nthat the duchess had come on purpose to see Newman, the gentlemen who\r\nsurrounded her turned a little and looked at him with sympathetic\r\ncuriosity. The marquis with supernatural gravity mentioned to him the\r\nname of each, while the gentleman who bore it bowed; they were all what\r\nare called in France _beaux noms_. \xe2\x80\x9cI wanted extremely to see you,\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\nduchess went on. \xe2\x80\x9c_C\xe2\x80\x99est positif_. In the first place, I am very fond\r\nof the person you are going to marry; she is the most charming creature\r\nin France. Mind you treat her well, or you shall hear some news of me.\r\nBut you look as if you were good. I am told you are very remarkable. I\r\nhave heard all sorts of extraordinary things about you. _Voyons_, are\r\nthey true?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you can have heard,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you have your _l\xc3\xa9gende_. We have heard that you have had a career\r\nthe most checkered, the most _bizarre_. What is that about your having\r\nfounded a city some ten years ago in the great West, a city which\r\ncontains to-day half a million of inhabitants? Isn\xe2\x80\x99t it half a million,\r\nmessieurs? You are exclusive proprietor of this flourishing settlement,\r\nand are consequently fabulously rich, and you would be richer still if\r\nyou didn\xe2\x80\x99t grant lands and houses free of rent to all new-comers who\r\nwill pledge themselves never to smoke cigars. At this game, in three\r\nyears, we are told, you are going to be made president of America.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe duchess recited this amazing \xe2\x80\x9clegend\xe2\x80\x9d with a smooth self-possession\r\nwhich gave the speech to Newman\xe2\x80\x99s mind, the air of being a bit of\r\namusing dialogue in a play, delivered by a veteran comic actress.\r\nBefore she had ceased speaking he had burst into loud, irrepressible\r\nlaughter. \xe2\x80\x9cDear duchess, dear duchess,\xe2\x80\x9d the marquis began to murmur,\r\nsoothingly. Two or three persons came to the door of the room to see\r\nwho was laughing at the duchess. But the lady continued with the soft,\r\nserene assurance of a person who, as a duchess, was certain of being\r\nlistened to, and, as a garrulous woman, was independent of the pulse of\r\nher auditors. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I know you are very remarkable. You must be, to have\r\nendeared yourself to this good marquis and to his admirable world. They\r\nare very exacting. I myself am not very sure at this hour of really\r\npossessing it. Eh, Bellegarde? To please you, I see, one must be an\r\nAmerican millionaire. But your real triumph, my dear sir, is pleasing\r\nthe countess; she is as difficult as a princess in a fairy tale. Your\r\nsuccess is a miracle. What is your secret? I don\xe2\x80\x99t ask you to reveal it\r\nbefore all these gentlemen, but come and see me some day and give me a\r\nspecimen of your talents.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe secret is with Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must ask her\r\nfor it. It consists in her having a great deal of charity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery pretty!\xe2\x80\x9d said the duchess. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a very nice specimen, to begin\r\nwith. What, Bellegarde, are you already taking monsieur away?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have a duty to perform, dear friend,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquis, pointing to\r\nthe other groups.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, for you I know what that means. Well, I have seen monsieur; that\r\nis what I wanted. He can\xe2\x80\x99t persuade me that he isn\xe2\x80\x99t very clever.\r\nFarewell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs Newman passed on with his host, he asked who the duchess was. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\ngreatest lady in France,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquis. M. de Bellegarde then\r\npresented his prospective brother-in-law to some twenty other persons\r\nof both sexes, selected apparently for their typically august\r\ncharacter. In some cases this character was written in good round hand\r\nupon the countenance of the wearer; in others Newman was thankful for\r\nsuch help as his companion\xe2\x80\x99s impressively brief intimation contributed\r\nto the discovery of it. There were large, majestic men, and small\r\ndemonstrative men; there were ugly ladies in yellow lace and quaint\r\njewels, and pretty ladies with white shoulders from which jewels and\r\neverything else were absent. Everyone gave Newman extreme attention,\r\neveryone smiled, everyone was charmed to make his acquaintance,\r\neveryone looked at him with that soft hardness of good society which\r\nputs out its hand but keeps its fingers closed over the coin. If the\r\nmarquis was going about as a bear-leader, if the fiction of Beauty and\r\nthe Beast was supposed to have found its companion-piece, the general\r\nimpression appeared to be that the bear was a very fair imitation of\r\nhumanity. Newman found his reception among the marquis\xe2\x80\x99s friends very\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cpleasant;\xe2\x80\x9d he could not have said more for it. It was pleasant to be\r\ntreated with so much explicit politeness; it was pleasant to hear\r\nneatly turned civilities, with a flavor of wit, uttered from beneath\r\ncarefully-shaped moustaches; it was pleasant to see clever\r\nFrenchwomen\xe2\x80\x94they all seemed clever\xe2\x80\x94turn their backs to their partners\r\nto get a good look at the strange American whom Claire de Cintr\xc3\xa9 was to\r\nmarry, and reward the object of the exhibition with a charming smile.\r\nAt last, as he turned away from a battery of smiles and other\r\namenities, Newman caught the eye of the marquis looking at him heavily;\r\nand thereupon, for a single instant, he checked himself. \xe2\x80\x9cAm I behaving\r\nlike a d\xe2\x80\x94d fool?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked himself. \xe2\x80\x9cAm I stepping about like a terrier\r\non his hind legs?\xe2\x80\x9d At this moment he perceived Mrs. Tristram at the\r\nother side of the room, and he waved his hand in farewell to M. de\r\nBellegarde and made his way toward her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAm I holding my head too high?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked. \xe2\x80\x9cDo I look as if I had the\r\nlower end of a pulley fastened to my chin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou look like all happy men, very ridiculous,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Tristram.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the usual thing, neither better nor worse. I have been watching\r\nyou for the last ten minutes, and I have been watching M. de\r\nBellegarde. He doesn\xe2\x80\x99t like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe more credit to him for putting it through,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I\r\nshall be generous. I shan\xe2\x80\x99t trouble him any more. But I am very happy.\r\nI can\xe2\x80\x99t stand still here. Please to take my arm and we will go for a\r\nwalk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe led Mrs. Tristram through all the rooms. There were a great many of\r\nthem, and, decorated for the occasion and filled with a stately crowd,\r\ntheir somewhat tarnished nobleness recovered its lustre. Mrs. Tristram,\r\nlooking about her, dropped a series of softly-incisive comments upon\r\nher fellow-guests. But Newman made vague answers; he hardly heard her,\r\nhis thoughts were elsewhere. They were lost in a cheerful sense of\r\nsuccess, of attainment and victory. His momentary care as to whether he\r\nlooked like a fool passed away, leaving him simply with a rich\r\ncontentment. He had got what he wanted. The savor of success had always\r\nbeen highly agreeable to him, and it had been his fortune to know it\r\noften. But it had never before been so sweet, been associated with so\r\nmuch that was brilliant and suggestive and entertaining. The lights,\r\nthe flowers, the music, the crowd, the splendid women, the jewels, the\r\nstrangeness even of the universal murmur of a clever foreign tongue\r\nwere all a vivid symbol and assurance of his having grasped his purpose\r\nand forced along his groove. If Newman\xe2\x80\x99s smile was larger than usual,\r\nit was not tickled vanity that pulled the strings; he had no wish to be\r\nshown with the finger or to achieve a personal success. If he could\r\nhave looked down at the scene, invisible, from a hole in the roof, he\r\nwould have enjoyed it quite as much. It would have spoken to him about\r\nhis own prosperity and deepened that easy feeling about life to which,\r\nsooner or later, he made all experience contribute. Just now the cup\r\nseemed full.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a very pretty party,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Tristram, after they had walked\r\na while. \xe2\x80\x9cI have seen nothing objectionable except my husband leaning\r\nagainst the wall and talking to an individual whom I suppose he takes\r\nfor a duke, but whom I more than suspect to be the functionary who\r\nattends to the lamps. Do you think you could separate them? Knock over\r\na lamp!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI doubt whether Newman, who saw no harm in Tristram\xe2\x80\x99s conversing with\r\nan ingenious mechanic, would have complied with this request; but at\r\nthis moment Valentin de Bellegarde drew near. Newman, some weeks\r\npreviously, had presented Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s youngest brother to Mrs.\r\nTristram, for whose merits Valentin professed a discriminating relish\r\nand to whom he had paid several visits.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you ever read Keats\xe2\x80\x99s Belle Dame sans Merci?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs. Tristram.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou remind me of the hero of the ballad:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n     \xe2\x80\x98Oh, what can ail thee, knight-at-arms,\r\n     Alone and palely loitering?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I am alone, it is because I have been deprived of your society,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Valentin. \xe2\x80\x9cBesides it is good manners for no man except Newman to\r\nlook happy. This is all to his address. It is not for you and me to go\r\nbefore the curtain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou promised me last spring,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman to Mrs. Tristram, \xe2\x80\x9cthat six\r\nmonths from that time I should get into a monstrous rage. It seems to\r\nme the time\xe2\x80\x99s up, and yet the nearest I can come to doing anything\r\nrough now is to offer you a _caf\xc3\xa9 glac\xc3\xa9_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told you we should do things grandly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nallude to the _caf\xc3\xa9s glac\xc3\xa9s_. But everyone is here, and my sister told\r\nme just now that Urbain had been adorable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a good fellow, he\xe2\x80\x99s a good fellow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI love him as a\r\nbrother. That reminds me that I ought to go and say something polite to\r\nyour mother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet it be something very polite indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin. \xe2\x80\x9cIt may be the\r\nlast time you will feel so much like it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman walked away, almost disposed to clasp old Madame de Bellegarde\r\nround the waist. He passed through several rooms and at last found the\r\nold marquise in the first saloon, seated on a sofa, with her young\r\nkinsman, Lord Deepmere, beside her. The young man looked somewhat\r\nbored; his hands were thrust into his pockets and his eyes were fixed\r\nupon the toes of his shoes, his feet being thrust out in front of him.\r\nMadame de Bellegarde appeared to have been talking to him with some\r\nintensity and to be waiting for an answer to what she had said, or for\r\nsome sign of the effect of her words. Her hands were folded in her lap,\r\nand she was looking at his lordship\xe2\x80\x99s simple physiognomy with an air of\r\npolitely suppressed irritation.\r\n\r\nLord Deepmere looked up as Newman approached, met his eyes, and changed\r\ncolor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid I disturb an interesting interview,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\nMadame de Bellegarde rose, and her companion rising at the same time,\r\nshe put her hand into his arm. She answered nothing for an instant, and\r\nthen, as he remained silent, she said with a smile, \xe2\x80\x9cIt would be polite\r\nfor Lord Deepmere to say it was very interesting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m not polite!\xe2\x80\x9d cried his lordship. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it _was_ interesting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadame de Bellegarde was giving you some good advice, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nNewman; \xe2\x80\x9ctoning you down a little?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was giving him some excellent advice,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquise, fixing her\r\nfresh, cold eyes upon our hero. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s for him to take it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake it, sir\xe2\x80\x94take it,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cAny advice the marquise\r\ngives you to-night must be good. For to-night, marquise, you must speak\r\nfrom a cheerful, comfortable spirit, and that makes good advice. You\r\nsee everything going on so brightly and successfully round you. Your\r\nparty is magnificent; it was a very happy thought. It is much better\r\nthan that thing of mine would have been.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you are pleased I am satisfied,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cMy\r\ndesire was to please you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you want to please me a little more?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cJust drop our\r\nlordly friend; I am sure he wants to be off and shake his heels a\r\nlittle. Then take my arm and walk through the rooms.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy desire was to please you,\xe2\x80\x9d the old lady repeated. And she liberated\r\nLord Deepmere, Newman rather wondering at her docility. \xe2\x80\x9cIf this young\r\nman is wise,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, \xe2\x80\x9che will go and find my daughter and ask her\r\nto dance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been endorsing your advice,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, bending over her and\r\nlaughing, \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose I must swallow that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLord Deepmere wiped his forehead and departed, and Madame de Bellegarde\r\ntook Newman\xe2\x80\x99s arm. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, it\xe2\x80\x99s a very pleasant, sociable entertainment,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nthe latter declared, as they proceeded on their circuit. \xe2\x80\x9cEveryone\r\nseems to know everyone and to be glad to see everyone. The marquis has\r\nmade me acquainted with ever so many people, and I feel quite like one\r\nof the family. It\xe2\x80\x99s an occasion,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman continued, wanting to say\r\nsomething thoroughly kind and comfortable, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I shall always\r\nremember, and remember very pleasantly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think it is an occasion that we shall none of us forget,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nmarquise, with her pure, neat enunciation.\r\n\r\nPeople made way for her as she passed, others turned round and looked\r\nat her, and she received a great many greetings and pressings of the\r\nhand, all of which she accepted with the most delicate dignity. But\r\nthough she smiled upon everyone, she said nothing until she reached the\r\nlast of the rooms, where she found her elder son. Then, \xe2\x80\x9cThis is\r\nenough, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d she declared with measured softness to Newman, and turned\r\nto the marquis. He put out both his hands and took both hers, drawing\r\nher to a seat with an air of the tenderest veneration. It was a most\r\nharmonious family group, and Newman discreetly retired. He moved\r\nthrough the rooms for some time longer, circulating freely, overtopping\r\nmost people by his great height, renewing acquaintance with some of the\r\ngroups to which Urbain de Bellegarde had presented him, and expending\r\ngenerally the surplus of his equanimity. He continued to find it all\r\nextremely agreeable; but the most agreeable things have an end, and the\r\nrevelry on this occasion began to deepen to a close. The music was\r\nsounding its ultimate strains and people were looking for the marquise,\r\nto make their farewells. There seemed to be some difficulty in finding\r\nher, and Newman heard a report that she had left the ball, feeling\r\nfaint. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has succumbed to the emotions of the evening,\xe2\x80\x9d he heard a\r\nlady say. \xe2\x80\x9cPoor, dear marquise; I can imagine all that they may have\r\nbeen for her!\xe2\x80\x9d But he learned immediately afterwards that she had\r\nrecovered herself and was seated in an armchair near the doorway,\r\nreceiving parting compliments from great ladies who insisted upon her\r\nnot rising. He himself set out in quest of Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. He had\r\nseen her move past him many times in the rapid circles of a waltz, but\r\nin accordance with her explicit instructions he had exchanged no words\r\nwith her since the beginning of the evening. The whole house having\r\nbeen thrown open, the apartments of the _rez-de-chauss\xc3\xa9e_ were also\r\naccessible, though a smaller number of persons had gathered there.\r\nNewman wandered through them, observing a few scattered couples to whom\r\nthis comparative seclusion appeared grateful and reached a small\r\nconservatory which opened into the garden. The end of the conservatory\r\nwas formed by a clear sheet of glass, unmasked by plants, and admitting\r\nthe winter starlight so directly that a person standing there would\r\nseem to have passed into the open air. Two persons stood there now, a\r\nlady and a gentleman; the lady Newman, from within the room and\r\nalthough she had turned her back to it, immediately recognized as\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. He hesitated as to whether he would advance, but as\r\nhe did so she looked round, feeling apparently that he was there. She\r\nrested her eyes on him a moment and then turned again to her companion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is almost a pity not to tell Mr. Newman,\xe2\x80\x9d she said softly, but in a\r\ntone that Newman could hear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell him if you like!\xe2\x80\x9d the gentleman answered, in the voice of Lord\r\nDeepmere.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, tell me by all means!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman advancing.\r\n\r\nLord Deepmere, he observed, was very red in the face, and he had\r\ntwisted his gloves into a tight cord as if he had been squeezing them\r\ndry. These, presumably, were tokens of violent emotion, and it seemed\r\nto Newman that the traces of corresponding agitation were visible in\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s face. The two had been talking with much vivacity.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat I should tell you is only to my lord\xe2\x80\x99s credit,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9, smiling frankly enough.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like it any better for that!\xe2\x80\x9d said my lord, with his\r\nawkward laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome; what\xe2\x80\x99s the mystery?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman demanded. \xe2\x80\x9cClear it up. I don\xe2\x80\x99t like\r\nmysteries.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must have some things we don\xe2\x80\x99t like, and go without some we do,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the ruddy young nobleman, laughing still.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s to Lord Deepmere\xe2\x80\x99s credit, but it is not to everyone\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMadam de Cintr\xc3\xa9. \xe2\x80\x9cSo I shall say nothing about it. You may be sure,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe added; and she put out her hand to the Englishman, who took it half\r\nshyly, half impetuously. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd now go and dance!\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, I feel awfully like dancing!\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall go and get\r\ntipsy.\xe2\x80\x9d And he walked away with a gloomy guffaw.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat has happened between you?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t tell you\xe2\x80\x94now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. \xe2\x80\x9cNothing that need make\r\nyou unhappy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas the little Englishman been trying to make love to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe hesitated, and then she uttered a grave \xe2\x80\x9cNo! he\xe2\x80\x99s a very honest\r\nlittle fellow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you are agitated. Something is the matter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing, I repeat, that need make you unhappy. My agitation is over.\r\nSome day I will tell you what it was; not now. I can\xe2\x80\x99t now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I confess,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to hear anything\r\nunpleasant. I am satisfied with everything\xe2\x80\x94most of all with you. I have\r\nseen all the ladies and talked with a great many of them; but I am\r\nsatisfied with you.\xe2\x80\x9d Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 covered him for a moment with her\r\nlarge, soft glance, and then turned her eyes away into the starry\r\nnight. So they stood silent a moment, side by side. \xe2\x80\x9cSay you are\r\nsatisfied with me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\nHe had to wait a moment for the answer; but it came at last, low yet\r\ndistinct: \xe2\x80\x9cI am very happy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was presently followed by a few words from another source, which\r\nmade them both turn round. \xe2\x80\x9cI am sadly afraid Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 will\r\ntake a chill. I have ventured to bring a shawl.\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Bread stood there\r\nsoftly solicitous, holding a white drapery in her hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, \xe2\x80\x9cthe sight of those cold stars\r\ngives one a sense of frost. I won\xe2\x80\x99t take your shawl, but we will go\r\nback into the house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe passed back and Newman followed her, Mrs. Bread standing\r\nrespectfully aside to make way for them. Newman paused an instant\r\nbefore the old woman, and she glanced up at him with a silent greeting.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cyou must come and live with us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then, sir, if you will,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered, \xe2\x80\x9cyou have not seen the\r\nlast of me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVII\r\n\r\n\r\nNewman was fond of music and went often to the opera. A couple of\r\nevenings after Madame de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s ball he sat listening to \xe2\x80\x9cDon\r\nGiovanni,\xe2\x80\x9d having in honor of this work, which he had never yet seen\r\nrepresented, come to occupy his orchestra-chair before the rising of\r\nthe curtain. Frequently he took a large box and invited a party of his\r\ncompatriots; this was a mode of recreation to which he was much\r\naddicted. He liked making up parties of his friends and conducting them\r\nto the theatre, and taking them to drive on high drags or to dine at\r\nremote restaurants. He liked doing things which involved his paying for\r\npeople; the vulgar truth is that he enjoyed \xe2\x80\x9ctreating\xe2\x80\x9d them. This was\r\nnot because he was what is called purse-proud; handling money in public\r\nwas on the contrary positively disagreeable to him; he had a sort of\r\npersonal modesty about it, akin to what he would have felt about making\r\na toilet before spectators. But just as it was a gratification to him\r\nto be handsomely dressed, just so it was a private satisfaction to him\r\n(he enjoyed it very clandestinely) to have interposed, pecuniarily, in\r\na scheme of pleasure. To set a large group of people in motion and\r\ntransport them to a distance, to have special conveyances, to charter\r\nrailway-carriages and steamboats, harmonized with his relish for bold\r\nprocesses, and made hospitality seem more active and more to the\r\npurpose. A few evenings before the occasion of which I speak he had\r\ninvited several ladies and gentlemen to the opera to listen to Madame\r\nAlboni\xe2\x80\x94a party which included Miss Dora Finch. It befell, however, that\r\nMiss Dora Finch, sitting near Newman in the box, discoursed\r\nbrilliantly, not only during the entr\xe2\x80\x99actes, but during many of the\r\nfinest portions of the performance, so that Newman had really come away\r\nwith an irritated sense that Madame Alboni had a thin, shrill voice,\r\nand that her musical phrase was much garnished with a laugh of the\r\ngiggling order. After this he promised himself to go for a while to the\r\nopera alone.\r\n\r\nWhen the curtain had fallen upon the first act of \xe2\x80\x9cDon Giovanni\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nturned round in his place to observe the house. Presently, in one of\r\nthe boxes, he perceived Urbain de Bellegarde and his wife. The little\r\nmarquise was sweeping the house very busily with a glass, and Newman,\r\nsupposing that she saw him, determined to go and bid her good evening.\r\nM. de Bellegarde was leaning against a column, motionless, looking\r\nstraight in front of him, with one hand in the breast of his white\r\nwaistcoat and the other resting his hat on his thigh. Newman was about\r\nto leave his place when he noticed in that obscure region devoted to\r\nthe small boxes which in France are called, not inaptly,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbathing-tubs,\xe2\x80\x9d a face which even the dim light and the distance could\r\nnot make wholly indistinct. It was the face of a young and pretty\r\nwoman, and it was surmounted with a _coiffure_ of pink roses and\r\ndiamonds. This person was looking round the house, and her fan was\r\nmoving to and fro with the most practiced grace; when she lowered it,\r\nNewman perceived a pair of plump white shoulders and the edge of a\r\nrose-colored dress. Beside her, very close to the shoulders and\r\ntalking, apparently with an earnestness which it pleased her scantily\r\nto heed, sat a young man with a red face and a very low shirt-collar. A\r\nmoment\xe2\x80\x99s gazing left Newman with no doubts; the pretty young woman was\r\nNo\xc3\xa9mie Nioche. He looked hard into the depths of the box, thinking her\r\nfather might perhaps be in attendance, but from what he could see the\r\nyoung man\xe2\x80\x99s eloquence had no other auditor. Newman at last made his way\r\nout, and in doing so he passed beneath the _baignoire_ of Mademoiselle\r\nNo\xc3\xa9mie. She saw him as he approached and gave him a nod and smile which\r\nseemed meant as an assurance that she was still a good-natured girl, in\r\nspite of her enviable rise in the world. Newman passed into the _foyer_\r\nand walked through it. Suddenly he paused in front of a gentleman\r\nseated on one of the divans. The gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s elbows were on his knees;\r\nhe was leaning forward and staring at the pavement, lost apparently in\r\nmeditations of a somewhat gloomy cast. But in spite of his bent head\r\nNewman recognized him, and in a moment sat down beside him. Then the\r\ngentleman looked up and displayed the expressive countenance of\r\nValentin de Bellegarde.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat in the world are you thinking of so hard?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA subject that requires hard thinking to do it justice,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nValentin. \xe2\x80\x9cMy immeasurable idiocy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the matter now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe matter now is that I am a man again, and no more a fool than\r\nusual. But I came within an inch of taking that girl _au s\xc3\xa9rieux_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean the young lady below stairs, in a _baignoire_ in a pink\r\ndress?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you notice what a brilliant kind of pink it was?\xe2\x80\x9d Valentin\r\ninquired, by way of answer. \xe2\x80\x9cIt makes her look as white as new milk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhite or black, as you please. But you have stopped going to see her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, bless you, no. Why should I stop? I have changed, but she hasn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Valentin. \xe2\x80\x9cI see she is a vulgar little wretch, after all. But she\r\nis as amusing as ever, and one _must_ be amused.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I am glad she strikes you so unpleasantly,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman rejoiced. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nsuppose you have swallowed all those fine words you used about her the\r\nother night. You compared her to a sapphire, or a topaz, or an\r\namethyst\xe2\x80\x94some precious stone; what was it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t remember,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin, \xe2\x80\x9cit may have been to a carbuncle!\r\nBut she won\xe2\x80\x99t make a fool of me now. She has no real charm. It\xe2\x80\x99s an\r\nawfully low thing to make a mistake about a person of that sort.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI congratulate you,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman declared, \xe2\x80\x9cupon the scales having fallen\r\nfrom your eyes. It\xe2\x80\x99s a great triumph; it ought to make you feel\r\nbetter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it makes me feel better!\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin, gaily. Then, checking\r\nhimself, he looked askance at Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI rather think you are laughing\r\nat me. If you were not one of the family I would take it up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, I\xe2\x80\x99m not laughing, any more than I am one of the family. You\r\nmake me feel badly. You are too clever a fellow, you are made of too\r\ngood stuff, to spend your time in ups and downs over that class of\r\ngoods. The idea of splitting hairs about Miss Nioche! It seems to me\r\nawfully foolish. You say you have given up taking her seriously; but\r\nyou take her seriously so long as you take her at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nValentin turned round in his place and looked a while at Newman,\r\nwrinkling his forehead and rubbing his knees. \xe2\x80\x9c_Vous parlez d\xe2\x80\x99or_. But\r\nshe has wonderfully pretty arms. Would you believe I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know it\r\ntill this evening?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut she is a vulgar little wretch, remember, all the same,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nNewman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; the other day she had the bad taste to begin to abuse her father,\r\nto his face, in my presence. I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have expected it of her; it\r\nwas a disappointment; heigho!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, she cares no more for her father than for her door-mat,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nNewman. \xe2\x80\x9cI discovered that the first time I saw her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that\xe2\x80\x99s another affair; she may think of the poor old beggar what\r\nshe pleases. But it was low in her to call him bad names; it quite\r\nthrew me off. It was about a frilled petticoat that he was to have\r\nfetched from the washer-woman\xe2\x80\x99s; he appeared to have neglected this\r\ngraceful duty. She almost boxed his ears. He stood there staring at her\r\nwith his little blank eyes and smoothing his old hat with his\r\ncoat-tail. At last he turned round and went out without a word. Then I\r\ntold her it was in very bad taste to speak so to one\xe2\x80\x99s papa. She said\r\nshe should be so thankful to me if I would mention it to her whenever\r\nher taste was at fault; she had immense confidence in mine. I told her\r\nI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have the bother of forming her manners; I had had an idea\r\nthey were already formed, after the best models. She had disappointed\r\nme. But I shall get over it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin, gaily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, time\xe2\x80\x99s a great consoler!\xe2\x80\x9d Newman answered with humorous sobriety.\r\nHe was silent a moment, and then he added, in another tone, \xe2\x80\x9cI wish you\r\nwould think of what I said to you the other day. Come over to America\r\nwith us, and I will put you in the way of doing some business. You have\r\na very good head, if you will only use it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nValentin made a genial grimace. \xe2\x80\x9cMy head is much obliged to you. Do you\r\nmean the place in a bank?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are several places, but I suppose you would consider the bank\r\nthe most aristocratic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nValentin burst into a laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear fellow, at night all cats are\r\ngray! When one derogates there are no degrees.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman answered nothing for a minute. Then, \xe2\x80\x9cI think you will find\r\nthere are degrees in success,\xe2\x80\x9d he said with a certain dryness.\r\n\r\nValentin had leaned forward again, with his elbows on his knees, and he\r\nwas scratching the pavement with his stick. At last he said, looking\r\nup, \xe2\x80\x9cDo you really think I ought to do something?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman laid his hand on his companion\xe2\x80\x99s arm and looked at him a moment\r\nthrough sagaciously-narrowed eyelids. \xe2\x80\x9cTry it and see. You are not good\r\nenough for it, but we will stretch a point.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you really think I can make some money? I should like to see how it\r\nfeels to have a little.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo what I tell you, and you shall be rich,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cThink of\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d And he looked at his watch and prepared to resume his way to\r\nMadame de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s box.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon my word I will think of it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin. \xe2\x80\x9cI will go and listen\r\nto Mozart another half hour\xe2\x80\x94I can always think better to music\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nprofoundly meditate upon it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe marquis was with his wife when Newman entered their box; he was\r\nbland, remote, and correct as usual; or, as it seemed to Newman, even\r\nmore than usual.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think of the opera?\xe2\x80\x9d asked our hero. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think of\r\nthe Don?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe all know what Mozart is,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquis; \xe2\x80\x9cour impressions don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndate from this evening. Mozart is youth, freshness, brilliancy,\r\nfacility\xe2\x80\x94a little too great facility, perhaps. But the execution is\r\nhere and there deplorably rough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very curious to see how it ends,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou speak as if it were a _feuilleton_ in the _Figaro_,\xe2\x80\x9d observed the\r\nmarquis. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have surely seen the opera before?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI am sure I should have remembered it. Donna\r\nElvira reminds me of Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9; I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean in her\r\ncircumstances, but in the music she sings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a very nice distinction,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed the marquis lightly. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is\r\nno great possibility, I imagine, of Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 being forsaken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot much!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cBut what becomes of the Don?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe devil comes down\xe2\x80\x94or comes up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde, \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\ncarries him off. I suppose Zerlina reminds you of me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will go to the _foyer_ for a few moments,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquis, \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\ngive you a chance to say that the commander\xe2\x80\x94the man of stone\xe2\x80\x94resembles\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d And he passed out of the box.\r\n\r\nThe little marquise stared an instant at the velvet ledge of the\r\nbalcony, and then murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cNot a man of stone, a man of wood.\xe2\x80\x9d Newman\r\nhad taken her husband\xe2\x80\x99s empty chair. She made no protest, and then she\r\nturned suddenly and laid her closed fan upon his arm. \xe2\x80\x9cI am very glad\r\nyou came in,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI want to ask you a favor. I wanted to do so\r\non Thursday, at my mother-in-law\xe2\x80\x99s ball, but you would give me no\r\nchance. You were in such very good spirits that I thought you might\r\ngrant my little favor then; not that you look particularly doleful now.\r\nIt is something you must promise me; now is the time to take you; after\r\nyou are married you will be good for nothing. Come, promise!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never sign a paper without reading it first,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cShow me\r\nyour document.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, you must sign with your eyes shut; I will hold your hand. Come,\r\nbefore you put your head into the noose. You ought to be thankful to me\r\nfor giving you a chance to do something amusing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it is so amusing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cit will be in even better season\r\nafter I am married.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn other words,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Madame de Bellegarde, \xe2\x80\x9cyou will not do it at\r\nall. You will be afraid of your wife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, if the thing is intrinsically improper,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t go\r\ninto it. If it is not, I will do it after my marriage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou talk like a treatise on logic, and English logic into the\r\nbargain!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Madame de Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cPromise, then, after you are\r\nmarried. After all, I shall enjoy keeping you to it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, after I am married,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman serenely.\r\n\r\nThe little marquise hesitated a moment, looking at him, and he wondered\r\nwhat was coming. \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you know what my life is,\xe2\x80\x9d she presently\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cI have no pleasure, I see nothing, I do nothing. I live in Paris\r\nas I might live at Poitiers. My mother-in-law calls me\xe2\x80\x94what is the\r\npretty word?\xe2\x80\x94a gad-about? accuses me of going to unheard-of places, and\r\nthinks it ought to be joy enough for me to sit at home and count over\r\nmy ancestors on my fingers. But why should I bother about my ancestors?\r\nI am sure they never bothered about me. I don\xe2\x80\x99t propose to live with a\r\ngreen shade on my eyes; I hold that things were made to look at. My\r\nhusband, you know, has principles, and the first on the list is that\r\nthe Tuileries are dreadfully vulgar. If the Tuileries are vulgar, his\r\nprinciples are tiresome. If I chose I might have principles quite as\r\nwell as he. If they grew on one\xe2\x80\x99s family tree I should only have to\r\ngive mine a shake to bring down a shower of the finest. At any rate, I\r\nprefer clever Bonapartes to stupid Bourbons.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I see; you want to go to court,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, vaguely conjecturing\r\nthat she might wish him to appeal to the United States legation to\r\nsmooth her way to the imperial halls.\r\n\r\nThe marquise gave a little sharp laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are a thousand miles away.\r\nI will take care of the Tuileries myself; the day I decide to go they\r\nwill be very glad to have me. Sooner or later I shall dance in an\r\nimperial quadrille. I know what you are going to say: \xe2\x80\x98How will you\r\ndare?\xe2\x80\x99 But I _shall_ dare. I am afraid of my husband; he is soft,\r\nsmooth, irreproachable; everything that you know; but I am afraid of\r\nhim\xe2\x80\x94horribly afraid of him. And yet I shall arrive at the Tuileries.\r\nBut that will not be this winter, nor perhaps next, and meantime I must\r\nlive. For the moment, I want to go somewhere else; it\xe2\x80\x99s my dream. I\r\nwant to go to the Bal Bullier.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo the Bal Bullier?\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Newman, for whom the words at first\r\nmeant nothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe ball in the Latin Quarter, where the students dance with their\r\nmistresses. Don\xe2\x80\x99t tell me you have not heard of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman; \xe2\x80\x9cI have heard of it; I remember now. I have even\r\nbeen there. And you want to go there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is silly, it is low, it is anything you please. But I want to go.\r\nSome of my friends have been, and they say it is awfully _dr\xc3\xb4le_. My\r\nfriends go everywhere; it is only I who sit moping at home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to me you are not at home now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cand I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nexactly say you were moping.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am bored to death. I have been to the opera twice a week for the\r\nlast eight years. Whenever I ask for anything my mouth is stopped with\r\nthat: Pray, madam, haven\xe2\x80\x99t you an opera box? Could a woman of taste\r\nwant more? In the first place, my opera box was down in my _contrat_;\r\nthey have to give it to me. To-night, for instance, I should have\r\npreferred a thousand times to go to the Palais Royal. But my husband\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t go to the Palais Royal because the ladies of the court go there\r\nso much. You may imagine, then, whether he would take me to Bullier\xe2\x80\x99s;\r\nhe says it is a mere imitation\xe2\x80\x94and a bad one\xe2\x80\x94of what they do at the\r\nPrincess Kleinfuss\xe2\x80\x99s. But as I don\xe2\x80\x99t go to the Princess Kleinfuss\xe2\x80\x99s,\r\nthe next best thing is to go to Bullier\xe2\x80\x99s. It is my dream, at any rate,\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s a fixed idea. All I ask of you is to give me your arm; you are\r\nless compromising than anyone else. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know why, but you are. I\r\ncan arrange it. I shall risk something, but that is my own affair.\r\nBesides, fortune favors the bold. Don\xe2\x80\x99t refuse me; it is my dream!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman gave a loud laugh. It seemed to him hardly worth while to be the\r\nwife of the Marquis de Bellegarde, a daughter of the crusaders, heiress\r\nof six centuries of glories and traditions, to have centred one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\naspirations upon the sight of a couple of hundred young ladies kicking\r\noff young men\xe2\x80\x99s hats. It struck him as a theme for the moralist; but he\r\nhad no time to moralize upon it. The curtain rose again; M. de\r\nBellegarde returned, and Newman went back to his seat.\r\n\r\nHe observed that Valentin de Bellegarde had taken his place in the\r\n_baignoire_ of Mademoiselle Nioche, behind this young lady and her\r\ncompanion, where he was visible only if one carefully looked for him.\r\nIn the next act Newman met him in the lobby and asked him if he had\r\nreflected upon possible emigration. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you really meant to meditate,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said, \xe2\x80\x9cyou might have chosen a better place for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, the place was not bad,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin. \xe2\x80\x9cI was not thinking of that\r\ngirl. I listened to the music, and, without thinking of the play or\r\nlooking at the stage, I turned over your proposal. At first it seemed\r\nquite fantastic. And then a certain fiddle in the orchestra\xe2\x80\x94I could\r\ndistinguish it\xe2\x80\x94began to say as it scraped away, \xe2\x80\x98Why not, why not?\xe2\x80\x99 And\r\nthen, in that rapid movement, all the fiddles took it up and the\r\nconductor\xe2\x80\x99s stick seemed to beat it in the air: \xe2\x80\x98Why not, why not?\xe2\x80\x99 I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nsure I can\xe2\x80\x99t say! I don\xe2\x80\x99t see why not. I don\xe2\x80\x99t see why I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do\r\nsomething. It appears to me really a very bright idea. This sort of\r\nthing is certainly very stale. And then I could come back with a trunk\r\nfull of dollars. Besides, I might possibly find it amusing. They call\r\nme a _raffin\xc3\xa9_; who knows but that I might discover an unsuspected\r\ncharm in shop-keeping? It would really have a certain romantic,\r\npicturesque side; it would look well in my biography. It would look as\r\nif I were a strong man, a first-rate man, a man who dominated\r\ncircumstances.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind how it would look,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cIt always looks well to\r\nhave half a million of dollars. There is no reason why you shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhave them if you will mind what I tell you\xe2\x80\x94I alone\xe2\x80\x94and not talk to\r\nother parties.\xe2\x80\x9d He passed his arm into that of his companion, and the\r\ntwo walked for some time up and down one of the less frequented\r\ncorridors. Newman\xe2\x80\x99s imagination began to glow with the idea of\r\nconverting his bright, impracticable friend into a first-class man of\r\nbusiness. He felt for the moment a sort of spiritual zeal, the zeal of\r\nthe propagandist. Its ardor was in part the result of that general\r\ndiscomfort which the sight of all uninvested capital produced in him;\r\nso fine an intelligence as Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s ought to be dedicated to high\r\nuses. The highest uses known to Newman\xe2\x80\x99s experience were certain\r\ntranscendent sagacities in the handling of railway stock. And then his\r\nzeal was quickened by his personal kindness for Valentin; he had a sort\r\nof pity for him which he was well aware he never could have made the\r\nComte de Bellegarde understand. He never lost a sense of its being\r\npitiable that Valentin should think it a large life to revolve in\r\nvarnished boots between the Rue d\xe2\x80\x99Anjou and the Rue de l\xe2\x80\x99Universit\xc3\xa9,\r\ntaking the Boulevard des Italiens on the way, when over there in\r\nAmerica one\xe2\x80\x99s promenade was a continent, and one\xe2\x80\x99s Boulevard stretched\r\nfrom New York to San Francisco. It mortified him, moreover, to think\r\nthat Valentin lacked money; there was a painful grotesqueness in it. It\r\naffected him as the ignorance of a companion, otherwise without\r\nreproach, touching some rudimentary branch of learning would have done.\r\nThere were things that one knew about as a matter of course, he would\r\nhave said in such a case. Just so, if one pretended to be easy in the\r\nworld, one had money as a matter of course, one had made it!
