There was\r\nsomething almost ridiculously anomalous to Newman in the sight of\r\nlively pretensions unaccompanied by large investments in railroads;\r\nthough I may add that he would not have maintained that such\r\ninvestments were in themselves a proper ground for pretensions. \xe2\x80\x9cI will\r\nmake you do something,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to Valentin; \xe2\x80\x9cI will put you through. I\r\nknow half a dozen things in which we can make a place for you. You will\r\nsee some lively work. It will take you a little while to get used to\r\nthe life, but you will work in before long, and at the end of six\r\nmonths\xe2\x80\x94after you have done a thing or two on your own account\xe2\x80\x94you will\r\nlike it. And then it will be very pleasant for you, having your sister\r\nover there. It will be pleasant for her to have you, too. Yes,\r\nValentin,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Newman, pressing his friend\xe2\x80\x99s arm genially, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nthink I see just the opening for you. Keep quiet and I\xe2\x80\x99ll push you\r\nright in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman pursued this favoring strain for some time longer. The two men\r\nstrolled about for a quarter of an hour. Valentin listened and\r\nquestioned, many of his questions making Newman laugh loud at the\r\n_na\xc3\xafvet\xc3\xa9_ of his ignorance of the vulgar processes of money-getting;\r\nsmiling himself, too, half ironical and half curious. And yet he was\r\nserious; he was fascinated by Newman\xe2\x80\x99s plain prose version of the\r\nlegend of El Dorado. It is true, however, that though to accept an\r\n\xe2\x80\x9copening\xe2\x80\x9d in an American mercantile house might be a bold, original,\r\nand in its consequences extremely agreeable thing to do, he did not\r\nquite see himself objectively doing it. So that when the bell rang to\r\nindicate the close of the entr\xe2\x80\x99acte, there was a certain mock-heroism\r\nin his saying, with his brilliant smile, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, put me through;\r\npush me in! I make myself over to you. Dip me into the pot and turn me\r\ninto gold.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey had passed into the corridor which encircled the row of\r\n_baignoires_, and Valentin stopped in front of the dusky little box in\r\nwhich Mademoiselle Nioche had bestowed herself, laying his hand on the\r\ndoorknob. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, come, are you going back there?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Mon Dieu, oui_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t you another place?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I have my usual place, in the stalls.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had better go and occupy it, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see her very well from there, too,\xe2\x80\x9d added Valentin, serenely, \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nto-night she is worth seeing. But,\xe2\x80\x9d he added in a moment, \xe2\x80\x9cI have a\r\nparticular reason for going back just now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I give you up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are infatuated!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it is only this. There is a young man in the box whom I shall\r\nannoy by going in, and I want to annoy him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry to hear it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t you leave the poor fellow\r\nalone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, he has given me cause. The box is not his. No\xc3\xa9mie came in alone\r\nand installed herself. I went and spoke to her, and in a few moments\r\nshe asked me to go and get her fan from the pocket of her cloak, which\r\nthe _ouvreuse_ had carried off. In my absence this gentleman came in\r\nand took the chair beside No\xc3\xa9mie in which I had been sitting. My\r\nreappearance disgusted him, and he had the grossness to show it. He\r\ncame within an ace of being impertinent. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know who he is; he is\r\nsome vulgar wretch. I can\xe2\x80\x99t think where she picks up such\r\nacquaintances. He has been drinking, too, but he knows what he is\r\nabout. Just now, in the second act, he was unmannerly again. I shall\r\nput in another appearance for ten minutes\xe2\x80\x94time enough to give him an\r\nopportunity to commit himself, if he feels inclined. I really can\xe2\x80\x99t let\r\nthe brute suppose that he is keeping me out of the box.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear fellow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, remonstrantly, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat child\xe2\x80\x99s play! You\r\nare not going to pick a quarrel about that girl, I hope.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat girl has nothing to do with it, and I have no intention of\r\npicking a quarrel. I am not a bully nor a fire-eater. I simply wish to\r\nmake a point that a gentleman must.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, damn your point!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is the trouble with you\r\nFrenchmen; you must be always making points. Well,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, \xe2\x80\x9cbe\r\nshort. But if you are going in for this kind of thing, we must ship you\r\noff to America in advance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good,\xe2\x80\x9d Valentin answered, \xe2\x80\x9cwhenever you please. But if I go to\r\nAmerica, I must not let this gentleman suppose that it is to run away\r\nfrom him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd they separated. At the end of the act Newman observed that Valentin\r\nwas still in the _baignoire_. He strolled into the corridor again,\r\nexpecting to meet him, and when he was within a few yards of\r\nMademoiselle Nioche\xe2\x80\x99s box saw his friend pass out, accompanied by the\r\nyoung man who had been seated beside its fair occupant. The two\r\ngentlemen walked with some quickness of step to a distant part of the\r\nlobby, where Newman perceived them stop and stand talking. The manner\r\nof each was perfectly quiet, but the stranger, who looked flushed, had\r\nbegun to wipe his face very emphatically with his pocket-handkerchief.\r\nBy this time Newman was abreast of the _baignoire_; the door had been\r\nleft ajar, and he could see a pink dress inside. He immediately went\r\nin. Mademoiselle Nioche turned and greeted him with a brilliant smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you have at last decided to come and see me?\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\njust save your politeness. You find me in a fine moment. Sit down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThere was a very becoming little flush in her cheek, and her eye had a\r\nnoticeable spark. You would have said that she had received some very\r\ngood news.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething has happened here!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, without sitting down.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou find me in a very fine moment,\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cTwo gentlemen\xe2\x80\x94one\r\nof them is M. de Bellegarde, the pleasure of whose acquaintance I owe\r\nto you\xe2\x80\x94have just had words about your humble servant. Very big words\r\ntoo. They can\xe2\x80\x99t come off without crossing swords. A duel\xe2\x80\x94that will give\r\nme a push!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie clapping her little hands.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_C\xe2\x80\x99est \xc3\xa7a qui pose une femme!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t mean to say that Bellegarde is going to fight about _you!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nexclaimed Newman disgustedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing else!\xe2\x80\x9d and she looked at him with a hard little smile. \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\r\nno, you are not _galant!_ And if you prevent this affair I shall owe\r\nyou a grudge\xe2\x80\x94and pay my debt!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman uttered an imprecation which, though brief\xe2\x80\x94it consisted simply\r\nof the interjection \xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d followed by a geographical, or more\r\ncorrectly, perhaps a theological noun in four letters\xe2\x80\x94had better not be\r\ntransferred to these pages. He turned his back without more ceremony\r\nupon the pink dress and went out of the box. In the corridor he found\r\nValentin and his companion walking towards him. The latter was\r\nthrusting a card into his waistcoat pocket. Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie\xe2\x80\x99s\r\njealous votary was a tall, robust young man with a thick nose, a\r\nprominent blue eye, a Germanic physiognomy, and a massive watch-chain.\r\nWhen they reached the box, Valentin with an emphasized bow made way for\r\nhim to pass in first. Newman touched Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s arm as a sign that he\r\nwished to speak with him, and Bellegarde answered that he would be with\r\nhim in an instant. Valentin entered the box after the robust young man,\r\nbut a couple of minutes afterwards he reappeared, largely smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is immensely tickled,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cShe says we will make her\r\nfortune. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be fatuous, but I think it is very possible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you are going to fight?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear fellow, don\xe2\x80\x99t look so mortally disgusted. It was not my\r\nchoice. The thing is all arranged.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told you so!\xe2\x80\x9d groaned Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told _him_ so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did he do to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy good friend, it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter what. He used an expression\xe2\x80\x94I took\r\nit up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I insist upon knowing; I can\xe2\x80\x99t, as your elder brother, have you\r\nrushing into this sort of nonsense.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very much obliged to you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin. \xe2\x80\x9cI have nothing to\r\nconceal, but I can\xe2\x80\x99t go into particulars now and here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will leave this place, then. You can tell me outside.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, I can\xe2\x80\x99t leave this place, why should I hurry away? I will go to\r\nmy orchestra-stall and sit out the opera.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will not enjoy it; you will be preoccupied.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nValentin looked at him a moment, colored a little, smiled, and patted\r\nhim on the arm. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are delightfully simple! Before an affair a man is\r\nquiet. The quietest thing I can do is to go straight to my place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cyou want her to see you there\xe2\x80\x94you and your\r\nquietness. I am not so simple! It is a poor business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nValentin remained, and the two men, in their respective places, sat out\r\nthe rest of the performance, which was also enjoyed by Mademoiselle\r\nNioche and her truculent admirer. At the end Newman joined Valentin\r\nagain, and they went into the street together. Valentin shook his head\r\nat his friend\xe2\x80\x99s proposal that he should get into Newman\xe2\x80\x99s own vehicle,\r\nand stopped on the edge of the pavement. \xe2\x80\x9cI must go off alone,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid; \xe2\x80\x9cI must look up a couple of friends who will take charge of this\r\nmatter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will take charge of it,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman declared. \xe2\x80\x9cPut it into my hands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very kind, but that is hardly possible. In the first place,\r\nyou are, as you said just now, almost my brother; you are about to\r\nmarry my sister. That alone disqualifies you; it casts doubts on your\r\nimpartiality. And if it didn\xe2\x80\x99t, it would be enough for me that I\r\nstrongly suspect you of disapproving of the affair. You would try to\r\nprevent a meeting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I should,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cWhoever your friends are, I hope\r\nthey will do that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnquestionably they will. They will urge that excuses be made, proper\r\nexcuses. But you would be too good-natured. You won\xe2\x80\x99t do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman was silent a moment. He was keenly annoyed, but he saw it was\r\nuseless to attempt interference. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen is this precious performance to\r\ncome off?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe sooner the better,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin. \xe2\x80\x9cThe day after to-morrow, I\r\nhope.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cI have certainly a claim to know the facts. I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t consent to shut my eyes to the matter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be most happy to tell you the facts,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are\r\nvery simple, and it will be quickly done. But now everything depends on\r\nmy putting my hands on my friends without delay. I will jump into a\r\ncab; you had better drive to my room and wait for me there. I will turn\r\nup at the end of an hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman assented protestingly, let his friend go, and then betook\r\nhimself to the picturesque little apartment in the Rue d\xe2\x80\x99Anjou. It was\r\nmore than an hour before Valentin returned, but when he did so he was\r\nable to announce that he had found one of his desired friends, and that\r\nthis gentleman had taken upon himself the care of securing an\r\nassociate. Newman had been sitting without lights by Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s faded\r\nfire, upon which he had thrown a log; the blaze played over the\r\nrichly-encumbered little sitting-room and produced fantastic gleams and\r\nshadows. He listened in silence to Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s account of what had\r\npassed between him and the gentleman whose card he had in his pocket\xe2\x80\x94M.\r\nStanislas Kapp, of Strasbourg\xe2\x80\x94after his return to Mademoiselle Nioche\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbox. This hospitable young lady had espied an acquaintance on the other\r\nside of the house, and had expressed her displeasure at his not having\r\nthe civility to come and pay her a visit. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, let him alone!\xe2\x80\x9d M.\r\nStanislas Kapp had hereupon exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are too many people in\r\nthe box already.\xe2\x80\x9d And he had fixed his eyes with a demonstrative stare\r\nupon M. de Bellegarde. Valentin had promptly retorted that if there\r\nwere too many people in the box it was easy for M. Kapp to diminish the\r\nnumber. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall be most happy to open the door for _you!_\xe2\x80\x9d M. Kapp\r\nexclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall be delighted to fling you into the pit!\xe2\x80\x9d Valentin\r\nhad answered. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, do make a rumpus and get into the papers!\xe2\x80\x9d Miss\r\nNo\xc3\xa9mie had gleefully ejaculated. \xe2\x80\x9cM. Kapp, turn him out; or, M. de\r\nBellegarde, pitch him into the pit, into the orchestra\xe2\x80\x94anywhere! I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t care who does which, so long as you make a scene.\xe2\x80\x9d Valentin\r\nanswered that they would make no scene, but that the gentleman would be\r\nso good as to step into the corridor with him. In the corridor, after a\r\nbrief further exchange of words, there had been an exchange of cards.\r\nM. Stanislas Kapp was very stiff. He evidently meant to force his\r\noffence home.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe man, no doubt, was insolent,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman said; \xe2\x80\x9cbut if you hadn\xe2\x80\x99t gone\r\nback into the box the thing wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have happened.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, don\xe2\x80\x99t you see,\xe2\x80\x9d Valentin replied, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the event proves the\r\nextreme propriety of my going back into the box? M. Kapp wished to\r\nprovoke me; he was awaiting his chance. In such a case\xe2\x80\x94that is, when he\r\nhas been, so to speak, notified\xe2\x80\x94a man must be on hand to receive the\r\nprovocation. My not returning would simply have been tantamount to my\r\nsaying to M. Stanislas Kapp, \xe2\x80\x98Oh, if you are going to be\r\ndisagreeable\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94 \xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98You must manage it by yourself; damned if I\xe2\x80\x99ll help you!\xe2\x80\x99 That would\r\nhave been a thoroughly sensible thing to say. The only attraction for\r\nyou seems to have been the prospect of M. Kapp\xe2\x80\x99s impertinence,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman\r\nwent on. \xe2\x80\x9cYou told me you were not going back for that girl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t mention that girl any more,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Valentin. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nbore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith all my heart. But if that is the way you feel about her, why\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you let her alone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nValentin shook his head with a fine smile. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think you quite\r\nunderstand, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe I can make you. She understood the\r\nsituation; she knew what was in the air; she was watching us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA cat may look at a king! What difference does that make?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, a man can\xe2\x80\x99t back down before a woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t call her a woman. You said yourself she was a stone,\xe2\x80\x9d cried\r\nNewman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d Valentin rejoined, \xe2\x80\x9cthere is no disputing about tastes. It\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nmatter of feeling; it\xe2\x80\x99s measured by one\xe2\x80\x99s sense of honor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, confound your sense of honor!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is vain talking,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin; \xe2\x80\x9cwords have passed, and the thing\r\nis settled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman turned away, taking his hat. Then pausing with his hand on the\r\ndoor, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you going to use?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is for M. Stanislas Kapp, as the challenged party, to decide. My\r\nown choice would be a short, light sword. I handle it well. I\xe2\x80\x99m an\r\nindifferent shot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman had put on his hat; he pushed it back, gently scratching his\r\nforehead, high up. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish it were pistols,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI could show you\r\nhow to lodge a bullet!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nValentin broke into a laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it some English poet says about\r\nconsistency? It\xe2\x80\x99s a flower, or a star, or a jewel. Yours has the beauty\r\nof all three!\xe2\x80\x9d But he agreed to see Newman again on the morrow, after\r\nthe details of his meeting with M. Stanislas Kapp should have been\r\narranged.\r\n\r\nIn the course of the day Newman received three lines from him, saying\r\nthat it had been decided that he should cross the frontier, with his\r\nadversary, and that he was to take the night express to Geneva. He\r\nshould have time, however, to dine with Newman. In the afternoon Newman\r\ncalled upon Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, but his visit was brief. She was as\r\ngracious and sympathetic as he had ever found her, but she was sad, and\r\nshe confessed, on Newman\xe2\x80\x99s charging her with her red eyes, that she had\r\nbeen crying. Valentin had been with her a couple of hours before, and\r\nhis visit had left her with a painful impression. He had laughed and\r\ngossiped, he had brought her no bad news, he had only been, in his\r\nmanner, rather more affectionate than usual. His fraternal tenderness\r\nhad touched her, and on his departure she had burst into tears. She had\r\nfelt as if something strange and sad were going to happen; she had\r\ntried to reason away the fancy, and the effort had only given her a\r\nheadache. Newman, of course, was perforce tongue-tied about Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nprojected duel, and his dramatic talent was not equal to satirizing\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s presentiment as pointedly as perfect security\r\ndemanded. Before he went away he asked Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 whether\r\nValentin had seen his mother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut he didn\xe2\x80\x99t make her cry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was in Newman\xe2\x80\x99s own apartment that Valentin dined, having brought\r\nhis portmanteau, so that he might adjourn directly to the railway. M.\r\nStanislas Kapp had positively declined to make excuses, and he, on his\r\nside, obviously, had none to offer. Valentin had found out with whom he\r\nwas dealing. M. Stanislas Kapp was the son of and heir of a rich brewer\r\nof Strasbourg, a youth of a sanguineous\xe2\x80\x94and sanguinary\xe2\x80\x94temperament. He\r\nwas making ducks and drakes of the paternal brewery, and although he\r\npassed in a general way for a good fellow, he had already been observed\r\nto be quarrelsome after dinner. \xe2\x80\x9c_Que voulez-vous?_\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBrought up on beer, he can\xe2\x80\x99t stand champagne.\xe2\x80\x9d He had chosen pistols.\r\nValentin, at dinner, had an excellent appetite; he made a point, in\r\nview of his long journey, of eating more than usual. He took the\r\nliberty of suggesting to Newman a slight modification in the\r\ncomposition of a certain fish-sauce; he thought it would be worth\r\nmentioning to the cook. But Newman had no thoughts for fish-sauce; he\r\nfelt thoroughly discontented. As he sat and watched his amiable and\r\nclever companion going through his excellent repast with the delicate\r\ndeliberation of hereditary epicurism, the folly of so charming a fellow\r\ntraveling off to expose his agreeable young life for the sake of M.\r\nStanislas and Mademoiselle No\xc3\xa9mie struck him with intolerable force. He\r\nhad grown fond of Valentin, he felt now how fond; and his sense of\r\nhelplessness only increased his irritation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, this sort of thing may be all very well,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried at last, \xe2\x80\x9cbut\r\nI declare I don\xe2\x80\x99t see it. I can\xe2\x80\x99t stop you, perhaps, but at least I can\r\nprotest. I do protest, violently.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear fellow, don\xe2\x80\x99t make a scene,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin. \xe2\x80\x9cScenes in these\r\ncases are in very bad taste.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour duel itself is a scene,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman; \xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s all it is! It\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nwretched theatrical affair. Why don\xe2\x80\x99t you take a band of music with you\r\noutright? It\xe2\x80\x99s d\xe2\x80\x94d barbarous and it\xe2\x80\x99s d\xe2\x80\x94d corrupt, both.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I can\xe2\x80\x99t begin, at this time of day, to defend the theory of\r\ndueling,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is our custom, and I think it is a good\r\nthing. Quite apart from the goodness of the cause in which a duel may\r\nbe fought, it has a kind of picturesque charm which in this age of vile\r\nprose seems to me greatly to recommend it. It\xe2\x80\x99s a remnant of a\r\nhigher-tempered time; one ought to cling to it. Depend upon it, a duel\r\nis never amiss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you mean by a higher-tempered time,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause your great-grandfather was an ass, is that any reason why you\r\nshould be? For my part I think we had better let our temper take care\r\nof itself; it generally seems to me quite high enough; I am not afraid\r\nof being too meek. If your great-grandfather were to make himself\r\nunpleasant to me, I think I could manage him yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear friend,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin, smiling, \xe2\x80\x9cyou can\xe2\x80\x99t invent anything\r\nthat will take the place of satisfaction for an insult. To demand it\r\nand to give it are equally excellent arrangements.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you call this sort of thing satisfaction?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman asked. \xe2\x80\x9cDoes it\r\nsatisfy you to receive a present of the carcass of that coarse fop?\r\ndoes it gratify you to make him a present of yours? If a man hits you,\r\nhit him back; if a man libels you, haul him up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHaul him up, into court? Oh, that is very nasty!\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe nastiness is his\xe2\x80\x94not yours. And for that matter, what you are\r\ndoing is not particularly nice. You are too good for it. I don\xe2\x80\x99t say\r\nyou are the most useful man in the world, or the cleverest, or the most\r\namiable. But you are too good to go and get your throat cut for a\r\nprostitute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nValentin flushed a little, but he laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cI shan\xe2\x80\x99t get my throat cut\r\nif I can help it. Moreover, one\xe2\x80\x99s honor hasn\xe2\x80\x99t two different measures.\r\nIt only knows that it is hurt; it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t ask when, or how, or where.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe more fool it is!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\nValentin ceased to laugh; he looked grave. \xe2\x80\x9cI beg you not to say any\r\nmore,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you do I shall almost fancy you don\xe2\x80\x99t care\r\nabout\xe2\x80\x94about\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and he paused.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout that matter\xe2\x80\x94about one\xe2\x80\x99s honor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFancy what you please,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cFancy while you are at it that I\r\ncare about _you_\xe2\x80\x94though you are not worth it. But come back without\r\ndamage,\xe2\x80\x9d he added in a moment, \xe2\x80\x9cand I will forgive you. And then,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ncontinued, as Valentin was going, \xe2\x80\x9cI will ship you straight off to\r\nAmerica.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Valentin, \xe2\x80\x9cif I am to turn over a new page, this may\r\nfigure as a tail-piece to the old.\xe2\x80\x9d And then he lit another cigar and\r\ndeparted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlast that girl!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman as the door closed upon Valentin.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVIII\r\n\r\n\r\nNewman went the next morning to see Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, timing his visit\r\nso as to arrive after the noonday breakfast. In the court of the\r\n_h\xc3\xb4tel_, before the portico, stood Madame de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s old square\r\ncarriage. The servant who opened the door answered Newman\xe2\x80\x99s inquiry\r\nwith a slightly embarrassed and hesitating murmur, and at the same\r\nmoment Mrs. Bread appeared in the background, dim-visaged as usual, and\r\nwearing a large black bonnet and shawl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cIs Madame la Comtesse at home, or\r\nnot?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread advanced, fixing her eyes upon him: he observed that she\r\nheld a sealed letter, very delicately, in her fingers. \xe2\x80\x9cThe countess\r\nhas left a message for you, sir; she has left this,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread,\r\nholding out the letter, which Newman took.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLeft it? Is she out? Is she gone away?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is going away, sir; she is leaving town,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLeaving town!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat has happened?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not for me to say, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread, with her eyes on the\r\nground. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I thought it would come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat would come, pray?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman demanded. He had broken the seal of the\r\nletter, but he still questioned. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is in the house? She is visible?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think she expected you this morning,\xe2\x80\x9d the old waiting-woman\r\nreplied. \xe2\x80\x9cShe was to leave immediately.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is she going?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo Fleuri\xc3\xa8res.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo Fleuri\xc3\xa8res? But surely I can see her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread hesitated a moment, and then clasping together her two\r\nhands, \xe2\x80\x9cI will take you!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. And she led the way upstairs. At\r\nthe top of the staircase she paused and fixed her dry, sad eyes upon\r\nNewman. \xe2\x80\x9cBe very easy with her,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cshe is most unhappy!\xe2\x80\x9d Then\r\nshe went on to Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s apartment; Newman, perplexed and\r\nalarmed, followed her rapidly. Mrs. Bread threw open the door, and\r\nNewman pushed back the curtain at the farther side of its deep\r\nembrasure. In the middle of the room stood Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9; her face\r\nwas pale and she was dressed for traveling. Behind her, before the\r\nfire-place, stood Urbain de Bellegarde, looking at his finger-nails;\r\nnear the marquis sat his mother, buried in an armchair, and with her\r\neyes immediately fixing themselves upon Newman. He felt, as soon as he\r\nentered the room, that he was in the presence of something evil; he was\r\nstartled and pained, as he would have been by a threatening cry in the\r\nstillness of the night. He walked straight to Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 and\r\nseized her by the hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked commandingly; \xe2\x80\x9cwhat is happening?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUrbain de Bellegarde stared, then left his place and came and leaned\r\nupon his mother\xe2\x80\x99s chair, behind. Newman\xe2\x80\x99s sudden irruption had\r\nevidently discomposed both mother and son. Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 stood\r\nsilent, with her eyes resting upon Newman\xe2\x80\x99s. She had often looked at\r\nhim with all her soul, as it seemed to him; but in this present gaze\r\nthere was a sort of bottomless depth. She was in distress; it was the\r\nmost touching thing he had ever seen. His heart rose into his throat,\r\nand he was on the point of turning to her companions, with an angry\r\nchallenge; but she checked him, pressing the hand that held her own.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething very grave has happened,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI cannot marry you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman dropped her hand and stood staring, first at her and then at the\r\nothers. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, as quietly as possible.\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 almost smiled, but the attempt was strange. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must\r\nask my mother, you must ask my brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy can\xe2\x80\x99t she marry me?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, looking at them.\r\n\r\nMadame de Bellegarde did not move in her place, but she was as pale as\r\nher daughter. The marquis looked down at her. She said nothing for some\r\nmoments, but she kept her keen, clear eyes upon Newman, bravely. The\r\nmarquis drew himself up and looked at the ceiling. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s impossible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said softly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s improper,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde.\r\n\r\nNewman began to laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, you are fooling!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy sister, you have no time; you are losing your train,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nmarquis.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, is he mad?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; don\xe2\x80\x99t think that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I am going away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are you going?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo the country, to Fleuri\xc3\xa8res; to be alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo leave me?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, slowly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t see you, now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Now_\xe2\x80\x94why not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am ashamed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, simply.\r\n\r\nNewman turned toward the marquis. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat have you done to her\xe2\x80\x94what does\r\nit mean?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked with the same effort at calmness, the fruit of his\r\nconstant practice in taking things easily. He was excited, but\r\nexcitement with him was only an intenser deliberateness; it was the\r\nswimmer stripped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt means that I have given you up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. \xe2\x80\x9cIt means\r\nthat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer face was too charged with tragic expression not fully to confirm\r\nher words. Newman was profoundly shocked, but he felt as yet no\r\nresentment against her. He was amazed, bewildered, and the presence of\r\nthe old marquise and her son seemed to smite his eyes like the glare of\r\na watchman\xe2\x80\x99s lantern. \xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t I see you alone?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be only more painful. I hoped I should not see you\xe2\x80\x94I should\r\nescape. I wrote to you. Good-bye.\xe2\x80\x9d And she put out her hand again.\r\n\r\nNewman put both his own into his pockets. \xe2\x80\x9cI will go with you,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\nShe laid her two hands on his arm. \xe2\x80\x9cWill you grant me a last request?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nand as she looked at him, urging this, her eyes filled with tears. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\r\nme go alone\xe2\x80\x94let me go in peace. I can\xe2\x80\x99t call it peace\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s death. But\r\nlet me bury myself. So\xe2\x80\x94good-bye.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman passed his hand into his hair and stood slowly rubbing his head\r\nand looking through his keenly-narrowed eyes from one to the other of\r\nthe three persons before him. His lips were compressed, and the two\r\nlines which had formed themselves beside his mouth might have made it\r\nappear at a first glance that he was smiling. I have said that his\r\nexcitement was an intenser deliberateness, and now he looked grimly\r\ndeliberate. \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems very much as if you had interfered, marquis,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid slowly. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought you said you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t interfere. I know you\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t like me; but that doesn\xe2\x80\x99t make any difference. I thought you\r\npromised me you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t interfere. I thought you swore on your honor\r\nthat you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t interfere. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you remember, marquis?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe marquis lifted his eyebrows; but he was apparently determined to be\r\neven more urbane than usual. He rested his two hands upon the back of\r\nhis mother\xe2\x80\x99s chair and bent forward, as if he were leaning over the\r\nedge of a pulpit or a lecture-desk. He did not smile, but he looked\r\nsoftly grave. \xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI assured you that I would\r\nnot influence my sister\xe2\x80\x99s decision. I adhered, to the letter, to my\r\nengagement. Did I not, sister?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t appeal, my son,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquise, \xe2\x80\x9cyour word is sufficient.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94she accepted me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is very true, I can\xe2\x80\x99t deny\r\nthat. She turned away,\r\nburying her face in her hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you have interfered now, haven\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Newman of the\r\nmarquis.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeither then nor now have I attempted to influence my sister. I used\r\nno persuasion then, I have used no persuasion to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what have you used?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have used authority,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde in a rich,\r\nbell-like voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you have used authority,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cThey have used\r\nauthority,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, turning to Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it? how\r\ndid they use it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy mother commanded,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCommanded you to give me up\xe2\x80\x94I see. And you obey\xe2\x80\x94I see. But why do you\r\nobey?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman.\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 looked across at the old marquise; her eyes slowly\r\nmeasured her from head to foot. \xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid of my mother,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nMadame de Bellegarde rose with a certain quickness, crying, \xe2\x80\x9cThis is a\r\nmost indecent scene!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no wish to prolong it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9; and turning to\r\nthe door she put out her hand again. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you can pity me a little, let\r\nme go alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman shook her hand quietly and firmly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll come down there,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. The _porti\xc3\xa8re_ dropped behind her, and Newman sank with a long\r\nbreath into the nearest chair. He leaned back in it, resting his hands\r\non the knobs of the arms and looking at Madame de Bellegarde and\r\nUrbain. There was a long silence. They stood side by side, with their\r\nheads high and their handsome eyebrows arched.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you make a distinction?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman said at last. \xe2\x80\x9cYou make a\r\ndistinction between persuading and commanding? That rather spoils it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have not the least objection to defining our position,\xe2\x80\x9d said M. de\r\nBellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cWe understand that it should not at first appear to you\r\nquite clear. It can\r\nnever be agreeable to you. It is a disappointment, and disappointments\r\nare unpleasant. I thought it over carefully and tried to arrange it\r\nbetter; but I only gave myself a headache and lost my sleep. Say what\r\nwe will, you will think yourself ill-treated, and you will publish your\r\nwrongs among your friends. But we are not afraid of that. I have never in my life been\r\npresent at a violent scene of any kind, and at my age I can\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nexpected to begin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs _that_ all you have got to say?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman, slowly rising out of\r\nhis chair. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a poor show for a clever lady like you, marquise.\r\nCome, try again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy mother goes to the point, with her usual honesty and intrepidity,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the marquis, toying with his watch-guard. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it is perhaps well\r\nto say a little more. We of course quite repudiate the charge of having\r\nbroken faith with you. We left you entirely at liberty to make yourself\r\nagreeable to my sister. We left her quite at liberty to entertain your\r\nproposal. When she accepted you we said nothing. We therefore quite\r\nobserved our promise. It was only at a later stage of the affair, and\r\non quite a different basis, as it were, that we determined to speak. It\r\nwould have been better, perhaps, if we had spoken before. But really,\r\nyou see, nothing has yet been done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing has yet been done?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman repeated the words, unconscious of\r\ntheir comical effect. He had lost the sense of what the marquis was\r\nsaying; M. de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s superior style was a mere humming in his\r\nears. All that he understood, in his deep and simple indignation, was\r\nthat the matter was not a violent joke, and that the people before him\r\nwere perfectly serious. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you suppose I can take this?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked. \xe2\x80\x9cDo\r\nyou suppose it can matter to me what you say? Do you suppose I can\r\nseriously listen to you? You are simply crazy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Bellegarde gave a rap with her fan in the palm of her hand.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you don\xe2\x80\x99t take it you can leave it, sir. It matters very little\r\nwhat you do. There is something very strange in\r\nit. There were some very nice people among them; you may\r\ndepend upon it there were some honest men and women.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur friends approve us,\xe2\x80\x9d said M. de Bellegarde, \xe2\x80\x9cthere is not a family\r\namong them that would have acted otherwise. The Bellegardes have been used to set the\r\nexample, not to wait for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou would have waited long before anyone would have set you such an\r\nexample as this,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cHave I done anything wrong?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ndemanded. \xe2\x80\x9cHave I given you reason to change your opinion? Have you\r\nfound out anything against me? I can\xe2\x80\x99t imagine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur opinion,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde, \xe2\x80\x9cis quite the same as at\r\nfirst\xe2\x80\x94exactly. We have no ill-will towards yourself; we are very far\r\nfrom accusing you of misconduct. Since your relations with us began you\r\nhave been, I frankly confess, less\xe2\x80\x94less peculiar than I expected. It is\r\nnot your disposition that we object to, it is your antecedents. We\r\nreally cannot reconcile ourselves to a commercial person. We fancied in\r\nan evil hour that we could; it was a great misfortune. We determined to\r\npersevere to the end, and to give you every advantage. I was resolved\r\nthat you should have no reason to accuse me of want of loyalty. We let\r\nthe thing certainly go very far; we introduced you to our friends. To\r\ntell the truth, it was that, I think, that broke me down. I succumbed\r\nto the scene that took place on Thursday night in these rooms. What you expect of me,\r\napparently, is to go my way, thanking you for favors received, and\r\npromising never to trouble you again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe expect of you to act like a clever man,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have shown yourself that already, and what we have done is\r\naltogether based upon your being so. When one must submit, one must.\r\nSince my daughter absolutely withdraws, what will be the use of your\r\nmaking a noise?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt remains to be seen whether your daughter absolutely withdraws. Your\r\ndaughter and I are still very good friends; nothing is changed in that.\r\nAs I say, I will talk it over with her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat will be of no use,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old lady. \xe2\x80\x9cI know my daughter well\r\nenough to know that words spoken as she just now spoke to you are\r\nfinal. I\xe2\x80\x99m the most unobjectionable fellow in the world.\r\nWhat if I am a commercial person? What under the sun do you mean? A\r\ncommercial person? I will be any sort of a person you want. I never\r\ntalked to you about business. Let her go, and I will ask no questions.\r\nI will take her away, and you shall never see me or hear of me again. I\r\nwill stay in America if you like. I\xe2\x80\x99ll sign a paper promising never to\r\ncome back to Europe! I\r\nknow it is near some old city on a hill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrecisely. Poitiers is on a hill,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow how old it is. We are not afraid to tell you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is Poitiers, is it? Very good,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall immediately\r\nfollow Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe trains after this hour won\xe2\x80\x99t serve you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Urbain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall hire a special train!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat will be a very silly waste of money,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will be time enough to talk about waste three days hence,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman\r\nanswered; and clapping his hat on his head, he departed.\r\n\r\nHe did not immediately start for Fleuri\xc3\xa8res; he was too stunned and\r\nwounded for consecutive action. He simply walked; he walked straight\r\nbefore him, following the river, till he got out of the _enceinte_ of\r\nParis. He had a burning, tingling sense of personal outrage. He had\r\nnever in his life received so absolute a check; he had never been\r\npulled up, or, as he would have said, \xe2\x80\x9clet down,\xe2\x80\x9d so short; and he\r\nfound the sensation intolerable; he strode along, tapping the trees and\r\nlamp-posts fiercely with his stick and inwardly raging. To lose Madame\r\nde Cintr\xc3\xa9 after he had taken such jubilant and triumphant possession of\r\nher was as great an affront to his pride as it was an injury to his\r\nhappiness. And to lose her by the interference and the dictation of\r\nothers, by an impudent old woman and a pretentious fop stepping in with\r\ntheir \xe2\x80\x9cauthority\xe2\x80\x9d! It was too preposterous, it was too pitiful. Upon\r\nwhat he deemed the unblushing treachery of the Bellegardes Newman\r\nwasted little thought; he consigned it, once for all, to eternal\r\nperdition. But the treachery of Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 herself amazed and\r\nconfounded him; there was a key to the mystery, of course, but he\r\ngroped for it in vain. Only three days had elapsed since she stood\r\nbeside him in the starlight, beautiful and tranquil as the trust with\r\nwhich he had inspired her, and told him that she was happy in the\r\nprospect of their marriage. What was the meaning of the change? of what\r\ninfernal potion had she tasted? Poor Newman had a terrible apprehension\r\nthat she had really changed. His very admiration for her attached the\r\nidea of force and weight to her rupture. But he did not rail at her as\r\nfalse, for he was sure she was unhappy. In his walk he had crossed one\r\nof the bridges of the Seine, and he still followed, unheedingly, the\r\nlong, unbroken quay. He had left Paris behind him, and he was almost in\r\nthe country; he was in the pleasant suburb of Auteuil. He stopped at\r\nlast, looked around him without seeing or caring for its pleasantness,\r\nand then slowly turned and at a slower pace retraced his steps. When he\r\ncame abreast of the fantastic embankment known as the Trocadero, he\r\nreflected, through his throbbing pain, that he was near Mrs. Tristram\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndwelling, and that Mrs. Tristram, on particular occasions, had much of\r\na woman\xe2\x80\x99s kindness in her utterance. He felt that he needed to pour out\r\nhis ire and he took the road to her house. Mrs. Tristram was at home\r\nand alone, and as soon as she had looked at him, on his entering the\r\nroom, she told him that she knew what he had come for. Newman sat down\r\nheavily, in silence, looking at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have backed out!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, you may think it strange, but\r\nI felt something the other night in the air.\xe2\x80\x9d Presently he told her his\r\nstory; she listened, with her eyes fixed on him. When he had finished\r\nshe said quietly, \xe2\x80\x9cThey want her to marry Lord Deepmere.\xe2\x80\x9d Newman\r\nstared. He did not know that she knew anything about Lord Deepmere.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t think she will,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Tristram added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_She_ marry that poor little cub!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Lord! And yet,\r\nwhy did she refuse me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that isn\xe2\x80\x99t the only thing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Tristram. \xe2\x80\x9cThey really\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t endure you any longer. They had overrated their courage. I\r\nmust say, to give the devil his due, that there is something rather\r\nfine in that. It was your commercial quality in the abstract they\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t swallow. That is really aristocratic. They wanted your money,\r\nbut they have given you up for an idea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman frowned most ruefully, and took up his hat again. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought you\r\nwould encourage me!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with almost childlike sadness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered very gently. \xe2\x80\x9cI feel none the less sorry for\r\nyou, especially as I am at the bottom of your troubles. I have not\r\nforgotten that I suggested the marriage to you. I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe that\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 has any intention of marrying Lord Deepmere. It is\r\ntrue he is not younger than she, as he looks. He is thirty-three years\r\nold; I looked in the Peerage. But no\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t believe her so horribly,\r\ncruelly false.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease say nothing against her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor woman, she _is_ cruel. But of course you will go after her and\r\nyou will plead powerfully. Do you know that as you are now,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs.\r\nTristram pursued, with characteristic audacity of comment, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are\r\nextremely eloquent, even without speaking? To resist you a woman must\r\nhave a very fixed idea in her head. I wish I had done you a wrong, that\r\nyou might come to me in that fine fashion! But go to Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\r\nat any rate, and tell her that she is a puzzle even to me. I am very\r\ncurious to see how far family discipline will go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman sat a while longer, leaning his elbows on his knees and his head\r\nin his hands, and Mrs. Tristram continued to temper charity with\r\nphilosophy and compassion with criticism. At last she inquired, \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nwhat does the Count Valentin say to it?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman started; he had not\r\nthought of Valentin and his errand on the Swiss frontier since the\r\nmorning. The reflection made him restless again, and he took his leave.\r\nHe went straight to his apartment, where, upon the table of the\r\nvestibule, he found a telegram. It ran (with the date and place) as\r\nfollows: \xe2\x80\x9cI am seriously ill; please to come to me as soon as possible.\r\nV. B.\xe2\x80\x9d Newman groaned at this miserable news, and at the necessity of\r\ndeferring his journey to the Ch\xc3\xa2teau de Fleuri\xc3\xa8res. But he wrote to\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 these few lines; they were all he had time for:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t give you up, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t really believe you give me up. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t understand it, but we shall clear it up together. I can\xe2\x80\x99t follow\r\nyou to-day, as I am called to see a friend at a distance who is very\r\nill, perhaps dying. But I shall come to you as soon as I can leave my\r\nfriend. Why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t I say that he is your brother? C. N.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter this he had only time to catch the night express to Geneva.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIX\r\n\r\n\r\nNewman possessed a remarkable talent for sitting still when it was\r\nnecessary, and he had an opportunity to use it on his journey to\r\nSwitzerland. The successive hours of the night brought him no sleep,\r\nbut he sat motionless in his corner of the railway-carriage, with his\r\neyes closed, and the most observant of his fellow-travelers might have\r\nenvied him his apparent slumber. Toward morning slumber really came, as\r\nan effect of mental rather than of physical fatigue. He slept for a\r\ncouple of hours, and at last, waking, found his eyes resting upon one\r\nof the snow-powdered peaks of the Jura, behind which the sky was just\r\nreddening with the dawn. But he saw neither the cold mountain nor the\r\nwarm sky; his consciousness began to throb again, on the very instant,\r\nwith a sense of his wrong. He got out of the train half an hour before\r\nit reached Geneva, in the cold morning twilight, at the station\r\nindicated in Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s telegram. A drowsy station-master was on the\r\nplatform with a lantern, and the hood of his overcoat over his head,\r\nand near him stood a gentleman who advanced to meet Newman. This\r\npersonage was a man of forty, with a tall lean figure, a sallow face, a\r\ndark eye, a neat moustache, and a pair of fresh gloves. He took off his\r\nhat, looking very grave, and pronounced Newman\xe2\x80\x99s name. Our hero\r\nassented and said, \xe2\x80\x9cYou are M. de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s friend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI unite with you in claiming that sad honor,\xe2\x80\x9d said the gentleman. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nhad placed myself at M. de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s service in this melancholy\r\naffair, together with M. de Grosjoyaux, who is now at his bedside. M.\r\nde Grosjoyaux, I believe, has had the honor of meeting you in Paris,\r\nbut as he is a better nurse than I he remained with our poor friend.\r\nBellegarde has been eagerly expecting you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how is Bellegarde?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cHe was badly hit?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe doctor has condemned him; we brought a surgeon with us. But he\r\nwill die in the best sentiments. I sent last evening for the cur\xc3\xa9 of\r\nthe nearest French village, who spent an hour with him. The cur\xc3\xa9 was\r\nquite satisfied.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeaven forgive us!\xe2\x80\x9d groaned Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI would rather the doctor were\r\nsatisfied! And can he see me\xe2\x80\x94shall he know me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I left him, half an hour ago, he had fallen asleep after a\r\nfeverish, wakeful night. But we shall see.\xe2\x80\x9d And Newman\xe2\x80\x99s companion\r\nproceeded to lead the way out of the station to the village, explaining\r\nas he went that the little party was lodged in the humblest of Swiss\r\ninns, where, however, they had succeeded in making M. de Bellegarde\r\nmuch more comfortable than could at first have been expected. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are\r\nold companions in arms,\xe2\x80\x9d said Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s second; \xe2\x80\x9cit is not the first\r\ntime that one of us has helped the other to lie easily. It is a very\r\nnasty wound, and the nastiest thing about it is that Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nadversary was not shot. He put his bullet where he could. It took it\r\ninto its head to walk straight into Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s left side, just below\r\nthe heart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs they picked their way in the gray, deceptive dawn, between the\r\nmanure-heaps of the village street, Newman\xe2\x80\x99s new acquaintance narrated\r\nthe particulars of the duel. The conditions of the meeting had been\r\nthat if the first exchange of shots should fail to satisfy one of the\r\ntwo gentlemen, a second should take place. Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s first bullet had\r\ndone exactly what Newman\xe2\x80\x99s companion was convinced he had intended it\r\nto do; it had grazed the arm of M. Stanislas Kapp, just scratching the\r\nflesh. M. Kapp\xe2\x80\x99s own projectile, meanwhile, had passed at ten good\r\ninches from the person of Valentin. The representatives of M. Stanislas\r\nhad demanded another shot, which was granted. Valentin had then fired\r\naside and the young Alsatian had done effective execution. \xe2\x80\x9cI saw, when\r\nwe met him on the ground,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman\xe2\x80\x99s informant, \xe2\x80\x9cthat he was not\r\ngoing to be _commode_. It is a kind of bovine temperament.\xe2\x80\x9d Valentin\r\nhad immediately been installed at the inn, and M. Stanislas and his\r\nfriends had withdrawn to regions unknown. The police authorities of the\r\ncanton had waited upon the party at the inn, had been extremely\r\nmajestic, and had drawn up a long _proc\xc3\xa8s-verbal_; but it was probable\r\nthat they would wink at so very gentlemanly a bit of bloodshed. Newman\r\nasked whether a message had not been sent to Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s family, and\r\nlearned that up to a late hour on the preceding evening Valentin had\r\nopposed it. He had refused to believe his wound was dangerous. But\r\nafter his interview with the cur\xc3\xa9 he had consented, and a telegram had\r\nbeen dispatched to his mother. \xe2\x80\x9cBut the marquise had better hurry!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Newman\xe2\x80\x99s conductor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\xe2\x80\x99s an abominable affair!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all I have to\r\nsay!\xe2\x80\x9d To say this, at least, in a tone of infinite disgust was an\r\nirresistible need.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you don\xe2\x80\x99t approve?\xe2\x80\x9d questioned his conductor, with curious\r\nurbanity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cApprove?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish that when I had him there, night\r\nbefore last, I had locked him up in my _cabinet de toilette!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nValentin\xe2\x80\x99s late second opened his eyes, and shook his head up and down\r\ntwo or three times, gravely, with a little flute-like whistle. But they\r\nhad reached the inn, and a stout maid-servant in a night-cap was at the\r\ndoor with a lantern, to take Newman\xe2\x80\x99s traveling-bag from the porter who\r\ntrudged behind him. Valentin was lodged on the ground-floor at the back\r\nof the house, and Newman\xe2\x80\x99s companion went along a stone-faced passage\r\nand softly opened a door. Then he beckoned to Newman, who advanced and\r\nlooked into the room, which was lighted by a single shaded candle.\r\nBeside the fire sat M. de Grosjoyaux asleep in his dressing-gown\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nlittle plump, fair man whom Newman had seen several times in Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncompany. On the bed lay Valentin, pale and still, with his eyes\r\nclosed\xe2\x80\x94a figure very shocking to Newman, who had seen it hitherto awake\r\nto its fingertips. M. de Grosjoyaux\xe2\x80\x99s colleague pointed to an open door\r\nbeyond, and whispered that the doctor was within, keeping guard. So\r\nlong as Valentin slept, or seemed to sleep, of course Newman could not\r\napproach him; so our hero withdrew for the present, committing himself\r\nto the care of the half-waked _bonne_. She took him to a room\r\nabove-stairs, and introduced him to a bed on which a magnified bolster,\r\nin yellow calico, figured as a counterpane. Newman lay down, and, in\r\nspite of his counterpane, slept for three or four hours. When he awoke,\r\nthe morning was advanced and the sun was filling his window, and he\r\nheard, outside of it, the clucking of hens. While he was dressing there\r\ncame to his door a messenger from M. de Grosjoyaux and his companion\r\nproposing that he should breakfast with them. Presently he went\r\ndownstairs to the little stone-paved dining-room, where the\r\nmaid-servant, who had taken off her night-cap, was serving the repast.\r\nM. de Grosjoyaux was there, surprisingly fresh for a gentleman who had\r\nbeen playing sick-nurse half the night, rubbing his hands and watching\r\nthe breakfast table attentively. Newman renewed acquaintance with him,\r\nand learned that Valentin was still sleeping; the surgeon, who had had\r\na fairly tranquil night, was at present sitting with him. Before M. de\r\nGrosjoyaux\xe2\x80\x99s associate reappeared, Newman learned that his name was M.\r\nLedoux, and that Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s acquaintance with him dated from the days\r\nwhen they served together in the Pontifical Zouaves. M. Ledoux was the\r\nnephew of a distinguished Ultramontane bishop. At last the bishop\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnephew came in with a toilet in which an ingenious attempt at harmony\r\nwith the peculiar situation was visible, and with a gravity tempered by\r\na decent deference to the best breakfast that the Croix Helv\xc3\xa9tique had\r\never set forth. Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s servant, who was allowed only in scanty\r\nmeasure the honor of watching with his master, had been lending a light\r\nParisian hand in the kitchen. The two Frenchmen did their best to prove\r\nthat if circumstances might overshadow, they could not really obscure,\r\nthe national talent for conversation, and M. Ledoux delivered a neat\r\nlittle eulogy on poor Bellegarde, whom he pronounced the most charming\r\nEnglishman he had ever known.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you call him an Englishman?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman asked.\r\n\r\nM. Ledoux smiled a moment and then made an epigram. _\xe2\x80\x9cC\xe2\x80\x99est plus qu\xe2\x80\x99un\r\nAnglais\xe2\x80\x94c\xe2\x80\x99est un Anglomane!\xe2\x80\x9d_ Newman said soberly that he had never\r\nnoticed it; and M. de Grosjoyaux remarked that it was really too soon\r\nto deliver a funeral oration upon poor Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cEvidently,\xe2\x80\x9d said M.\r\nLedoux. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help observing this morning to Mr. Newman that\r\nwhen a man has taken such excellent measures for his salvation as our\r\ndear friend did last evening, it seems almost a pity he should put it\r\nin peril again by returning to the world.\xe2\x80\x9d M. Ledoux was a great\r\nCatholic, and Newman thought him a queer mixture. His countenance, by\r\ndaylight, had a sort of amiably saturnine cast; he had a very large\r\nthin nose, and looked like a Spanish picture. He appeared to think\r\ndueling a very perfect arrangement, provided, if one should get hit,\r\none could promptly see the priest. He seemed to take a great\r\nsatisfaction in Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s interview with the cur\xc3\xa9, and yet his\r\nconversation did not at all indicate a sanctimonious habit of mind. M.\r\nLedoux had evidently a high sense of the becoming, and was prepared to\r\nbe urbane and tasteful on all points. He was always furnished with a\r\nsmile (which pushed his moustache up under his nose) and an\r\nexplanation. _Savoir-vivre_\xe2\x80\x94knowing how to live\xe2\x80\x94was his specialty, in\r\nwhich he included knowing how to die; but, as Newman reflected, with a\r\ngood deal of dumb irritation, he seemed disposed to delegate to others\r\nthe application of his learning on this latter point. M. de Grosjoyaux\r\nwas of quite another complexion, and appeared to regard his friend\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntheological unction as the sign of an inaccessibly superior mind. He\r\nwas evidently doing his utmost, with a kind of jovial tenderness, to\r\nmake life agreeable to Valentin to the last, and help him as little as\r\npossible to miss the Boulevard des Italiens; but what chiefly occupied\r\nhis mind was the mystery of a bungling brewer\xe2\x80\x99s son making so neat a\r\nshot. He himself could snuff a candle, etc., and yet he confessed that\r\nhe could not have done better than this. He hastened to add that on the\r\npresent occasion he would have made a point of not doing so well. It\r\nwas not an occasion for that sort of murderous work, _que diable!_ He\r\nwould have picked out some quiet fleshy spot and just tapped it with a\r\nharmless ball. M. Stanislas Kapp had been deplorably heavy-handed; but\r\nreally, when the world had come to that pass that one granted a meeting\r\nto a brewer\xe2\x80\x99s son!... This was M. de Grosjoyaux\xe2\x80\x99s nearest approach to a\r\ngeneralization. He kept looking through the window, over the shoulder\r\nof M. Ledoux, at a slender tree which stood at the end of a lane,\r\nopposite to the inn, and seemed to be measuring its distance from his\r\nextended arm and secretly wishing that, since the subject had been\r\nintroduced, propriety did not forbid a little speculative\r\npistol-practice.\r\n\r\nNewman was in no humor to enjoy good company. He could neither eat nor\r\ntalk; his soul was sore with grief and anger, and the weight of his\r\ndouble sorrow was intolerable. He sat with his eyes fixed upon his\r\nplate, counting the minutes, wishing at one moment that Valentin would\r\nsee him and leave him free to go in quest of Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 and his\r\nlost happiness, and mentally calling himself a vile brute the next, for\r\nthe impatient egotism of the wish. He was very poor company, himself,\r\nand even his acute preoccupation and his general lack of the habit of\r\npondering the impression he produced did not prevent him from\r\nreflecting that his companions must be puzzled to see how poor\r\nBellegarde came to take such a fancy to this taciturn Yankee that he\r\nmust needs have him at his death-bed. After breakfast he strolled forth\r\nalone into the village and looked at the fountain, the geese, the open\r\nbarn doors, the brown, bent old women, showing their hugely darned\r\nstocking-heels at the ends of their slowly-clicking sabots, and the\r\nbeautiful view of snowy Alps and purple Jura at either end of the\r\nlittle street. The day was brilliant; early spring was in the air and\r\nin the sunshine, and the winter\xe2\x80\x99s damp was trickling out of the cottage\r\neaves. It was birth and brightness for all nature, even for chirping\r\nchickens and waddling goslings, and it was to be death and burial for\r\npoor, foolish, generous, delightful Bellegarde. Newman walked as far as\r\nthe village church, and went into the small graveyard beside it, where\r\nhe sat down and looked at the awkward tablets which were planted\r\naround. They were all sordid and hideous, and Newman could feel nothing\r\nbut the hardness and coldness of death. He got up and came back to the\r\ninn, where he found M. Ledoux having coffee and a cigarette at a little\r\ngreen table which he had caused to be carried into the small garden.\r\nNewman, learning that the doctor was still sitting with Valentin, asked\r\nM. Ledoux if he might not be allowed to relieve him; he had a great\r\ndesire to be useful to his poor friend. This was easily arranged; the\r\ndoctor was very glad to go to bed. He was a youthful and rather jaunty\r\npractitioner, but he had a clever face, and the ribbon of the Legion of\r\nHonor in his buttonhole; Newman listened attentively to the\r\ninstructions he gave him before retiring, and took mechanically from\r\nhis hand a small volume which the surgeon recommended as a help to\r\nwakefulness, and which turned out to be an old copy of \xe2\x80\x9cLes Liaisons\r\nDangereuses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nValentin was still lying with his eyes closed, and there was no visible\r\nchange in his condition. Newman sat down near him, and for a long time\r\nnarrowly watched him. Then his eyes wandered away with his thoughts\r\nupon his own situation, and rested upon the chain of the Alps,\r\ndisclosed by the drawing of the scant white cotton curtain of the\r\nwindow, through which the sunshine passed and lay in squares upon the\r\nred-tiled floor. He tried to interweave his reflections with hope, but\r\nhe only half succeeded. What had happened to him seemed to have, in its\r\nviolence and audacity, the force of a real calamity\xe2\x80\x94the strength and\r\ninsolence of Destiny herself. It was unnatural and monstrous, and he\r\nhad no arms against it. At last a sound struck upon the stillness, and\r\nhe heard Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt can\xe2\x80\x99t be about _me_ you are pulling that long face!\xe2\x80\x9d He found, when\r\nhe turned, that Valentin was lying in the same position; but his eyes\r\nwere open, and he was even trying to smile.
