I apologize for my\r\nfamily. For my mother. For my brother. For the ancient house of\r\nBellegarde. _Voil\xc3\xa0!_\xe2\x80\x9d he added softly.\r\n\r\nNewman for an answer took his hand and pressed it with a world of\r\nkindness. Valentin remained quiet, and at the end of half an hour the\r\ndoctor softly came in. Behind him, through the half-open door, Newman\r\nsaw the two questioning faces of MM. de Grosjoyaux and Ledoux. The\r\ndoctor laid his hand on Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s wrist and sat looking at him. He\r\ngave no sign and the two gentlemen came in, M. Ledoux having first\r\nbeckoned to someone outside. This was M. le Cur\xc3\xa9, who carried in his\r\nhand an object unknown to Newman, and covered with a white napkin. M.\r\nle Cur\xc3\xa9 was short, round, and red: he advanced, pulling off his little\r\nblack cap to Newman, and deposited his burden on the table; and then he\r\nsat down in the best armchair, with his hands folded across his person.\r\nThe other gentlemen had exchanged glances which expressed unanimity as\r\nto the timeliness of their presence. But for a long time Valentin\r\nneither spoke nor moved. It was Newman\xe2\x80\x99s belief, afterwards, that M. le\r\nCur\xc3\xa9 went to sleep. At last abruptly, Valentin pronounced Newman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nname. His friend went to him, and he said in French, \xe2\x80\x9cYou are not\r\nalone. I want to speak to you alone.\xe2\x80\x9d Newman looked at the doctor, and\r\nthe doctor looked at the cur\xc3\xa9, who looked back at him; and then the\r\ndoctor and the cur\xc3\xa9, together, gave a shrug. \xe2\x80\x9cAlone\xe2\x80\x94for five minutes,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nValentin repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cPlease leave us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe cur\xc3\xa9 took up his burden again and led the way out, followed by his\r\ncompanions. Newman closed the door behind them and came back to\r\nValentin\xe2\x80\x99s bedside. Bellegarde had watched all this intently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very bad, it\xe2\x80\x99s very bad,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, after Newman had seated\r\nhimself close to him. \xe2\x80\x9cThe more I think of it the worse it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t think of it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\nBut Valentin went on, without heeding him. \xe2\x80\x9cEven if they should come\r\nround again, the shame\xe2\x80\x94the baseness\xe2\x80\x94is there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, they won\xe2\x80\x99t come round!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you can make them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMake them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can tell you something\xe2\x80\x94a great secret\xe2\x80\x94an immense secret. You can use\r\nit against them\xe2\x80\x94frighten them, force them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA secret!\xe2\x80\x9d Newman repeated. The idea of letting Valentin, on his\r\ndeath-bed, confide him an \xe2\x80\x9cimmense secret\xe2\x80\x9d shocked him, for the moment,\r\nand made him draw back. It seemed an illicit way of arriving at\r\ninformation, and even had a vague analogy with listening at a keyhole.\r\nThen, suddenly, the thought of \xe2\x80\x9cforcing\xe2\x80\x9d Madame de Bellegarde and her\r\nson became attractive, and Newman bent his head closer to Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlips. For some time, however, the dying man said nothing more. He only\r\nlay and looked at his friend with his kindled, expanded, troubled eye,\r\nand Newman began to believe that he had spoken in delirium. But at last\r\nhe said,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere was something done\xe2\x80\x94something done at Fleuri\xc3\xa8res. It was foul\r\nplay. My father\xe2\x80\x94something happened to him. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know; I have been\r\nashamed\xe2\x80\x94afraid to know. But I know there is something. My mother\r\nknows\xe2\x80\x94Urbain knows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething happened to your father?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, urgently.\r\n\r\nValentin looked at him, still more wide-eyed. \xe2\x80\x9cHe didn\xe2\x80\x99t get well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet well of what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the immense effort which Valentin had made, first to decide to\r\nutter these words and then to bring them out, appeared to have taken\r\nhis last strength. He lapsed again into silence, and Newman sat\r\nwatching him. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you understand?\xe2\x80\x9d he began again, presently. \xe2\x80\x9cAt\r\nFleuri\xc3\xa8res. You can find out. Mrs. Bread knows. Tell her I begged you\r\nto ask her. Then tell them that, and see. It may help you. If not, tell\r\neveryone. It will\xe2\x80\x94it will\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94here Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s voice sank to the feeblest\r\nmurmur\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cit will avenge you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe words died away in a long, soft groan. Newman stood up, deeply\r\nimpressed, not knowing what to say; his heart was beating violently.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last. \xe2\x80\x9cI am much obliged.\xe2\x80\x9d But Valentin seemed\r\nnot to hear him, he remained silent, and his silence continued. At last\r\nNewman went and opened the door. M. le Cur\xc3\xa9 re-entered, bearing his\r\nsacred vessel and followed by the three gentlemen and by Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nservant. It was almost processional.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XX\r\n\r\n\r\nValentin de Bellegarde died tranquilly, just as the cold faint March\r\ndawn began to illumine the faces of the little knot of friends gathered\r\nabout his bedside. An hour afterwards Newman left the inn and drove to\r\nGeneva; he was naturally unwilling to be present at the arrival of\r\nMadame de Bellegarde and her first-born. At Geneva, for the moment, he\r\nremained. He was like a man who has had a fall and wants to sit still\r\nand count his bruises. He instantly wrote to Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, relating\r\nto her the circumstances of her brother\xe2\x80\x99s death\xe2\x80\x94with certain\r\nexceptions\xe2\x80\x94and asking her what was the earliest moment at which he\r\nmight hope that she would consent to see him. M. Ledoux had told him\r\nthat he had reason to know that Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s will\xe2\x80\x94Bellegarde had a great\r\ndeal of elegant personal property to dispose of\xe2\x80\x94contained a request\r\nthat he should be buried near his father in the churchyard of\r\nFleuri\xc3\xa8res, and Newman intended that the state of his own relations\r\nwith the family should not deprive him of the satisfaction of helping\r\nto pay the last earthly honors to the best fellow in the world. He\r\nreflected that Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s friendship was older than Urbain\xe2\x80\x99s enmity,\r\nand that at a funeral it was easy to escape notice. Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nanswer to his letter enabled him to time his arrival at Fleuri\xc3\xa8res.\r\nThis answer was very brief; it ran as follows:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thank you for your letter, and for your being with Valentin. It is a\r\nmost inexpressible sorrow to me that I was not. To see you will be\r\nnothing but a distress to me; there is no need, therefore, to wait for\r\nwhat you call brighter days. It is all one now, and I shall have no\r\nbrighter days. Come when you please; only notify me first. My brother\r\nis to be buried here on Friday, and my family is to remain here. C. de\r\nC.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs soon as he received this letter Newman went straight to Paris and to\r\nPoitiers. The journey took him far southward, through green Touraine\r\nand across the far-shining Loire, into a country where the early spring\r\ndeepened about him as he went. But he had never made a journey during\r\nwhich he heeded less what he would have called the lay of the land. He\r\nobtained lodging at the inn at Poitiers, and the next morning drove in\r\na couple of hours to the village of Fleuri\xc3\xa8res. But here, preoccupied\r\nthough he was, he could not fail to notice the picturesqueness of the\r\nplace. It was what the French call a _petit bourg_; it lay at the base\r\nof a sort of huge mound on the summit of which stood the crumbling\r\nruins of a feudal castle, much of whose sturdy material, as well as\r\nthat of the wall which dropped along the hill to enclose the clustered\r\nhouses defensively, had been absorbed into the very substance of the\r\nvillage. The church was simply the former chapel of the castle,\r\nfronting upon its grass-grown court, which, however, was of generous\r\nenough width to have given up its quaintest corner to a little\r\ngraveyard. Here the very headstones themselves seemed to sleep, as they\r\nslanted into the grass; the patient elbow of the rampart held them\r\ntogether on one side, and in front, far beneath their mossy lids, the\r\ngreen plains and blue distances stretched away. The way to church, up\r\nthe hill, was impracticable to vehicles. It was lined with peasants,\r\ntwo or three rows deep, who stood watching old Madame de Bellegarde\r\nslowly ascend it, on the arm of her elder son, behind the pall-bearers\r\nof the other. Newman chose to lurk among the common mourners who\r\nmurmured \xe2\x80\x9cMadame la Comtesse\xe2\x80\x9d as a tall figure veiled in black passed\r\nbefore them. He stood in the dusky little church while the service was\r\ngoing forward, but at the dismal tomb-side he turned away and walked\r\ndown the hill. He went back to Poitiers, and spent two days in which\r\npatience and impatience were singularly commingled. On the third day he\r\nsent Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 a note, saying that he would call upon her in the\r\nafternoon, and in accordance with this he again took his way to\r\nFleuri\xc3\xa8res. He left his vehicle at the tavern in the village street,\r\nand obeyed the simple instructions which were given him for finding the\r\nch\xc3\xa2teau.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is just beyond there,\xe2\x80\x9d said the landlord, and pointed to the\r\ntree-tops of the park, above the opposite houses. Newman followed the\r\nfirst cross-road to the right\xe2\x80\x94it was bordered with mouldy cottages\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nin a few moments saw before him the peaked roofs of the towers.\r\nAdvancing farther, he found himself before a vast iron gate, rusty and\r\nclosed; here he paused a moment, looking through the bars. The ch\xc3\xa2teau\r\nwas near the road; this was at once its merit and its defect; but its\r\naspect was extremely impressive. Newman learned afterwards, from a\r\nguide-book of the province, that it dated from the time of Henry IV. It\r\npresented to the wide, paved area which preceded it and which was edged\r\nwith shabby farm-buildings an immense fa\xc3\xa7ade of dark time-stained\r\nbrick, flanked by two low wings, each of which terminated in a little\r\nDutch-looking pavilion capped with a fantastic roof. Two towers rose\r\nbehind, and behind the towers was a mass of elms and beeches, now just\r\nfaintly green.\r\n\r\nBut the great feature was a wide, green river which washed the\r\nfoundations of the ch\xc3\xa2teau. The building rose from an island in the\r\ncircling stream, so that this formed a perfect moat spanned by a\r\ntwo-arched bridge without a parapet. The dull brick walls, which here\r\nand there made a grand, straight sweep; the ugly little cupolas of the\r\nwings, the deep-set windows, the long, steep pinnacles of mossy slate,\r\nall mirrored themselves in the tranquil river. Newman rang at the gate,\r\nand was almost frightened at the tone with which a big rusty bell above\r\nhis head replied to him. An old woman came out from the gate-house and\r\nopened the creaking portal just wide enough for him to pass, and he\r\nwent in, across the dry, bare court and the little cracked white slabs\r\nof the causeway on the moat. At the door of the ch\xc3\xa2teau he waited for\r\nsome moments, and this gave him a chance to observe that Fleuri\xc3\xa8res was\r\nnot \xe2\x80\x9ckept up,\xe2\x80\x9d and to reflect that it was a melancholy place of\r\nresidence. \xe2\x80\x9cIt looks,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman to himself\xe2\x80\x94and I give the comparison\r\nfor what it is worth\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9clike a Chinese penitentiary.\xe2\x80\x9d At last the door\r\nwas opened by a servant whom he remembered to have seen in the Rue de\r\nl\xe2\x80\x99Universit\xc3\xa9. The man\xe2\x80\x99s dull face brightened as he perceived our hero,\r\nfor Newman, for indefinable reasons, enjoyed the confidence of the\r\nliveried gentry. The footman led the way across a great central\r\nvestibule, with a pyramid of plants in tubs in the middle of glass\r\ndoors all around, to what appeared to be the principal drawing-room of\r\nthe ch\xc3\xa2teau. Newman crossed the threshold of a room of superb\r\nproportions, which made him feel at first like a tourist with a\r\nguide-book and a cicerone awaiting a fee. But when his guide had left\r\nhim alone, with the observation that he would call Madame la Comtesse,\r\nNewman perceived that the salon contained little that was remarkable\r\nsave a dark ceiling with curiously carved rafters, some curtains of\r\nelaborate, antiquated tapestry, and a dark oaken floor, polished like a\r\nmirror. He waited some minutes, walking up and down; but at length, as\r\nhe turned at the end of the room, he saw that Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 had come\r\nin by a distant door. She wore a black dress, and she stood looking at\r\nhim. As the length of the immense room lay between them he had time to\r\nlook at her before they met in the middle of it.\r\n\r\nHe was dismayed at the change in her appearance. Pale, heavy-browed,\r\nalmost haggard with a sort of monastic rigidity in her dress, she had\r\nlittle but her pure features in common with the woman whose radiant\r\ngood grace he had hitherto admired. She let her eyes rest on his own,\r\nand she let him take her hand; but her eyes looked like two rainy\r\nautumn moons, and her touch was portentously lifeless.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was at your brother\xe2\x80\x99s funeral,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman said. \xe2\x80\x9cThen I waited three\r\ndays. But I could wait no longer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing can be lost or gained by waiting,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. \xe2\x80\x9cBut\r\nit was very considerate of you to wait, wronged as you have been.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad you think I have been wronged,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, with that oddly\r\nhumorous accent with which he often uttered words of the gravest\r\nmeaning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo I need to say so?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I have wronged,\r\nseriously, many persons; certainly not consciously. To you, to whom I\r\nhave done this hard and cruel thing, the only reparation I can make is\r\nto say, \xe2\x80\x98I know it, I feel it!\xe2\x80\x99 The reparation is pitifully small!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s a great step forward!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, with a gracious smile of\r\nencouragement. He pushed a chair towards her and held it, looking at\r\nher urgently. She sat down, mechanically, and he seated himself near\r\nher; but in a moment he got up, restlessly, and stood before her. She\r\nremained seated, like a troubled creature who had passed through the\r\nstage of restlessness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say nothing is to be gained by my seeing you,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on, \xe2\x80\x9cand yet\r\nI am very glad you came. Now I can tell you what I feel. It is a\r\nselfish pleasure, but it is one of the last I shall have.\xe2\x80\x9d And she\r\npaused, with her great misty eyes fixed upon him. \xe2\x80\x9cI know how I have\r\ndeceived and injured you; I know how cruel and cowardly I have been. I\r\nsee it as vividly as you do\xe2\x80\x94I feel it to the ends of my fingers.\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\nshe unclasped her hands, which were locked together in her lap, lifted\r\nthem, and dropped them at her side. \xe2\x80\x9cAnything that you may have said of\r\nme in your angriest passion is nothing to what I have said to myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn my angriest passion,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cI have said nothing hard of\r\nyou. The very worst thing I have said of you yet is that you are the\r\nloveliest of women.\xe2\x80\x9d And he seated himself before her again abruptly.\r\n\r\nShe flushed a little, but even her flush was pale. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is because you\r\nthink I will come back. But I will not come back. It is in that hope\r\nyou have come here, I know; I am very sorry for you. I would do almost\r\nanything for you. To say that, after what I have done, seems simply\r\nimpudent; but what can I say that will not seem impudent? To wrong you\r\nand apologize\xe2\x80\x94that is easy enough. I should not have wronged you.\xe2\x80\x9d She\r\nstopped a moment, looking at him, and motioned him to let her go on. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nought never to have listened to you at first; that was the wrong. No\r\ngood could come of it. I felt it, and yet I listened; that was your\r\nfault. I liked you too much; I believed in you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd don\xe2\x80\x99t you believe in me now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore than ever. But now it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter. I have given you up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman gave a powerful thump with his clenched fist upon his knee.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, why, why?\xe2\x80\x9d he cried. \xe2\x80\x9cGive me a reason\xe2\x80\x94a decent reason. You are\r\nnot a child\xe2\x80\x94you are not a minor, nor an idiot. You are not obliged to\r\ndrop me because your mother told you to. Such a reason isn\xe2\x80\x99t worthy of\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know that; it\xe2\x80\x99s not worthy of me. But it\xe2\x80\x99s the only one I have to\r\ngive. After all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, throwing out her hands, \xe2\x80\x9cthink\r\nme an idiot and forget me! That will be the simplest way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman got up and walked away with a crushing sense that his cause was\r\nlost, and yet with an equal inability to give up fighting. He went to\r\none of the great windows, and looked out at the stiffly embanked river\r\nand the formal gardens which lay beyond it. When he turned round,\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 had risen; she stood there silent and passive. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nare not frank,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman; \xe2\x80\x9cyou are not honest. Instead of saying\r\nthat you are imbecile, you should say that other people are wicked.\r\nYour mother and your brother have been false and cruel; they have been\r\nso to me, and I am sure they have been so to you. Why do you try to\r\nshield them? Why do you sacrifice me to them? I\xe2\x80\x99m not false; I\xe2\x80\x99m not\r\ncruel. You don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you give up; I can tell you that\xe2\x80\x94you don\xe2\x80\x99t.\r\nThey bully you and plot about you; and I\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94And he paused, holding out\r\nhis hands. She turned away and began to leave him. \xe2\x80\x9cYou told me the\r\nother day that you were afraid of your mother,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, following her.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 shook her head. \xe2\x80\x9cI remember; I was sorry afterwards.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were sorry when she came down and put on the thumbscrews. In God\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nname what _is_ it she does to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing. Nothing that you can understand. And now that I have given\r\nyou up, I must not complain of her to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s no reasoning!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cComplain of her, on the contrary.\r\nTell me all about it, frankly and trustfully, as you ought, and we will\r\ntalk it over so satisfactorily that you won\xe2\x80\x99t give me up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 looked down some moments, fixedly; and then, raising\r\nher eyes, she said, \xe2\x80\x9cOne good at least has come of this: I have made\r\nyou judge me more fairly. You thought of me in a way that did me great\r\nhonor; I don\xe2\x80\x99t know why you had taken it into your head. But it left me\r\nno loophole for escape\xe2\x80\x94no chance to be the common, weak creature I am.\r\nIt was not my fault; I warned you from the first. But I ought to have\r\nwarned you more. I ought to have convinced you that I was doomed to\r\ndisappoint you. But I _was_, in a way, too proud. You see what my\r\nsuperiority amounts to, I hope!\xe2\x80\x9d she went on, raising her voice with a\r\ntremor which even then and there Newman thought beautiful. \xe2\x80\x9cI am too\r\nproud to be honest, I am not too proud to be faithless. I am timid and\r\ncold and selfish. I am afraid of being uncomfortable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you call marrying me uncomfortable!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman staring.\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 blushed a little and seemed to say that if begging his\r\npardon in words was impudent, she might at least thus mutely express\r\nher perfect comprehension of his finding her conduct odious. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is not\r\nmarrying you; it is doing all that would go with it. It\xe2\x80\x99s the rupture,\r\nthe defiance, the insisting upon being happy in my own way. What right\r\nhave I to be happy when\xe2\x80\x94when\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94And she paused.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen what?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen others have been most unhappy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat others?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman asked. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat have you to do with any others but\r\nme? Besides you said just now that you wanted happiness, and that you\r\nshould find it by obeying your mother. You contradict yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I contradict myself; that shows you that I am not even\r\nintelligent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are laughing at me!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are mocking me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked at him intently, and an observer might have said that she\r\nwas asking herself whether she might not most quickly end their common\r\npain by confessing that she was mocking him. \xe2\x80\x9cNo; I am not,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\npresently said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGranting that you are not intelligent,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you are\r\nweak, that you are common, that you are nothing that I have believed\r\nyou were\xe2\x80\x94what I ask of you is not heroic effort, it is a very common\r\neffort. There is a great deal on my side to make it easy. The simple\r\ntruth is that you don\xe2\x80\x99t care enough about me to make it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am cold,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, \xe2\x80\x9cI am as cold as that flowing\r\nriver.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman gave a great rap on the floor with his stick, and a long, grim\r\nlaugh. \xe2\x80\x9cGood, good!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried. \xe2\x80\x9cYou go altogether too far\xe2\x80\x94you overshoot\r\nthe mark. There isn\xe2\x80\x99t a woman in the world as bad as you would make\r\nyourself out. I see your game; it\xe2\x80\x99s what I said. You are blackening\r\nyourself to whiten others. You don\xe2\x80\x99t want to give me up, at all; you\r\nlike me\xe2\x80\x94you like me. I know you do; you have shown it, and I have felt\r\nit. After that, you may be as cold as you please! They have bullied\r\nyou, I say; they have tortured you. It\xe2\x80\x99s an outrage, and I insist upon\r\nsaving you from the extravagance of your own generosity. Would you chop\r\noff your hand if your mother requested it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 looked a little frightened. \xe2\x80\x9cI spoke of my mother too\r\nblindly, the other day. I am my own mistress, by law and by her\r\napproval. She can do nothing to me; she has done nothing. She has never\r\nalluded to those hard words I used about her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has made you feel them, I\xe2\x80\x99ll promise you!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s my conscience that makes me feel them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour conscience seems to me to be rather mixed!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Newman,\r\npassionately.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has been in great trouble, but now it is very clear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame\r\nde Cintr\xc3\xa9. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t give you up for any worldly advantage or for any\r\nworldly happiness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you don\xe2\x80\x99t give me up for Lord Deepmere, I know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t pretend, even to provoke you, that I think that. But that\xe2\x80\x99s what\r\nyour mother and your brother wanted, and your mother, at that\r\nvillainous ball of hers\xe2\x80\x94I liked it at the time, but the very thought of\r\nit now makes me rabid\xe2\x80\x94tried to push him on to make up to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho told you this?\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 softly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot Valentin. I observed it. I guessed it. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know at the time\r\nthat I was observing it, but it stuck in my memory. And afterwards, you\r\nrecollect, I saw Lord Deepmere with you in the conservatory. You said\r\nthen that you would tell me at another time what he had said to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was before\xe2\x80\x94before _this_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman; \xe2\x80\x9cand, besides, I think I know. He\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nan honest little Englishman. He came and told you what your mother was\r\nup to\xe2\x80\x94that she wanted him to supplant me; not being a commercial\r\nperson. If he would make you an offer she would undertake to bring you\r\nover and give me the slip. Lord Deepmere isn\xe2\x80\x99t very intellectual, so\r\nshe had to spell it out to him. He said he admired you \xe2\x80\x98no end,\xe2\x80\x99 and\r\nthat he wanted you to know it; but he didn\xe2\x80\x99t like being mixed up with\r\nthat sort of underhand work, and he came to you and told tales. That\r\nwas about the amount of it, wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it? And then you said you were\r\nperfectly happy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see why we should talk of Lord Deepmere,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was not for that you came here. And about my mother, it\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter what you suspect and what you know. When once my mind\r\nhas been made up, as it is now, I should not discuss these things.\r\nDiscussing anything, now, is very idle. We must try and live each as we\r\ncan. I believe you will be happy again; even, sometimes, when you think\r\nof me. When you do so, think this\xe2\x80\x94that it was not easy, and that I did\r\nthe best I could. I have things to reckon with that you don\xe2\x80\x99t know. I\r\nmean I have feelings. I must do as they force me\xe2\x80\x94I must, I must. They\r\nwould haunt me otherwise,\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, with vehemence; \xe2\x80\x9cthey would kill\r\nme!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know what your feelings are: they are superstitions! They are the\r\nfeeling that, after all, though I _am_ a good fellow, I have been in\r\nbusiness; the feeling that your mother\xe2\x80\x99s looks are law and your\r\nbrother\xe2\x80\x99s words are gospel; that you all hang together, and that it\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\npart of the everlasting proprieties that they should have a hand in\r\neverything you do. It makes my blood boil. That _is_ cold; you are\r\nright. And what I feel here,\xe2\x80\x9d and Newman struck his heart and became\r\nmore poetical than he knew, \xe2\x80\x9cis a glowing fire!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA spectator less preoccupied than Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s distracted wooer\r\nwould have felt sure from the first that her appealing calm of manner\r\nwas the result of violent effort, in spite of which the tide of\r\nagitation was rapidly rising. On these last words of Newman\xe2\x80\x99s it\r\noverflowed, though at first she spoke low, for fear of her voice\r\nbetraying her. \xe2\x80\x9cNo. I was not right\xe2\x80\x94I am not cold! I believe that if I\r\nam doing what seems so bad, it is not mere weakness and falseness. Mr.\r\nNewman, it\xe2\x80\x99s like a religion. I can\xe2\x80\x99t tell you\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t! It\xe2\x80\x99s cruel of\r\nyou to insist. I don\xe2\x80\x99t see why I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ask you to believe me\xe2\x80\x94and\r\npity me. It\xe2\x80\x99s like a religion. There\xe2\x80\x99s a curse upon the house; I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow what\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know why\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t ask me. We must all bear it. I have\r\nbeen too selfish; I wanted to escape from it. You offered me a great\r\nchance\xe2\x80\x94besides my liking you. It seemed good to change completely, to\r\nbreak, to go away. And then I admired you. But I can\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94it has overtaken\r\nand come back to me.\xe2\x80\x9d Her self-control had now completely abandoned\r\nher, and her words were broken with long sobs. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy do such dreadful\r\nthings happen to us\xe2\x80\x94why is my brother Valentin killed, like a beast in\r\nthe midst of his youth and his gaiety and his brightness and all that\r\nwe loved him for? Why are there things I can\xe2\x80\x99t ask about\xe2\x80\x94that I am\r\nafraid to know? Why are there places I can\xe2\x80\x99t look at, sounds I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhear? I was made to be happy in a quiet, natural way.\xe2\x80\x9d At this\r\nNewman gave a most expressive groan, but Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 went on. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwas made to do gladly and gratefully what is expected of me. My mother\r\nhas always been very good to me; that\xe2\x80\x99s all I can say. I must not judge\r\nher; I must not criticize her. If I did, it would come back to me. I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t change!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, bitterly; \xe2\x80\x9c_I_ must change\xe2\x80\x94if I break in two in the\r\neffort!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are different.
