You were born\xe2\x80\x94you were trained, to changes.\r\nBesides\xe2\x80\x94besides, I shall always think of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care for that!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are cruel\xe2\x80\x94you are terribly\r\ncruel. God forgive you! You may have the best reasons and the finest\r\nfeelings in the world; that makes no difference. You are a mystery to\r\nme; I don\xe2\x80\x99t see how such hardness can go with such loveliness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 fixed him a moment with her swimming eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nbelieve I am hard, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman answered her look, and then broke out, \xe2\x80\x9cYou are a perfect,\r\nfaultless creature! Stay by me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I am hard,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on. \xe2\x80\x9cWhenever we give pain we are hard.\r\nAnd we _must_ give pain; that\xe2\x80\x99s the world,\xe2\x80\x94the hateful, miserable\r\nworld! Ah!\xe2\x80\x9d and she gave a long, deep sigh, \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t even say I am glad\r\nto have known you\xe2\x80\x94though I am. That too is to wrong you. I can say\r\nnothing that is not cruel. Therefore let us part, without more of this.\r\nGood-bye!\xe2\x80\x9d And she put out her hand.\r\n\r\nNewman stood and looked at it without taking it, and raised his eyes to\r\nher face. He felt, himself, like shedding tears of rage. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you\r\ngoing to do?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere are you going?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere I shall give no more pain and suspect no more evil. I am going\r\nout of the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOut of the world?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am going into a convent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cInto a convent!\xe2\x80\x9d Newman repeated the words with the deepest dismay; it\r\nwas as if she had said she was going into an hospital. \xe2\x80\x9cInto a\r\nconvent\xe2\x80\x94_you!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told you that it was not for my worldly advantage or pleasure I was\r\nleaving you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut still Newman hardly understood. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are going to be a nun,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nwent on, \xe2\x80\x9cin a cell\xe2\x80\x94for life\xe2\x80\x94with a gown and white veil?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA nun\xe2\x80\x94a Carmelite nun,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. \xe2\x80\x9cFor life, with God\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nleave.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe idea struck Newman as too dark and horrible for belief, and made\r\nhim feel as he would have done if she had told him that she was going\r\nto mutilate her beautiful face, or drink some potion that would make\r\nher mad. He clasped his hands and began to tremble, visibly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, don\xe2\x80\x99t, don\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI beseech you! On my knees,\r\nif you like, I\xe2\x80\x99ll beseech you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe laid her hand upon his arm, with a tender, pitying, almost\r\nreassuring gesture. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t understand,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have wrong\r\nideas. It\xe2\x80\x99s nothing horrible. It is only peace and safety. It is to be\r\nout of the world, where such troubles as this come to the innocent, to\r\nthe best. And for life\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s the blessing of it! They can\xe2\x80\x99t begin\r\nagain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman dropped into a chair and sat looking at her with a long,\r\ninarticulate murmur. That this superb woman, in whom he had seen all\r\nhuman grace and household force, should turn from him and all the\r\nbrightness that he offered her\xe2\x80\x94him and his future and his fortune and\r\nhis fidelity\xe2\x80\x94to muffle herself in ascetic rags and entomb herself in a\r\ncell was a confounding combination of the inexorable and the grotesque.\r\nAs the image deepened before him the grotesque seemed to expand and\r\noverspread it; it was a reduction to the absurd of the trial to which\r\nhe was subjected. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x94you a nun!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed; \xe2\x80\x9cyou with your beauty\r\ndefaced\xe2\x80\x94you behind locks and bars! Never, never, if I can prevent it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAnd he sprang to his feet with a violent laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t prevent it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, \xe2\x80\x9cand it ought\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nlittle\xe2\x80\x94to satisfy you. Do you suppose I will go on living in the world,\r\nstill beside you, and yet not with you? It is all arranged. Good-bye,\r\ngood-bye.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis time he took her hand, took it in both his own. \xe2\x80\x9cForever?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. Her lips made an inaudible movement and his own uttered a deep\r\nimprecation. She closed her eyes, as if with the pain of hearing it;\r\nthen he drew her towards him and clasped her to his breast. He kissed\r\nher white face; for an instant she resisted and for a moment she\r\nsubmitted; then, with force, she disengaged herself and hurried away\r\nover the long shining floor. The next moment the door closed behind\r\nher.\r\n\r\nNewman made his way out as he could.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXI\r\n\r\n\r\nThere is a pretty public walk at Poitiers, laid out upon the crest of\r\nthe high hill around which the little city clusters, planted with thick\r\ntrees and looking down upon the fertile fields in which the old English\r\nprinces fought for their right and held it. Newman paced up and down\r\nthis quiet promenade for the greater part of the next day and let his\r\neyes wander over the historic prospect; but he would have been sadly at\r\na loss to tell you afterwards whether the latter was made up of\r\ncoal-fields or of vineyards. He was wholly given up to his grievance,\r\nof which reflection by no means diminished the weight. He feared that\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 was irretrievably lost; and yet, as he would have said\r\nhimself, he didn\xe2\x80\x99t see his way clear to giving her up. He found it\r\nimpossible to turn his back upon Fleuri\xc3\xa8res and its inhabitants; it\r\nseemed to him that some germ of hope or reparation must lurk there\r\nsomewhere, if he could only stretch his arm out far enough to pluck it.\r\nIt was as if he had his hand on a door-knob and were closing his\r\nclenched fist upon it: he had thumped, he had called, he had pressed\r\nthe door with his powerful knee and shaken it with all his strength,\r\nand dead, damning silence had answered him. And yet something held him\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x94something hardened the grasp of his fingers. Newman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsatisfaction had been too intense, his whole plan too deliberate and\r\nmature, his prospect of happiness too rich and comprehensive for this\r\nfine moral fabric to crumble at a stroke. The very foundation seemed\r\nfatally injured, and yet he felt a stubborn desire still to try to save\r\nthe edifice. He was filled with a sorer sense of wrong than he had ever\r\nknown, or than he had supposed it possible he should know. To accept\r\nhis injury and walk away without looking behind him was a stretch of\r\ngood-nature of which he found himself incapable. He looked behind him\r\nintently and continually, and what he saw there did not assuage his\r\nresentment. He saw himself trustful, generous, liberal, patient, easy,\r\npocketing frequent irritation and furnishing unlimited modesty. To have\r\neaten humble pie, to have been snubbed and patronized and satirized and\r\nhave consented to take it as one of the conditions of the bargain\xe2\x80\x94to\r\nhave done this, and done it all for nothing, surely gave one a right to\r\nprotest. And to be turned off because one was a commercial person! As\r\nif he had ever talked or dreamt of the commercial since his connection\r\nwith the Bellegardes began\xe2\x80\x94as if he had made the least circumstance of\r\nthe commercial\xe2\x80\x94as if he would not have consented to confound the\r\ncommercial fifty times a day, if it might have increased by a hair\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbreadth the chance of the Bellegardes\xe2\x80\x99 not playing him a trick! Granted\r\nthat being commercial was fair ground for having a trick played upon\r\none, how little they knew about the class so designed and its\r\nenterprising way of not standing upon trifles! It was in the light of\r\nhis injury that the weight of Newman\xe2\x80\x99s past endurance seemed so heavy;\r\nhis actual irritation had not been so great, merged as it was in his\r\nvision of the cloudless blue that overarched his immediate wooing. But\r\nnow his sense of outrage was deep, rancorous, and ever present; he felt\r\nthat he was a good fellow wronged. As for Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s conduct,\r\nit struck him with a kind of awe, and the fact that he was powerless to\r\nunderstand it or feel the reality of its motives only deepened the\r\nforce with which he had attached himself to her. He had never let the\r\nfact of her Catholicism trouble him; Catholicism to him was nothing but\r\na name, and to express a mistrust of the form in which her religious\r\nfeelings had moulded themselves would have seemed to him on his own\r\npart a rather pretentious affectation of Protestant zeal. If such\r\nsuperb white flowers as that could bloom in Catholic soil, the soil was\r\nnot insalubrious. But it was one thing to be a Catholic, and another to\r\nturn nun\xe2\x80\x94on your hand! There was something lugubriously comical in the\r\nway Newman\xe2\x80\x99s thoroughly contemporaneous optimism was confronted with\r\nthis dusky old-world expedient. To see a woman made for him and for\r\nmotherhood to his children juggled away in this tragic travesty\xe2\x80\x94it was\r\na thing to rub one\xe2\x80\x99s eyes over, a nightmare, an illusion, a hoax. But\r\nthe hours passed away without disproving the thing, and leaving him\r\nonly the after-sense of the vehemence with which he had embraced Madame\r\nde Cintr\xc3\xa9. He remembered her words and her looks; he turned them over\r\nand tried to shake the mystery out of them and to infuse them with an\r\nendurable meaning. What had she meant by her feeling being a kind of\r\nreligion? It was the religion simply of the family laws, the religion\r\nof which her implacable little mother was the high priestess. Twist the\r\nthing about as her generosity would, the one certain fact was that they\r\nhad used force against her. Her generosity had tried to screen them,\r\nbut Newman\xe2\x80\x99s heart rose into his throat at the thought that they should\r\ngo scot-free.\r\n\r\nThe twenty-four hours wore themselves away, and the next morning Newman\r\nsprang to his feet with the resolution to return to Fleuri\xc3\xa8res and\r\ndemand another interview with Madame de Bellegarde and her son. He lost\r\nno time in putting it into practice. As he rolled swiftly over the\r\nexcellent road in the little cal\xc3\xa8che furnished him at the inn at\r\nPoitiers, he drew forth, as it were, from the very safe place in his\r\nmind to which he had consigned it, the last information given him by\r\npoor Valentin. Valentin had told him he could do something with it, and\r\nNewman thought it would be well to have it at hand. This was of course\r\nnot the first time, lately, that Newman had given it his attention. It\r\nwas information in the rough,\xe2\x80\x94it was dark and puzzling; but Newman was\r\nneither helpless nor afraid. Valentin had evidently meant to put him in\r\npossession of a powerful instrument, though he could not be said to\r\nhave placed the handle very securely within his grasp. But if he had\r\nnot really told him the secret, he had at least given him the clew to\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94a clew of which that queer old Mrs. Bread held the other end. Mrs.\r\nBread had always looked to Newman as if she knew secrets; and as he\r\napparently enjoyed her esteem, he suspected she might be induced to\r\nshare her knowledge with him. So long as there was only Mrs. Bread to\r\ndeal with, he felt easy. As to what there was to find out, he had only\r\none fear\xe2\x80\x94that it might not be bad enough. Then, when the image of the\r\nmarquise and her son rose before him again, standing side by side, the\r\nold woman\xe2\x80\x99s hand in Urbain\xe2\x80\x99s arm, and the same cold, unsociable\r\nfixedness in the eyes of each, he cried out to himself that the fear\r\nwas groundless. There was blood in the secret at the very least! He\r\narrived at Fleuri\xc3\xa8res almost in a state of elation; he had satisfied\r\nhimself, logically, that in the presence of his threat of exposure they\r\nwould, as he mentally phrased it, rattle down like unwound buckets. He\r\nremembered indeed that he must first catch his hare\xe2\x80\x94first ascertain\r\nwhat there was to expose; but after that, why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t his happiness\r\nbe as good as new again? Mother and son would drop their lovely victim\r\nin terror and take to hiding, and Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9, left to herself,\r\nwould surely come back to him. Give her a chance and she would rise to\r\nthe surface, return to the light. How could she fail to perceive that\r\nhis house would be much the most comfortable sort of convent?\r\n\r\nNewman, as he had done before, left his conveyance at the inn and\r\nwalked the short remaining distance to the ch\xc3\xa2teau. When he reached the\r\ngate, however, a singular feeling took possession of him\xe2\x80\x94a feeling\r\nwhich, strange as it may seem, had its source in its unfathomable good\r\nnature. He stood there a while, looking through the bars at the large,\r\ntime-stained face of the edifice, and wondering to what crime it was\r\nthat the dark old house, with its flowery name, had given convenient\r\noccasion. It had given occasion, first and last, to tyrannies and\r\nsufferings enough, Newman said to himself; it was an evil-looking place\r\nto live in. Then, suddenly, came the reflection\xe2\x80\x94What a horrible\r\nrubbish-heap of iniquity to fumble in! The attitude of inquisitor\r\nturned its ignobler face, and with the same movement Newman declared\r\nthat the Bellegardes should have another chance. He would appeal once\r\nmore directly to their sense of fairness, and not to their fear, and if\r\nthey should be accessible to reason, he need know nothing worse about\r\nthem than what he already knew. That was bad enough.\r\n\r\nThe gate-keeper let him in through the same stiff crevice as before,\r\nand he passed through the court and over the little rustic bridge on\r\nthe moat. The door was opened before he had reached it, and, as if to\r\nput his clemency to rout with the suggestion of a richer opportunity,\r\nMrs. Bread stood there awaiting him. Her face, as usual, looked as\r\nhopelessly blank as the tide-smoothed sea-sand, and her black garments\r\nseemed of an intenser sable. Newman had already learned that her\r\nstrange inexpressiveness could be a vehicle for emotion, and he was not\r\nsurprised at the muffled vivacity with which she whispered, \xe2\x80\x9cI thought\r\nyou would try again, sir. I was looking out for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad to see you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman; \xe2\x80\x9cI think you are my friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread looked at him opaquely. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish you well sir; but it\xe2\x80\x99s vain\r\nwishing now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know, then, how they have treated me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread, dryly, \xe2\x80\x9cI know everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman hesitated a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cEverything?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread gave him a glance somewhat more lucent. \xe2\x80\x9cI know at least too\r\nmuch, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne can never know too much. I congratulate you. I have come to see\r\nMadame de Bellegarde and her son,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman added. \xe2\x80\x9cAre they at home? If\r\nthey are not, I will wait.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy lady is always at home,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Bread replied, \xe2\x80\x9cand the marquis is\r\nmostly with her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease then tell them\xe2\x80\x94one or the other, or both\xe2\x80\x94that I am here and\r\nthat I desire to see them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread hesitated. \xe2\x80\x9cMay I take a great liberty, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have never taken a liberty but you have justified it,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nNewman, with diplomatic urbanity.\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread dropped her wrinkled eyelids as if she were curtseying; but\r\nthe curtsey stopped there; the occasion was too grave. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have come\r\nto plead with them again, sir? Perhaps you don\xe2\x80\x99t know this\xe2\x80\x94that Madame\r\nde Cintr\xc3\xa9 returned this morning to Paris.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, she\xe2\x80\x99s gone!\xe2\x80\x9d And Newman, groaning, smote the pavement with his\r\nstick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has gone straight to the convent\xe2\x80\x94the Carmelites they call it. I\r\nsee you know, sir. My lady and the marquis take it very ill. It was\r\nonly last night she told them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, she had kept it back, then?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cGood, good! And they\r\nare very fierce?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are not pleased,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread. \xe2\x80\x9cBut they may well dislike it.\r\nThey tell me it\xe2\x80\x99s most dreadful, sir; of all the nuns in Christendom\r\nthe Carmelites are the worst. You may say they are really not human,\r\nsir; they make you give up everything\xe2\x80\x94forever. And to think of _her_\r\nthere! If I was one that cried, sir, I could cry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman looked at her an instant. \xe2\x80\x9cWe mustn\xe2\x80\x99t cry, Mrs. Bread; we must\r\nact. Go and call them!\xe2\x80\x9d And he made a movement to enter farther.\r\n\r\nBut Mrs. Bread gently checked him. \xe2\x80\x9cMay I take another liberty? I am\r\ntold you were with my dearest Mr. Valentin, in his last hours. If you\r\nwould tell me a word about him! The poor count was my own boy, sir; for\r\nthe first year of his life he was hardly out of my arms; I taught him\r\nto speak. And the count spoke so well, sir! He always spoke well to his\r\npoor old Bread. When he grew up and took his pleasure he always had a\r\nkind word for me. And to die in that wild way! They have a story that\r\nhe fought with a wine-merchant. I can\xe2\x80\x99t believe that, sir! And was he\r\nin great pain?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are a wise, kind old woman, Mrs. Bread,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI hoped I\r\nmight see you with my own children in your arms. Perhaps I shall, yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAnd he put out his hand. Mrs. Bread looked for a moment at his open\r\npalm, and then, as if fascinated by the novelty of the gesture,\r\nextended her own ladylike fingers. Newman held her hand firmly and\r\ndeliberately, fixing his eyes upon her. \xe2\x80\x9cYou want to know all about Mr.\r\nValentin?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be a sad pleasure, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can tell you everything. Can you sometimes leave this place?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe ch\xc3\xa2teau, sir? I really don\xe2\x80\x99t know. I never tried.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTry, then; try hard. Try this evening, at dusk. Come to me in the old\r\nruin there on the hill, in the court before the church. I will wait for\r\nyou there; I have something very important to tell you. An old woman\r\nlike you can do as she pleases.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread stared, wondering, with parted lips. \xe2\x80\x9cIs it from the count,\r\nsir?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom the count\xe2\x80\x94from his death-bed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will come, then. I will be bold, for once, for _him_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe led Newman into the great drawing-room with which he had already\r\nmade acquaintance, and retired to execute his commands. Newman waited a\r\nlong time; at last he was on the point of ringing and repeating his\r\nrequest. He was looking round him for a bell when the marquis came in\r\nwith his mother on his arm. It will be seen that Newman had a logical\r\nmind when I say that he declared to himself, in perfect good faith, as\r\na result of Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s dark hints, that his adversaries looked grossly\r\nwicked. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is no mistake about it now,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself as they\r\nadvanced. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re a bad lot; they have pulled off the mask.\xe2\x80\x9d Madame de\r\nBellegarde and her son certainly bore in their faces the signs of\r\nextreme perturbation; they looked like people who had passed a\r\nsleepless night. Confronted, moreover, with an annoyance which they\r\nhoped they had disposed of, it was not natural that they should have\r\nany very tender glances to bestow upon Newman. He stood before them,\r\nand such eye-beams as they found available they leveled at him; Newman\r\nfeeling as if the door of a sepulchre had suddenly been opened, and the\r\ndamp darkness were being exhaled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see I have come back,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI have come to try again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be ridiculous,\xe2\x80\x9d said M. de Bellegarde, \xe2\x80\x9cto pretend that we\r\nare glad to see you or that we don\xe2\x80\x99t question the taste of your visit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t talk about taste,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, with a laugh, \xe2\x80\x9cor that will\r\nbring us round to yours! If I consulted my taste I certainly shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncome to see you. Besides, I will make as short work as you please.\r\nPromise me to raise the blockade\xe2\x80\x94to set Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 at liberty\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nI will retire instantly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe hesitated as to whether we would see you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de\r\nBellegarde; \xe2\x80\x9cand we were on the point of declining the honor. But it\r\nseemed to me that we should act with civility, as we have always done,\r\nand I wished to have the satisfaction of informing you that there are\r\ncertain weaknesses that people of our way of feeling can be guilty of\r\nbut once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may be weak but once, but you will be audacious many times,\r\nmadam,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t come however, for conversational\r\npurposes. I came to say this, simply: that if you will write\r\nimmediately to your daughter that you withdraw your opposition to her\r\nmarriage, I will take care of the rest. You don\xe2\x80\x99t want her to turn\r\nnun\xe2\x80\x94you know more about the horrors of it than I do. Marrying a\r\ncommercial person is better than that. Give me a letter to her, signed\r\nand sealed, saying you retract and that she may marry me with your\r\nblessing, and I will take it to her at the convent and bring her out.\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s your chance\xe2\x80\x94I call those easy terms.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe look at the matter otherwise, you know. We call them very hard\r\nterms,\xe2\x80\x9d said Urbain de Bellegarde. They had all remained standing\r\nrigidly in the middle of the room. \xe2\x80\x9cI think my mother will tell you\r\nthat she would rather her daughter should become S\xc5\x93ur Catherine than\r\nMrs. Newman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the old lady, with the serenity of supreme power, let her son make\r\nher epigrams for her. She only smiled, almost sweetly, shaking her head\r\nand repeating, \xe2\x80\x9cBut once, Mr. Newman; but once!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNothing that Newman had ever seen or heard gave him such a sense of\r\nmarble hardness as this movement and the tone that accompanied it.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCould anything compel you?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you know of anything that\r\nwould force you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis language, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquis, \xe2\x80\x9caddressed to people in\r\nbereavement and grief is beyond all qualification.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn most cases,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman answered, \xe2\x80\x9cyour objection would have some\r\nweight, even admitting that Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s present intentions make\r\ntime precious. But I have thought of what you speak of, and I have come\r\nhere to-day without scruple simply because I consider your brother and\r\nyou two very different parties. I see no connection between you. Your\r\nbrother was ashamed of you. Lying there wounded and dying, the poor\r\nfellow apologized to me for your conduct. He apologized to me for that\r\nof his mother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a moment the effect of these words was as if Newman had struck a\r\nphysical blow. A quick flush leaped into the faces of Madame de\r\nBellegarde and her son, and they exchanged a glance like a twinkle of\r\nsteel. Urbain uttered two words which Newman but half heard, but of\r\nwhich the sense came to him as it were in the reverberation of the\r\nsound, \xe2\x80\x9c_Le mis\xc3\xa9rable!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou show little respect for the living,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut at least respect the dead. Don\xe2\x80\x99t profane\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t insult\xe2\x80\x94the memory\r\nof my innocent son.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI speak the simple truth,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman declared, \xe2\x80\x9cand I speak it for a\r\npurpose. I repeat it\xe2\x80\x94distinctly. Your son was utterly disgusted\xe2\x80\x94your\r\nson apologized.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUrbain de Bellegarde was frowning portentously, and Newman supposed he\r\nwas frowning at poor Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s invidious image. Taken by surprise, his\r\nscant affection for his brother had made a momentary concession to\r\ndishonor. But not for an appreciable instant did his mother lower her\r\nflag. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are immensely mistaken, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cMy son was\r\nsometimes light, but he was never indecent. He died faithful to his\r\nname.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou simply misunderstood him,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquis, beginning to rally.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou affirm the impossible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t care for poor Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s apology,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was\r\nfar more painful than pleasant to me. This atrocious thing was not his\r\nfault; he never hurt me, or anyone else; he was the soul of honor. But\r\nit shows how he took it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you wish to prove that my poor brother, in his last moments, was\r\nout of his head, we can only say that under the melancholy\r\ncircumstances nothing was more possible. But confine yourself to that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was quite in his right mind,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, with gentle but\r\ndangerous doggedness; \xe2\x80\x9cI have never seen him so bright and clever. It\r\nwas terrible to see that witty, capable fellow dying such a death. You\r\nknow I was very fond of your brother. And I have further proof of his\r\nsanity,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman concluded.\r\n\r\nThe marquise gathered herself together majestically. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is too\r\ngross!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried. \xe2\x80\x9cWe decline to accept your story, sir\xe2\x80\x94we repudiate\r\nit. Urbain, open the door.\xe2\x80\x9d She turned away, with an imperious motion\r\nto her son, and passed rapidly down the length of the room. The marquis\r\nwent with her and held the door open. Newman was left standing.\r\n\r\nHe lifted his finger, as a sign to M. de Bellegarde, who closed the\r\ndoor behind his mother and stood waiting. Newman slowly advanced, more\r\nsilent, for the moment, than life. The two men stood face to face. Then\r\nNewman had a singular sensation; he felt his sense of injury almost\r\nbrimming over into jocularity. \xe2\x80\x9cCome,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cyou don\xe2\x80\x99t treat me\r\nwell; at least admit that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nM. de Bellegarde looked at him from head to foot, and then, in the most\r\ndelicate, best-bred voice, \xe2\x80\x9cI detest you personally,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the way I feel to you, but for politeness sake I don\xe2\x80\x99t say it,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s singular I should want so much to be your\r\nbrother-in-law, but I can\xe2\x80\x99t give it up. Let me try once more.\xe2\x80\x9d And he\r\npaused a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have a secret\xe2\x80\x94you have a skeleton in the closet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nM. de Bellegarde continued to look at him hard, but Newman could not\r\nsee whether his eyes betrayed anything; the look of his eyes was always\r\nso strange. Newman paused again, and then went on. \xe2\x80\x9cYou and your mother\r\nhave committed a crime.\xe2\x80\x9d At this M. de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s eyes certainly did\r\nchange; they seemed to flicker, like blown candles. Newman could see\r\nthat he was profoundly startled; but there was something admirable in\r\nhis self-control.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cContinue,\xe2\x80\x9d said M. de Bellegarde.\r\n\r\nNewman lifted a finger and made it waver a little in the air. \xe2\x80\x9cNeed I\r\ncontinue? You are trembling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray where did you obtain this interesting information?\xe2\x80\x9d M. de\r\nBellegarde asked, very softly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be strictly accurate,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t pretend to know\r\nmore than I do. At present that is all I know. You have done something\r\nthat you must hide, something that would damn you if it were known,\r\nsomething that would disgrace the name you are so proud of. I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow what it is, but I can find out. Persist in your present course and\r\nI _will_ find out. Change it, let your sister go in peace, and I will\r\nleave you alone. It\xe2\x80\x99s a bargain?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe marquis almost succeeded in looking untroubled; the breaking up of\r\nthe ice in his handsome countenance was an operation that was\r\nnecessarily gradual. But Newman\xe2\x80\x99s mildly-syllabled argumentation seemed\r\nto press, and press, and presently he averted his eyes. He stood some\r\nmoments, reflecting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy brother told you this,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, looking up.\r\n\r\nNewman hesitated a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, your brother told me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe marquis smiled, handsomely. \xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t I say that he was out of his\r\nmind?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was out of his mind if I don\xe2\x80\x99t find out. He was very much in it if\r\nI do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nM. de Bellegarde gave a shrug. \xe2\x80\x9cEh, sir, find out or not, as you\r\nplease.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t frighten you?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s for you to judge.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it\xe2\x80\x99s for you to judge, at your leisure. Think it over, feel\r\nyourself all round. I will give you an hour or two. I can\xe2\x80\x99t give you\r\nmore, for how do we know how fast they may be making Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 a\r\nnun? Talk it over with your mother; let her judge whether she is\r\nfrightened. I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe she is as easily frightened, in general, as\r\nyou; but you will see. I will go and wait in the village, at the inn,\r\nand I beg you to let me know as soon as possible. Say by three o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\r\nA simple _yes_ or _no_ on paper will do. Only, you know, in case of a\r\n_yes_ I shall expect you, this time, to stick to your bargain.\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\nwith this Newman opened the door and let himself out. The marquis did\r\nnot move, and Newman, retiring, gave him another look. \xe2\x80\x9cAt the inn, in\r\nthe village,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated. Then he turned away altogether and passed\r\nout of the house.\r\n\r\nHe was extremely excited by what he had been doing, for it was\r\ninevitable that there should be a certain emotion in calling up the\r\nspectre of dishonor before a family a thousand years old. But he went\r\nback to the inn and contrived to wait there, deliberately, for the next\r\ntwo hours. He thought it more than probable that Urbain de Bellegarde\r\nwould give no sign; for an answer to his challenge, in either sense,\r\nwould be a confession of guilt. What he most expected was silence\xe2\x80\x94in\r\nother words defiance. But he prayed that, as he imagined it, his shot\r\nmight bring them down. It did bring, by three o\xe2\x80\x99clock, a note,\r\ndelivered by a footman; a note addressed in Urbain de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhandsome English hand. It ran as follows:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot deny myself the satisfaction of letting you know that I\r\nreturn to Paris, to-morrow, with my mother, in order that we may see my\r\nsister and confirm her in the resolution which is the most effectual\r\nreply to your audacious pertinacity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHENRI-URBAIN DE BELLEGARDE.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nNewman put the letter into his pocket, and continued his walk up and\r\ndown the inn-parlor. He had spent most of his time, for the past week,\r\nin walking up and down. He continued to measure the length of the\r\nlittle _salle_ of the Armes de France until the day began to wane, when\r\nhe went out to keep his rendezvous with Mrs. Bread. The path which led\r\nup the hill to the ruin was easy to find, and Newman in a short time\r\nhad followed it to the top. He passed beneath the rugged arch of the\r\ncastle wall, and looked about him in the early dusk for an old woman in\r\nblack. The castle yard was empty, but the door of the church was open.\r\nNewman went into the little nave and of course found a deeper dusk than\r\nwithout. A couple of tapers, however, twinkled on the altar and just\r\nenabled him to perceive a figure seated by one of the pillars. Closer\r\ninspection helped him to recognize Mrs. Bread, in spite of the fact\r\nthat she was dressed with unwonted splendor. She wore a large black\r\nsilk bonnet, with imposing bows of crape, and an old black satin dress\r\ndisposed itself in vaguely lustrous folds about her person. She had\r\njudged it proper to the occasion to appear in her stateliest apparel.\r\nShe had been sitting with her eyes fixed upon the ground, but when\r\nNewman passed before her she looked up at him, and then she rose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you a Catholic, Mrs. Bread?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir; I\xe2\x80\x99m a good Church-of-England woman, very Low,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I thought I should be safer in here than outside. I was never out\r\nin the evening before, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall be safer,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cwhere no one can hear us.\xe2\x80\x9d And he\r\nled the way back into the castle court and then followed a path beside\r\nthe church, which he was sure must lead into another part of the ruin.\r\nHe was not deceived. It wandered along the crest of the hill and\r\nterminated before a fragment of wall pierced by a rough aperture which\r\nhad once been a door. Through this aperture Newman passed and found\r\nhimself in a nook peculiarly favorable to quiet conversation, as\r\nprobably many an earnest couple, otherwise assorted than our friends,\r\nhad assured themselves. The hill sloped abruptly away, and on the\r\nremnant of its crest were scattered two or three fragments of stone.\r\nBeneath, over the plain, lay the gathered twilight, through which, in\r\nthe near distance, gleamed two or three lights from the ch\xc3\xa2teau. Mrs.\r\nBread rustled slowly after her guide, and Newman, satisfying himself\r\nthat one of the fallen stones was steady, proposed to her to sit upon\r\nit. She cautiously complied, and he placed himself upon another, near\r\nher.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXII\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very much obliged to you for coming,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman said. \xe2\x80\x9cI hope it\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t get you into trouble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I shall be missed. My lady, in these days, is not fond\r\nof having me about her.\xe2\x80\x9d This was said with a certain fluttered\r\neagerness which increased Newman\xe2\x80\x99s sense of having inspired the old\r\nwoman with confidence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom the first, you know,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, \xe2\x80\x9cyou took an interest in my\r\nprospects. You were on my side. That gratified me, I assure you. And\r\nnow that you know what they have done to me, I am sure you are with me\r\nall the more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have not done well\xe2\x80\x94I must say it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you\r\nmustn\xe2\x80\x99t blame the poor countess; they pressed her hard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would give a million of dollars to know what they did to her!\xe2\x80\x9d cried\r\nNewman.\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread sat with a dull, oblique gaze fixed upon the lights of the\r\nch\xc3\xa2teau. \xe2\x80\x9cThey worked on her feelings; they knew that was the way. She\r\nis a delicate creature. They made her feel wicked. She is only too\r\ngood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, they made her feel wicked,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, slowly; and then he\r\nrepeated it. \xe2\x80\x9cThey made her feel wicked,\xe2\x80\x94they made her feel wicked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThe words seemed to him for the moment a vivid description of infernal\r\ningenuity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was because she was so good that she gave up\xe2\x80\x94poor sweet lady!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nadded Mrs. Bread.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut she was better to them than to me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was afraid,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread, very confidently; \xe2\x80\x9cshe has always\r\nbeen afraid, or at least for a long time. That was the real trouble,\r\nsir. She was like a fair peach, I may say, with just one little speck.\r\nShe had one little sad spot. You pushed her into the sunshine, sir, and\r\nit almost disappeared. Then they pulled her back into the shade and in\r\na moment it began to spread. Before we knew it she was gone. She was a\r\ndelicate creature.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis singular attestation of Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s delicacy, for all its\r\nsingularity, set Newman\xe2\x80\x99s wound aching afresh. \xe2\x80\x9cI see,\xe2\x80\x9d he presently\r\nsaid; \xe2\x80\x9cshe knew something bad about her mother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir, she knew nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread, holding her head very\r\nstiff and keeping her eyes fixed upon the glimmering windows of the\r\nch\xc3\xa2teau.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe guessed something, then, or suspected it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was afraid to know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut _you_ know, at any rate,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\nShe slowly turned her vague eyes upon Newman, squeezing her hands\r\ntogether in her lap. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are not quite faithful, sir. I thought it was\r\nto tell me about Mr. Valentin you asked me to come here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, the more we talk of Mr. Valentin the better,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nexactly what I want. I was with him, as I told you, in his last hour.\r\nHe was in a great deal of pain, but he was quite himself. You know what\r\nthat means; he was bright and lively and clever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, he would always be clever, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd did he know\r\nof your trouble?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he guessed it of himself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what did he say to it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe said it was a disgrace to his name\xe2\x80\x94but it was not the first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord, Lord!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Mrs. Bread.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe said that his mother and his brother had once put their heads\r\ntogether and invented something even worse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have listened to that, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps not. But I _did_ listen, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t forget it. Now I want to\r\nknow what it is they did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread gave a soft moan. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you have enticed me up into this\r\nstrange place to tell you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be alarmed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t say a word that shall be\r\ndisagreeable to you. Tell me as it suits you, and when it suits you.\r\nOnly remember that it was Mr. Valentin\xe2\x80\x99s last wish that you should.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid he say that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe said it with his last breath\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98Tell Mrs. Bread I told you to ask\r\nher.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t he tell you himself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was too long a story for a dying man; he had no breath left in his\r\nbody. He could only say that he wanted me to know\xe2\x80\x94that, wronged as I\r\nwas, it was my right to know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how will it help you, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s for me to decide. Mr. Valentin believed it would, and that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhy he told me. Your name was almost the last word he spoke.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread was evidently awe-struck by this statement; she shook her\r\nclasped hands slowly up and down. \xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cif I\r\ntake a great liberty. Is it the solemn truth you are speaking? I _must_\r\nask you that; must I not, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s no offense. It _is_ the solemn truth; I solemnly swear it. Mr.\r\nValentin himself would certainly have told me more if he had been\r\nable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, sir, if he knew more!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you suppose he did?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s no saying what he knew about anything,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread, with\r\na mild head-shake. \xe2\x80\x9cHe was so mightily clever. He could make you\r\nbelieve he knew things that he didn\xe2\x80\x99t, and that he didn\xe2\x80\x99t know others\r\nthat he had better not have known.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suspect he knew something about his brother that kept the marquis\r\ncivil to him,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman propounded; \xe2\x80\x9che made the marquis feel him. What\r\nhe wanted now was to put me in his place; he wanted to give me a chance\r\nto make the marquis feel _me_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMercy on us!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the old waiting-woman, \xe2\x80\x9chow wicked we all are!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman; \xe2\x80\x9csome of us are wicked, certainly. I am\r\nvery angry, I am very sore, and I am very bitter, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that\r\nI am wicked. I have been cruelly injured. They have hurt me, and I want\r\nto hurt them. I don\xe2\x80\x99t deny that; on the contrary, I tell you plainly\r\nthat it is the use I want to make of your secret.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread seemed to hold her breath. \xe2\x80\x9cYou want to publish them\xe2\x80\x94you\r\nwant to shame them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to bring them down,\xe2\x80\x94down, down, down! I want to turn the tables\r\nupon them\xe2\x80\x94I want to mortify them as they mortified me. They took me up\r\ninto a high place and made me stand there for all the world to see me,\r\nand then they stole behind me and pushed me into this bottomless pit,\r\nwhere I lie howling and gnashing my teeth! I made a fool of myself\r\nbefore all their friends; but I shall make something worse of them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis passionate sally, which Newman uttered with the greater fervor\r\nthat it was the first time he had had a chance to say all this aloud,\r\nkindled two small sparks in Mrs. Bread\xe2\x80\x99s fixed eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you\r\nhave a right to your anger, sir; but think of the dishonor you will\r\ndraw down on Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 is buried alive,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are honor or\r\ndishonor to her? The door of the tomb is at this moment closing behind\r\nher.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it\xe2\x80\x99s most awful,\xe2\x80\x9d moaned Mrs. Bread.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has moved off, like her brother Valentin, to give me room to work.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s as if it were done on purpose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread, apparently impressed by the ingenuity of\r\nthis reflection. She was silent for some moments; then she added, \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nwould you bring my lady before the courts?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe courts care nothing for my lady,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman replied. \xe2\x80\x9cIf she has\r\ncommitted a crime, she will be nothing for the courts but a wicked old\r\nwoman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd will they hang her, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat depends upon what she has done.\xe2\x80\x9d And Newman eyed Mrs. Bread\r\nintently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would break up the family most terribly, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s time such a family should be broken up!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, with a\r\nlaugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd me at my age out of place, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Mrs. Bread.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I will take care of you! You shall come and live with me. You\r\nshall be my housekeeper, or anything you like. I will pension you for\r\nlife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear, dear, sir, you think of everything.\xe2\x80\x9d And she seemed to fall\r\na-brooding.\r\n\r\nNewman watched her a while, and then he said suddenly. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, Mrs. Bread,\r\nyou are too fond of my lady!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked at him as quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have you say that, sir. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t think it any part of my duty to be fond of my lady. I have served\r\nher faithfully this many a year; but if she were to die to-morrow, I\r\nbelieve, before Heaven I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t shed a tear for her.\xe2\x80\x9d Then, after a\r\npause, \xe2\x80\x9cI have no reason to love her!\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Bread added. \xe2\x80\x9cThe most she\r\nhas done for me has been not to turn me out of the house.\xe2\x80\x9d Newman felt\r\nthat decidedly his companion was more and more confidential\xe2\x80\x94that if\r\nluxury is corrupting, Mrs. Bread\xe2\x80\x99s conservative habits were already\r\nrelaxed by the spiritual comfort of this preconcerted interview, in a\r\nremarkable locality, with a free-spoken millionaire. All his native\r\nshrewdness admonished him that his part was simply to let her take her\r\ntime\xe2\x80\x94let the charm of the occasion work. So he said nothing; he only\r\nlooked at her kindly. Mrs. Bread sat nursing her lean elbows. \xe2\x80\x9cMy lady\r\nonce did me a great wrong,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on at last. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has a terrible\r\ntongue when she is vexed. It was many a year ago, but I have never\r\nforgotten it. I have never mentioned it to a human creature; I have\r\nkept my grudge to myself. I dare say I have been wicked, but my grudge\r\nhas grown old with me. It has grown good for nothing, too, I dare say;\r\nbut it has lived along, as I have lived. It will die when I die,\xe2\x80\x94not\r\nbefore!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what _is_ your grudge?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman asked.\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread dropped her eyes and hesitated. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I were a foreigner, sir,\r\nI should make less of telling you; it comes harder to a decent\r\nEnglishwoman. But I sometimes think I have picked up too many foreign\r\nways. What I was telling you belongs to a time when I was much younger\r\nand very different looking to what I am now. I had a very high color,\r\nsir, if you can believe it, indeed I was a very smart lass. My lady was\r\nyounger, too, and the late marquis was youngest of all\xe2\x80\x94I mean in the\r\nway he went on, sir; he had a very high spirit; he was a magnificent\r\nman. He was fond of his pleasure, like most foreigners, and it must be\r\nowned that he sometimes went rather below him to take it. My lady was\r\noften jealous, and, if you\xe2\x80\x99ll believe it, sir, she did me the honor to\r\nbe jealous of me. One day I had a red ribbon in my cap, and my lady\r\nflew out at me and ordered me to take it off. She accused me of putting\r\nit on to make the marquis look at me. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I was\r\nimpertinent, but I spoke up like an honest girl and didn\xe2\x80\x99t count my\r\nwords. A red ribbon indeed! As if it was my ribbons the marquis looked\r\nat! My lady knew afterwards that I was perfectly respectable, but she\r\nnever said a word to show that she believed it. But the marquis did!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nMrs. Bread presently added, \xe2\x80\x9cI took off my red ribbon and put it away\r\nin a drawer, where I have kept it to this day. It\xe2\x80\x99s faded now, it\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nvery pale pink; but there it lies. My grudge has faded, too; the red\r\nhas all gone out of it; but it lies here yet.\xe2\x80\x9d And Mrs. Bread stroked\r\nher black satin bodice.\r\n\r\nNewman listened with interest to this decent narrative, which seemed to\r\nhave opened up the deeps of memory to his companion. Then, as she\r\nremained silent, and seemed to be losing herself in retrospective\r\nmeditation upon her perfect respectability, he ventured upon a short\r\ncut to his goal. \xe2\x80\x9cSo Madame de Bellegarde was jealous; I see. And M. de\r\nBellegarde admired pretty women, without distinction of class. I\r\nsuppose one mustn\xe2\x80\x99t be hard upon him, for they probably didn\xe2\x80\x99t all\r\nbehave so properly as you. But years afterwards it could hardly have\r\nbeen jealousy that turned Madame de Bellegarde into a criminal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread gave a weary sigh. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are using dreadful words, sir, but I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t care now. I see you have your idea, and I have no will of my own.\r\nMy will was the will of my children, as I called them; but I have lost\r\nmy children now. They are dead\xe2\x80\x94I may say it of both of them; and what\r\nshould I care for the living? What is anyone in the house to me\r\nnow\xe2\x80\x94what am I to them? My lady objects to me\xe2\x80\x94she has objected to me\r\nthese thirty years. I should have been glad to be something to young\r\nMadame de Bellegarde, though I never was nurse to the present marquis.\r\nWhen he was a baby I was too young; they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t trust me with him.\r\nBut his wife told her own maid, Mamselle Clarisse, the opinion she had\r\nof me. Perhaps you would like to hear it, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, immensely,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe said that if I would sit in her children\xe2\x80\x99s schoolroom I should do\r\nvery well for a penwiper! When things have come to that I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I\r\nneed stand upon ceremony.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDecidedly not,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cGo on, Mrs. Bread.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread, however, relapsed again into troubled dumbness, and all\r\nNewman could do was to fold his arms and wait. But at last she appeared\r\nto have set her memories in order. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was when the late marquis was an\r\nold man and his eldest son had been two years married. It was when the\r\ntime came on for marrying Mademoiselle Claire; that\xe2\x80\x99s the way they talk\r\nof it here, you know, sir. The marquis\xe2\x80\x99s health was bad; he was very\r\nmuch broken down. My lady had picked out M. de Cintr\xc3\xa9, for no good\r\nreason that I could see. But there are reasons, I very well know, that\r\nare beyond me, and you must be high in the world to understand them.\r\nOld M. de Cintr\xc3\xa9 was very high, and my lady thought him almost as good\r\nas herself; that\xe2\x80\x99s saying a good deal. Mr. Urbain took sides with his\r\nmother, as he always did. The trouble, I believe, was that my lady\r\nwould give very little money, and all the other gentlemen asked more.\r\nIt was only M. de Cintr\xc3\xa9 that was satisfied. The Lord willed it he\r\nshould have that one soft spot; it was the only one he had. He may have\r\nbeen very grand in his birth, and he certainly was very grand in his\r\nbows and speeches; but that was all the grandeur he had. I think he was\r\nlike what I have heard of comedians; not that I have ever seen one. But\r\nI know he painted his face. He might paint it all he would; he could\r\nnever make me like it! The marquis couldn\xe2\x80\x99t abide him, and declared\r\nthat sooner than take such a husband as that Mademoiselle Claire should\r\ntake none at all. He and my lady had a great scene; it came even to our\r\nears in the servants\xe2\x80\x99 hall. It was not their first quarrel, if the\r\ntruth must be told. They were not a loving couple, but they didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\noften come to words, because, I think, neither of them thought the\r\nother\xe2\x80\x99s doings worth the trouble. My lady had long ago got over her\r\njealousy, and she had taken to indifference. In this, I must say, they\r\nwere well matched. The marquis was very easy-going; he had a most\r\ngentlemanly temper. He got angry only once a year, but then it was very\r\nbad. He always took to bed directly afterwards. This time I speak of he\r\ntook to bed as usual, but he never got up again. I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid the poor\r\ngentleman was paying for his dissipation; isn\xe2\x80\x99t it true they mostly do,\r\nsir, when they get old? My lady and Mr. Urbain kept quiet, but I know\r\nmy lady wrote letters to M. de Cintr\xc3\xa9. The marquis got worse and the\r\ndoctors gave him up. My lady, she gave him up too, and if the truth\r\nmust be told, she gave him up gladly. When once he was out of the way\r\nshe could do what she pleased with her daughter, and it was all\r\narranged that my poor innocent child should be handed over to M. de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9. You don\xe2\x80\x99t know what Mademoiselle was in those days, sir; she\r\nwas the sweetest young creature in France, and knew as little of what\r\nwas going on around her as the lamb does of the butcher. I used to\r\nnurse the marquis, and I was always in his room. It was here at\r\nFleuri\xc3\xa8res, in the autumn. We had a doctor from Paris, who came and\r\nstayed two or three weeks in the house. Then there came two others, and\r\nthere was a consultation, and these two others, as I said, declared\r\nthat the marquis couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be saved. After this they went off, pocketing\r\ntheir fees, but the other one stayed and did what he could. The marquis\r\nhimself kept crying out that he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t die, that he didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to\r\ndie, that he would live and look after his daughter. Mademoiselle\r\nClaire and the viscount\xe2\x80\x94that was Mr. Valentin, you know\xe2\x80\x94were both in\r\nthe house. The doctor was a clever man,\xe2\x80\x94that I could see myself,\xe2\x80\x94and I\r\nthink he believed that the marquis might get well. We took good care of\r\nhim, he and I, between us, and one day, when my lady had almost ordered\r\nher mourning, my patient suddenly began to mend. He got better and\r\nbetter, till the doctor said he was out of danger. What was killing him\r\nwas the dreadful fits of pain in his stomach. But little by little they\r\nstopped, and the poor marquis began to make his jokes again. The doctor\r\nfound something that gave him great comfort\xe2\x80\x94some white stuff that we\r\nkept in a great bottle on the chimney-piece. I used to give it to the\r\nmarquis through a glass tube; it always made him easier. Then the\r\ndoctor went away, after telling me to keep on giving him the mixture\r\nwhenever he was bad. After that there was a little doctor from\r\nPoitiers, who came every day. So we were alone in the house\xe2\x80\x94my lady and\r\nher poor husband and their three children. Young Madame de Bellegarde\r\nhad gone away, with her little girl, to her mothers. You know she is\r\nvery lively, and her maid told me that she didn\xe2\x80\x99t like to be where\r\npeople were dying.\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Bread paused a moment, and then she went on\r\nwith the same quiet consistency. \xe2\x80\x9cI think you have guessed, sir, that\r\nwhen the marquis began to turn my lady was disappointed.\xe2\x80\x9d And she\r\npaused again, bending upon Newman a face which seemed to grow whiter as\r\nthe darkness settled down upon them.\r\n\r\nNewman had listened eagerly\xe2\x80\x94with an eagerness greater even than that\r\nwith which he had bent his ear to Valentin de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s last words.\r\nEvery now and then, as his companion looked up at him, she reminded him\r\nof an ancient tabby cat, protracting the enjoyment of a dish of milk.\r\nEven her triumph was measured and decorous; the faculty of exultation\r\nhad been chilled by disuse. She presently continued. \xe2\x80\x9cLate one night I\r\nwas sitting by the marquis in his room, the great red room in the west\r\ntower. He had been complaining a little, and I gave him a spoonful of\r\nthe doctor\xe2\x80\x99s dose. My lady had been there in the early part of the\r\nevening; she sat far more than an hour by his bed. Then she went away\r\nand left me alone. After midnight she came back, and her eldest son was\r\nwith her. They went to the bed and looked at the marquis, and my lady\r\ntook hold of his hand. Then she turned to me and said he was not so\r\nwell; I remember how the marquis, without saying anything, lay staring\r\nat her. I can see his white face, at this moment, in the great black\r\nsquare between the bed-curtains. I said I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think he was very bad;\r\nand she told me to go to bed\xe2\x80\x94she would sit a while with him. When the\r\nmarquis saw me going he gave a sort of groan, and called out to me not\r\nto leave him; but Mr. Urbain opened the door for me and pointed the way\r\nout. The present marquis\xe2\x80\x94perhaps you have noticed, sir\xe2\x80\x94has a very proud\r\nway of giving orders, and I was there to take orders. I went to my\r\nroom, but I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t easy; I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t tell you why. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t undress; I\r\nsat there waiting and listening. For what, would you have said, sir? I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have told you; for surely a poor gentleman might be\r\ncomfortable with his wife and his son. It was as if I expected to hear\r\nthe marquis moaning after me again. I listened, but I heard nothing. It\r\nwas a very still night; I never knew a night so still. At last the very\r\nstillness itself seemed to frighten me, and I came out of my room and\r\nwent very softly downstairs. In the anteroom, outside of the marquis\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nchamber, I found Mr. Urbain walking up and down. He asked me what I\r\nwanted, and I said I came back to relieve my lady. He said _he_ would\r\nrelieve my lady, and ordered me back to bed; but as I stood there,\r\nunwilling to turn away, the door of the room opened and my lady came\r\nout. I noticed she was very pale; she was very strange. She looked a\r\nmoment at the count and at me, and then she held out her arms to the\r\ncount. He went to her, and she fell upon him and hid her face. I went\r\nquickly past her into the room and to the marquis\xe2\x80\x99s bed. He was lying\r\nthere, very white, with his eyes shut, like a corpse. I took hold of\r\nhis hand and spoke to him, and he felt to me like a dead man. Then I\r\nturned round; my lady and Mr. Urbain were there. \xe2\x80\x98My poor Bread,\xe2\x80\x99 said\r\nmy lady, \xe2\x80\x98M. le Marquis is gone.\xe2\x80\x99 Mr. Urbain knelt down by the bed and\r\nsaid softly, \xe2\x80\x98_Mon p\xc3\xa8re, mon p\xc3\xa8re_.\xe2\x80\x99 I thought it wonderful strange,\r\nand asked my lady what in the world had happened, and why she hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncalled me. She said nothing had happened; that she had only been\r\nsitting there with the marquis, very quiet. She had closed her eyes,\r\nthinking she might sleep, and she had slept, she didn\xe2\x80\x99t know how long.\r\nWhen she woke up he was dead. \xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s death, my son, it\xe2\x80\x99s death,\xe2\x80\x99 she\r\nsaid to the count. Mr. Urbain said they must have the doctor,\r\nimmediately, from Poitiers, and that he would ride off and fetch him.\r\nHe kissed his father\xe2\x80\x99s face, and then he kissed his mother and went\r\naway. My lady and I stood there at the bedside. As I looked at the poor\r\nmarquis it came into my head that he was not dead, that he was in a\r\nkind of swoon. And then my lady repeated, \xe2\x80\x98My poor Bread, it\xe2\x80\x99s death,\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s death;\xe2\x80\x99 and I said, \xe2\x80\x98Yes, my lady, it\xe2\x80\x99s certainly death.\xe2\x80\x99 I said\r\njust the opposite to what I believed; it was my notion. Then my lady\r\nsaid we must wait for the doctor, and we sat there and waited. It was a\r\nlong time; the poor marquis neither stirred nor changed. \xe2\x80\x98I have seen\r\ndeath before,\xe2\x80\x99 said my lady, \xe2\x80\x98and it\xe2\x80\x99s terribly like this.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Yes,\r\nplease, my lady,\xe2\x80\x99 said I; and I kept thinking. The night wore away\r\nwithout the count\xe2\x80\x99s coming back, and my lady began to be frightened.\r\nShe was afraid he had had an accident in the dark, or met with some\r\nwild people. At last she got so restless that she went below to watch\r\nin the court for her son\xe2\x80\x99s return. I sat there alone and the marquis\r\nnever stirred.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere Mrs. Bread paused again, and the most artistic of romancers could\r\nnot have been more effective. Newman made a movement as if he were\r\nturning over the page of a novel. \xe2\x80\x9cSo he _was_ dead!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree days afterwards he was in his grave,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread,\r\nsententiously. \xe2\x80\x9cIn a little while I went away to the front of the house\r\nand looked out into the court, and there, before long, I saw Mr. Urbain\r\nride in alone. I waited a bit, to hear him come upstairs with his\r\nmother, but they stayed below, and I went back to the marquis\xe2\x80\x99s room. I\r\nwent to the bed and held up the light to him, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t know why I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t let the candlestick fall. The marquis\xe2\x80\x99s eyes were open\xe2\x80\x94open\r\nwide! they were staring at me. I knelt down beside him and took his\r\nhands, and begged him to tell me, in the name of wonder, whether he was\r\nalive or dead. Still he looked at me a long time, and then he made me a\r\nsign to put my ear close to him: \xe2\x80\x98I am dead,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, \xe2\x80\x98I am dead. The\r\nmarquise has killed me.\xe2\x80\x99 I was all in a tremble; I didn\xe2\x80\x99t understand\r\nhim. He seemed both a man and a corpse, if you can fancy, sir. \xe2\x80\x98But\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll get well now, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 I said. And then he whispered again, ever so\r\nweak; \xe2\x80\x98I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t get well for a kingdom. I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be that woman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhusband again.\xe2\x80\x99 And then he said more; he said she had murdered him. I\r\nasked him what she had done to him, but he only replied, \xe2\x80\x98Murder,\r\nmurder. And she\xe2\x80\x99ll kill my daughter,\xe2\x80\x99 he said; \xe2\x80\x98my poor unhappy child.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nAnd he begged me to prevent that, and then he said that he was dying,\r\nthat he was dead. I was afraid to move or to leave him; I was almost\r\ndead myself. All of a sudden he asked me to get a pencil and write for\r\nhim; and then I had to tell him that I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t manage a pencil. He\r\nasked me to hold him up in bed while he wrote himself, and I said he\r\ncould never, never do such a thing. But he seemed to have a kind of\r\nterror that gave him strength. I found a pencil in the room and a piece\r\nof paper and a book, and I put the paper on the book and the pencil\r\ninto his hand, and moved the candle near him. You will think all this\r\nvery strange, sir; and very strange it was. The strangest part of it\r\nwas that I believed he was dying, and that I was eager to help him to\r\nwrite. I sat on the bed and put my arm round him, and held him up. I\r\nfelt very strong; I believe I could have lifted him and carried him. It\r\nwas a wonder how he wrote, but he did write, in a big scratching hand;\r\nhe almost covered one side of the paper. It seemed a long time; I\r\nsuppose it was three or four minutes. He was groaning, terribly, all\r\nthe while. Then he said it was ended, and I let him down upon his\r\npillows and he gave me the paper and told me to fold it, and hide it,\r\nand give it to those who would act upon it. \xe2\x80\x98Whom do you mean?\xe2\x80\x99 I said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Who are those who will act upon it?\xe2\x80\x99 But he only groaned, for an\r\nanswer; he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t speak, for weakness. In a few minutes he told me to\r\ngo and look at the bottle on the chimney-piece. I knew the bottle he\r\nmeant; the white stuff that was good for his stomach. I went and looked\r\nat it, but it was empty. When I came back his eyes were open and he was\r\nstaring at me; but soon he closed them and he said no more. I hid the\r\npaper in my dress; I didn\xe2\x80\x99t look at what was written upon it, though I\r\ncan read very well, sir, if I haven\xe2\x80\x99t any handwriting. I sat down near\r\nthe bed, but it was nearly half an hour before my lady and the count\r\ncame in. The marquis looked as he did when they left him, and I never\r\nsaid a word about his having been otherwise. Mr. Urbain said that the\r\ndoctor had been called to a person in childbirth, but that he promised\r\nto set out for Fleuri\xc3\xa8res immediately. In another half hour he arrived,\r\nand as soon as he had examined the marquis he said that we had had a\r\nfalse alarm. The poor gentleman was very low, but he was still living.\r\nI watched my lady and her son when he said this, to see if they looked\r\nat each other, and I am obliged to admit that they didn\xe2\x80\x99t. The doctor\r\nsaid there was no reason he should die; he had been going on so well.\r\nAnd then he wanted to know how he had suddenly fallen off; he had left\r\nhim so very hearty. My lady told her little story again\xe2\x80\x94what she had\r\ntold Mr. Urbain and me\xe2\x80\x94and the doctor looked at her and said nothing.\r\nHe stayed all the next day at the ch\xc3\xa2teau, and hardly left the marquis.\r\nI was always there. Mademoiselle and Mr. Valentin came and looked at\r\ntheir father, but he never stirred. It was a strange, deathly stupor.\r\nMy lady was always about; her face was as white as her husband\xe2\x80\x99s, and\r\nshe looked very proud, as I had seen her look when her orders or her\r\nwishes had been disobeyed. It was as if the poor marquis had defied\r\nher; and the way she took it made me afraid of her. The apothecary from\r\nPoitiers kept the marquis along through the day, and we waited for the\r\nother doctor from Paris, who, as I told you, had been staying at\r\nFleuri\xc3\xa8res. They had telegraphed for him early in the morning, and in\r\nthe evening he arrived. He talked a bit outside with the doctor from\r\nPoitiers, and then they came in to see the marquis together. I was with\r\nhim, and so was Mr. Urbain. My lady had been to receive the doctor from\r\nParis, and she didn\xe2\x80\x99t come back with him into the room. He sat down by\r\nthe marquis; I can see him there now, with his hand on the marquis\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwrist, and Mr. Urbain watching him with a little looking-glass in his\r\nhand. \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m sure he\xe2\x80\x99s better,\xe2\x80\x99 said the little doctor from Poitiers;\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m sure he\xe2\x80\x99ll come back.\xe2\x80\x99 A few moments after he had said this the\r\nmarquis opened his eyes, as if he were waking up, and looked at us,\r\nfrom one to the other. I saw him look at me very softly, as you\xe2\x80\x99d say.\r\nAt the same moment my lady came in on tiptoe; she came up to the bed\r\nand put in her head between me and the count. The marquis saw her and\r\ngave a long, most wonderful moan. He said something we couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nunderstand, and he seemed to have a kind of spasm. He shook all over\r\nand then closed his eyes, and the doctor jumped up and took hold of my\r\nlady. He held her for a moment a bit roughly. The marquis was stone\r\ndead! This time there were those there that knew.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman felt as if he had been reading by starlight the report of highly\r\nimportant evidence in a great murder case. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd the paper\xe2\x80\x94the paper!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said, excitedly. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat was written upon it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t tell you, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mrs. Bread. \xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t read it; it\r\nwas in French.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut could no one else read it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never asked a human creature.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one has ever seen it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you see it you\xe2\x80\x99ll be the first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman seized the old woman\xe2\x80\x99s hand in both his own and pressed it\r\nvigorously. \xe2\x80\x9cI thank you ever so much for that,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried. \xe2\x80\x9cI want to\r\nbe the first, I want it to be my property and no one else\xe2\x80\x99s! You\xe2\x80\x99re the\r\nwisest old woman in Europe. And what did you do with the paper?\xe2\x80\x9d This\r\ninformation had made him feel extraordinarily strong. \xe2\x80\x9cGive it to me\r\nquick!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread got up with a certain majesty. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is not so easy as that,\r\nsir. If you want the paper, you must wait.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut waiting is horrible, you know,\xe2\x80\x9d urged Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure _I_ have waited; I have waited these many years,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\nBread.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is very true. You have waited for me. I won\xe2\x80\x99t forget it. And yet,\r\nhow comes it you didn\xe2\x80\x99t do as M. de Bellegarde said, show the paper to\r\nsomeone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo whom should I show it?\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mrs. Bread, mournfully. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was\r\nnot easy to know, and many\xe2\x80\x99s the night I have lain awake thinking of\r\nit. Six months afterwards, when they married Mademoiselle to her\r\nvicious old husband, I was very near bringing it out. I thought it was\r\nmy duty to do something with it, and yet I was mightily afraid. I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t know what was written on the paper or how bad it might be, and\r\nthere was no one I could trust enough to ask. And it seemed to me a\r\ncruel kindness to do that sweet young creature, letting her know that\r\nher father had written her mother down so shamefully; for that\xe2\x80\x99s what\r\nhe did, I suppose. I thought she would rather be unhappy with her\r\nhusband than be unhappy that way. It was for her and for my dear Mr.\r\nValentin I kept quiet. Quiet I call it, but for me it was a weary\r\nquietness. It worried me terribly, and it changed me altogether. But\r\nfor others I held my tongue, and no one, to this hour, knows what\r\npassed between the poor marquis and me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut evidently there were suspicions,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere did Mr.\r\nValentin get his ideas?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was the little doctor from Poitiers. He was very ill-satisfied, and\r\nhe made a great talk. He was a sharp Frenchman, and coming to the\r\nhouse, as he did day after day, I suppose he saw more than he seemed to\r\nsee. And indeed the way the poor marquis went off as soon as his eyes\r\nfell on my lady was a most shocking sight for anyone. The medical\r\ngentleman from Paris was much more accommodating, and he hushed up the\r\nother. But for all he could do Mr. Valentin and Mademoiselle heard\r\nsomething; they knew their father\xe2\x80\x99s death was somehow against nature.\r\nOf course they couldn\xe2\x80\x99t accuse their mother, and, as I tell you, I was\r\nas dumb as that stone. Once I\r\nwent up to him and took a great liberty; I kissed him, as I had kissed\r\nhim when he was a child. \xe2\x80\x98You oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to look so sad, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 I said;\r\n\xe2\x80\x98believe your poor old Bread. Such a gallant, handsome young man can\r\nhave nothing to be sad about.\xe2\x80\x99 And I think he understood me; he\r\nunderstood that I was begging off, and he made up his mind in his own\r\nway. She didn\xe2\x80\x99t know what\r\nhappened; she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t know. My lady and Mr. Urbain asked me no\r\nquestions because they had no reason. I was as still as a mouse. When I\r\nwas younger my lady thought me a hussy, and now she thought me a fool.\r\nHow should I have any ideas?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you say the little doctor from Poitiers made a talk,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid no one take it up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI heard nothing of it, sir. They are always talking scandal in these\r\nforeign countries you may have noticed\xe2\x80\x94and I suppose they shook their\r\nheads over Madame de Bellegarde. But after all, what could they say?\r\nThe marquis had been ill, and the marquis had died; he had as good a\r\nright to die as anyone. The doctor couldn\xe2\x80\x99t say he had not come\r\nhonestly by his cramps. The next year the little doctor left the place\r\nand bought a practice in Bordeaux, and if there has been any gossip it\r\ndied out. And I don\xe2\x80\x99t think there could have been much gossip about my\r\nlady that anyone would listen to. My lady is so very respectable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman, at this last affirmation, broke into an immense, resounding\r\nlaugh. Mrs. Bread had begun to move away from the spot where they were\r\nsitting, and he helped her through the aperture in the wall and along\r\nthe homeward path. \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cmy lady\xe2\x80\x99s respectability is\r\ndelicious; it will be a great crash!\xe2\x80\x9d They reached the empty space in\r\nfront of the church, where they stopped a moment, looking at each other\r\nwith something of an air of closer fellowship\xe2\x80\x94like two sociable\r\nconspirators. \xe2\x80\x9cBut what was it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat was it she did to\r\nher husband? She didn\xe2\x80\x99t stab him or poison him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know, sir; no one saw it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnless it was Mr. Urbain. You say he was walking up and down, outside\r\nthe room. Perhaps he looked through the keyhole. But no; I think that\r\nwith his mother he would take it on trust.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may be sure I have often thought of it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread. \xe2\x80\x9cI am\r\nsure she didn\xe2\x80\x99t touch him with her hands. I saw nothing on him,\r\nanywhere. I believe it was in this way. He had a fit of his great pain,\r\nand he asked her for his medicine. Instead of giving it to him she went\r\nand poured it away, before his eyes. Then he saw what she meant, and,\r\nweak and helpless as he was, he was frightened, he was terrified. \xe2\x80\x98You\r\nwant to kill me,\xe2\x80\x99 he said. \xe2\x80\x98Yes, M. le Marquis, I want to kill you,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsays my lady, and sits down and fixes her eyes upon him.
