It was like a frost on\r\nflowers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you are a very intelligent woman; you have shown great\r\ndiscretion,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall value your services as housekeeper\r\nextremely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey had begun to descend the hill, and Mrs. Bread said nothing until\r\nthey reached the foot. Newman strolled lightly beside her; his head was\r\nthrown back and he was gazing at all the stars; he seemed to himself to\r\nbe riding his vengeance along the Milky Way. \xe2\x80\x9cSo you are serious, sir,\r\nabout that?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread, softly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout your living with me? Why of course I will take care of you to\r\nthe end of your days. You can\xe2\x80\x99t live with those people any longer. And\r\nyou oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to, you know, after this. You give me the paper, and you\r\nmove away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems very flighty in me to be taking a new place at this time of\r\nlife,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Mrs. Bread, lugubriously. \xe2\x80\x9cBut if you are going to turn\r\nthe house upside down, I would rather be out of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, in the cheerful tone of a man who feels rich in\r\nalternatives. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I shall bring in the constables, if that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhat you mean. Whatever Madame de Bellegarde did, I am afraid the law\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t take hold of it. But I am glad of that; it leaves it altogether\r\nto me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are a mighty bold gentleman, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Mrs. Bread, looking at\r\nhim round the edge of her great bonnet.\r\n\r\nHe walked with her back to the ch\xc3\xa2teau; the curfew had tolled for the\r\nlaborious villagers of Fleuri\xc3\xa8res, and the street was unlighted and\r\nempty. She promised him that he should have the marquis\xe2\x80\x99s manuscript in\r\nhalf an hour. Mrs. Bread choosing not to go in by the great gate, they\r\npassed round by a winding lane to a door in the wall of the park, of\r\nwhich she had the key, and which would enable her to enter the ch\xc3\xa2teau\r\nfrom behind. Newman arranged with her that he should await outside the\r\nwall her return with the coveted document.\r\n\r\nShe went in, and his half hour in the dusky lane seemed very long. But\r\nhe had plenty to think about. At last the door in the wall opened and\r\nMrs. Bread stood there, with one hand on the latch and the other\r\nholding out a scrap of white paper, folded small. In a moment he was\r\nmaster of it, and it had passed into his waistcoat pocket. \xe2\x80\x9cCome and\r\nsee me in Paris,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cwe are to settle your future, you know; and\r\nI will translate poor M. de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s French to you.\xe2\x80\x9d Never had he\r\nfelt so grateful as at this moment for M. Nioche\xe2\x80\x99s instructions.\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread\xe2\x80\x99s dull eyes had followed the disappearance of the paper, and\r\nshe gave a heavy sigh. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, you have done what you would with me,\r\nsir, and I suppose you will do it again. You _must_ take care of me\r\nnow. You are a terribly positive gentleman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m a terribly impatient gentleman!\xe2\x80\x9d And he\r\nbade her good-night and walked rapidly back to the inn. He ordered his\r\nvehicle to be prepared for his return to Poitiers, and then he shut the\r\ndoor of the common salle and strode toward the solitary lamp on the\r\nchimney-piece. He pulled out the paper and quickly unfolded it. It was\r\ncovered with pencil-marks, which at first, in the feeble light, seemed\r\nindistinct. But Newman\xe2\x80\x99s fierce curiosity forced a meaning from the\r\ntremulous signs. The English of them was as follows:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy wife has tried to kill me, and she has done it; I am dying, dying\r\nhorribly. It is to marry my dear daughter to M. de Cintr\xc3\xa9. With all my\r\nsoul I protest,\xe2\x80\x94I forbid it. I am not insane,\xe2\x80\x94ask the doctors, ask Mrs.\r\nB\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94. It was alone with me here, to-night; she attacked me and put me to\r\ndeath. It is murder, if murder ever was. Ask the doctors.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHENRI-URBAIN DE BELLEGARDE\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIII\r\n\r\n\r\nNewman returned to Paris the second day after his interview with Mrs.\r\nBread. The morrow he had spent at Poitiers, reading over and over again\r\nthe little document which he had lodged in his pocket-book, and\r\nthinking what he would do in the circumstances and how he would do it.\r\nHe would not have said that Poitiers was an amusing place; yet the day\r\nseemed very short. Domiciled once more in the Boulevard Haussmann, he\r\nwalked over to the Rue de l\xe2\x80\x99Universit\xc3\xa9 and inquired of Madame de\r\nBellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s portress whether the marquise had come back. The portress\r\ntold him that she had arrived, with M. le Marquis, on the preceding\r\nday, and further informed him that if he desired to enter, Madame de\r\nBellegarde and her son were both at home. As she said these words the\r\nlittle white-faced old woman who peered out of the dusky gate-house of\r\nthe H\xc3\xb4tel de Bellegarde gave a small wicked smile\xe2\x80\x94a smile which seemed\r\nto Newman to mean, \xe2\x80\x9cGo in if you dare!\xe2\x80\x9d She was evidently versed in the\r\ncurrent domestic history; she was placed where she could feel the pulse\r\nof the house. Newman stood a moment, twisting his moustache and looking\r\nat her; then he abruptly turned away. But this was not because he was\r\nafraid to go in\xe2\x80\x94though he doubted whether, if he did so, he should be\r\nable to make his way, unchallenged, into the presence of Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s relatives. Confidence\xe2\x80\x94excessive confidence, perhaps\xe2\x80\x94quite as\r\nmuch as timidity prompted his retreat. He was nursing his thunderbolt;\r\nhe loved it; he was unwilling to part with it. He seemed to be holding\r\nit aloft in the rumbling, vaguely-flashing air, directly over the heads\r\nof his victims, and he fancied he could see their pale, upturned faces.\r\nFew specimens of the human countenance had ever given him such pleasure\r\nas these, lighted in the lurid fashion I have hinted at, and he was\r\ndisposed to sip the cup of contemplative revenge in a leisurely\r\nfashion. It must be added, too, that he was at a loss to see exactly\r\nhow he could arrange to witness the operation of his thunder. To send\r\nin his card to Madame de Bellegarde would be a waste of ceremony; she\r\nwould certainly decline to receive him. On the other hand he could not\r\nforce his way into her presence. It annoyed him keenly to think that he\r\nmight be reduced to the blind satisfaction of writing her a letter; but\r\nhe consoled himself in a measure with the reflection that a letter\r\nmight lead to an interview. He went home, and feeling rather\r\ntired\xe2\x80\x94nursing a vengeance was, it must be confessed, a rather fatiguing\r\nprocess; it took a good deal out of one\xe2\x80\x94flung himself into one of his\r\nbrocaded fauteuils, stretched his legs, thrust his hands into his\r\npockets, and, while he watched the reflected sunset fading from the\r\nornate house-tops on the opposite side of the Boulevard, began mentally\r\nto compose a cool epistle to Madame de Bellegarde. While he was so\r\noccupied his servant threw open the door and announced ceremoniously,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadame Brett!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman roused himself, expectantly, and in a few moments perceived upon\r\nhis threshold the worthy woman with whom he had conversed to such good\r\npurpose on the starlit hill-top of Fleuri\xc3\xa8res. Mrs. Bread had made for\r\nthis visit the same toilet as for her former expedition. Newman was\r\nstruck with her distinguished appearance. His lamp was not lit, and as\r\nher large, grave face gazed at him through the light dusk from under\r\nthe shadow of her ample bonnet, he felt the incongruity of such a\r\nperson presenting herself as a servant. He greeted her with high\r\ngeniality and bade her come in and sit down and make herself\r\ncomfortable. There was something which might have touched the springs\r\nboth of mirth and of melancholy in the ancient maidenliness with which\r\nMrs. Bread endeavored to comply with these directions. She was not\r\nplaying at being fluttered, which would have been simply ridiculous;\r\nshe was doing her best to carry herself as a person so humble that, for\r\nher, even embarrassment would have been pretentious; but evidently she\r\nhad never dreamed of its being in her horoscope to pay a visit, at\r\nnight-fall, to a friendly single gentleman who lived in\r\ntheatrical-looking rooms on one of the new Boulevards.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI truly hope I am not forgetting my place, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cForgetting your place?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you are remembering it.\r\nThis is your place, you know. You are already in my service; your\r\nwages, as housekeeper, began a fortnight ago. I can tell you my house\r\nwants keeping! Why don\xe2\x80\x99t you take off your bonnet and stay?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake off my bonnet?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread, with timid literalness. \xe2\x80\x9cOh,\r\nsir, I haven\xe2\x80\x99t my cap. And with your leave, sir, I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t keep house\r\nin my best gown.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind your gown,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, cheerfully. \xe2\x80\x9cYou shall have a\r\nbetter gown than that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread stared solemnly and then stretched her hands over her\r\nlustreless satin skirt, as if the perilous side of her situation were\r\ndefining itself. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, sir, I am fond of my own clothes,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope you have left those wicked people, at any rate,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, sir, here I am!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all I can tell you.\r\nHere I sit, poor Catherine Bread. It\xe2\x80\x99s a strange place for me to be. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know myself; I never supposed I was so bold. But indeed, sir, I\r\nhave gone as far as my own strength will bear me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, come, Mrs. Bread,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, almost caressingly, \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t make\r\nyourself uncomfortable. Now\xe2\x80\x99s the time to feel lively, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe began to speak again with a trembling voice. \xe2\x80\x9cI think it would be\r\nmore respectable if I could\xe2\x80\x94if I could\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and her voice trembled to a\r\npause.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you could give up this sort of thing altogether?\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman\r\nkindly, trying to anticipate her meaning, which he supposed might be a\r\nwish to retire from service.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I could give up everything, sir! All I should ask is a decent\r\nProtestant burial.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBurial!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman, with a burst of laughter. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, to bury you now\r\nwould be a sad piece of extravagance. It\xe2\x80\x99s only rascals who have to be\r\nburied to get respectable. Honest folks like you and me can live our\r\ntime out\xe2\x80\x94and live together. Come! Did you bring your baggage?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy box is locked and corded; but I haven\xe2\x80\x99t yet spoken to my lady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeak to her, then, and have done with it. I should like to have your\r\nchance!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would gladly give it you, sir. I have passed some weary hours in my\r\nlady\xe2\x80\x99s dressing-room; but this will be one of the longest. She will tax\r\nme with ingratitude.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cso long as you can tax her with murder\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, sir, I can\xe2\x80\x99t; not I,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Mrs. Bread.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t mean to say anything about it? So much the better. Leave\r\nthat to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf she calls me a thankless old woman,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread, \xe2\x80\x9cI shall have\r\nnothing to say. But it is better so,\xe2\x80\x9d she softly added. \xe2\x80\x9cShe shall be\r\nmy lady to the last. That will be more respectable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd then you will come to me and I shall be your gentleman,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nNewman; \xe2\x80\x9cthat will be more respectable still!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread rose, with lowered eyes, and stood a moment; then, looking\r\nup, she rested her eyes upon Newman\xe2\x80\x99s face. The disordered proprieties\r\nwere somehow settling to rest. She looked at Newman so long and so\r\nfixedly, with such a dull, intense devotedness, that he himself might\r\nhave had a pretext for embarrassment. At last she said gently, \xe2\x80\x9cYou are\r\nnot looking well, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s natural enough,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cI have nothing to feel well\r\nabout. To be very indifferent and very fierce, very dull and very\r\njovial, very sick and very lively, all at once,\xe2\x80\x94why, it rather mixes\r\none up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread gave a noiseless sigh. \xe2\x80\x9cI can tell you something that will\r\nmake you feel duller still, if you want to feel all one way. About\r\nMadame de Cintr\xc3\xa9.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat can you tell me?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman demanded. \xe2\x80\x9cNot that you have seen her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe shook her head. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, indeed, sir, nor ever shall. That\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\ndullness of it. Nor my lady. Nor M. de Bellegarde.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean that she is kept so close.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClose, close,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread, very softly.\r\n\r\nThese words, for an instant, seemed to check the beating of Newman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nheart. He leaned back in his chair, staring up at the old woman. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\r\nhave tried to see her, and she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94she couldn\xe2\x80\x99t?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe refused\xe2\x80\x94forever! I had it from my lady\xe2\x80\x99s own maid,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\nBread, \xe2\x80\x9cwho had it from my lady. To speak of it to such a person my\r\nlady must have felt the shock. Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 won\xe2\x80\x99t see them now, and\r\nnow is her only chance. A while hence she will have no chance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean the other women\xe2\x80\x94the mothers, the daughters, the sisters; what\r\nis it they call them?\xe2\x80\x94won\xe2\x80\x99t let her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is what they call the rule of the house,\xe2\x80\x94or of the order, I\r\nbelieve,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is no rule so strict as that of the\r\nCarmelites. The bad women in the reformatories are fine ladies to them.\r\nThey wear old brown cloaks\xe2\x80\x94so the _femme de chambre_ told me\xe2\x80\x94that you\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t use for a horse blanket. And the poor countess was so fond of\r\nsoft-feeling dresses; she would never have anything stiff! They sleep\r\non the ground,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Bread went on; \xe2\x80\x9cthey are no better, no\r\nbetter,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and she hesitated for a comparison,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthey are no better than\r\ntinkers\xe2\x80\x99 wives. They give up everything, down to the very name their\r\npoor old nurses called them by. They give up father and mother, brother\r\nand sister,\xe2\x80\x94to say nothing of other persons,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Bread delicately\r\nadded. \xe2\x80\x9cThey wear a shroud under their brown cloaks and a rope round\r\ntheir waists, and they get up on winter nights and go off into cold\r\nplaces to pray to the Virgin Mary. The Virgin Mary is a hard mistress!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread, dwelling on these terrible facts, sat dry-eyed and pale,\r\nwith her hands clasped in her satin lap. Newman gave a melancholy groan\r\nand fell forward, leaning his head on his hands. There was a long\r\nsilence, broken only by the ticking of the great gilded clock on the\r\nchimney-piece.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is this place\xe2\x80\x94where is the convent?\xe2\x80\x9d Newman asked at last,\r\nlooking up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are two houses,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread. \xe2\x80\x9cI found out; I thought you\r\nwould like to know\xe2\x80\x94though it\xe2\x80\x99s poor comfort, I think. One is in the\r\nAvenue de Messine; they have learned that Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 is there.\r\nThe other is in the Rue d\xe2\x80\x99Enfer. That\xe2\x80\x99s a terrible name; I suppose you\r\nknow what it means.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman got up and walked away to the end of his long room. When he came\r\nback Mrs. Bread had got up, and stood by the fire with folded hands.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me this,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cCan I get near her\xe2\x80\x94even if I don\xe2\x80\x99t see her?\r\nCan I look through a grating, or some such thing, at the place where\r\nshe is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt is said that all women love a lover, and Mrs. Bread\xe2\x80\x99s sense of the\r\npre-established harmony which kept servants in their \xe2\x80\x9cplace,\xe2\x80\x9d even as\r\nplanets in their orbits (not that Mrs. Bread had ever consciously\r\nlikened herself to a planet), barely availed to temper the maternal\r\nmelancholy with which she leaned her head on one side and gazed at her\r\nnew employer. She probably felt for the moment as if, forty years\r\nbefore, she had held him also in her arms. \xe2\x80\x9cThat wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t help you,\r\nsir. It would only make her seem farther away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to go there, at all events,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cAvenue de Messine,\r\nyou say? And what is it they call themselves?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCarmelites,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall remember that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Bread hesitated a moment, and then, \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s my duty to tell you\r\nthis, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on. \xe2\x80\x9cThe convent has a chapel, and some people are\r\nadmitted on Sunday to the mass. You don\xe2\x80\x99t see the poor creatures that\r\nare shut up there, but I am told you can hear them sing. It\xe2\x80\x99s a wonder\r\nthey have any heart for singing! Some Sunday I shall make bold to go.\r\nIt seems to me I should know _her_ voice in fifty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman looked at his visitor very gratefully; then he held out his hand\r\nand shook hers. \xe2\x80\x9cThank you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIf anyone can get in, I will.\xe2\x80\x9d A\r\nmoment later Mrs. Bread proposed, deferentially, to retire, but he\r\nchecked her and put a lighted candle into her hand. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are half a\r\ndozen rooms there I don\xe2\x80\x99t use,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, pointing through an open door.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo and look at them and take your choice. You can live in the one you\r\nlike best.\xe2\x80\x9d From this bewildering opportunity Mrs. Bread at first\r\nrecoiled; but finally, yielding to Newman\xe2\x80\x99s gentle, reassuring push,\r\nshe wandered off into the dusk with her tremulous taper. She remained\r\nabsent a quarter of an hour, during which Newman paced up and down,\r\nstopped occasionally to look out of the window at the lights on the\r\nBoulevard, and then resumed his walk. Mrs. Bread\xe2\x80\x99s relish for her\r\ninvestigation apparently increased as she proceeded; but at last she\r\nreappeared and deposited her candlestick on the chimney-piece.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, have you picked one out?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA room, sir? They are all too fine for a dingy old body like me. There\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t one that hasn\xe2\x80\x99t a bit of gilding.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s only tinsel, Mrs. Bread,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you stay there a while\r\nit will all peel off of itself.\xe2\x80\x9d And he gave a dismal smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, sir, there are things enough peeling off already!\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Mrs.\r\nBread, with a head-shake. \xe2\x80\x9cSince I was there I thought I would look\r\nabout me. I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe you know, sir. The corners are most dreadful.\r\nYou do want a housekeeper, that you do; you want a tidy Englishwoman\r\nthat isn\xe2\x80\x99t above taking hold of a broom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman assured her that he suspected, if he had not measured, his\r\ndomestic abuses, and that to reform them was a mission worthy of her\r\npowers. She held her candlestick aloft again and looked around the\r\nsalon with compassionate glances; then she intimated that she accepted\r\nthe mission, and that its sacred character would sustain her in her\r\nrupture with Madame de Bellegarde. With this she curtsied herself away.\r\n\r\nShe came back the next day with her worldly goods, and Newman, going\r\ninto his drawing-room, found her upon her aged knees before a divan,\r\nsewing up some detached fringe. He questioned her as to her\r\nleave-taking with her late mistress, and she said it had proved easier\r\nthan she feared. \xe2\x80\x9cI was perfectly civil, sir, but the Lord helped me to\r\nremember that a good woman has no call to tremble before a bad one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think so!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd does she know you have come to\r\nme?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe asked me where I was going, and I mentioned your name,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\nBread.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did she say to that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe looked at me very hard, and she turned very red. Then she bade me\r\nleave her. I was all ready to go, and I had got the coachman, who is an\r\nEnglishman, to bring down my poor box and to fetch me a cab. But when I\r\nwent down myself to the gate I found it closed. My lady had sent orders\r\nto the porter not to let me pass, and by the same orders the porter\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwife\xe2\x80\x94she is a dreadful sly old body\xe2\x80\x94had gone out in a cab to fetch home\r\nM. de Bellegarde from his club.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman slapped his knee. \xe2\x80\x9cShe _is_ scared! she _is_ scared!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried,\r\nexultantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was frightened too, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Bread, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I was also mightily\r\nvexed. I took it very high with the porter and asked him by what right\r\nhe used violence to an honorable Englishwoman who had lived in the\r\nhouse for thirty years before he was heard of. Oh, sir, I was very\r\ngrand, and I brought the man down. He drew his bolts and let me out,\r\nand I promised the cabman something handsome if he would drive fast.\r\nBut he was terribly slow; it seemed as if we should never reach your\r\nblessed door. I am all of a tremble still; it took me five minutes,\r\njust now, to thread my needle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman told her, with a gleeful laugh, that if she chose she might have\r\na little maid on purpose to thread her needles; and he went away\r\nmurmuring to himself again that the old woman _was_ scared\xe2\x80\x94she _was_\r\nscared!\r\n\r\nHe had not shown Mrs. Tristram the little paper that he carried in his\r\npocket-book, but since his return to Paris he had seen her several\r\ntimes, and she had told him that he seemed to her to be in a strange\r\nway\xe2\x80\x94an even stranger way than his sad situation made natural. Had his\r\ndisappointment gone to his head? He looked like a man who was going to\r\nbe ill, and yet she had never seen him more restless and active. One\r\nday he would sit hanging his head and looking as if he were firmly\r\nresolved never to smile again; another he would indulge in laughter\r\nthat was almost unseemly and make jokes that were bad even for him. If\r\nhe was trying to carry off his sorrow, he at such times really went too\r\nfar. She begged him of all things not to be \xe2\x80\x9cstrange.\xe2\x80\x9d Feeling in a\r\nmeasure responsible as she did for the affair which had turned out so\r\nill for him, she could endure anything but his strangeness. He might be\r\nmelancholy if he would, or he might be stoical; he might be cross and\r\ncantankerous with her and ask her why she had ever dared to meddle with\r\nhis destiny: to this she would submit; for this she would make\r\nallowances. Only, for Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s sake, let him not be incoherent. That\r\nwould be extremely unpleasant. It was like people talking in their\r\nsleep; they always frightened her. And Mrs. Tristram intimated that,\r\ntaking very high ground as regards the moral obligation which events\r\nhad laid upon her, she proposed not to rest quiet until she should have\r\nconfronted him with the least inadequate substitute for Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9 that the two hemispheres contained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cwe are even now, and we had better not open a new\r\naccount! You may bury me some day, but you shall never marry me. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntoo rough. I hope, at any rate,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, \xe2\x80\x9cthat there is nothing\r\nincoherent in this\xe2\x80\x94that I want to go next Sunday to the Carmelite\r\nchapel in the Avenue de Messine. You know one of the Catholic\r\nministers\xe2\x80\x94an abb\xc3\xa9, is that it?\xe2\x80\x94I have seen him here, you know; that\r\nmotherly old gentleman with the big waistband. Please ask him if I need\r\na special leave to go in, and if I do, beg him to obtain it for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Tristram gave expression to the liveliest joy. \xe2\x80\x9cI am so glad you\r\nhave asked me to do something!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried. \xe2\x80\x9cYou shall get into the\r\nchapel if the abb\xc3\xa9 is disfrocked for his share in it.\xe2\x80\x9d And two days\r\nafterwards she told him that it was all arranged; the abb\xc3\xa9 was\r\nenchanted to serve him, and if he would present himself civilly at the\r\nconvent gate there would be no difficulty.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIV\r\n\r\n\r\nSunday was as yet two days off; but meanwhile, to beguile his\r\nimpatience, Newman took his way to the Avenue de Messine and got what\r\ncomfort he could in staring at the blank outer wall of Madame de\r\nCintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s present residence. The street in question, as some travelers\r\nwill remember, adjoins the Parc Monceau, which is one of the prettiest\r\ncorners of Paris. The quarter has an air of modern opulence and\r\nconvenience which seems at variance with the ascetic institution, and\r\nthe impression made upon Newman\xe2\x80\x99s gloomily-irritated gaze by the\r\nfresh-looking, windowless expanse behind which the woman he loved was\r\nperhaps even then pledging herself to pass the rest of her days was\r\nless exasperating than he had feared. The place suggested a convent\r\nwith the modern improvements\xe2\x80\x94an asylum in which privacy, though\r\nunbroken, might be not quite identical with privation, and meditation,\r\nthough monotonous, might be of a cheerful cast. And yet he knew the\r\ncase was otherwise; only at present it was not a reality to him. It was\r\ntoo strange and too mocking to be real; it was like a page torn out of\r\na romance, with no context in his own experience.\r\n\r\nOn Sunday morning, at the hour which Mrs. Tristram had indicated, he\r\nrang at the gate in the blank wall. It instantly opened and admitted\r\nhim into a clean, cold-looking court, from beyond which a dull, plain\r\nedifice looked down upon him. A robust lay sister with a cheerful\r\ncomplexion emerged from a porter\xe2\x80\x99s lodge, and, on his stating his\r\nerrand, pointed to the open door of the chapel, an edifice which\r\noccupied the right side of the court and was preceded by the high\r\nflight of steps. Newman ascended the steps and immediately entered the\r\nopen door. Service had not yet begun; the place was dimly lighted, and\r\nit was some moments before he could distinguish its features. Then he\r\nsaw it was divided by a large close iron screen into two unequal\r\nportions. The altar was on the hither side of the screen, and between\r\nit and the entrance were disposed several benches and chairs. Three or\r\nfour of these were occupied by vague, motionless figures\xe2\x80\x94figures that\r\nhe presently perceived to be women, deeply absorbed in their devotion.\r\nThe place seemed to Newman very cold; the smell of the incense itself\r\nwas cold. Besides this there was a twinkle of tapers and here and there\r\na glow of colored glass. Newman seated himself; the praying women kept\r\nstill, with their backs turned. He saw they were visitors like himself\r\nand he would have liked to see their faces; for he believed that they\r\nwere the mourning mothers and sisters of other women who had had the\r\nsame pitiless courage as Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9. But they were better off\r\nthan he, for they at least shared the faith to which the others had\r\nsacrificed themselves. Three or four persons came in; two of them were\r\nelderly gentlemen. Everyone was very quiet. Newman fastened his eyes\r\nupon the screen behind the altar. That was the convent, the real\r\nconvent, the place where she was. But he could see nothing; no light\r\ncame through the crevices. He got up and approached the partition very\r\ngently, trying to look through. But behind it there was darkness, with\r\nnothing stirring. He went back to his place, and after that a priest\r\nand two altar boys came in and began to say mass.\r\n\r\nNewman watched their genuflections and gyrations with a grim, still\r\nenmity; they seemed aids and abettors of Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s desertion;\r\nthey were mouthing and droning out their triumph. The priest\xe2\x80\x99s long,\r\ndismal intonings acted upon his nerves and deepened his wrath; there\r\nwas something defiant in his unintelligible drawl; it seemed meant for\r\nNewman himself. Suddenly there arose from the depths of the chapel,\r\nfrom behind the inexorable grating, a sound which drew his attention\r\nfrom the altar\xe2\x80\x94the sound of a strange, lugubrious chant, uttered by\r\nwomen\xe2\x80\x99s voices. It began softly, but it presently grew louder, and as\r\nit increased it became more of a wail and a dirge. It was the chant of\r\nthe Carmelite nuns, their only human utterance. It was their dirge over\r\ntheir buried affections and over the vanity of earthly desires. At\r\nfirst Newman was bewildered\xe2\x80\x94almost stunned\xe2\x80\x94by the strangeness of the\r\nsound; then, as he comprehended its meaning, he listened intently and\r\nhis heart began to throb. He listened for Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s voice, and\r\nin the very heart of the tuneless harmony he imagined he made it out.\r\n(We are obliged to believe that he was wrong, inasmuch as she had\r\nobviously not yet had time to become a member of the invisible\r\nsisterhood.) The chant kept on, mechanical and monotonous, with dismal\r\nrepetitions and despairing cadences. It was hideous, it was horrible;\r\nas it continued, Newman felt that he needed all his self-control. He\r\nwas growing more agitated; he felt tears in his eyes. At last, as in\r\nits full force the thought came over him that this confused, impersonal\r\nwail was all that either he or the world she had deserted should ever\r\nhear of the voice he had found so sweet, he felt that he could bear it\r\nno longer. He rose abruptly and made his way out. On the threshold he\r\npaused, listened again to the dreary strain, and then hastily descended\r\ninto the court. As he did so he saw the good sister with the\r\nhigh-colored cheeks and the fanlike frill to her coiffure, who had\r\nadmitted him, was in conference at the gate with two persons who had\r\njust come in. A second glance informed him that these persons were\r\nMadame de Bellegarde and her son, and that they were about to avail\r\nthemselves of that method of approach to Madame de Cintr\xc3\xa9 which Newman\r\nhad found but a mockery of consolation. As he crossed the court M. de\r\nBellegarde recognized him; the marquis was coming to the steps, leading\r\nhis mother. The old lady also gave Newman a look, and it resembled that\r\nof her son. Both faces expressed a franker perturbation, something more\r\nakin to the humbleness of dismay, than Newman had yet seen in them.\r\nEvidently he startled the Bellegardes, and they had not their grand\r\nbehavior immediately in hand. Newman hurried past them, guided only by\r\nthe desire to get out of the convent walls and into the street. The\r\ngate opened itself at his approach; he strode over the threshold and it\r\nclosed behind him. A carriage which appeared to have been standing\r\nthere, was just turning away from the sidewalk. Newman looked at it for\r\na moment, blankly; then he became conscious, through the dusky mist\r\nthat swam before his eyes, that a lady seated in it was bowing to him.\r\nThe vehicle had turned away before he recognized her; it was an ancient\r\nlandau with one half the cover lowered. The lady\xe2\x80\x99s bow was very\r\npositive and accompanied with a smile; a little girl was seated beside\r\nher. He raised his hat, and then the lady bade the coachman stop. The\r\ncarriage halted again beside the pavement, and she sat there and\r\nbeckoned to Newman\xe2\x80\x94beckoned with the demonstrative grace of Madame\r\nUrbain de Bellegarde. Newman hesitated a moment before he obeyed her\r\nsummons, during this moment he had time to curse his stupidity for\r\nletting the others escape him. He had been wondering how he could get\r\nat them; fool that he was for not stopping them then and there! What\r\nbetter place than beneath the very prison walls to which they had\r\nconsigned the promise of his joy? He had been too bewildered to stop\r\nthem, but now he felt ready to wait for them at the gate. Madame\r\nUrbain, with a certain attractive petulance, beckoned to him again, and\r\nthis time he went over to the carriage. She leaned out and gave him her\r\nhand, looking at him kindly, and smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cyou don\xe2\x80\x99t include me in your wrath? I had\r\nnothing to do with it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose _you_ could have prevented it!\xe2\x80\x9d Newman answered in\r\na tone which was not that of studied gallantry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat you say is too true for me to resent the small account it makes\r\nof my influence. I forgive you, at any rate, because you look as if you\r\nhad seen a ghost.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have!\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad, then, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t go in with Madame de Bellegarde and my\r\nhusband. You must have seen them, eh? Was the meeting affectionate? Did\r\nyou hear the chanting? They say it\xe2\x80\x99s like the lamentations of the\r\ndamned. I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t go in: one is certain to hear that soon enough. Poor\r\nClaire\xe2\x80\x94in a white shroud and a big brown cloak! That\xe2\x80\x99s the _toilette_\r\nof the Carmelites, you know. Well, she was always fond of long, loose\r\nthings. But I must not speak of her to you; only I must say that I am\r\nvery sorry for you, that if I could have helped you I would, and that I\r\nthink everyone has been very shabby. I was afraid of it, you know; I\r\nfelt it in the air for a fortnight before it came. When I saw you at my\r\nmother-in-law\xe2\x80\x99s ball, taking it all so easily, I felt as if you were\r\ndancing on your grave. But what could I do? I wish you all the good I\r\ncan think of. You will say that isn\xe2\x80\x99t much! Yes; they have been very\r\nshabby; I am not a bit afraid to say it; I assure you everyone thinks\r\nso. We are not all like that. I am sorry I am not going to see you\r\nagain; you know I think you very good company. I would prove it by\r\nasking you to get into the carriage and drive with me for a quarter of\r\nan hour, while I wait for my mother-in-law. Only if we were\r\nseen\xe2\x80\x94considering what has passed, and everyone knows you have been\r\nturned away\xe2\x80\x94it might be thought I was going a little too far, even for\r\nme. But I shall see you sometimes\xe2\x80\x94somewhere, eh? You know\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94this was\r\nsaid in English\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwe have a plan for a little amusement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman stood there with his hand on the carriage-door listening to this\r\nconsolatory murmur with an unlighted eye. He hardly knew what Madame de\r\nBellegarde was saying; he was only conscious that she was chattering\r\nineffectively. But suddenly it occurred to him that, with her pretty\r\nprofessions, there was a way of making her effective; she might help\r\nhim to get at the old woman and the marquis. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are coming back\r\nsoon\xe2\x80\x94your companions?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are waiting for them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey will hear the mass out; there is nothing to keep them longer.\r\nClaire has refused to see them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to speak to them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman; \xe2\x80\x9cand you can help me, you can\r\ndo me a favor. Delay your return for five minutes and give me a chance\r\nat them. I will wait for them here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Bellegarde clasped her hands with a tender grimace. \xe2\x80\x9cMy poor\r\nfriend, what do you want to do to them? To beg them to come back to\r\nyou? It will be wasted words. They will never come back!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to speak to them, all the same. Pray do what I ask you. Stay\r\naway and leave them to me for five minutes; you needn\xe2\x80\x99t be afraid; I\r\nshall not be violent; I am very quiet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you look very quiet! If they had _le c\xc5\x93ur tendre_ you would move\r\nthem. But they haven\xe2\x80\x99t! However, I will do better for you than what you\r\npropose. The understanding is not that I shall come back for them. I am\r\ngoing into the Parc Monceau with my little girl to give her a walk, and\r\nmy mother-in-law, who comes so rarely into this quarter, is to profit\r\nby the same opportunity to take the air. We are to wait for her in the\r\npark, where my husband is to bring her to us. Follow me now; just\r\nwithin the gates I shall get out of my carriage. Sit down on a chair in\r\nsome quiet corner and I will bring them near you. There\xe2\x80\x99s devotion for\r\nyou! _Le reste vous regarde_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis proposal seemed to Newman extremely felicitous; it revived his\r\ndrooping spirit, and he reflected that Madame Urbain was not such a\r\ngoose as she seemed. He promised immediately to overtake her, and the\r\ncarriage drove away.\r\n\r\nThe Parc Monceau is a very pretty piece of landscape-gardening, but\r\nNewman, passing into it, bestowed little attention upon its elegant\r\nvegetation, which was full of the freshness of spring. He found Madame\r\nde Bellegarde promptly, seated in one of the quiet corners of which she\r\nhad spoken, while before her, in the alley, her little girl, attended\r\nby the footman and the lap-dog, walked up and down as if she were\r\ntaking a lesson in deportment. Newman sat down beside the mamma, and\r\nshe talked a great deal, apparently with the design of convincing him\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x94if he would only see it\xe2\x80\x94poor dear Claire did not belong to the\r\nmost fascinating type of woman. She was too tall and thin, too stiff\r\nand cold; her mouth was too wide and her nose too narrow. She had no\r\ndimples anywhere. And then she was eccentric, eccentric in cold blood;\r\nshe was an Anglaise, after all. Newman was very impatient; he was\r\ncounting the minutes until his victims should reappear. He sat silent,\r\nleaning upon his cane, looking absently and insensibly at the little\r\nmarquise. At length Madame de Bellegarde said she would walk toward the\r\ngate of the park and meet her companions; but before she went she\r\ndropped her eyes, and, after playing a moment with the lace of her\r\nsleeve, looked up again at Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you remember,\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, \xe2\x80\x9cthe promise you made me three weeks\r\nago?\xe2\x80\x9d And then, as Newman, vainly consulting his memory, was obliged to\r\nconfess that the promise had escaped it, she declared that he had made\r\nher, at the time, a very queer answer\xe2\x80\x94an answer at which, viewing it in\r\nthe light of the sequel, she had fair ground for taking offense. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\npromised to take me to Bullier\xe2\x80\x99s after your marriage. After your\r\nmarriage\xe2\x80\x94you made a great point of that. Three days after that your\r\nmarriage was broken off. Do you know, when I heard the news, the first\r\nthing I said to myself? \xe2\x80\x98Oh heaven, now he won\xe2\x80\x99t go with me to\r\nBullier\xe2\x80\x99s!\xe2\x80\x99 And I really began to wonder if you had not been expecting\r\nthe rupture.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my dear lady,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Newman, looking down the path to see if\r\nthe others were not coming.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be good-natured,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde. \xe2\x80\x9cOne must not ask\r\ntoo much of a gentleman who is in love with a cloistered nun. Besides,\r\nI can\xe2\x80\x99t go to Bullier\xe2\x80\x99s while we are in mourning. But I haven\xe2\x80\x99t given\r\nit up for that. The _partie_ is arranged; I have my cavalier. Lord\r\nDeepmere, if you please! He has gone back to his dear Dublin; but a few\r\nmonths hence I am to name any evening and he will come over from\r\nIreland, on purpose. That\xe2\x80\x99s what I call gallantry!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShortly after this Madame de Bellegarde walked away with her little\r\ngirl. Newman sat in his place; the time seemed terribly long. He felt\r\nhow fiercely his quarter of an hour in the convent chapel had raked\r\nover the glowing coals of his resentment. Madame de Bellegarde kept him\r\nwaiting, but she proved as good as her word. At last she reappeared at\r\nthe end of the path, with her little girl and her footman; beside her\r\nslowly walked her husband, with his mother on his arm. They were a long\r\ntime advancing, during which Newman sat unmoved. Tingling as he was\r\nwith passion, it was extremely characteristic of him that he was able\r\nto moderate his expression of it, as he would have turned down a\r\nflaring gas-burner. His native coolness, shrewdness, and\r\ndeliberateness, his life-long submissiveness to the sentiment that\r\nwords were acts and acts were steps in life, and that in this matter of\r\ntaking steps curveting and prancing were exclusively reserved for\r\nquadrupeds and foreigners\xe2\x80\x94all this admonished him that rightful wrath\r\nhad no connection with being a fool and indulging in spectacular\r\nviolence. So as he rose, when old Madame de Bellegarde and her son were\r\nclose to him, he only felt very tall and light. He had been sitting\r\nbeside some shrubbery, in such a way as not to be noticeable at a\r\ndistance; but M. de Bellegarde had evidently already perceived him. His\r\nmother and he were holding their course, but Newman stepped in front of\r\nthem, and they were obliged to pause. He lifted his hat slightly, and\r\nlooked at them for a moment; they were pale with amazement and disgust.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me for stopping you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said in a low tone, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I must profit\r\nby the occasion. I have ten words to say to you. Will you listen to\r\nthem?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe marquis glared at him and then turned to his mother. \xe2\x80\x9cCan Mr.\r\nNewman possibly have anything to say that is worth our listening to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI assure you I have something,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cbesides, it is my duty\r\nto say it. It\xe2\x80\x99s a notification\xe2\x80\x94a warning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour duty?\xe2\x80\x9d said old Madame de Bellegarde, her thin lips curving like\r\nscorched paper. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is your affair, not ours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame Urbain meanwhile had seized her little girl by the hand, with a\r\ngesture of surprise and impatience which struck Newman, intent as he\r\nwas upon his own words, with its dramatic effectiveness. \xe2\x80\x9cIf Mr. Newman\r\nis going to make a scene in public,\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cI will take my\r\npoor child out of the _m\xc3\xaal\xc3\xa9e_. She is too young to see such\r\nnaughtiness!\xe2\x80\x9d and she instantly resumed her walk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had much better listen to me,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman went on. \xe2\x80\x9cWhether you do or\r\nnot, things will be disagreeable for you; but at any rate you will be\r\nprepared.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have already heard something of your threats,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquis,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand you know what we think of them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou think a good deal more than you admit. A moment,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman added in\r\nreply to an exclamation of the old lady. \xe2\x80\x9cI remember perfectly that we\r\nare in a public place, and you see I am very quiet. I am not going to\r\ntell your secret to the passers-by; I shall keep it, to begin with, for\r\ncertain picked listeners. Anyone who observes us will think that we are\r\nhaving a friendly chat, and that I am complimenting you, madam, on your\r\nvenerable virtues.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe marquis gave three short sharp raps on the ground with his stick.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI demand of you to step out of our path!\xe2\x80\x9d he hissed.\r\n\r\nNewman instantly complied, and M. de Bellegarde stepped forward with\r\nhis mother. Then Newman said, \xe2\x80\x9cHalf an hour hence Madame de Bellegarde\r\nwill regret that she didn\xe2\x80\x99t learn exactly what I mean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe marquise had taken a few steps, but at these words she paused,\r\nlooking at Newman with eyes like two scintillating globules of ice.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are like a peddler with something to sell,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with a\r\nlittle cold laugh which only partially concealed the tremor in her\r\nvoice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, not to sell,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman rejoined; \xe2\x80\x9cI give it to you for nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAnd he approached nearer to her, looking her straight in the eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nkilled your husband,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, almost in a whisper. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is, you tried\r\nonce and failed, and then, without trying, you succeeded.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Bellegarde closed her eyes and gave a little cough, which, as\r\na piece of dissimulation, struck Newman as really heroic. \xe2\x80\x9cDear\r\nmother,\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquis, \xe2\x80\x9cdoes this stuff amuse you so much?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe rest is more amusing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cYou had better not lose it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Bellegarde opened her eyes; the scintillations had gone out\r\nof them; they were fixed and dead. But she smiled superbly with her\r\nnarrow little lips, and repeated Newman\xe2\x80\x99s word. \xe2\x80\x9cAmusing? Have I killed\r\nsomeone else?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t count your daughter,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cthough I might! Your\r\nhusband knew what you were doing. I have a proof of it whose existence\r\nyou have never suspected.\xe2\x80\x9d And he turned to the marquis, who was\r\nterribly white\xe2\x80\x94whiter than Newman had ever seen anyone out of a\r\npicture. \xe2\x80\x9cA paper written by the hand, and signed with the name, of\r\nHenri-Urbain de Bellegarde. Written after you, madam, had left him for\r\ndead, and while you, sir, had gone\xe2\x80\x94not very fast\xe2\x80\x94for the doctor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe marquis looked at his mother; she turned away, looking vaguely\r\nround her. \xe2\x80\x9cI must sit down,\xe2\x80\x9d she said in a low tone, going toward the\r\nbench on which Newman had been sitting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you have spoken to me alone?\xe2\x80\x9d said the marquis to Newman,\r\nwith a strange look.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, yes, if I could have been sure of speaking to your mother alone,\r\ntoo,\xe2\x80\x9d Newman answered. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I have had to take you as I could get you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame de Bellegarde, with a movement very eloquent of what he would\r\nhave called her \xe2\x80\x9cgrit,\xe2\x80\x9d her steel-cold pluck and her instinctive appeal\r\nto her own personal resources, drew her hand out of her son\xe2\x80\x99s arm and\r\nwent and seated herself upon the bench. There she remained, with her\r\nhands folded in her lap, looking straight at Newman. The expression of\r\nher face was such that he fancied at first that she was smiling; but he\r\nwent and stood in front of her and saw that her elegant features were\r\ndistorted by agitation. He saw, however, equally, that she was\r\nresisting her agitation with all the rigor of her inflexible will, and\r\nthere was nothing like either fear or submission in her stony stare.\r\nShe had been startled, but she was not terrified. Newman had an\r\nexasperating feeling that she would get the better of him still; he\r\nwould not have believed it possible that he could so utterly fail to be\r\ntouched by the sight of a woman (criminal or other) in so tight a\r\nplace. Madame de Bellegarde gave a glance at her son which seemed\r\ntantamount to an injunction to be silent and leave her to her own\r\ndevices. The marquis stood beside her, with his hands behind him,\r\nlooking at Newman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat paper is this you speak of?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the old lady, with an\r\nimitation of tranquillity which would have been applauded in a veteran\r\nactress.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly what I have told you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cA paper written by your\r\nhusband after you had left him for dead, and during the couple of hours\r\nbefore you returned. You see he had the time; you shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have stayed\r\naway so long. It declares distinctly his wife\xe2\x80\x99s murderous intent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to see it,\xe2\x80\x9d Madame de Bellegarde observed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you might,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman, \xe2\x80\x9cand I have taken a copy.\xe2\x80\x9d And he\r\ndrew from his waistcoat pocket a small, folded sheet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive it to my son,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame de Bellegarde. He had\r\nmore than time to read it, but still he said nothing; he stood staring\r\nat it. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere is the original?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Madame de Bellegarde, in a voice\r\nwhich was really a consummate negation of impatience.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn a very safe place. I have made out\r\na list.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a moment neither Madame de Bellegarde nor her son said a word; the\r\nold lady sat with her eyes upon the ground; M. de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s blanched\r\npupils were fixed upon her face. She was\r\ntoo good-hearted to make use of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old lady appeared for an instant to hesitate, and then, \xe2\x80\x9cShe was my\r\nhusband\xe2\x80\x99s mistress,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, softly. It was only a very superior style of\r\nbrazen assurance. \xe2\x80\x9cWait till she reads the paper!\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself;\r\nand he concluded that he should hear from her soon.\r\n\r\nHe heard sooner than he expected. The next morning, before midday, when\r\nhe was about to give orders for his breakfast to be served, M. de\r\nBellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s card was brought to him. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has read the paper and she\r\nhas passed a bad night,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. The ambassador, at\r\nall events, had passed a bad night, and his faultlessly careful toilet\r\nonly threw into relief the frigid rancor in his eyes and the mottled\r\ntones of his refined complexion. He stood before Newman a moment,\r\nbreathing quickly and softly, and shaking his forefinger curtly as his\r\nhost pointed to a chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat I have come to say is soon said,\xe2\x80\x9d he declared \xe2\x80\x9cand can only be\r\nsaid without ceremony.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am good for as much or for as little as you desire,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman.\r\n\r\nThe marquis looked round the room a moment, and then, \xe2\x80\x9cOn what terms\r\nwill you part with your scrap of paper?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn none!\xe2\x80\x9d And while Newman, with his head on one side and his hands\r\nbehind him sounded the marquis\xe2\x80\x99s turbid gaze with his own, he added,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, that is not worth sitting down about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nM. de Bellegarde meditated a moment, as if he had not heard Newman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nrefusal. \xe2\x80\x9cMy mother and I, last evening,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9ctalked over your\r\nstory. You will be surprised to learn that we think your little\r\ndocument is\xe2\x80\x94a\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and he held back his word a moment\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cis genuine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou forget that with you I am used to surprises!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Newman,\r\nwith a laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe very smallest amount of respect that we owe to my father\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmemory,\xe2\x80\x9d the marquis continued, \xe2\x80\x9cmakes us desire that he should not be\r\nheld up to the world as the author of so\xe2\x80\x94so infernal an attack upon the\r\nreputation of a wife whose only fault was that she had been submissive\r\nto accumulated injury.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I see,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s for your father\xe2\x80\x99s sake.\xe2\x80\x9d And he laughed\r\nthe laugh in which he indulged when he was most amused\xe2\x80\x94a noiseless\r\nlaugh, with his lips closed.\r\n\r\nBut M. de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s gravity held good. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are a few of my\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s particular friends for whom the knowledge of so\xe2\x80\x94so unfortunate\r\nan\xe2\x80\x94inspiration\xe2\x80\x94would be a real grief. Even say we firmly established by\r\nmedical evidence the presumption of a mind disordered by fever, _il en\r\nresterait quelque chose_. At the best it would look ill in him. Very\r\nill!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t try medical evidence,\xe2\x80\x9d said Newman. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t touch the doctors and\r\nthey won\xe2\x80\x99t touch you. I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind your knowing that I have not written\r\nto them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNewman fancied that he saw signs in M. de Bellegarde\xe2\x80\x99s discolored mask\r\nthat this information was extremely pertinent.
